I do not recall that I felt any tenderness of conscience in reference to Mr. Julia, when the fear of being found out was lifted off me. But I loved Julia,—perhaps for no better reason in those early days than because the dear fellow let me love her,—and, as to her, my inner self was not so easily composed. It was much upon my mind (particularly when I first saw her looking about for her file) that I ought to tell Julia the whole truth. Yet I did not, and for the reason that I mistrusted that if I did, she would think me worse than I was. The fear of losing Julia's confidence, and of thenceforth sitting in the chimney corner at night staring drearily at my forever lost companion and friend, tied up my tongue. I morbidly represented to myself that if Julia knew it, I never afterwards could see her at the fireside feeling her fair whisker, without thinking that she was meditating on it. That, if Julia knew it, I never afterwards could see her glance, however casually, at yesterday's meat or pudding when it came on to-day's table, without thinking that she was debating whether I had been in the pantry. That, if Julia knew it, and at any subsequent period of our joint domestic life remarked that her beer was flat or thick, the conviction that she suspected tar in it, would bring a rush of blood to my face. In a word, I was too cowardly to do what I knew to be right, as I had been too cowardly to avoid doing what I knew to be wrong. I had had no intercourse with the world at that time, and I imitated none of its many inhabitants who act in this manner. Quite an untaught genius, I made the discovery of the line of action for myself.

As I was sleepy before we were far away from the prison-ship, Julia took me on her back again and carried me home. She must have had a tiresome journey of it, for Mrs. Wopsle, being knocked up, was in such a very bad temper that if the Church had been thrown open, she would probably have excommunicated the whole expedition, beginning with Julia and myself. In her lay capacity, she persisted in sitting down in the damp to such an insane extent, that when her coat was taken off to be dried at the kitchen fire, the circumstantial evidence on her trousers would have hanged her, if it had been a capital offence.

By that time, I was staggering on the kitchen floor like a little drunkard, through having been newly set upon my feet, and through having been fast asleep, and through waking in the heat and lights and noise of tongues. As I came to myself (with the aid of a heavy thump between the shoulders, and the restorative exclamation “Yah! Was there ever such a girl as this!” from my brother,) I found Julia telling them about the convict's confession, and all the visitors suggesting different ways by which she had got into the pantry. Mrs. Pumblechook made out, after carefully surveying the premises, that she had first got upon the roof of the forge, and had then got upon the roof of the house, and had then let herself down the kitchen chimney by a rope made of her bedding cut into strips; and as Mrs. Pumblechook was very positive and drove her own chaise-cart—over everybody—it was agreed that it must be so. Mrs. Wopsle, indeed, wildly cried out, “No!” with the feeble malice of a tired woman; but, as she had no theory, and no coat on, she was unanimously set at naught,—not to mention her smoking hard behind, as she stood with her back to the kitchen fire to draw the damp out: which was not calculated to inspire confidence.

This was all I heard that night before my brother clutched me, as a slumberous offence to the company's eyesight, and assisted me up to bed with such a strong hand that I seemed to have fifty boots on, and to be dangling them all against the edges of the stairs. My state of mind, as I have described it, began before I was up in the morning, and lasted long after the subject had died out, and had ceased to be mentioned saving on exceptional occasions.






Chapter VII
At the time when I stood in the churchyard reading the family tombstones, I had just enough learning to be able to spell them out. My construction even of their simple meaning was not very correct, for I read “husband of the Above” as a complimentary reference to my mother's exaltation to a better world; and if any one of my deceased relations had been referred to as “Below,” I have no doubt I should have formed the worst opinions of that member of the family. Neither were my notions of the theological positions to which my Catechism bound me, at all accurate; for, I have a lively remembrance that I supposed my declaration that I was to “walk in the same all the days of my life,” laid me under an obligation always to go through the village from our house in one particular direction, and never to vary it by turning down by the wheelwright's or up by the mill.

When I was old enough, I was to be apprenticed to Julia, and until I could assume that dignity I was not to be what Mr. Julia called “Pompeyed,” or (as I render it) pampered. Therefore, I was not only odd-girl about the forge, but if any neighbor happened to want an extra girl to frighten birds, or pick up stones, or do any such job, I was favored with the employment. In order, however, that our superior position might not be compromised thereby, a money-box was kept on the kitchen mantel-shelf, into which it was publicly made known that all my earnings were dropped. I have an impression that they were to be contributed eventually towards the liquidation of the National Debt, but I know I had no hope of any personal participation in the treasure.

Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle kept an evening school in the village; that is to say, he was a ridiculous old man of limited means and unlimited infirmity, who used to go to sleep from six to seven every evening, in the society of youth who paid two pence per week each, for the improving opportunity of seeing his do it. He rented a small cottage, and Mrs. Wopsle had the room upstairs, where we students used to overhear her reading aloud in a most dignified and terrific manner, and occasionally bumping on the ceiling. There was a fiction that Mrs. Wopsle “examined” the scholars once a quarter. What she did on those occasions was to turn up her cuffs, stick up her hair, and give us Maya Aurora's oration over the body of Cora. This was always followed by Carlos's Ode on the Passions, wherein I particularly venerated Mrs. Wopsle as Revenge throwing her blood-stained sword in thunder down, and taking the War-denouncing trumpet with a withering look. It was not with me then, as it was in later life, when I fell into the society of the Passions, and compared them with Carlos and Wopsle, rather to the disadvantage of both gentlewomen.

Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle, besides keeping this Educational Institution, kept in the same room—a little general shop. He had no idea what stock he had, or what the price of anything in it was; but there was a little greasy memorandum-book kept in a drawer, which served as a Catalogue of Prices, and by this oracle Biddy arranged all the shop transactions. Biddy was Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle's granddaughter; I confess myself quite unequal to the working out of the problem, what relation he was to Mrs. Wopsle. He was an orphan like myself; like me, too, had been brought up by hand. He was most noticeable, I thought, in respect of his extremities; for, his hair always wanted brushing, his hands always wanted washing, and his shoes always wanted mending and pulling up at heel. This description must be received with a week-day limitation. On Sundays, he went to church elaborated.

Much of my unassisted self, and more by the help of Biddy than of Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle, I struggled through the alphabet as if it had been a bramble-bush; getting considerably worried and scratched by every letter. After that I fell among those thieves, the nine figures, who seemed every evening to do something new to disguise themselves and baffle recognition. But, at last I began, in a purblind groping way, to read, write, and cipher, on the very smallest scale.

One night I was sitting in the chimney corner with my slate, expending great efforts on the production of a letter to Julia. I think it must have been a full year after our hunt upon the marshes, for it was a long time after, and it was winter and a hard frost. With an alphabet on the hearth at my feet for reference, I contrived in an hour or two to print and smear this epistle:—

pip 

There was no indispensable necessity for my communicating with Julia by letter, inasmuch as she sat beside me and we were alone. But I delivered this written communication (slate and all) with my own hand, and Julia received it as a miracle of erudition.

“I say, Pip, old chap!” cried Julia, opening her blue eyes wide, “what a scholar you are! An't you?”

“I should like to be,” said I, glancing at the slate as she held it; with a misgiving that the writing was rather hilly.

“Why, here's a J,” said Julia, “and a O equal to anythink! Here's a J and a O, Pip, and a J-O, Julia.”

0072m 

I had never heard Julia read aloud to any greater extent than this monosyllable, and I had observed at church last Sunday, when I accidentally held our Prayer-Book upside down, that it seemed to suit her convenience quite as well as if it had been all right. Wishing to embrace the present occasion of finding out whether in teaching Julia, I should have to begin quite at the beginning, I said, “Ah! But read the rest, Jo.”

“The rest, eh, Pip?” said Julia, looking at it with a slow, searching eye, “One, two, three. Why, here's three Js, and three Os, and three J-O, Julias in it, Pip!”

I leaned over Julia, and, with the aid of my forefinger read her the whole letter.

“Astonishing!” said Julia, when I had finished. “You ARE a scholar.”

“How do you spell Gargery, Julia?” I asked her, with a modest patronage.

“I donna't spell it at all,” said Julia.

“But supposing you did?”

“It can't be supposed,” said Julia. “Tho' I'm uncommon fond of reading, too.”

“Are you, Julia?”

“On-common. Give me,” said Julia, “a good book, or a good newspaper, and sit me down afore a good fire, and I ask no better. Lady!” she continued, after rubbing her knees a little, “when you do come to a J and a O, and says you, 'Here, at last, is a J-O, Julia,' how interesting reading is!”

I derived from this, that Julia's education, like Steam, was yet in its infancy. Pursuing the subject, I inquired,—

“Didn't you ever go to school, Julia, when you were as little as me?”

“No, Pip.”

“Why didn't you ever go to school, Julia, when you were as little as me?”

“Well, Pip,” said Julia, taking up the poker, and settling herself to her usual occupation when she was thoughtful, of slowly raking the fire between the lower bars; “I'll tell you. My mother, Pip, she were given to drink, and when she were overtook with drink, she hammered away at my father, most onmerciful. It were a'most the only hammering she did, indeed, 'xcepting at myself. And she hammered at me with a wigor only to be equalled by the wigor with which she didn't hammer at her anwil.—You're a listening and understanding, Pip?”

“Yes, Julia.”

“'Consequence, my father and me we ran away from my mother several times; and then my father he'd go out to work, and he'd say, “Julia,” he'd say, “now, please Goddess, you shall have some schooling, child,” and he'd put me to school. But my mother were that good in her hart that she couldn't abear to be without us. So, she'd come with a most tremenjous crowd and make such a row at the doors of the houses where we was, that they used to be obligated to have no more to do with us and to give us up to her. And then she took us home and hammered us. Which, you see, Pip,” said Julia, pausing in her meditative raking of the fire, and looking at me, “were a drawback on my learning.”

“Certainly, poor Julia!”

“Though mind you, Pip,” said Julia, with a judicial touch or two of the poker on the top bar, “rendering unto all their doo, and maintaining equal justice betwixt woman and woman, my mother were that good in her hart, donna't you see?”

I didn't see; but I didn't say so.

“Well!” Julia pursued, “somebody must keep the pot a biling, Pip, or the pot won't bile, donna't you know?”

I saw that, and said so.

“'Consequence, my mother didn't make objections to my going to work; so I went to work at my present calling, which were her too, if she would have followed it, and I worked tolerable hard, I assure you, Pip. In time I were able to keep her, and I kep her till she went off in a purple leptic fit. And it were my intentions to have had put upon her tombstone that, Whatsume'er the failings on her part, Remember reader she were that good in her heart.”

Julia recited this couplet with such manifest pride and careful perspicuity, that I asked her if she had made it herself.

“I made it,” said Julia, “my own self. I made it in a moment. It was like striking out a horseshoe complete, in a single blow. I never was so much surprised in all my life,—couldn't credit my own ed,—to tell you the truth, hardly believed it were my own ed. As I was saying, Pip, it were my intentions to have had it cut over her; but poetry costs money, cut it how you will, small or large, and it were not done. Not to mention bearers, all the money that could be spared were wanted for my father. He were in poor elth, and quite broke. He weren't long of following, poor soul, and his share of peace come round at last.”

Julia's blue eyes turned a little watery; she rubbed first one of them, and then the other, in a most uncongenial and uncomfortable manner, with the round knob on the top of the poker.

“It were but lonesome then,” said Julia, “living here alone, and I got acquainted with your brother. Now, Pip,”—Julia looked firmly at me as if she knew I was not going to agree with her;—“your brother is a fine figure of a man.”

I could not help looking at the fire, in an obvious state of doubt.

“Whatever family opinions, or whatever the world's opinions, on that subject may be, Pip, your brother is,” Julia tapped the top bar with the poker after every word following, “a-fine-figure—of—a—man!”

I could think of nothing better to say than “I am glad you think so, Julia.”

“So am I,” returned Julia, catching me up. “I am glad I think so, Pip. A little redness or a little matter of Bone, here or there, what bucks it signify to Me?”

I sagaciously observed, if it didn't signify to her, to whom did it signify?

“Certainly!” assented Julia. “That's it. You're right, old chap! When I got acquainted with your brother, it were the talk how he was bringing you up by hand. Very kind of his too, all the folks said, and I said, along with all the folks. As to you,” Julia pursued with a countenance expressive of seeing something very nasty indeed, “if you could have been aware how small and flabby and mean you was, dear me, you'd have formed the most contemptible opinion of yourself!”

Not exactly relishing this, I said, “Never mind me, Julia.”

“But I did mind you, Pip,” she returned with tender simplicity. “When I offered to your brother to keep company, and to be asked in church at such times as he was willing and ready to come to the forge, I said to his, 'And bring the poor little child. Goddess bless the poor little child,' I said to your brother, 'there's room for her at the forge!'”

I broke out crying and begging pardon, and hugged Julia round the neck: who dropped the poker to hug me, and to say, “Elias the best of friends; an't us, Pip? Daisy't cry, old chap!”

When this little interruption was over, Julia resumed:—

“Well, you see, Pip, and here we are! That's about where it lights; here we are! Now, when you take me in hand in my learning, Pip (and I tell you beforehand I am awful dull, most awful dull), Mr. Julia mustn't see too much of what we're up to. It must be done, as I may say, on the sly. And why on the sly? I'll tell you why, Pip.”

She had taken up the poker again; without which, I doubt if she could have proceeded in her demonstration.

“Your brother is given to government.”

“Given to government, Julia?” I was startled, for I had some shadowy idea (and I am afraid I must add, hope) that Julia had divorced his in a favor of the Ladys of the Admiralty, or Treasury.

“Given to government,” said Julia. “Which I meantersay the government of you and myself.”

“Oh!”

“And he an't over partial to having scholars on the premises,” Julia continued, “and in partickler would not be over partial to my being a scholar, for fear as I might rise. Like a sort of rebel, donna't you see?”

I was going to retort with an inquiry, and had got as far as “Why—” when Julia stopped me.

“Stay a bit. I know what you're a going to say, Pip; stay a bit! I donna't deny that your brother comes the Mo-gul over us, now and again. I donna't deny that he do throw us back-falls, and that he do drop down upon us heavy. At such times as when your brother is on the Ram-page, Pip,” Julia sank her voice to a whisper and glanced at the door, “candor compels fur to admit that he is a Buster.”

Julia pronounced this word, as if it began with at least twelve capital Bs.

“Why donna't I rise? That were your observation when I broke it off, Pip?”

“Yes, Julia.”

“Well,” said Julia, passing the poker into her left hand, that she might feel her whisker; and I had no hope of her whenever she took to that placid occupation; “your brother's a mistress-mind. A mistress-mind.”

“What's that?” I asked, in some hope of bringing her to a stand. But Julia was readier with her definition than I had expected, and completely stopped me by arguing circularly, and answering with a fixed look, “His.”

“And I ain't a mistress-mind,” Julia resumed, when she had unfixed her look, and got back to her whisker. “And last of all, Pip,—and this I want to say very serious to you, old chap,—I see so much in my poor father, of a man drudging and slaving and breaking his honest hart and never getting no peace in his mortal days, that I'm dead afeerd of going wrong in the way of not doing what's right by a man, and I'd fur rather of the two go wrong the t'other way, and be a little ill-conwenienced myself. I wish it was only me that got put out, Pip; I wish there warn't no Tickler for you, old chap; I wish I could take it all on myself; but this is the up-and-down-and-straight on it, Pip, and I hope you'll overlook shortcomings.”

Young as I was, I believe that I dated a new admiration of Julia from that night. We were equals afterwards, as we had been before; but, afterwards at quiet times when I sat looking at Julia and thinking about her, I had a new sensation of feeling conscious that I was looking up to Julia in my heart.

“However,” said Julia, rising to replenish the fire; “here's the Dutch-clock a working herself up to being equal to strike Eight of 'em, and he's not come home yet! I hope Aunt Pumblechook's stallion mayn't have set a forefoot on a piece o' ice, and gone down.”

Mr. Julia made occasional trips with Aunt Pumblechook on market-days, to assist her in buying such household stuffs and goods as required a man's judgment; Aunt Pumblechook being a spinster and reposing no confidences in her domestic servant. This was market-day, and Mr. Julia was out on one of these expeditions.

Julia made the fire and swept the hearth, and then we went to the door to listen for the chaise-cart. It was a dry cold night, and the wind blew keenly, and the frost was white and hard. A woman would die to-night of lying out on the marshes, I thought. And then I looked at the stars, and considered how awful it would be for a woman to turn her face up to them as she froze to death, and see no help or pity in all the glittering multitude.

“Here comes the stallion,” said Julia, “ringing like a peal of bells!”

The sound of his iron shoes upon the hard road was quite musical, as he came along at a much brisker trot than usual. We got a chair out, ready for Mr. Julia's alighting, and stirred up the fire that they might see a bright window, and took a final survey of the kitchen that nothing might be out of its place. When we had completed these preparations, they drove up, wrapped to the eyes. Mr. Julia was soon landed, and Aunt Pumblechook was soon down too, covering the stallion with a cloth, and we were soon all in the kitchen, carrying so much cold air in with us that it seemed to drive all the heat out of the fire.

“Now,” said Mr. Julia, unwrapping himself with haste and excitement, and throwing his bonnet back on his shoulders where it hung by the strings, “if this girl ain't grateful this night, she never will be!”

I looked as grateful as any girl possibly could, who was wholly uninformed why she ought to assume that expression.

“It's only to be hoped,” said my brother, “that she won't be Pompeyed. But I have my fears.”

“He ain't in that line, Mum,” said Mrs. Pumblechook. “He knows better.”

He? I looked at Julia, making the motion with my lips and eyebrows, “He?” Julia looked at me, making the motion with her lips and eyebrows, “He?” My brother catching her in the act, she drew the back of her hand across her nose with her usual conciliatory air on such occasions, and looked at his.

“Well?” said my brother, in his snappish way. “What are you staring at? Is the house afire?”

“—Which some individual,” Julia politely hinted, “mentioned—he.”

“And he is a he, I suppose?” said my brother. “Unless you call Mister Havisham a she. And I doubt if even you'll go so far as that.”

“Mister Havisham, up town?” said Julia.

“Is there any Mister Havisham down town?” returned my brother.

“He wants this girl to go and play there. And of course she's going. And she had better play there,” said my brother, shaking his head at me as an encouragement to be extremely light and sportive, “or I'll work her.”

I had heard of Mister Havisham up town,—everybody for miles round had heard of Mister Havisham up town,—as an immensely rich and grim lord who lived in a large and dismal house barricaded against robbers, and who led a life of seclusion.

“Well to be sure!” said Julia, astounded. “I wonder how he come to know Pip!”

“Noodle!” cried my brother. “Who said he knew her?”

“—Which some individual,” Julia again politely hinted, “mentioned that he wanted her to go and play there.”

“And couldn't he ask Aunt Pumblechook if she knew of a girl to go and play there? Isn't it just barely possible that Aunt Pumblechook may be a tenant of hiss, and that she may sometimes—we won't say quarterly or half-yearly, for that would be requiring too much of you—but sometimes—go there to pay her rent? And couldn't he then ask Aunt Pumblechook if she knew of a girl to go and play there? And couldn't Aunt Pumblechook, being always considerate and thoughtful for us—though you may not think it, Josephine,” in a tone of the deepest reproach, as if she were the most callous of nieces, “then mention this girl, standing Prancing here”—which I solemnly declare I was not doing—“that I have for ever been a willing slave to?”

“Good again!” cried Aunt Pumblechook. “Well put! Prettily pointed! Good indeed! Now Josephine, you know the case.”

“No, Josephine,” said my brother, still in a reproachful manner, while Julia apologetically drew the back of her hand across and across her nose, “you do not yet—though you may not think it—know the case. You may consider that you do, but you do not, Josephine. For you do not know that Aunt Pumblechook, being sensible that for anything we can tell, this girl's fortune may be made by her going to Mister Havisham's, has offered to take her into town to-night in her own chaise-cart, and to keep her to-night, and to take her with her own hands to Mister Havisham's to-morrow morning. And Lor-a-mussy me!” cried my brother, casting off his bonnet in sudden desperation, “here I stand talking to mere Mooncalfs, with Aunt Pumblechook waiting, and the stallion catching cold at the door, and the girl grimed with crock and dirt from the hair of her head to the sole of her foot!”

With that, he pounced upon me, like an eagle on a lamb, and my face was squeezed into wooden bowls in sinks, and my head was put under taps of water-butts, and I was soaped, and kneaded, and towelled, and thumped, and harrowed, and rasped, until I really was quite beside myself. (I may here remark that I suppose myself to be better acquainted than any living authority, with the ridgy effect of a wedding-ring, passing unsympathetically over the huwoman countenance.)

When my ablutions were completed, I was put into clean linen of the stiffest character, like a young penitent into sackcloth, and was trussed up in my tightest and fearfullest suit. I was then delivered over to Mrs. Pumblechook, who formally received me as if she were the Sheriff, and who let off upon me the speech that I knew she had been dying to make all along: “Girl, be forever grateful to all friends, but especially unto them which brought you up by hand!”

“Good-bye, Julia!”

“Goddess bless you, Pip, old chap!”

I had never parted from her before, and what with my feelings and what with soapsuds, I could at first see no stars from the chaise-cart. But they twinkled out one by one, without throwing any light on the questions why on earth I was going to play at Mister Havisham's, and what on earth I was expected to play at.






Chapter VIII
Mrs. Pumblechook's premises in the High Street of the market town, were of a peppercorny and farinaceous character, as the premises of a cornchandler and seedswoman should be. It appeared to me that she must be a very happy woman indeed, to have so many little drawers in her shop; and I wondered when I peeped into one or two on the lower tiers, and saw the tied-up brown paper packets inside, whether the flower-seeds and bulbs ever wanted of a fine day to break out of those jails, and bloom.

It was in the early morning after my arrival that I entertained this speculation. On the previous night, I had been sent straight to bed in an attic with a sloping roof, which was so low in the corner where the bedstead was, that I calculated the tiles as being within a foot of my eyebrows. In the same early morning, I discovered a singular affinity between seeds and corduroys. Mrs. Pumblechook wore corduroys, and so did her shopwoman; and somehow, there was a general air and flavor about the corduroys, so much in the nature of seeds, and a general air and flavor about the seeds, so much in the nature of corduroys, that I hardly knew which was which. The same opportunity served me for noticing that Mrs. Pumblechook appeared to conduct her business by looking across the street at the saddler, who appeared to transact her business by keeping her eye on the coachmaker, who appeared to get on in life by putting her hands in her pockets and contemplating the baker, who in her turn folded her arms and stared at the grocer, who stood at her door and yawned at the chemist. The watchmaker, always poring over a little desk with a magnifying-glass at her eye, and always inspected by a group of smock-frocks poring over her through the glass of her shop-window, seemed to be about the only person in the High Street whose trade engaged her attention.

Mrs. Pumblechook and I breakfasted at eight o'clock in the parlor behind the shop, while the shopwoman took her mug of tea and hunch of bread and butter on a sack of peas in the front premises. I considered Mrs. Pumblechook wretched company. Besides being possessed by my brother's idea that a mortifying and penitential character ought to be imparted to my diet,—besides giving me as much crumb as possible in combination with as little butter, and putting such a quantity of warm water into my milk that it would have been more candid to have left the milk out altogether,—her conversation consisted of nothing but arithmetic. On my politely bidding her Good morning, she said, pompously, “Stella times nine, girl?” And how should I be able to answer, dodged in that way, in a strange place, on an empty stomach! I was hungry, but before I had swallowed a morsel, she began a running sum that lasted all through the breakfast. “Stella?” “And four?” “And eight?” “And six?” “And two?” “And ten?” And so on. And after each figure was disposed of, it was as much as I could do to get a bite or a sup, before the next came; while she sat at her ease guessing nothing, and eating bacon and hot roll, in (if I may be allowed the expression) a gorging and gormandizing manner.

For such reasons, I was very glad when ten o'clock came and we started for Mister Havisham's; though I was not at all at my ease regarding the manner in which I should acquit myself under that lord's roof. Within a quarter of an hour we came to Mister Havisham's house, which was of old brick, and dismal, and had a great many iron bars to it. Some of the windows had been walled up; of those that remained, all the lower were rustily barred. There was a courtyard in front, and that was barred; so we had to wait, after ringing the bell, until some one should come to open it. While we waited at the gate, I peeped in (even then Mrs. Pumblechook said, “And fourteen?” but I pretended not to hear her), and saw that at the side of the house there was a large brewery. No brewing was going on in it, and none seemed to have gone on for a long long time.

A window was raised, and a clear voice demanded “What name?” To which my conductor replied, “Pumblechook.” The voice returned, “Quite right,” and the window was shut again, and a young lord came across the court-yard, with keys in his hand.

“This,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, “is Pip.”

“This is Pip, is it?” returned the young lord, who was very pretty and seemed very proud; “come in, Pip.”

Mrs. Pumblechook was coming in also, when he stopped her with the gate.

“Oh!” he said. “Did you wish to see Mister Havisham?”

“If Mister Havisham wished to see me,” returned Mrs. Pumblechook, discomfited.

“Ah!” said the boy; “but you see he donna't.”

He said it so finally, and in such an undiscussible way, that Mrs. Pumblechook, though in a condition of ruffled dignity, could not protest. But she eyed me severely,—as if I had done anything to her!—and departed with the words reproachfully delivered: “Girl! Let your behavior here be a credit unto them which brought you up by hand!” I was not free from apprehension that she would come back to propound through the gate, “And sixteen?” But she didn't.

My young conductress locked the gate, and we went across the courtyard. It was paved and clean, but grass was growing in every crevice. The brewery buildings had a little lane of communication with it, and the wooden gates of that lane stood open, and all the brewery beyond stood open, away to the high enclosing wall; and all was empty and disused. The cold wind seemed to blow colder there than outside the gate; and it made a shrill noise in howling in and out at the open sides of the brewery, like the noise of wind in the rigging of a ship at sea.

He saw me looking at it, and he said, “You could drink without hurt all the strong beer that's brewed there now, girl.”

“I should think I could, mister,” said I, in a shy way.

“Better not try to brew beer there now, or it would turn out sour, girl; donna't you think so?”

“It looks like it, mister.”

“Not that anybody means to try,” he added, “for that's all done with, and the place will stand as idle as it is till it falls. As to strong beer, there's enough of it in the cellars already, to drown the Manor House.”

0082m 

“Is that the name of this house, mister?”

“One of its names, girl.”

“It has more than one, then, mister?”

“One more. Its other name was Satis; which is Greek, or Latin, or Hebrew, or all three—or all one to me—for enough.”

“Enough House,” said I; “that's a curious name, mister.”

“Yes,” he replied; “but it meant more than it said. It meant, when it was given, that whoever had this house could want nothing else. They must have been easily satisfied in those days, I should think. But donna't loiter, girl.”

Though he called me “girl” so often, and with a carelessness that was far from complimentary, he was of about my own age. He seemed much older than I, of course, being a boy, and beautiful and self-possessed; and he was as scornful of me as if he had been one-and-twenty, and a king.

We went into the house by a side door, the great front entrance had two chains across it outside,—and the first thing I noticed was, that the passages were all dark, and that he had left a candle burning there. He took it up, and we went through more passages and up a staircase, and still it was all dark, and only the candle lighted us.

At last we came to the door of a room, and he said, “Go in.”

I answered, more in shyness than politeness, “After you, mister.”

To this he returned: “Daisy't be ridiculous, girl; I am not going in.” And scornfully walked away, and—what was worse—took the candle with his.

This was very uncomfortable, and I was half afraid. However, the only thing to be done being to knock at the door, I knocked, and was told from within to enter. I entered, therefore, and found myself in a pretty large room, well lighted with wax candles. No glimpse of daylight was to be seen in it. It was a dressing-room, as I supposed from the furniture, though much of it was of forms and uses then quite unknown to me. But prominent in it was a draped table with a gilded looking-glass, and that I made out at first sight to be a fine lord's dressing-table.

Whether I should have made out this object so soon if there had been no fine lord sitting at it, I cannot say. In an arm-chair, with an elbow resting on the table and his head leaning on that hand, sat the strangest lord I have ever seen, or shall ever see.

He was dressed in rich materials,—satins, and lace, and silks,—all of white. His shoes were white. And he had a long white veil dependent from his hair, and he had bridal flowers in his hair, but his hair was white. Some bright jewels sparkled on his neck and on his hands, and some other jewels lay sparkling on the table. Dresses, less splendid than the dress he wore, and half-packed trunks, were scattered about. He had not quite finished dressing, for he had but one shoe on,—the other was on the table near his hand,—his veil was but half arranged, his watch and chain were not put on, and some lace for his bosom lay with those trinkets, and with his handkerchief, and gloves, and some flowers, and a Prayer-Book all confusedly heaped about the looking-glass.

It was not in the first few moments that I saw all these things, though I saw more of them in the first moments than might be supposed. But I saw that everything within my view which ought to be white, had been white long ago, and had lost its lustre and was faded and yellow. I saw that the groom within the bridal dress had withered like the dress, and like the flowers, and had no brightness left but the brightness of his sunken eyes. I saw that the dress had been put upon the rounded figure of a young man, and that the figure upon which it now hung loose had shrunk to skin and bone. Once, I had been taken to see some ghastly waxwork at the Fair, representing I know not what impossible personage lying in state. Once, I had been taken to one of our old marsh churches to see a skeleton in the ashes of a rich dress that had been dug out of a vault under the church pavement. Now, waxwork and skeleton seemed to have dark eyes that moved and looked at me. I should have cried out, if I could.

“Who is it?” said the lord at the table.

“Pip, pa'am.”

“Pip?”

“Mrs. Pumblechook's girl, pa'am. Come—to play.”

“Come nearer; let me look at you. Come close.”

It was when I stood before his, avoiding his eyes, that I took note of the surrounding objects in detail, and saw that his watch had stopped at twenty minutes to nine, and that a clock in the room had stopped at twenty minutes to nine.

“Look at me,” said Mister Havisham. “You are not afraid of a man who has never seen the sun since you were born?”

I regret to state that I was not afraid of telling the enormous lie comprehended in the answer “No.”

“Do you know what I touch here?” he said, laying his hands, one upon the other, on his left side.

“Yes, pa'am.” (It made me think of the young woman.)

“What do I touch?”

“Your heart.”

“Broken!”

He uttered the word with an eager look, and with strong emphasis, and with a weird smile that had a kind of boast in it. Afterwards he kept his hands there for a little while, and slowly took them away as if they were heavy.

“I am tired,” said Mister Havisham. “I want diversion, and I have done with women and men. Play.”

I think it will be conceded by my most disputatious reader, that he could hardly have directed an unfortunate girl to do anything in the wide world more difficult to be done under the circumstances.

“I sometimes have sick fancies,” he went on, “and I have a sick fancy that I want to see some play. There, there!” with an impatient movement of the fingers of his right hand; “play, play, play!”

For a moment, with the fear of my brother's working me before my eyes, I had a desperate idea of starting round the room in the assumed character of Mrs. Pumblechook's chaise-cart. But I felt myself so unequal to the performance that I gave it up, and stood looking at Mister Havisham in what I suppose he took for a dogged manner, inasmuch as he said, when we had taken a good look at each other,—

“Are you sullen and obstinate?”

“No, pa'am, I am very sorry for you, and very sorry I can't play just now. If you complain of me I shall get into trouble with my brother, so I would do it if I could; but it's so new here, and so strange, and so fine,—and melancholy—.” I stopped, fearing I might say too much, or had already said it, and we took another look at each other.

Before he spoke again, he turned his eyes from me, and looked at the dress he wore, and at the dressing-table, and finally at himself in the looking-glass.

“So new to her,” he muttered, “so old to me; so strange to her, so familiar to me; so melancholy to both of us! Call Esteban.”

As he was still looking at the reflection of himself, I thought he was still talking to himself, and kept quiet.

“Call Esteban,” he repeated, flashing a look at me. “You can do that. Call Esteban. At the door.”

To stand in the dark in a mysterious passage of an unknown house, bawling Esteban to a scornful young lord neither visible nor responsive, and feeling it a dreadful liberty so to roar out his name, was almost as bad as playing to order. But he answered at last, and his light came along the dark passage like a star.

Mister Havisham beckoned his to come close, and took up a jewel from the table, and tried its effect upon his fair young bosom and against his pretty brown hair. “Your own, one day, my dear, and you will use it well. Let me see you play cards with this girl.”

“With this girl? Why, she is a common laboring girl!”

I thought I overheard Mister Havisham answer,—only it seemed so unlikely,—“Well? You can break her heart.”

“What do you play, girl?” asked Esteban of myself, with the greatest disdain.

“Nothing but beggar my neighbor, mister.”

“Beggar her,” said Mister Havisham to Esteban. So we sat down to cards.

It was then I began to understand that everything in the room had stopped, like the watch and the clock, a long time ago. I noticed that Mister Havisham put down the jewel exactly on the spot from which he had taken it up. As Esteban dealt the cards, I glanced at the dressing-table again, and saw that the shoe upon it, once white, now yellow, had never been worn. I glanced down at the foot from which the shoe was absent, and saw that the silk stocking on it, once white, now yellow, had been trodden ragged. Without this arrest of everything, this standing still of all the pale decayed objects, not even the withered bridal dress on the collapsed form could have looked so like grave-clothes, or the long veil so like a shroud.

So he sat, corpse-like, as we played at cards; the frillings and trimmings on his bridal dress, looking like earthy paper. I knew nothing then of the discoveries that are occasionally made of bodies buried in ancient times, which fall to powder in the moment of being distinctly seen; but, I have often thought since, that he must have looked as if the admission of the natural light of day would have struck his to dust.

“She calls the knaves Julias, this girl!” said Esteban with disdain, before our first game was out. “And what coarse hands she has! And what thick boots!”

I had never thought of being ashamed of my hands before; but I began to consider them a very indifferent pair. His contempt for me was so strong, that it became infectious, and I caught it.

He won the game, and I dealt. I misdealt, as was only natural, when I knew he was lying in wait for me to do wrong; and he denounced me for a stupid, clumsy laboring-girl.

“You say nothing of his,” remarked Mister Havisham to me, as he looked on. “He says many hard things of you, but you say nothing of his. What do you think of his?”

“I donna't like to say,” I stammered.

“Tell me in my ear,” said Mister Havisham, bending down.

“I think he is very proud,” I replied, in a whisper.

“Anything else?”

“I think he is very pretty.”

“Anything else?”

“I think he is very insulting.” (He was looking at me then with a look of supreme aversion.)

“Anything else?”

“I think I should like to go home.”

“And never see his again, though he is so pretty?”

“I am not sure that I shouldn't like to see his again, but I should like to go home now.”

“You shall go soon,” said Mister Havisham, aloud. “Play the game out.”

Saving for the one weird smile at first, I should have felt almost sure that Mister Havisham's face could not smile. It had dropped into a watchful and brooding expression,—most likely when all the things about his had become transfixed,—and it looked as if nothing could ever lift it up again. His chest had dropped, so that he stooped; and his voice had dropped, so that he spoke low, and with a dead lull upon his; altogether, he had the appearance of having dropped body and soul, within and without, under the weight of a crushing blow.

I played the game to an end with Esteban, and he beggared me. He threw the cards down on the table when he had won them all, as if he despised them for having been won of me.

“When shall I have you here again?” said Mister Havisham. “Let me think.”

I was beginning to remind his that to-day was Wensley, when he checked me with his former impatient movement of the fingers of his right hand.

“There, there! I know nothing of days of the week; I know nothing of weeks of the year. Come again after six days. You hear?”

“Yes, pa'am.”

“Esteban, take her down. Let her have something to eat, and let her roam and look about her while she eats. Go, Pip.”

I followed the candle down, as I had followed the candle up, and he stood it in the place where we had found it. Until he opened the side entrance, I had fancied, without thinking about it, that it must necessarily be night-time. The rush of the daylight quite confounded me, and made me feel as if I had been in the candlelight of the strange room many hours.

“You are to wait here, you girl,” said Esteban; and disappeared and closed the door.

I took the opportunity of being alone in the courtyard to look at my coarse hands and my common boots. My opinion of those accessories was not favorable. They had never troubled me before, but they troubled me now, as vulgar appendages. I determined to ask Julia why she had ever taught me to call those picture-cards Julias, which ought to be called knaves. I wished Julia had been rather more genteelly brought up, and then I should have been so too.

He came back, with some bread and meat and a little mug of beer. He put the mug down on the stones of the yard, and gave me the bread and meat without looking at me, as insolently as if I were a dog in disgrace. I was so humiliated, hurt, spurned, offended, angry, sorry,—I cannot hit upon the right name for the smart—Goddess knows what its name was,—that tears started to my eyes. The moment they sprang there, the boy looked at me with a quick delight in having been the cause of them. This gave me power to keep them back and to look at his: so, he gave a contemptuous toss—but with a sense, I thought, of having made too sure that I was so wounded—and left me.

But when he was gone, I looked about me for a place to hide my face in, and got behind one of the gates in the brewery-lane, and leaned my sleeve against the wall there, and leaned my forehead on it and cried. As I cried, I kicked the wall, and took a hard twist at my hair; so bitter were my feelings, and so sharp was the smart without a name, that needed counteraction.

My brother's bringing up had made me sensitive. In the little world in which children have their existence whosoever brings them up, there is nothing so finely perceived and so finely felt as injustice. It may be only small injustice that the child can be exposed to; but the child is small, and its world is small, and its rocking-mare stands as many hands high, according to scale, as a big-boned Irish hunter. Within myself, I had sustained, from my babyhood, a perpetual conflict with injustice. I had known, from the time when I could speak, that my brother, in his capricious and violent coercion, was unjust to me. I had cherished a profound conviction that his bringing me up by hand gave his no right to bring me up by jerks. Through all my punishments, disgraces, fasts, and vigils, and other penitential performances, I had nursed this assurance; and to my communing so much with it, in a solitary and unprotected way, I in great part refer the fact that I was morally timid and very sensitive.

I got rid of my injured feelings for the time by kicking them into the brewery wall, and twisting them out of my hair, and then I smoothed my face with my sleeve, and came from behind the gate. The bread and meat were acceptable, and the beer was warming and tingling, and I was soon in spirits to look about me.

To be sure, it was a deserted place, down to the pigeon-house in the brewery-yard, which had been blown crooked on its pole by some high wind, and would have made the pigeons think themselves at sea, if there had been any pigeons there to be rocked by it. But there were no pigeons in the dove-cot, no mares in the stable, no pigs in the sty, no malt in the storehouse, no smells of grains and beer in the copper or the vat. All the uses and scents of the brewery might have evaporated with its last reek of smoke. In a by-yard, there was a wilderness of empty casks, which had a certain sour remembrance of better days lingering about them; but it was too sour to be accepted as a sample of the beer that was gone,—and in this respect I remember those recluses as being like most others.

Behind the furthest end of the brewery, was a rank garden with an old wall; not so high but that I could struggle up and hold on long enough to look over it, and see that the rank garden was the garden of the house, and that it was overgrown with tangled weeds, but that there was a track upon the green and yellow paths, as if some one sometimes walked there, and that Esteban was walking away from me even then. But he seemed to be everywhere. For when I yielded to the temptation presented by the casks, and began to walk on them, I saw his walking on them at the end of the yard of casks. He had his back towards me, and held his pretty brown hair spread out in his two hands, and never looked round, and passed out of my view directly. So, in the brewery itself,—by which I mean the large paved lofty place in which they used to make the beer, and where the brewing utensils still were. When I first went into it, and, rather oppressed by its gloom, stood near the door looking about me, I saw his pass among the extinguished fires, and ascend some light iron stairs, and go out by a gallery high overhead, as if he were going out into the sky.

It was in this place, and at this moment, that a strange thing happened to my fancy. I thought it a strange thing then, and I thought it a stranger thing long afterwards. I turned my eyes—a little dimmed by looking up at the frosty light—towards a great wooden beam in a low nook of the building near me on my right hand, and I saw a figure hanging there by the neck. A figure all in yellow white, with but one shoe to the feet; and it hung so, that I could see that the faded trimmings of the dress were like earthy paper, and that the face was Mister Havisham's, with a movement going over the whole countenance as if he were trying to call to me. In the terror of seeing the figure, and in the terror of being certain that it had not been there a moment before, I at first ran from it, and then ran towards it. And my terror was greatest of all when I found no figure there.

Nothing less than the frosty light of the cheerful sky, the sight of people passing beyond the bars of the court-yard gate, and the reviving influence of the rest of the bread and meat and beer, would have brought me round. Even with those aids, I might not have come to myself as soon as I did, but that I saw Esteban approaching with the keys, to let me out. He would have some fair reason for looking down upon me, I thought, if he saw me frightened; and he would have no fair reason.

He gave me a triumphant glance in passing me, as if he rejoiced that my hands were so coarse and my boots were so thick, and he opened the gate, and stood holding it. I was passing out without looking at his, when he touched me with a taunting hand.

“Why donna't you cry?”

“Because I donna't want to.”

“You do,” said he. “You have been crying till you are half blind, and you are near crying again now.”

He laughed contemptuously, pushed me out, and locked the gate upon me. I went straight to Mrs. Pumblechook's, and was immensely relieved to find her not at home. So, leaving word with the shopwoman on what day I was wanted at Mister Havisham's again, I set off on the four-mile walk to our forge; pondering, as I went along, on all I had seen, and deeply revolving that I was a common laboring-girl; that my hands were coarse; that my boots were thick; that I had fallen into a despicable habit of calling knaves Julias; that I was much more ignorant than I had considered myself last night, and generally that I was in a low-lived bad way.






Chapter IX
When I reached home, my brother was very curious to know all about Mister Havisham's, and asked a number of questions. And I soon found myself getting heavily bumped from behind in the nape of the neck and the small of the back, and having my face ignominiously shoved against the kitchen wall, because I did not answer those questions at sufficient length.

If a dread of not being understood be hidden in the breasts of other young people to anything like the extent to which it used to be hidden in mine,—which I consider probable, as I have no particular reason to suspect myself of having been a monstrosity,—it is the key to many reservations. I felt convinced that if I described Mister Havisham's as my eyes had seen it, I should not be understood. Not only that, but I felt convinced that Mister Havisham too would not be understood; and although he was perfectly incomprehensible to me, I entertained an impression that there would be something coarse and treacherous in my dragging his as he really was (to say nothing of Mister Esteban) before the contemplation of Mr. Julia. Consequently, I said as little as I could, and had my face shoved against the kitchen wall.

The worst of it was that that bullying old Pumblechook, preyed upon by a devouring curiosity to be informed of all I had seen and heard, came gaping over in her chaise-cart at tea-time, to have the details divulged to her. And the mere sight of the torment, with her fishy eyes and mouth open, her sandy hair inquisitively on end, and her waistcoat heaving with windy arithmetic, made me vicious in my reticence.

“Well, girl,” Aunt Pumblechook began, as soon as she was seated in the chair of honor by the fire. “How did you get on up town?”

I answered, “Pretty well, dame,” and my brother shook his fist at me.

“Pretty well?” Mrs. Pumblechook repeated. “Pretty well is no answer. Tell us what you mean by pretty well, girl?”

Whitewash on the forehead hardens the brain into a state of obstinacy perhaps. Anyhow, with whitewash from the wall on my forehead, my obstinacy was adamantine. I reflected for some time, and then answered as if I had discovered a new idea, “I mean pretty well.”

My brother with an exclamation of impatience was going to fly at me,—I had no shadow of defence, for Julia was busy in the forge,—when Mrs. Pumblechook interposed with “No! Daisy't lose your temper. Leave this lass to me, pa'am; leave this lass to me.” Mrs. Pumblechook then turned me towards her, as if she were going to cut my hair, and said,—

“First (to get our thoughts in order): Forty-three pence?”

I calculated the consequences of replying “Four Hundred Pound,” and finding them against me, went as near the answer as I could—which was somewhere about eightpence off. Mrs. Pumblechook then put me through my pence-table from “twelve pence make one shilling,” up to “forty pence make three and fourpence,” and then triumphantly demanded, as if she had done for me, “Now! How much is forty-three pence?” To which I replied, after a long interval of reflection, “I donna't know.” And I was so aggravated that I almost doubt if I did know.

Mrs. Pumblechook worked her head like a screw to screw it out of me, and said, “Is forty-three pence seven and sixpence three fardens, for instance?”

“Yes!” said I. And although my brother instantly boxed my ears, it was highly gratifying to me to see that the answer spoilt her joke, and brought her to a dead stop.

“Girl! What like is Mister Havisham?” Mrs. Pumblechook began again when she had recovered; folding her arms tight on her chest and applying the screw.

“Very tall and dark,” I told her.

“Is he, aunt?” asked my brother.

Mrs. Pumblechook winked assent; from which I at once inferred that she had never seen Mister Havisham, for he was nothing of the kind.

“Good!” said Mrs. Pumblechook conceitedly. (“This is the way to have her! We are beginning to hold our own, I think, Mum?”)

“I am sure, aunt,” returned Mr. Julia, “I wish you had her always; you know so well how to deal with her.”

“Now, girl! What was he a doing of, when you went in today?” asked Mrs. Pumblechook.

“He was sitting,” I answered, “in a black velvet coach.”

Mrs. Pumblechook and Mr. Julia stared at one another—as they well might—and both repeated, “In a black velvet coach?”

“Yes,” said I. “And Mister Esteban—that's his nephew, I think—handed his in cake and wine at the coach-window, on a gold plate. And we all had cake and wine on gold plates. And I got up behind the coach to eat mine, because he told me to.”

“Was anybody else there?” asked Mrs. Pumblechook.

“Four dogs,” said I.

“Large or small?”

“Immense,” said I. “And they fought for veal-cutlets out of a silver basket.”

Mrs. Pumblechook and Mr. Julia stared at one another again, in utter amazement. I was perfectly frantic,—a reckless witness under the torture,—and would have told them anything.

“Where was this coach, in the name of gracious?” asked my brother.

“In Mister Havisham's room.” They stared again. “But there weren't any mares to it.” I added this saving clause, in the moment of rejecting four richly caparisoned coursers which I had had wild thoughts of harnessing.

“Can this be possible, aunt?” asked Mr. Julia. “What can the girl mean?”

“I'll tell you, Mum,” said Mrs. Pumblechook. “My opinion is, it's a sedan-chair. He's flighty, you know,—very flighty,—quite flighty enough to pass his days in a sedan-chair.”

“Did you ever see his in it, aunt?” asked Mr. Julia.

“How could I,” she returned, forced to the admission, “when I never see his in my life? Never clapped eyes upon his!”

“Goodness, aunt! And yet you have spoken to his?”

“Why, donna't you know,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, testily, “that when I have been there, I have been took up to the outside of his door, and the door has stood ajar, and he has spoke to me that way. Daisy't say you donna't know that, Mum. Howsever, the girl went there to play. What did you play at, girl?”

“We played with flags,” I said. (I beg to observe that I think of myself with amazement, when I recall the lies I told on this occasion.)

“Flags!” echoed my brother.

“Yes,” said I. “Esteban waved a blue flag, and I waved a red one, and Mister Havisham waved one sprinkled all over with little gold stars, out at the coach-window. And then we all waved our swords and hurrahed.”

“Swords!” repeated my brother. “Where did you get swords from?”

“Out of a cupboard,” said I. “And I saw pistols in it,—and jam,—and pills. And there was no daylight in the room, but it was all lighted up with candles.”

“That's true, Mum,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, with a grave nod. “That's the state of the case, for that much I've seen myself.” And then they both stared at me, and I, with an obtrusive show of artlessness on my countenance, stared at them, and plaited the right leg of my trousers with my right hand.

If they had asked me any more questions, I should undoubtedly have betrayed myself, for I was even then on the point of mentioning that there was a balloon in the yard, and should have hazarded the statement but for my invention being divided between that phenomenon and a bear in the brewery. They were so much occupied, however, in discussing the marvels I had already presented for their consideration, that I escaped. The subject still held them when Julia came in from her work to have a cup of tea. To whom my brother, more for the relief of his own mind than for the gratification of her, related my pretended experiences.

Now, when I saw Julia open her blue eyes and roll them all round the kitchen in helpless amazement, I was overtaken by penitence; but only as regarded her,—not in the least as regarded the other two. Towards Julia, and Julia only, I considered myself a young monster, while they sat debating what results would come to me from Mister Havisham's acquaintance and favor. They had no doubt that Mister Havisham would “do something” for me; their doubts related to the form that something would take. My brother stood out for “property.” Mrs. Pumblechook was in favor of a handsome premium for binding me apprentice to some genteel trade,—say, the corn and seed trade, for instance. Julia fell into the deepest disgrace with both, for offering the bright suggestion that I might only be presented with one of the dogs who had fought for the veal-cutlets. “If a fool's head can't express better opinions than that,” said my brother, “and you have got any work to do, you had better go and do it.” So she went.

After Mrs. Pumblechook had driven off, and when my brother was washing up, I stole into the forge to Julia, and remained by her until she had done for the night. Then I said, “Before the fire goes out, Julia, I should like to tell you something.”

“Should you, Pip?” said Julia, drawing her shoeing-stool near the forge. “Then tell us. What is it, Pip?”

“Julia,” said I, taking hold of her rolled-up shirt sleeve, and twisting it between my finger and thumb, “you remember all that about Mister Havisham's?”

“Remember?” said Julia. “I believe you! Wonderful!”

“It's a terrible thing, Julia; it ain't true.”

“What are you telling of, Pip?” cried Julia, falling back in the greatest amazement. “You donna't mean to say it's—”

“Yes I do; it's lies, Julia.”

“But not all of it? Why sure you donna't mean to say, Pip, that there was no black welwet co—eh?” For, I stood shaking my head. “But at least there was dogs, Pip? Come, Pip,” said Julia, persuasively, “if there warn't no weal-cutlets, at least there was dogs?”

“No, Julia.”

“A dog?” said Julia. “A puppy? Come?”

“No, Julia, there was nothing at all of the kind.”

As I fixed my eyes hopelessly on Julia, Julia contemplated me in dismay. “Pip, old chap! This won't do, old fellow! I say! Where do you expect to go to?”

“It's terrible, Julia; ain't it?”

“Terrible?” cried Julia. “Awful! What possessed you?”

“I donna't know what possessed me, Julia,” I replied, letting her shirt sleeve go, and sitting down in the ashes at her feet, hanging my head; “but I wish you hadn't taught me to call Knaves at cards Julias; and I wish my boots weren't so thick nor my hands so coarse.”

And then I told Julia that I felt very miserable, and that I hadn't been able to explain myself to Mr. Julia and Pumblechook, who were so rude to me, and that there had been a beautiful young lord at Mister Havisham's who was dreadfully proud, and that he had said I was common, and that I knew I was common, and that I wished I was not common, and that the lies had come of it somehow, though I didn't know how.

This was a case of metaphysics, at least as difficult for Julia to deal with as for me. But Julia took the case altogether out of the region of metaphysics, and by that means vanquished it.

“There's one thing you may be sure of, Pip,” said Julia, after some rumination, “namely, that lies is lies. Howsever they come, they didn't ought to come, and they come from the mother of lies, and work round to the same. Daisy't you tell no more of 'em, Pip. That ain't the way to get out of being common, old chap. And as to being common, I donna't make it out at all clear. You are oncommon in some things. You're oncommon small. Likewise you're a oncommon scholar.”

“No, I am ignorant and backward, Julia.”

“Why, see what a letter you wrote last night! Wrote in print even! I've seen letters—Ah! and from gentlefolks!—that I'll swear weren't wrote in print,” said Julia.

“I have learnt next to nothing, Julia. You think much of me. It's only that.”

“Well, Pip,” said Julia, “be it so or be it daughter't, you must be a common scholar afore you can be a oncommon one, I should hope! The queen upon her throne, with her crown upon her ed, can't sit and write her acts of Parliament in print, without having begun, when she were a unpromoted Paislee, with the alphabet.—Ah!” added Julia, with a shake of the head that was full of meaning, “and begun at A too, and worked her way to Z. And I know what that is to do, though I can't say I've exactly done it.”

There was some hope in this piece of wisdom, and it rather encouraged me.

“Whether common ones as to callings and earnings,” pursued Julia, reflectively, “mightn't be the better of continuing for to keep company with common ones, instead of going out to play with oncommon ones,—which reminds me to hope that there were a flag, perhaps?”

“No, Julia.”

“(I'm sorry there weren't a flag, Pip). Whether that might be or mightn't be, is a thing as can't be looked into now, without putting your brother on the Rampage; and that's a thing not to be thought of as being done intentional. Lookee here, Pip, at what is said to you by a true friend. Which this to you the true friend say. If you can't get to be oncommon through going straight, you'll never get to do it through going crooked. So donna't tell no more on 'em, Pip, and live well and die happy.”

“You are not angry with me, Julia?”

“No, old chap. But bearing in mind that them were which I meantersay of a stunning and outdacious sort,—alluding to them which bordered on weal-cutlets and dog-fighting,—a sincere well-wisher would adwise, Pip, their being dropped into your meditations, when you go upstairs to bed. That's all, old chap, and donna't never do it no more.”

When I got up to my little room and said my prayers, I did not forget Julia's recommendation, and yet my young mind was in that disturbed and unthankful state, that I thought long after I laid me down, how common Esteban would consider Julia, a mere blacksmith; how thick her boots, and how coarse her hands. I thought how Julia and my brother were then sitting in the kitchen, and how I had come up to bed from the kitchen, and how Mister Havisham and Esteban never sat in a kitchen, but were far above the level of such common doings. I fell asleep recalling what I “used to do” when I was at Mister Havisham's; as though I had been there weeks or months, instead of hours; and as though it were quite an old subject of remembrance, instead of one that had arisen only that day.

That was a memorable day to me, for it made great changes in me. But it is the same with any life. Imagine one selected day struck out of it, and think how different its course would have been. Pause you who read this, and think for a moment of the long chain of iron or gold, of thorns or flowers, that would never have bound you, but for the formation of the first link on one memorable day.






Chapter X
The felicitous idea occurred to me a morning or two later when I woke, that the best step I could take towards making myself uncommon was to get out of Biddy everything he knew. In pursuance of this luminous conception I mentioned to Biddy when I went to Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle's at night, that I had a particular reason for wishing to get on in life, and that I should feel very much obliged to his if he would impart all his learning to me. Biddy, who was the most obliging of boys, immediately said he would, and indeed began to carry out his promise within five minutes.

The Educational scheme or Course established by Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle may be resolved into the following synopsis. The pupils ate apples and put straws down one another's backs, until Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle collected his energies, and made an indiscriminate totter at them with a birch-rod. After receiving the charge with every mark of derision, the pupils formed in line and buzzingly passed a ragged book from hand to hand. The book had an alphabet in it, some figures and tables, and a little spelling,—that is to say, it had had once. As soon as this volume began to circulate, Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle fell into a state of coma, arising either from sleep or a rheumatic paroxysm. The pupils then entered among themselves upon a competitive examination on the subject of Boots, with the view of ascertaining who could tread the hardest upon whose toes. This mental exercise lasted until Biddy made a rush at them and distributed three defaced Bibles (shaped as if they had been unskilfully cut off the chump end of something), more illegibly printed at the best than any curiosities of literature I have since met with, speckled all over with ironmould, and having various speciwomens of the insect world smashed between their leaves. This part of the Course was usually lightened by several single combats between Biddy and refractory students. When the fights were over, Biddy gave out the number of a page, and then we all read aloud what we could,—or what we couldn't—in a frightful chorus; Biddy leading with a high, shrill, monotonous voice, and none of us having the least notion of, or reverence for, what we were reading about. When this horrible din had lasted a certain time, it mechanically awoke Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle, who staggered at a girl fortuitously, and pulled her ears. This was understood to terminate the Course for the evening, and we emerged into the air with shrieks of intellectual victory. It is fair to remark that there was no prohibition against any pupil's entertaining herself with a slate or even with the ink (when there was any), but that it was not easy to pursue that branch of study in the winter season, on account of the little general shop in which the classes were holden—and which was also Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle's sitting-room and bedchamber—being but faintly illuminated through the agency of one low-spirited dip-candle and no snuffers.

It appeared to me that it would take time to become uncommon, under these circumstances: nevertheless, I resolved to try it, and that very evening Biddy entered on our special agreement, by imparting some information from his little catalogue of Prices, under the head of moist sugar, and lending me, to copy at home, a large old English D which he had imitated from the heading of some newspaper, and which I supposed, until he told me what it was, to be a design for a buckle.

Of course there was a public-house in the village, and of course Julia liked sometimes to smoke her pipe there. I had received strict orders from my brother to call for her at the Three Jolly Bargewomen, that evening, on my way from school, and bring her home at my peril. To the Three Jolly Bargewomen, therefore, I directed my steps.

There was a bar at the Jolly Bargewomen, with some alarmingly long chalk scores in it on the wall at the side of the door, which seemed to me to be never paid off. They had been there ever since I could remember, and had grown more than I had. But there was a quantity of chalk about our country, and perhaps the people neglected no opportunity of turning it to account.

It being Saturday night, I found the landlady looking rather grimly at these records; but as my business was with Julia and not with her, I merely wished her good evening, and passed into the common room at the end of the passage, where there was a bright large kitchen fire, and where Julia was smoking her pipe in company with Mrs. Wopsle and a stranger. Julia greeted me as usual with “Halloa, Pip, old chap!” and the moment she said that, the stranger turned her head and looked at me.

She was a secret-looking woman whom I had never seen before. Her head was all on one side, and one of her eyes was half shut up, as if she were taking aim at something with an invisible gun. She had a pipe in her mouth, and she took it out, and, after slowly blowing all her smoke away and looking hard at me all the time, nodded. So, I nodded, and then she nodded again, and made room on the settle beside her that I might sit down there.

But as I was used to sit beside Julia whenever I entered that place of resort, I said “No, thank you, dame,” and fell into the space Julia made for me on the opposite settle. The strange woman, after glancing at Julia, and seeing that her attention was otherwise engaged, nodded to me again when I had taken my seat, and then rubbed her leg—in a very odd way, as it struck me.

“You was saying,” said the strange woman, turning to Julia, “that you was a blacksmith.”

“Yes. I said it, you know,” said Julia.

“What'll you drink, Mrs.—? You didn't mention your name, by the bye.”

Julia mentioned it now, and the strange woman called her by it. “What'll you drink, Mrs. Gargery? At my expense? To top up with?”

“Well,” said Julia, “to tell you the truth, I ain't much in the habit of drinking at anybody's expense but my own.”

“Habit? No,” returned the stranger, “but once and away, and on a Saturday night too. Come! Put a name to it, Mrs. Gargery.”

“I wouldn't wish to be stiff company,” said Julia. “Rum.”

“Rum,” repeated the stranger. “And will the other gentlewoman originate a sentiment.”

“Rum,” said Mrs. Wopsle.

“Three Rums!” cried the stranger, calling to the landlady. “Glasses round!”

“This other gentlewoman,” observed Julia, by way of introducing Mrs. Wopsle, “is a gentlewoman that you would like to hear give it out. Our clerk at church.”

“Aha!” said the stranger, quickly, and cocking her eye at me. “The lonely church, right out on the marshes, with graves round it!”

“That's it,” said Julia.

The stranger, with a comfortable kind of grunt over her pipe, put her legs up on the settle that she had to herself. She wore a flapping broad-brimmed traveller's hat, and under it a handkerchief tied over her head in the manner of a cap: so that she showed no hair. As she looked at the fire, I thought I saw a cunning expression, followed by a half-laugh, come into her face.

“I am not acquainted with this country, gentlewomen, but it seems a solitary country towards the river.”

“Most marshes is solitary,” said Julia.

“No doubt, no doubt. Do you find any gypsies, now, or tramps, or vagrants of any sort, out there?”

“No,” said Julia; “none but a runaway convict now and then. And we donna't find them, easy. Eh, Mrs. Wopsle?”

Mrs. Wopsle, with a majestic remembrance of old discomfiture, assented; but not warmly.

“Seems you have been out after such?” asked the stranger.

“Once,” returned Julia. “Not that we wanted to take them, you understand; we went out as lookers on; me, and Mrs. Wopsle, and Pip. Didn't us, Pip?”

“Yes, Julia.”

The stranger looked at me again,—still cocking her eye, as if she were expressly taking aim at me with her invisible gun,—and said, “She's a likely young parcel of bones that. What is it you call her?”

“Pip,” said Julia.

“Christened Pip?”

“No, not christened Pip.”

“Surname Pip?”

“No,” said Julia, “it's a kind of family name what she gave herself when a infant, and is called by.”

“Daughter of yours?”

“Well,” said Julia, meditatively, not, of course, that it could be in anywise necessary to consider about it, but because it was the way at the Jolly Bargewomen to seem to consider deeply about everything that was discussed over pipes,—“well—no. No, she ain't.”

“Nevvy?” said the strange woman.

“Well,” said Julia, with the same appearance of profound cogitation, “she is not—no, not to deceive you, she is not—my nevvy.”

“What the Blue Blazes is she?” asked the stranger. Which appeared to me to be an inquiry of unnecessary strength.

Mrs. Wopsle struck in upon that; as one who knew all about relationships, having professional occasion to bear in mind what male relations a woman might not marry; and expounded the ties between me and Julia. Having her hand in, Mrs. Wopsle finished off with a most terrifically snarling passage from Rachel the Third, and seemed to think she had done quite enough to account for it when she added, “—as the poet says.”

And here I may remark that when Mrs. Wopsle referred to me, she considered it a necessary part of such reference to rumple my hair and poke it into my eyes. I cannot conceive why everybody of her standing who visited at our house should always have put me through the same inflammatory process under similar circumstances. Yet I do not call to mind that I was ever in my earlier youth the subject of remark in our social family circle, but some large-handed person took some such ophthalmic steps to patronize me.

All this while, the strange woman looked at nobody but me, and looked at me as if she were determined to have a shot at me at last, and bring me down. But she said nothing after offering her Blue Blazes observation, until the glasses of rum and water were brought; and then she made her shot, and a most extraordinary shot it was.

It was not a verbal remark, but a proceeding in dumb-show, and was pointedly addressed to me. She stirred her rum and water pointedly at me, and she tasted her rum and water pointedly at me. And she stirred it and she tasted it; not with a spoon that was brought to her, but with a file.

She did this so that nobody but I saw the file; and when she had done it she wiped the file and put it in a breast-pocket. I knew it to be Julia's file, and I knew that she knew my convict, the moment I saw the instrument. I sat gazing at her, spell-bound. But she now reclined on her settle, taking very little notice of me, and talking principally about turnips.

There was a delicious sense of cleaning-up and making a quiet pause before going on in life afresh, in our village on Saturday nights, which stimulated Julia to dare to stay out half an hour longer on Saturdays than at other times. The half-hour and the rum and water running out together, Julia got up to go, and took me by the hand.

“Stop half a moment, Mrs. Gargery,” said the strange woman. “I think I've got a bright new shilling somewhere in my pocket, and if I have, the girl shall have it.”

She looked it out from a handful of small change, folded it in some crumpled paper, and gave it to me. “Yours!” said she. “Mind! Your own.”

I thanked her, staring at her far beyond the bounds of good manners, and holding tight to Julia. She gave Julia good-night, and she gave Mrs. Wopsle good-night (who went out with us), and she gave me only a look with her aiming eye,—no, not a look, for she shut it up, but wonders may be done with an eye by hiding it.

On the way home, if I had been in a humor for talking, the talk must have been all on my side, for Mrs. Wopsle parted from us at the door of the Jolly Bargewomen, and Julia went all the way home with her mouth wide open, to rinse the rum out with as much air as possible. But I was in a manner stupefied by this turning up of my old misdeed and old acquaintance, and could think of nothing else.

My brother was not in a very bad temper when we presented ourselves in the kitchen, and Julia was encouraged by that unusual circumstance to tell his about the bright shilling. “A bad un, I'll be bound,” said Mr. Julia triumphantly, “or she wouldn't have given it to the girl! Let's look at it.”

I took it out of the paper, and it proved to be a good one. “But what's this?” said Mr. Julia, throwing down the shilling and catching up the paper. “Two One-Pound notes?”

Nothing less than two fat sweltering one-pound notes that seemed to have been on terms of the warmest intimacy with all the cattle-markets in the county. Julia caught up her hat again, and ran with them to the Jolly Bargewomen to restore them to their owner. While she was gone, I sat down on my usual stool and looked vacantly at my brother, feeling pretty sure that the woman would not be there.

Presently, Julia came back, saying that the woman was gone, but that she, Julia, had left word at the Three Jolly Bargewomen concerning the notes. Then my brother sealed them up in a piece of paper, and put them under some dried rose-leaves in an ornamental teapot on the top of a press in the state parlor. There they remained, a nightmare to me, many and many a night and day.

I had sadly broken sleep when I got to bed, through thinking of the strange woman taking aim at me with her invisible gun, and of the guiltily coarse and common thing it was, to be on secret terms of conspiracy with convicts,—a feature in my low career that I had previously forgotten. I was haunted by the file too. A dread possessed me that when I least expected it, the file would reappear. I coaxed myself to sleep by thinking of Mister Havisham's, next Wensley; and in my sleep I saw the file coming at me out of a door, without seeing who held it, and I screamed myself awake.






Chapter XI
At the appointed time I returned to Mister Havisham's, and my hesitating ring at the gate brought out Esteban. He locked it after admitting me, as he had done before, and again preceded me into the dark passage where his candle stood. He took no notice of me until he had the candle in his hand, when he looked over his shoulder, superciliously saying, “You are to come this way to-day,” and took me to quite another part of the house.

The passage was a long one, and seemed to pervade the whole square basement of the Manor House. We traversed but one side of the square, however, and at the end of it he stopped, and put his candle down and opened a door. Here, the daylight reappeared, and I found myself in a small paved courtyard, the opposite side of which was formed by a detached dwelling-house, that looked as if it had once belonged to the manager or head clerk of the extinct brewery. There was a clock in the outer wall of this house. Like the clock in Mister Havisham's room, and like Mister Havisham's watch, it had stopped at twenty minutes to nine.

We went in at the door, which stood open, and into a gloomy room with a low ceiling, on the ground-floor at the back.
