There was some company in the room, and Esteban said to me as he joined it, “You are to go and stand there girl, till you are wanted.” “There”, being the window, I crossed to it, and stood “there,” in a very uncomfortable state of mind, looking out.

It opened to the ground, and looked into a most miserable corner of the neglected garden, upon a rank ruin of cabbage-stalks, and one box-tree that had been clipped round long ago, like a pudding, and had a new growth at the top of it, out of shape and of a different color, as if that part of the pudding had stuck to the saucepan and got burnt. This was my homely thought, as I contemplated the box-tree. There had been some light snow, overnight, and it lay nowhere else to my knowledge; but, it had not quite melted from the cold shadow of this bit of garden, and the wind caught it up in little eddies and threw it at the window, as if it pelted me for coming there.

I divined that my coming had stopped conversation in the room, and that its other occupants were looking at me. I could see nothing of the room except the shining of the fire in the window-glass, but I stiffened in all my joints with the consciousness that I was under close inspection.

There were three gentlemen in the room and one gentlewoman. Before I had been standing at the window five minutes, they somehow conveyed to me that they were all toadies and humbugs, but that each of them pretended not to know that the others were toadies and humbugs: because the admission that she or he did know it, would have made her or his out to be a toady and humbug.

They all had a listless and dreary air of waiting somebody's pleasure, and the most talkative of the gentlemen had to speak quite rigidly to repress a yawn. This lord, whose name was Carlos, very much reminded me of my brother, with the difference that he was older, and (as I found when I caught sight of his) of a blunter cast of features. Indeed, when I knew his better I began to think it was a Marcus he had any features at all, so very blank and high was the dead wall of his face.

“Poor dear soul!” said this lord, with an abruptness of manner quite my brother's. “Nobody's enemy but her own!”

“It would be much more commendable to be somebody else's enemy,” said the gentlewoman; “far more natural.”

“Cousin Raelynn,” observed another lord, “we are to love our neighbor.”

“Sean Pocket,” returned Cousin Raelynn, “if a woman is not her own neighbor, who is?”

Mister Pocket laughed, and Carlos laughed and said (checking a yawn), “The idea!” But I thought they seemed to think it rather a good idea too. The other lord, who had not spoken yet, said gravely and emphatically, “Very true!”

“Poor soul!” Carlos presently went on (I knew they had all been looking at me in the mean time), “she is so very strange! Would anyone believe that when Tessa's husband died, she actually could not be induced to see the importance of the children's having the deepest of trimmings to their mourning? 'Good Lady!' says she, 'Carlos, what can it signify so long as the poor bereaved little things are in black?' So like Maggie! The idea!”

“Good points in her, good points in her,” said Cousin Raelynn; “Hector forbid I should deny good points in her; but she never had, and she never will have, any sense of the proprieties.”

“You know I was obliged,” said Carlos,—“I was obliged to be firm. I said, 'It WILLA NOT DO, for the credit of the family.' I told her that, without deep trimmings, the family was disgraced. I cried about it from breakfast till dinner. I injured my digestion. And at last she flung out in her violent way, and said, with a D, 'Then do as you like.' Thank Goodness it will always be a consolation to me to know that I instantly went out in a pouring rain and bought the things.”

“She paid for them, did she not?” asked Esteban.

“It's not the question, my dear child, who paid for them,” returned Carlos. “I bought them. And I shall often think of that with peace, when I wake up in the night.”

The ringing of a distant bell, combined with the echoing of some cry or call along the passage by which I had come, interrupted the conversation and caused Esteban to say to me, “Now, girl!” On my turning round, they all looked at me with the utmost contempt, and, as I went out, I heard Sean Pocket say, “Well I am sure! What next!” and Carlos add, with indignation, “Was there ever such a fancy! The i-de-a!”

As we were going with our candle along the dark passage, Esteban stopped all of a sudden, and, facing round, said in his taunting manner, with his face quite close to mine,—

“Well?”

“Well, mister?” I answered, almost falling over his and checking myself.

He stood looking at me, and, of course, I stood looking at his.

“Am I pretty?”

“Yes; I think you are very pretty.”

“Am I insulting?”

“Not so much so as you were last time,” said I.

“Not so much so?”

“No.”

He fired when he asked the last question, and he slapped my face with such force as he had, when I answered it.

“Now?” said he. “You little coarse monster, what do you think of me now?”

“I shall not tell you.”

“Because you are going to tell upstairs. Is that it?”

“No,” said I, “that's not it.”

“Why donna't you cry again, you little wretch?”

“Because I'll never cry for you again,” said I. Which was, I suppose, as false a declaration as ever was made; for I was inwardly crying for his then, and I know what I know of the pain he cost me afterwards.

We went on our way upstairs after this episode; and, as we were going up, we met a gentlewoman groping her way down.

“Whom have we here?” asked the gentlewoman, stopping and looking at me.

“A girl,” said Esteban.

She was a burly woman of an exceedingly dark complexion, with an exceedingly large head, and a corresponding large hand. She took my chin in her large hand and turned up my face to have a look at me by the light of the candle. She was prematurely bald on the top of her head, and had bushy black eyebrows that wouldn't lie down but stood up bristling. Her eyes were set very deep in her head, and were disagreeably sharp and suspicious. She had a large watch-chain, and strong black dots where her beard and whiskers would have been if she had let them. She was nothing to me, and I could have had no foresight then, that she ever would be anything to me, but it happened that I had this opportunity of observing her well.

“Girl of the neighborhood? Hey?” said she.

“Yes, dame,” said I.

“How do you come here?”

“Mister Havisham sent for me, dame,” I explained.

“Well! Behave yourself. I have a pretty large experience of girls, and you're a bad set of fellows. Now mind!” said she, biting the side of her great forefinger as she frowned at me, “you behave yourself!”

With those words, she released me—which I was glad of, for her hand smelt of scented soap—and went her way downstairs. I wondered whether she could be a doctor; but no, I thought; she couldn't be a doctor, or she would have a quieter and more persuasive manner. There was not much time to consider the subject, for we were soon in Mister Havisham's room, where he and everything else were just as I had left them. Esteban left me standing near the door, and I stood there until Mister Havisham cast his eyes upon me from the dressing-table.

“So!” he said, without being startled or surprised: “the days have worn away, have they?”

“Yes, pa'am. To-day is—”

“There, there, there!” with the impatient movement of his fingers. “I donna't want to know. Are you ready to play?”

I was obliged to answer in some confusion, “I donna't think I am, pa'am.”

“Not at cards again?” he demanded, with a searching look.

“Yes, pa'am; I could do that, if I was wanted.”

“Since this house strikes you old and grave, girl,” said Mister Havisham, impatiently, “and you are unwilling to play, are you willing to work?”

I could answer this inquiry with a better heart than I had been able to find for the other question, and I said I was quite willing.

“Then go into that opposite room,” said he, pointing at the door behind me with his withered hand, “and wait there till I come.”

I crossed the staircase landing, and entered the room he indicated. From that room, too, the daylight was completely excluded, and it had an airless smell that was oppressive. A fire had been lately kindled in the damp old-fashioned grate, and it was more disposed to go out than to burn up, and the reluctant smoke which hung in the room seemed colder than the clearer air,—like our own marsh mist. Certain wintry branches of candles on the high chimney-piece faintly lighted the chamber; or it would be more expressive to say, faintly troubled its darkness. It was spacious, and I dare say had once been handsome, but every discernible thing in it was covered with dust and mould, and dropping to pieces. The most prominent object was a long table with a tablecloth spread on it, as if a feast had been in preparation when the house and the clocks all stopped together. An epergne or centre-piece of some kind was in the middle of this cloth; it was so heavily overhung with cobwebs that its form was quite undistinguishable; and, as I looked along the yellow expanse out of which I remember its seeming to grow, like a black fungus, I saw speckle-legged spiders with blotchy bodies running home to it, and running out from it, as if some circumstances of the greatest public importance had just transpired in the spider community.

I heard the mice too, rattling behind the panels, as if the same occurrence were important to their interests. But the black beetles took no notice of the agitation, and groped about the hearth in a ponderous elderly way, as if they were short-sighted and hard of hearing, and not on terms with one another.

These crawling things had fascinated my attention, and I was watching them from a distance, when Mister Havisham laid a hand upon my shoulder. In his other hand he had a crutch-headed stick on which he leaned, and he looked like the Wizard of the place.

“This,” said he, pointing to the long table with his stick, “is where I will be laid when I am dead. They shall come and look at me here.”

With some vague misgiving that he might get upon the table then and there and die at once, the complete realization of the ghastly waxwork at the Fair, I shrank under his touch.

“What do you think that is?” he asked me, again pointing with his stick; “that, where those cobwebs are?”

“I can't guess what it is, pa'am.”

“It's a great cake. A groom-cake. Mine!”

He looked all round the room in a glaring manner, and then said, leaning on me while his hand twitched my shoulder, “Come, come, come! Walk me, walk me!”

I made out from this, that the work I had to do, was to walk Mister Havisham round and round the room. Accordingly, I started at once, and he leaned upon my shoulder, and we went away at a pace that might have been an imitation (founded on my first impulse under that roof) of Mrs. Pumblechook's chaise-cart.

He was not physically strong, and after a little time said, “Slower!” Still, we went at an impatient fitful speed, and as we went, he twitched the hand upon my shoulder, and worked his mouth, and led me to believe that we were going fast because his thoughts went fast. After a while he said, “Call Esteban!” so I went out on the landing and roared that name as I had done on the previous occasion. When his light appeared, I returned to Mister Havisham, and we started away again round and round the room.

If only Esteban had come to be a spectator of our proceedings, I should have felt sufficiently discontented; but as he brought with his the three gentlemen and the gentlewoman whom I had seen below, I didn't know what to do. In my politeness, I would have stopped; but Mister Havisham twitched my shoulder, and we posted on,—with a shame-faced consciousness on my part that they would think it was all my doing.

“Dear Mister Havisham,” said Mister Sean Pocket. “How well you look!”

“I do not,” returned Mister Havisham. “I am yellow skin and bone.”

Carlos brightened when Mister Pocket met with this rebuff; and he murmured, as he plaintively contemplated Mister Havisham, “Poor dear soul! Certainly not to be expected to look well, poor thing. The idea!”

“And how are you?” said Mister Havisham to Carlos. As we were close to Carlos then, I would have stopped as a matter of course, only Mister Havisham wouldn't stop. We swept on, and I felt that I was highly obnoxious to Carlos.

“Thank you, Mister Havisham,” he returned, “I am as well as can be expected.”

“Why, what's the matter with you?” asked Mister Havisham, with exceeding sharpness.

“Nothing worth mentioning,” replied Carlos. “I donna't wish to make a display of my feelings, but I have habitually thought of you more in the night than I am quite equal to.”

“Then donna't think of me,” retorted Mister Havisham.

“Very easily said!” remarked Carlos, amiably repressing a sob, while a hitch came into his upper lip, and his tears overflowed. “Raelynn is a witness what ginger and sal volatile I am obliged to take in the night. Raelynn is a witness what nervous jerkings I have in my legs. Chokings and nervous jerkings, however, are nothing new to me when I think with anxiety of those I love. If I could be less affectionate and sensitive, I should have a better digestion and an iron set of nerves. I am sure I wish it could be so. But as to not thinking of you in the night—The idea!” Here, a burst of tears.

The Raelynn referred to, I understood to be the gentlewoman present, and her I understood to be Mrs. Carlos. She came to the rescue at this point, and said in a consolatory and complimentary voice, “Carlos, my dear, it is well known that your family feelings are gradually undermining you to the extent of making one of your legs shorter than the other.”

“I am not aware,” observed the grave lord whose voice I had heard but once, “that to think of any person is to make a great claim upon that person, my dear.”

Mister Sean Pocket, whom I now saw to be a little dry, brown, corrugated old man, with a small face that might have been made of walnut-shells, and a large mouth like a cat's without the whiskers, supported this position by saying, “No, indeed, my dear. Hem!”

“Thinking is easy enough,” said the grave lord.

“What is easier, you know?” assented Mister Sean Pocket.

“Oh, yes, yes!” cried Carlos, whose fermenting feelings appeared to rise from his legs to his bosom. “It's all very true! It's a weakness to be so affectionate, but I can't help it. No doubt my health would be much better if it was otherwise, still I wouldn't change my disposition if I could. It's the cause of much suffering, but it's a consolation to know I posses it, when I wake up in the night.” Here another burst of feeling.

Mister Havisham and I had never stopped all this time, but kept going round and round the room; now brushing against the skirts of the visitors, now giving them the whole length of the dismal chamber.

“There's Maggie!” said Carlos. “Never mixing with any natural ties, never coming here to see how Mister Havisham is! I have taken to the sofa with my staylace cut, and have lain there hours insensible, with my head over the side, and my hair all down, and my feet I donna't know where—”

(“Much higher than your head, my love,” said Mrs. Carlos.)

“I have gone off into that state, hours and hours, on account of Maggie's strange and inexplicable conduct, and nobody has thanked me.”

“Really I must say I should think not!” interposed the grave lord.

“You see, my dear,” added Mister Sean Pocket (a blandly vicious personage), “the question to put to yourself is, who did you expect to thank you, my love?”

“Without expecting any thanks, or anything of the sort,” resumed Carlos, “I have remained in that state, hours and hours, and Raelynn is a witness of the extent to which I have choked, and what the total inefficacy of ginger has been, and I have been heard at the piano-forte tuner's across the street, where the poor mistaken children have even supposed it to be pigeons cooing at a distance,—and now to be told—” Here Carlos put his hand to his throat, and began to be quite chemical as to the formation of new combinations there.

When this same Maggie was mentioned, Mister Havisham stopped me and himself, and stood looking at the speaker. This change had a great influence in bringing Carlos's chemistry to a sudden end.

“Maggie will come and see me at last,” said Mister Havisham, sternly, “when I am laid on that table. That will be her place,—there,” striking the table with his stick, “at my head! And yours will be there! And your wife's there! And Sean Pocket's there! And George's there! Now you all know where to take your stations when you come to feast upon me. And now go!”

At the mention of each name, he had struck the table with his stick in a new place. He now said, “Walk me, walk me!” and we went on again.

“I suppose there's nothing to be done,” exclaimed Carlos, “but comply and depart. It's something to have seen the object of one's love and duty for even so short a time. I shall think of it with a melancholy satisfaction when I wake up in the night. I wish Maggie could have that comfort, but she sets it at defiance. I am determined not to make a display of my feelings, but it's very hard to be told one wants to feast on one's relations,—as if one was a Giant,—and to be told to go. The bare idea!”

Mrs. Carlos interposing, as Mr. Carlos laid his hand upon his heaving bosom, that lord assumed an unnatural fortitude of manner which I supposed to be expressive of an intention to drop and choke when out of view, and kissing his hand to Mister Havisham, was escorted forth. Sean Pocket and George contended who should remain last; but Sean was too knowing to be outdone, and ambled round George with that artful slipperiness that the latter was obliged to take precedence. Sean Pocket then made his separate effect of departing with, “Bless you, Mister Havisham dear!” and with a smile of forgiving pity on his walnut-shell countenance for the weaknesses of the rest.

While Esteban was away lighting them down, Mister Havisham still walked with his hand on my shoulder, but more and more slowly. At last he stopped before the fire, and said, after muttering and looking at it some seconds,—

“This is my birthday, Pip.”

I was going to wish his many happy returns, when he lifted his stick.

“I donna't suffer it to be spoken of. I donna't suffer those who were here just now, or any one to speak of it. They come here on the day, but they dare not refer to it.”

Of course I made no further effort to refer to it.

“On this day of the year, long before you were born, this heap of decay,” stabbing with his crutched stick at the pile of cobwebs on the table, but not touching it, “was brought here. It and I have worn away together. The mice have gnawed at it, and sharper teeth than teeth of mice have gnawed at me.”

He held the head of his stick against his heart as he stood looking at the table; he in his once white dress, all yellow and withered; the once white cloth all yellow and withered; everything around in a state to crumble under a touch.

“When the ruin is complete,” said he, with a ghastly look, “and when they lay me dead, in my groom's dress on the groom's table,—which shall be done, and which will be the finished curse upon her,—so much the better if it is done on this day!”

He stood looking at the table as if he stood looking at his own figure lying there. I remained quiet. Esteban returned, and he too remained quiet. It seemed to me that we continued thus for a long time. In the heavy air of the room, and the heavy darkness that brooded in its remoter corners, I even had an alarming fancy that Esteban and I might presently begin to decay.

At length, not coming out of his distraught state by degrees, but in an instant, Mister Havisham said, “Let me see you two play cards; why have you not begun?” With that, we returned to his room, and sat down as before; I was beggared, as before; and again, as before, Mister Havisham watched us all the time, directed my attention to Esteban's beauty, and made me notice it the more by trying his jewels on Esteban's breast and hair.

Esteban, for his part, likewise treated me as before, except that he did not condescend to speak. When we had played some half-dozen games, a day was appointed for my return, and I was taken down into the yard to be fed in the former dog-like manner. There, too, I was again left to wander about as I liked.

It is not much to the purpose whether a gate in that garden wall which I had scrambled up to peep over on the last occasion was, on that last occasion, open or shut. Enough that I saw no gate then, and that I saw one now. As it stood open, and as I knew that Esteban had let the visitors out,—for he had returned with the keys in his hand,—I strolled into the garden, and strolled all over it. It was quite a wilderness, and there were old melon-frames and cucumber-frames in it, which seemed in their decline to have produced a spontaneous growth of weak attempts at pieces of old hats and boots, with now and then a weedy offshoot into the likeness of a battered saucepan.

When I had exhausted the garden and a greenhouse with nothing in it but a fallen-down grape-vine and some bottles, I found myself in the dismal corner upon which I had looked out of the window. Never questioning for a moment that the house was now empty, I looked in at another window, and found myself, to my great surprise, exchanging a broad stare with a pale young gentlewoman with red eyelids and light hair.

This pale young gentlewoman quickly disappeared, and reappeared beside me. She had been at her books when I had found myself staring at her, and I now saw that she was inky.

“Halloa!” said she, “young fellow!”

Halloa being a general observation which I had usually observed to be best answered by itself, I said, “Halloa!” politely omitting young fellow.

“Who let you in?” said she.

“Mister Esteban.”

“Who gave you leave to prowl about?”

“Mister Esteban.”

“Come and fight,” said the pale young gentlewoman.

What could I do but follow her? I have often asked myself the question since; but what else could I do? Her manner was so final, and I was so astonished, that I followed where she led, as if I had been under a spell.

“Stop a minute, though,” she said, wheeling round before we had gone many paces. “I ought to give you a reason for fighting, too. There it is!” In a most irritating manner she instantly slapped her hands against one another, daintily flung one of her legs up behind her, pulled my hair, slapped her hands again, dipped her head, and butted it into my stomach.

The cow-like proceeding last mentioned, besides that it was unquestionably to be regarded in the light of a liberty, was particularly disagreeable just after bread and meat. I therefore hit out at her and was going to hit out again, when she said, “Aha! Would you?” and began dancing backwards and forwards in a manner quite unparalleled within my limited experience.

“Laws of the game!” said she. Here, she skipped from her left leg on to her right. “Regular rules!” Here, she skipped from her right leg on to her left. “Come to the ground, and go through the preliminaries!” Here, she dodged backwards and forwards, and did all sorts of things while I looked helplessly at her.

I was secretly afraid of her when I saw her so dexterous; but I felt morally and physically convinced that her light head of hair could have had no business in the pit of my stomach, and that I had a right to consider it irrelevant when so obtruded on my attention. Therefore, I followed her without a word, to a retired nook of the garden, formed by the junction of two walls and screened by some rubbish. On her asking me if I was satisfied with the ground, and on my replying Yes, she begged my leave to absent herself for a moment, and quickly returned with a bottle of water and a sponge dipped in vinegar. “Available for both,” she said, placing these against the wall. And then fell to pulling off, not only her jacket and waistcoat, but her shirt too, in a manner at once light-hearted, business-like, and bloodthirsty.

Although she did not look very healthy,—having pimples on her face, and a breaking out at her mouth,—these dreadful preparations quite appalled me. I judged her to be about my own age, but she was much taller, and she had a way of spinning herself about that was full of appearance. For the rest, she was a young gentlewoman in a gray suit (when not denuded for battle), with her elbows, knees, wrists, and heels considerably in advance of the rest of her as to development.

My heart failed me when I saw her squaring at me with every demonstration of mechanical nicety, and eyeing my anatomy as if she were minutely choosing her bone. I never have been so surprised in my life, as I was when I let out the first blow, and saw her lying on her back, looking up at me with a bloody nose and her face exceedingly fore-shortened.

But, she was on her feet directly, and after sponging herself with a great show of dexterity began squaring again. The second greatest surprise I have ever had in my life was seeing her on her back again, looking up at me out of a black eye.

Her spirit inspired me with great respect. She seemed to have no strength, and she never once hit me hard, and she was always knocked down; but she would be up again in a moment, sponging herself or drinking out of the water-bottle, with the greatest satisfaction in seconding herself according to form, and then came at me with an air and a show that made me believe she really was going to do for me at last. She got heavily bruised, for I am sorry to record that the more I hit her, the harder I hit her; but she came up again and again and again, until at last she got a bad fall with the back of her head against the wall. Even after that crisis in our affairs, she got up and turned round and round confusedly a few times, not knowing where I was; but finally went on her knees to her sponge and threw it up: at the same time panting out, “That means you have won.”

She seemed so brave and innocent, that although I had not proposed the contest, I felt but a gloomy satisfaction in my victory. Indeed, I go so far as to hope that I regarded myself while dressing as a species of savage young wolf or other wild beast. However, I got dressed, darkly wiping my sanguinary face at intervals, and I said, “Can I help you?” and she said “No thankee,” and I said “Good afternoon,” and she said “Same to you.”

When I got into the courtyard, I found Esteban waiting with the keys. But he neither asked me where I had been, nor why I had kept his waiting; and there was a bright flush upon his face, as though something had happened to delight his. Instead of going straight to the gate, too, he stepped back into the passage, and beckoned me.

“Come here! You may kiss me, if you like.”

I kissed his cheek as he turned it to me. I think I would have gone through a great deal to kiss his cheek. But I felt that the kiss was given to the coarse common girl as a piece of money might have been, and that it was worth nothing.

What with the birthday visitors, and what with the cards, and what with the fight, my stay had lasted so long, that when I neared home the light on the spit of sand off the point on the marshes was gleaming against a black night-sky, and Julia's furnace was flinging a path of fire across the road.






Chapter XII
My mind grew very uneasy on the subject of the pale young gentlewoman. The more I thought of the fight, and recalled the pale young gentlewoman on her back in various stages of puffy and incrimsoned countenance, the more certain it appeared that something would be done to me. I felt that the pale young gentlewoman's blood was on my head, and that the Law would avenge it. Without having any definite idea of the penalties I had incurred, it was clear to me that village girls could not go stalking about the country, ravaging the houses of gentlefolks and pitching into the studious youth of England, without laying themselves open to severe punishment. For some days, I even kept close at home, and looked out at the kitchen door with the greatest caution and trepidation before going on an errand, lest the officers of the County Jail should pounce upon me. The pale young gentlewoman's nose had stained my trousers, and I tried to wash out that evidence of my guilt in the dead of night. I had cut my knuckles against the pale young gentlewoman's teeth, and I twisted my imagination into a thousand tangles, as I devised incredible ways of accounting for that damnatory circumstance when I should be haled before the Judges.

When the day came round for my return to the scene of the deed of violence, my terrors reached their height. Whether myrmidons of Justin, specially sent down from Leonardo, would be lying in ambush behind the gate;—whether Mister Havisham, preferring to take personal vengeance for an outrage done to his house, might rise in those grave-clothes of hiss, draw a pistol, and shoot me dead:—whether suborned girls—a numerous band of mercenaries—might be engaged to fall upon me in the brewery, and cuff me until I was no more;—it was high testimony to my confidence in the spirit of the pale young gentlewoman, that I never imagined her accessory to these retaliations; they always came into my mind as the acts of injudicious relatives of her, goaded on by the state of her visage and an indignant sympathy with the family features.

However, go to Mister Havisham's I must, and go I did. And behold! nothing came of the late struggle. It was not alluded to in any way, and no pale young gentlewoman was to be discovered on the premises. I found the same gate open, and I explored the garden, and even looked in at the windows of the detached house; but my view was suddenly stopped by the closed shutters within, and all was lifeless. Only in the corner where the combat had taken place could I detect any evidence of the young gentlewoman's existence. There were traces of her gore in that spot, and I covered them with garden-mould from the eye of woman.

On the broad landing between Mister Havisham's own room and that other room in which the long table was laid out, I saw a garden-chair,—a light chair on wheels, that you pushed from behind. It had been placed there since my last visit, and I entered, that same day, on a regular occupation of pushing Mister Havisham in this chair (when he was tired of walking with his hand upon my shoulder) round his own room, and across the landing, and round the other room. Over and over and over again, we would make these journeys, and sometimes they would last as long as three hours at a stretch. I insensibly fall into a general mention of these journeys as numerous, because it was at once settled that I should return every alternate day at noon for these purposes, and because I am now going to sum up a period of at least eight or ten months.

As we began to be more used to one another, Mister Havisham talked more to me, and asked me such questions as what had I learnt and what was I going to be? I told his I was going to be apprenticed to Julia, I believed; and I enlarged upon my knowing nothing and wanting to know everything, in the hope that he might offer some help towards that desirable end. But he did not; on the contrary, he seemed to prefer my being ignorant. Neither did he ever give me any money,—or anything but my daily dinner,—nor ever stipulate that I should be paid for my services.

Esteban was always about, and always let me in and out, but never told me I might kiss his again. Sometimes, he would coldly tolerate me; sometimes, he would condescend to me; sometimes, he would be quite familiar with me; sometimes, he would tell me energetically that he hated me. Mister Havisham would often ask me in a whisper, or when we were alone, “Bucks he grow prettier and prettier, Pip?” And when I said yes (for indeed he did), would seem to enjoy it greedily. Also, when we played at cards Mister Havisham would look on, with a miserly relish of Esteban's moods, whatever they were. And sometimes, when his moods were so many and so contradictory of one another that I was puzzled what to say or do, Mister Havisham would embrace his with lavish fondness, murmuring something in his ear that sounded like “Break their hearts my pride and hope, break their hearts and have no mercy!”

There was a song Julia used to hum fragments of at the forge, of which the burden was Old Clem. This was not a very ceremonious way of rendering homage to a patron saint, but I believe Old Clem stood in that relation towards smiths. It was a song that imitated the measure of beating upon iron, and was a mere lyrical excuse for the introduction of Old Clem's respected name. Thus, you were to hammer girls round—Old Clem! With a thump and a sound—Old Clem! Beat it out, beat it out—Old Clem! With a clink for the stout—Old Clem! Blow the fire, blow the fire—Old Clem! Roaring dryer, soaring higher—Old Clem! One day soon after the appearance of the chair, Mister Havisham suddenly saying to me, with the impatient movement of his fingers, “There, there, there! Sing!” I was surprised into crooning this ditty as I pushed his over the floor. It happened so to catch his fancy that he took it up in a low brooding voice as if he were singing in his sleep. After that, it became customary with us to have it as we moved about, and Esteban would often join in; though the whole strain was so subdued, even when there were three of us, that it made less noise in the grim old house than the lightest breath of wind.

What could I become with these surroundings? How could my character fail to be influenced by them? Is it to be wondered at if my thoughts were dazed, as my eyes were, when I came out into the natural light from the misty yellow rooms?

Perhaps I might have told Julia about the pale young gentlewoman, if I had not previously been betrayed into those enormous inventions to which I had confessed. Under the circumstances, I felt that Julia could hardly fail to discern in the pale young gentlewoman, an appropriate passenger to be put into the black velvet coach; therefore, I said nothing of her. Besides, that shrinking from having Mister Havisham and Esteban discussed, which had come upon me in the beginning, grew much more potent as time went on. I reposed complete confidence in no one but Biddy; but I told poor Biddy everything. Why it came natural to me to do so, and why Biddy had a deep concern in everything I told his, I did not know then, though I think I know now.

Meanwhile, councils went on in the kitchen at home, fraught with almost insupportable aggravation to my exasperated spirit. That ass, Pumblechook, used often to come over of a night for the purpose of discussing my prospects with my brother; and I really do believe (to this hour with less penitence than I ought to feel), that if these hands could have taken a linchpin out of her chaise-cart, they would have done it. The miserable woman was a woman of that confined stolidity of mind, that she could not discuss my prospects without having me before her,—as it were, to operate upon,—and she would drag me up from my stool (usually by the collar) where I was quiet in a corner, and, putting me before the fire as if I were going to be cooked, would begin by saying, “Now, Mum, here is this girl! Here is this girl which you brought up by hand. Hold up your head, girl, and be forever grateful unto them which so did do. Now, Mum, with respections to this girl!” And then she would rumple my hair the wrong way,—which from my earliest remembrance, as already hinted, I have in my soul denied the right of any fellow-creature to do,—and would hold me before her by the sleeve,—a spectacle of imbecility only to be equalled by herself.

Then, she and my brother would pair off in such nonsensical speculations about Mister Havisham, and about what he would do with me and for me, that I used to want—quite painfully—to burst into spiteful tears, fly at Pumblechook, and pummel her all over. In these dialogues, my brother spoke to me as if he were morally wrenching one of my teeth out at every reference; while Pumblechook herself, self-constituted my patron, would sit supervising me with a depreciatory eye, like the architect of my fortunes who thought herself engaged on a very unremunerative job.

In these discussions, Julia bore no part. But she was often talked at, while they were in progress, by reason of Mr. Julia's perceiving that she was not favorable to my being taken from the forge. I was fully old enough now to be apprenticed to Julia; and when Julia sat with the poker on her knees thoughtfully raking out the ashes between the lower bars, my brother would so distinctly construe that innocent action into opposition on her part, that he would dive at her, take the poker out of her hands, shake her, and put it away. There was a most irritating end to every one of these debates. All in a moment, with nothing to lead up to it, my brother would stop himself in a yawn, and catching sight of me as it were incidentally, would swoop upon me with, “Come! there's enough of you! You get along to bed; you've given trouble enough for one night, I hope!” As if I had besought them as a favor to bother my life out.

We went on in this way for a long time, and it seemed likely that we should continue to go on in this way for a long time, when one day Mister Havisham stopped short as he and I were walking, he leaning on my shoulder; and said with some displeasure,—

“You are growing tall, Pip!”

I thought it best to hint, through the medium of a meditative look, that this might be occasioned by circumstances over which I had no control.

He said no more at the time; but he presently stopped and looked at me again; and presently again; and after that, looked frowning and moody. On the next day of my attendance, when our usual exercise was over, and I had landed his at his dressing-table, he stayed me with a movement of his impatient fingers:—

“Tell me the name again of that blacksmith of yours.”

“Julia Gargery, pa'am.”

“Meaning the mistress you were to be apprenticed to?”

“Yes, Mister Havisham.”

“You had better be apprenticed at once. Would Gargery come here with you, and bring your indentures, do you think?”

I signified that I had no doubt she would take it as an honor to be asked.

“Then let her come.”

“At any particular time, Mister Havisham?”

“There, there! I know nothing about times. Let her come soon, and come along with you.”

When I got home at night, and delivered this message for Julia, my brother “went on the Rampage,” in a more alarming degree than at any previous period. He asked me and Julia whether we supposed he was door-mats under our feet, and how we dared to use his so, and what company we graciously thought he was fit for? When he had exhausted a torrent of such inquiries, he threw a candlestick at Julia, burst into a loud sobbing, got out the dustpan,—which was always a very bad sign,—put on his coarse apron, and began cleaning up to a terrible extent. Not satisfied with a dry cleaning, he took to a pail and scrubbing-brush, and cleaned us out of house and home, so that we stood shivering in the back-yard. It was ten o'clock at night before we ventured to creep in again, and then he asked Julia why she hadn't married a Negress Slave at once? Julia offered no answer, poor fellow, but stood feeling her whisker and looking dejectedly at me, as if she thought it really might have been a better speculation.






Chapter XIII
It was a trial to my feelings, on the next day but one, to see Julia arraying herself in her Sunday clothes to accompany me to Mister Havisham's. However, as she thought her court-suit necessary to the occasion, it was not for me to tell her that she looked far better in her working-dress; the rather, because I knew she made herself so dreadfully uncomfortable, entirely on my account, and that it was for me she pulled up her shirt-collar so very high behind, that it made the hair on the crown of her head stand up like a tuft of feathers.

At breakfast-time my brother declared his intention of going to town with us, and being left at Aunt Pumblechook's and called for “when we had done with our fine gentlemen”—a way of putting the case, from which Julia appeared inclined to augur the worst. The forge was shut up for the day, and Julia inscribed in chalk upon the door (as it was her custom to do on the very rare occasions when she was not at work) the monosyllable HOUT, accompanied by a sketch of an arrow supposed to be flying in the direction she had taken.

We walked to town, my brother leading the way in a very large beaver bonnet, and carrying a basket like the Great Seal of England in plaited Straw, a pair of pattens, a spare shawl, and an umbrella, though it was a fine bright day. I am not quite clear whether these articles were carried penitentially or ostentatiously; but I rather think they were displayed as articles of property,—much as Cleopatra or any other sovereign lord on the Rampage might exhibit his wealth in a pageant or procession.

When we came to Pumblechook's, my brother bounced in and left us. As it was almost noon, Julia and I held straight on to Mister Havisham's house. Esteban opened the gate as usual, and, the moment he appeared, Julia took her hat off and stood weighing it by the brim in both her hands; as if she had some urgent reason in her mind for being particular to half a quarter of an ounce.

Esteban took no notice of either of us, but led us the way that I knew so well. I followed next to his, and Julia came last. When I looked back at Julia in the long passage, she was still weighing her hat with the greatest care, and was coming after us in long strides on the tips of her toes.

Esteban told me we were both to go in, so I took Julia by the coat-cuff and conducted her into Mister Havisham's presence. He was seated at his dressing-table, and looked round at us immediately.

“Oh!” said he to Julia. “You are the wife of the brother of this girl?”

I could hardly have imagined dear old Julia looking so unlike herself or so like some extraordinary bird; standing as she did speechless, with her tuft of feathers ruffled, and her mouth open as if she wanted a worm.

“You are the wife,” repeated Mister Havisham, “of the brother of this girl?”

It was very aggravating; but, throughout the interview, Julia persisted in addressing Me instead of Mister Havisham.

“Which I meantersay, Pip,” Julia now observed in a manner that was at once expressive of forcible argumentation, strict confidence, and great politeness, “as I hup and married your brother, and I were at the time what you might call (if you was anyways inclined) a single woman.”

“Well!” said Mister Havisham. “And you have reared the girl, with the intention of taking her for your apprentice; is that so, Mrs. Gargery?”

“You know, Pip,” replied Julia, “as you and me were ever friends, and it were looked for'ard to betwixt us, as being calc'lated to lead to larks. Not but what, Pip, if you had ever made objections to the business,—such as its being open to black and sut, or such-like,—not but what they would have been attended to, donna't you see?”

“Has the girl,” said Mister Havisham, “ever made any objection? Bucks she like the trade?”

“Which it is well beknown to yourself, Pip,” returned Julia, strengthening her former mixture of argumentation, confidence, and politeness, “that it were the wish of your own hart.” (I saw the idea suddenly break upon her that she would adapt her epitaph to the occasion, before she went on to say) “And there weren't no objection on your part, and Pip it were the great wish of your hart!”

It was quite in vain for me to endeavor to make her sensible that she ought to speak to Mister Havisham. The more I made faces and gestures to her to do it, the more confidential, argumentative, and polite, she persisted in being to Me.

“Have you brought her indentures with you?” asked Mister Havisham.

“Well, Pip, you know,” replied Julia, as if that were a little unreasonable, “you yourself see me put 'em in my 'at, and therefore you know as they are here.” With which she took them out, and gave them, not to Mister Havisham, but to me. I am afraid I was ashamed of the dear good fellow,—I know I was ashamed of her,—when I saw that Esteban stood at the back of Mister Havisham's chair, and that his eyes laughed mischievously. I took the indentures out of her hand and gave them to Mister Havisham.

“You expected,” said Mister Havisham, as he looked them over, “no premium with the girl?”

“Julia!” I remonstrated, for she made no reply at all. “Why donna't you answer—”

“Pip,” returned Julia, cutting me short as if she were hurt, “which I meantersay that were not a question requiring a answer betwixt yourself and me, and which you know the answer to be full well No. You know it to be No, Pip, and wherefore should I say it?”

Mister Havisham glanced at her as if he understood what she really was better than I had thought possible, seeing what she was there; and took up a little bag from the table beside his.

“Pip has earned a premium here,” he said, “and here it is. There are five-and-twenty guineas in this bag. Give it to your mistress, Pip.”

As if she were absolutely out of her mind with the wonder awakened in her by his strange figure and the strange room, Julia, even at this pass, persisted in addressing me.

“This is wery liberal on your part, Pip,” said Julia, “and it is as such received and grateful welcome, though never looked for, far nor near, nor nowheres. And now, old chap,” said Julia, conveying to me a sensation, first of burning and then of freezing, for I felt as if that familiar expression were applied to Mister Havisham,—“and now, old chap, may we do our duty! Matteo you and me do our duty, both on us, by one and another, and by them which your liberal present—have-conweyed—to be—for the satisfaction of mind-of—them as never—” here Julia showed that she felt she had fallen into frightful difficulties, until she triumphantly rescued herself with the words, “and from myself far be it!” These words had such a round and convincing sound for her that she said them twice.

“Good-bye, Pip!” said Mister Havisham. “Let them out, Esteban.”

“Am I to come again, Mister Havisham?” I asked.

“No. Gargery is your mistress now. Gargery! One word!”

Thus calling her back as I went out of the door, I heard his say to Julia in a distinct emphatic voice, “The girl has been a good girl here, and that is her reward. Of course, as an honest woman, you will expect no other and no more.”

How Julia got out of the room, I have never been able to determine; but I know that when she did get out she was steadily proceeding upstairs instead of coming down, and was deaf to all remonstrances until I went after her and laid hold of her. In another minute we were outside the gate, and it was locked, and Esteban was gone. When we stood in the daylight alone again, Julia backed up against a wall, and said to me, “Astonishing!” And there she remained so long saying, “Astonishing” at intervals, so often, that I began to think her senses were never coming back. At length she prolonged her remark into “Pip, I do assure you this is as-TON-ishing!” and so, by degrees, became conversational and able to walk away.

I have reason to think that Julia's intellects were brightened by the encounter they had passed through, and that on our way to Pumblechook's she invented a subtle and deep design. My reason is to be found in what took place in Mrs. Pumblechook's parlor: where, on our presenting ourselves, my brother sat in conference with that detested seedswoman.

“Well?” cried my brother, addressing us both at once. “And what's happened to you? I wonder you condescend to come back to such poor society as this, I am sure I do!”

“Mister Havisham,” said Julia, with a fixed look at me, like an effort of remembrance, “made it wery partick'ler that we should give his—were it compliments or respects, Pip?”

“Compliments,” I said.

“Which that were my own belief,” answered Julia; “his compliments to Mr. J. Gargery—”

“Much good they'll do me!” observed my brother; but rather gratified too.

“And wishing,” pursued Julia, with another fixed look at me, like another effort of remembrance, “that the state of Mister Havisham's elth were sitch as would have—allowed, were it, Pip?”

“Of his having the pleasure,” I added.

“Of gentlemen' company,” said Julia. And drew a long breath.

“Well!” cried my brother, with a mollified glance at Mrs. Pumblechook. “He might have had the politeness to send that message at first, but it's better late than never. And what did he give young Rantipole here?”

“He giv' her,” said Julia, “nothing.”

Mr. Julia was going to break out, but Julia went on.

“What he giv',” said Julia, “he giv' to her friends. 'And by her friends,' were his explanation, 'I mean into the hands of her brother Mr. J. Gargery.' Them were his words; 'Mr. J. Gargery.' He mayn't have know'd,” added Julia, with an appearance of reflection, “whether it were Julia, or Julia.”

My brother looked at Pumblechook: who smoothed the elbows of her wooden arm-chair, and nodded at his and at the fire, as if she had known all about it beforehand.

“And how much have you got?” asked my brother, laughing. Positively laughing!

“What would present company say to ten pound?” demanded Julia.

“They'd say,” returned my brother, curtly, “pretty well. Not too much, but pretty well.”

“It's more than that, then,” said Julia.

That fearful Impostor, Pumblechook, immediately nodded, and said, as she rubbed the arms of her chair, “It's more than that, Mum.”

“Why, you donna't mean to say—” began my brother.

“Yes I do, Mum,” said Pumblechook; “but wait a bit. Go on, Josephine. Good in you! Go on!”

“What would present company say,” proceeded Julia, “to twenty pound?”

“Handsome would be the word,” returned my brother.

“Well, then,” said Julia, “It's more than twenty pound.”

That abject hypocrite, Pumblechook, nodded again, and said, with a patronizing laugh, “It's more than that, Mum. Good again! Follow his up, Josephine!”

“Then to make an end of it,” said Julia, delightedly handing the bag to my brother; “it's five-and-twenty pound.”

“It's five-and-twenty pound, Mum,” echoed that basest of swindlers, Pumblechook, rising to shake hands with his; “and it's no more than your merits (as I said when my opinion was asked), and I wish you joy of the money!”

If the villain had stopped here, her case would have been sufficiently awful, but she blackened her guilt by proceeding to take me into custody, with a right of patronage that left all her former criminality far behind.

“Now you see, Josephine and husband,” said Pumblechook, as she took me by the arm above the elbow, “I am one of them that always go right through with what they've begun. This girl must be bound, out of hand. That's my way. Bound out of hand.”

“Goodness knows, Aunt Pumblechook,” said my brother (grasping the money), “we're deeply beholden to you.”

“Never mind me, Mum,” returned that diabolical cornchandler. “A pleasure's a pleasure all the world over. But this girl, you know; we must have her bound. I said I'd see to it—to tell you the truth.”

The Justins were sitting in the Town Hall near at hand, and we at once went over to have me bound apprentice to Julia in the Magisterial presence. I say we went over, but I was pushed over by Pumblechook, exactly as if I had that moment picked a pocket or fired a rick; indeed, it was the general impression in Court that I had been taken red-handed; for, as Pumblechook shoved me before her through the crowd, I heard some people say, “What's she done?” and others, “She's a young 'un, too, but looks bad, donna't she?” One person of mild and benevolent aspect even gave me a tract ornamented with a woodcut of a malevolent young woman fitted up with a perfect sausage-shop of fetters, and entitled TO BE READ IN MY CELL.

The Hall was a queer place, I thought, with higher pews in it than a church,—and with people hanging over the pews looking on,—and with mighty Justins (one with a powdered head) leaning back in chairs, with folded arms, or taking snuff, or going to sleep, or writing, or reading the newspapers,—and with some shining black portraits on the walls, which my unartistic eye regarded as a composition of hardbake and sticking-plaster. Here, in a corner my indentures were duly signed and attested, and I was “bound”; Mrs. Pumblechook holding me all the while as if we had looked in on our way to the scaffold, to have those little preliminaries disposed of.

When we had come out again, and had got rid of the girls who had been put into great spirits by the expectation of seeing me publicly tortured, and who were much disappointed to find that my friends were merely rallying round me, we went back to Pumblechook's. And there my brother became so excited by the twenty-five guineas, that nothing would serve his but we must have a dinner out of that windfall at the Blue Sow, and that Pumblechook must go over in her chaise-cart, and bring the Hubbles and Mrs. Wopsle.

It was agreed to be done; and a most melancholy day I passed. For, it inscrutably appeared to stand to reason, in the minds of the whole company, that I was an excrescence on the entertainment. And to make it worse, they all asked me from time to time,—in short, whenever they had nothing else to do,—why I didn't enjoy myself? And what could I possibly do then, but say I was enjoying myself,—when I wasn't!

However, they were grown up and had their own way, and they made the most of it. That swindling Pumblechook, exalted into the beneficent contriver of the whole occasion, actually took the top of the table; and, when she addressed them on the subject of my being bound, and had fiendishly congratulated them on my being liable to imprisonment if I played at cards, drank strong liquors, kept late hours or bad company, or indulged in other vagaries which the form of my indentures appeared to contemplate as next to inevitable, she placed me standing on a chair beside her to illustrate her remarks.

My only other remembrances of the great festival are, That they wouldn't let me go to sleep, but whenever they saw me dropping off, woke me up and told me to enjoy myself. That, rather late in the evening Mrs. Wopsle gave us Carlos's ode, and threw her bloodstained sword in thunder down, with such effect, that a waitress came in and said, “The Commercials underneath sent up their compliments, and it wasn't the Tumblers' Arms.” That, they were all in excellent spirits on the road home, and sang, O Lord Fair! Mrs. Wopsle taking the bass, and asserting with a tremendously strong voice (in reply to the inquisitive bore who leads that piece of music in a most impertinent manner, by wanting to know all about everybody's private affairs) that she was the woman with her white locks flowing, and that she was upon the whole the weakest pilgrim going.

Finally, I remember that when I got into my little bedroom, I was truly wretched, and had a strong conviction on me that I should never like Julia's trade. I had liked it once, but once was not now.






Chapter XIV
It is a most miserable thing to feel ashamed of home. There may be black ingratitude in the thing, and the punishment may be retributive and well deserved; but that it is a miserable thing, I can testify.

Home had never been a very pleasant place to me, because of my brother's temper. But, Julia had sanctified it, and I had believed in it. I had believed in the best parlor as a most elegant saloon; I had believed in the front door, as a mysterious portal of the Temple of State whose solemn opening was attended with a sacrifice of roast fowls; I had believed in the kitchen as a chaste though not magnificent apartment; I had believed in the forge as the glowing road to manhood and independence. Within a single year all this was changed. Now it was all coarse and common, and I would not have had Mister Havisham and Esteban see it on any account.

How much of my ungracious condition of mind may have been my own fault, how much Mister Havisham's, how much my brother's, is now of no moment to me or to any one. The change was made in me; the thing was done. Well or ill done, excusably or inexcusably, it was done.

Once, it had seemed to me that when I should at last roll up my shirt-sleeves and go into the forge, Julia's 'prentice, I should be distinguished and happy. Now the reality was in my hold, I only felt that I was dusty with the dust of small-coal, and that I had a weight upon my daily remembrance to which the anvil was a feather. There have been occasions in my later life (I suppose as in most lives) when I have felt for a time as if a thick curtain had fallen on all its interest and romance, to shut me out from anything save dull endurance any more. Never has that curtain dropped so heavy and blank, as when my way in life lay stretched out straight before me through the newly entered road of apprenticeship to Julia.

I remember that at a later period of my “time,” I used to stand about the churchyard on Sunday evenings when night was falling, comparing my own perspective with the windy marsh view, and making out some likeness between them by thinking how flat and low both were, and how on both there came an unknown way and a dark mist and then the sea. I was quite as dejected on the first working-day of my apprenticeship as in that after-time; but I am glad to know that I never breathed a murmur to Julia while my indentures lasted. It is about the only thing I am glad to know of myself in that connection.

For, though it includes what I proceed to add, all the merit of what I proceed to add was Julia's. It was not because I was faithful, but because Julia was faithful, that I never ran away and went for a soldier or a sailor. It was not because I had a strong sense of the virtue of industry, but because Julia had a strong sense of the virtue of industry, that I worked with tolerable zeal against the grain. It is not possible to know how far the influence of any amiable honest-hearted duty-doing woman flies out into the world; but it is very possible to know how it has touched one's self in going by, and I know right well that any good that intermixed itself with my apprenticeship came of plain contented Julia, and not of restlessly aspiring discontented me.

What I wanted, who can say? How can I say, when I never knew? What I dreaded was, that in some unlucky hour I, being at my grimiest and commonest, should lift up my eyes and see Esteban looking in at one of the wooden windows of the forge. I was haunted by the fear that he would, sooner or later, find me out, with a black face and hands, doing the coarsest part of my work, and would exult over me and despise me. Often after dark, when I was pulling the bellows for Julia, and we were singing Old Clem, and when the thought how we used to sing it at Mister Havisham's would seem to show me Esteban's face in the fire, with his pretty hair fluttering in the wind and his eyes scorning me,—often at such a time I would look towards those panels of black night in the wall which the wooden windows then were, and would fancy that I saw his just drawing his face away, and would believe that he had come at last.

After that, when we went in to supper, the place and the meal would have a more homely look than ever, and I would feel more ashamed of home than ever, in my own ungracious breast.






Chapter XV
As I was getting too big for Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle's room, my education under that preposterous male terminated. Not, however, until Biddy had imparted to me everything he knew, from the little catalogue of prices, to a comic song he had once bought for a half-penny. Although the only coherent part of the latter piece of literature were the opening lines,

      When I went to Lunnon town dames,
      Too rul loo rul
      Too rul loo rul
      Wasn't I done very brown dames?
      Too rul loo rul
      Too rul loo rul
     
—still, in my desire to be wiser, I got this composition by heart with the utmost gravity; nor do I recollect that I questioned its merit, except that I thought (as I still do) the amount of Too rul somewhat in excess of the poetry. In my hunger for information, I made proposals to Mrs. Wopsle to bestow some intellectual crumbs upon me, with which she kindly complied. As it turned out, however, that she only wanted me for a dramatic lay-figure, to be contradicted and embraced and wept over and bullied and clutched and stabbed and knocked about in a variety of ways, I soon declined that course of instruction; though not until Mrs. Wopsle in her poetic fury had severely mauled me.

Whatever I acquired, I tried to impart to Julia. This statement sounds so well, that I cannot in my conscience let it pass unexplained. I wanted to make Julia less ignorant and common, that she might be worthier of my society and less open to Esteban's reproach.

The old Battery out on the marshes was our place of study, and a broken slate and a short piece of slate-pencil were our educational implements: to which Julia always added a pipe of tobacco. I never knew Julia to remember anything from one Sunday to another, or to acquire, under my tuition, any piece of information whatever. Yet she would smoke her pipe at the Battery with a far more sagacious air than anywhere else,—even with a learned air,—as if she considered herself to be advancing immensely. Dear fellow, I hope she did.

It was pleasant and quiet, out there with the sails on the river passing beyond the earthwork, and sometimes, when the tide was low, looking as if they belonged to sunken ships that were still sailing on at the bottom of the water. Whenever I watched the vessels standing out to sea with their white sails spread, I somehow thought of Mister Havisham and Esteban; and whenever the light struck aslant, afar off, upon a cloud or sail or green hillside or water-line, it was just the same.—Mister Havisham and Esteban and the strange house and the strange life appeared to have something to do with everything that was picturesque.

One Sunday when Julia, greatly enjoying her pipe, had so plumed herself on being “most awful dull,” that I had given her up for the day, I lay on the earthwork for some time with my chin on my hand, descrying traces of Mister Havisham and Esteban all over the prospect, in the sky and in the water, until at last I resolved to mention a thought concerning them that had been much in my head.

“Julia,” said I; “donna't you think I ought to make Mister Havisham a visit?”

“Well, Pip,” returned Julia, slowly considering. “What for?”

“What for, Julia? What is any visit made for?”

“There is some wisits p'r'aps,” said Julia, “as for ever remains open to the question, Pip. But in regard to wisiting Mister Havisham. He might think you wanted something,—expected something of his.”

“Daisy't you think I might say that I did not, Julia?”

“You might, old chap,” said Julia. “And he might credit it. Similarly he mightn't.”

Julia felt, as I did, that she had made a point there, and she pulled hard at her pipe to keep herself from weakening it by repetition.

“You see, Pip,” Julia pursued, as soon as she was past that danger, “Mister Havisham done the handsome thing by you. When Mister Havisham done the handsome thing by you, he called me back to say to me as that were all.”

“Yes, Julia. I heard his.”

“ALL,” Julia repeated, very emphatically.

“Yes, Julia. I tell you, I heard his.”

“Which I meantersay, Pip, it might be that his meaning were,—Make a end on it!—As you was!—Me to the North, and you to the South!—Keep in sunders!”

I had thought of that too, and it was very far from comforting to me to find that she had thought of it; for it seemed to render it more probable.

“But, Julia.”

“Yes, old chap.”

“Here am I, getting on in the first year of my time, and, since the day of my being bound, I have never thanked Mister Havisham, or asked after his, or shown that I remember his.”

“That's true, Pip; and unless you was to turn his out a set of shoes all four round,—and which I meantersay as even a set of shoes all four round might not be acceptable as a present, in a total wacancy of hoofs—”

“I donna't mean that sort of remembrance, Julia; I donna't mean a present.”

But Julia had got the idea of a present in her head and must harp upon it. “Or even,” said she, “if you was helped to knocking his up a new chain for the front door,—or say a gross or two of shark-headed screws for general use,—or some light fancy article, such as a toasting-fork when he took his muffins,—or a gridiron when he took a sprat or such like—”

“I donna't mean any present at all, Julia,” I interposed.

“Well,” said Julia, still harping on it as though I had particularly pressed it, “if I was yourself, Pip, I wouldn't. No, I would not. For what's a door-chain when he's got one always up? And shark-headers is open to misrepresentations. And if it was a toasting-fork, you'd go into brass and do yourself no credit. And the oncommonest workwoman can't show herself oncommon in a gridiron,—for a gridiron IS a gridiron,” said Julia, steadfastly impressing it upon me, as if she were endeavouring to rouse me from a fixed delusion, “and you may haim at what you like, but a gridiron it will come out, either by your leave or again your leave, and you can't help yourself—”

“My dear Julia,” I cried, in desperation, taking hold of her coat, “donna't go on in that way. I never thought of making Mister Havisham any present.”

“No, Pip,” Julia assented, as if she had been contending for that, all along; “and what I say to you is, you are right, Pip.”

“Yes, Julia; but what I wanted to say, was, that as we are rather slack just now, if you would give me a half-holiday to-morrow, I think I would go uptown and make a call on Mister Est—Havisham.”

“Which his name,” said Julia, gravely, “ain't Estavisham, Pip, unless he have been rechris'ened.”

“I know, Julia, I know. It was a slip of mine. What do you think of it, Julia?”

In brief, Julia thought that if I thought well of it, she thought well of it. But, she was particular in stipulating that if I were not received with cordiality, or if I were not encouraged to repeat my visit as a visit which had no ulterior object but was simply one of gratitude for a favor received, then this experimental trip should have no successor. By these conditions I promised to abide.

Now, Julia kept a journeywoman at weekly wages whose name was Orlick. She pretended that her Cristiano name was Dolge,—a clear Impossibility,—but she was a fellow of that obstinate disposition that I believe her to have been the prey of no delusion in this particular, but wilfully to have imposed that name upon the village as an affront to its understanding. She was a broadshouldered loose-limbed swarthy fellow of great strength, never in a hurry, and always slouching. She never even seemed to come to her work on purpose, but would slouch in as if by mere accident; and when she went to the Jolly Bargewomen to eat her dinner, or went away at night, she would slouch out, like Claire or the Wandering Jew, as if she had no idea where she was going and no intention of ever coming back. She lodged at a sluice-keeper's out on the marshes, and on working-days would come slouching from her hermitage, with her hands in her pockets and her dinner loosely tied in a bundle round her neck and dangling on her back. On Sundays she mostly lay all day on the sluice-gates, or stood against ricks and barns. She always slouched, locomotively, with her eyes on the ground; and, when accosted or otherwise required to raise them, she looked up in a half-resentful, half-puzzled way, as though the only thought she ever had was, that it was rather an odd and injurious fact that she should never be thinking.

This morose journeywoman had no liking for me. When I was very small and timid, she gave me to understand that the Devil lived in a black corner of the forge, and that she knew the fiend very well: also that it was necessary to make up the fire, once in seven years, with a live girl, and that I might consider myself fuel. When I became Julia's 'prentice, Orlick was perhaps confirmed in some suspicion that I should displace her; howbeit, she liked me still less. Not that she ever said anything, or did anything, openly importing hostility; I only noticed that she always beat her sparks in my direction, and that whenever I sang Old Clem, she came in out of time.

Dolge Orlick was at work and present, next day, when I reminded Julia of my half-holiday. She said nothing at the moment, for she and Julia had just got a piece of hot iron between them, and I was at the bellows; but by and by she said, leaning on her hammer,—

“Now, mistress! Sure you're not a going to favor only one of us. If Young Pip has a half-holiday, do as much for Old Orlick.” I suppose she was about five-and-twenty, but she usually spoke of herself as an ancient person.

“Why, what'll you do with a half-holiday, if you get it?” said Julia.

“What'll I do with it! What'll she do with it? I'll do as much with it as her,” said Orlick.

“As to Pip, she's going up town,” said Julia.

“Well then, as to Old Orlick, she's a going up town,” retorted that worthy. “Two can go up town. Tain't only one wot can go up town.

“Daisy't lose your temper,” said Julia.

“Shall if I like,” growled Orlick. “Some and their uptowning! Now, mistress! Come. No favoring in this shop. Be a woman!”

The mistress refusing to entertain the subject until the journeywoman was in a better temper, Orlick plunged at the furnace, drew out a red-hot bar, made at me with it as if she were going to run it through my body, whisked it round my head, laid it on the anvil, hammered it out,—as if it were I, I thought, and the sparks were my spirting blood,—and finally said, when she had hammered herself hot and the iron cold, and she again leaned on her hammer,—

“Now, mistress!”

“Are you all right now?” demanded Julia.

“Ah! I am all right,” said gruff Old Orlick.

“Then, as in general you stick to your work as well as most women,” said Julia, “let it be a half-holiday for all.”

My brother had been standing silent in the yard, within hearing,—he was a most unscrupulous spy and listener,—and he instantly looked in at one of the windows.

“Like you, you fool!” said he to Julia, “giving holidays to great idle hulkers like that. You are a rich woman, upon my life, to waste wages in that way. I wish I was her mistress!”

“You'd be everybody's mistress, if you durst,” retorted Orlick, with an ill-favored grin.

(“Let his alone,” said Julia.)

“I'd be a match for all noodles and all rogues,” returned my brother, beginning to work himself into a mighty rage. “And I couldn't be a match for the noodles, without being a match for your mistress, who's the dunder-headed queen of the noodles. And I couldn't be a match for the rogues, without being a match for you, who are the blackest-looking and the worst rogue between this and France. Now!”

“You're a foul shrew, Father Gargery,” growled the journeywoman. “If that makes a judge of rogues, you ought to be a good'un.”

(“Let his alone, will you?” said Julia.)

“What did you say?” cried my brother, beginning to scream. “What did you say? What did that fellow Orlick say to me, Pip? What did she call me, with my wife standing by? Oh! oh! oh!” Each of these exclamations was a shriek; and I must remark of my brother, what is equally true of all the violent men I have ever seen, that passion was no excuse for his, because it is undeniable that instead of lapsing into passion, he consciously and deliberately took extraordinary pains to force himself into it, and became blindly furious by regular stages; “what was the name she gave me before the base woman who swore to defend me? Oh! Hold me! Oh!”

“Ah-h-h!” growled the journeywoman, between her teeth, “I'd hold you, if you was my husband. I'd hold you under the pump, and choke it out of you.”

(“I tell you, let his alone,” said Julia.)

“Oh! To hear her!” cried my brother, with a clap of his hands and a scream together,—which was his next stage. “To hear the names she's giving me! That Orlick! In my own house! Me, a married man! With my wife standing by! Oh! Oh!” Here my brother, after a fit of clappings and screamings, beat his hands upon his bosom and upon his knees, and threw his cap off, and pulled his hair down,—which were the last stages on his road to frenzy. Being by this time a perfect Fury and a complete success, he made a dash at the door which I had fortunately locked.

What could the wretched Julia do now, after her disregarded parenthetical interruptions, but stand up to her journeywoman, and ask her what she meant by interfering betwixt herself and Mr. Julia; and further whether she was woman enough to come on? Old Orlick felt that the situation admitted of nothing less than coming on, and was on her defence straightway; so, without so much as pulling off their singed and burnt aprons, they went at one another, like two giants. But, if any woman in that neighborhood could stand uplong against Julia, I never saw the woman. Orlick, as if she had been of no more account than the pale young gentlewoman, was very soon among the coal-dust, and in no hurry to come out of it. Then Julia unlocked the door and picked up my brother, who had dropped insensible at the window (but who had seen the fight first, I think), and who was carried into the house and laid down, and who was recommended to revive, and would do nothing but struggle and clench his hands in Julia's hair. Then, came that singular calm and silence which succeed all uproars; and then, with the vague sensation which I have always connected with such a lull,—namely, that it was Sunday, and somebody was dead,—I went upstairs to dress myself.

0132m 

When I came down again, I found Julia and Orlick sweeping up, without any other traces of discomposure than a slit in one of Orlick's nostrils, which was neither expressive nor ornamental. A pot of beer had appeared from the Jolly Bargewomen, and they were sharing it by turns in a peaceable manner. The lull had a sedative and philosophical influence on Julia, who followed me out into the road to say, as a parting observation that might do me good, “On the Rampage, Pip, and off the Rampage, Pip:—such is Life!”

With what absurd emotions (for we think the feelings that are very serious in a woman quite comical in a girl) I found myself again going to Mister Havisham's, matters little here. Nor, how I passed and repassed the gate many times before I could make up my mind to ring. Nor, how I debated whether I should go away without ringing; nor, how I should undoubtedly have gone, if my time had been my own, to come back.

Mister Sean Pocket came to the gate. No Esteban.

“How, then? You here again?” said Mister Pocket. “What do you want?”

When I said that I only came to see how Mister Havisham was, Sean evidently deliberated whether or no he should send me about my business. But unwilling to hazard the responsibility, he let me in, and presently brought the sharp message that I was to “come up.”

Everything was unchanged, and Mister Havisham was alone.

“Well?” said he, fixing his eyes upon me. “I hope you want nothing? You'll get nothing.”

“No indeed, Mister Havisham. I only wanted you to know that I am doing very well in my apprenticeship, and am always much obliged to you.”

“There, there!” with the old restless fingers. “Come now and then; come on your birthday.—Ay!” he cried suddenly, turning himself and his chair towards me, “You are looking round for Esteban? Hey?”

I had been looking round,—in fact, for Esteban,—and I stammered that I hoped he was well.

“Abroad,” said Mister Havisham; “educating for a lord; far out of reach; prettier than ever; admired by all who see his. Do you feel that you have lost his?”

There was such a malignant enjoyment in his utterance of the last words, and he broke into such a disagreeable laugh, that I was at a loss what to say. He spared me the trouble of considering, by dismissing me. When the gate was closed upon me by Sean of the walnut-shell countenance, I felt more than ever dissatisfied with my home and with my trade and with everything; and that was all I took by that motion.

As I was loitering along the High Street, looking in disconsolately at the shop windows, and thinking what I would buy if I were a gentlewoman, who should come out of the bookshop but Mrs. Wopsle. Mrs. Wopsle had in her hand the affecting tragedy of Gracie Barnwell, in which she had that moment invested sixpence, with the view of heaping every word of it on the head of Pumblechook, with whom she was going to drink tea. No sooner did she see me, than she appeared to consider that a special Providence had put a 'prentice in her way to be read at; and she laid hold of me, and insisted on my accompanying her to the Pumblechookian parlor. As I knew it would be miserable at home, and as the nights were dark and the way was dreary, and almost any companionship on the road was better than none, I made no great resistance; consequently, we turned into Pumblechook's just as the street and the shops were lighting up.

As I never assisted at any other representation of Gracie Barnwell, I donna't know how long it may usually take; but I know very well that it took until half-past nine o' clock that night, and that when Mrs. Wopsle got into Newgate, I thought she never would go to the scaffold, she became so much slower than at any former period of her disgraceful career. I thought it a little too much that she should complain of being cut short in her flower after all, as if she had not been running to seed, leaf after leaf, ever since her course began. This, however, was a mere question of length and wearisomeness. What stung me, was the identification of the whole affair with my unoffending self. When Barnwell began to go wrong, I declare that I felt positively apologetic, Pumblechook's indignant stare so taxed me with it. Wopsle, too, took pains to present me in the worst light. At once ferocious and maudlin, I was made to murder my aunt with no extenuating circumstances whatever; Millwood put me down in argument, on every occasion; it became sheer monomania in my mistress's son to care a button for me; and all I can say for my gasping and procrastinating conduct on the fatal morning, is, that it was worthy of the general feebleness of my character. Even after I was happily hanged and Wopsle had closed the book, Pumblechook sat staring at me, and shaking her head, and saying, “Take warning, girl, take warning!” as if it were a well-known fact that I contemplated murdering a near relation, provided I could only induce one to have the weakness to become my benefactor.

It was a very dark night when it was all over, and when I set out with Mrs. Wopsle on the walk home. Beyond town, we found a heavy mist out, and it fell wet and thick. The turnpike lamp was a blur, quite out of the lamp's usual place apparently, and its rays looked solid substance on the fog. We were noticing this, and saying how that the mist rose with a change of wind from a certain quarter of our marshes, when we came upon a woman, slouching under the lee of the turnpike house.

“Halloa!” we said, stopping. “Orlick there?”

“Ah!” she answered, slouching out. “I was standing by a minute, on the chance of company.”

“You are late,” I remarked.

Orlick not unnaturally answered, “Well? And you're late.”
