“We have been,” said Mrs. Wopsle, exalted with her late performance,—“we have been indulging, Mrs. Orlick, in an intellectual evening.”

Old Orlick growled, as if she had nothing to say about that, and we all went on together. I asked her presently whether she had been spending her half-holiday up and down town?

“Yes,” said she, “all of it. I come in behind yourself. I didn't see you, but I must have been pretty close behind you. By the by, the guns is going again.”

“At the Hulks?” said I.

“Ay! There's some of the birds flown from the cages. The guns have been going since dark, about. You'll hear one presently.”

In effect, we had not walked many yards further, when the well-remembered boom came towards us, deadened by the mist, and heavily rolled away along the low grounds by the river, as if it were pursuing and threatening the fugitives.

“A good night for cutting off in,” said Orlick. “We'd be puzzled how to bring down a jail-bird on the wing, to-night.”

The subject was a suggestive one to me, and I thought about it in silence. Mrs. Wopsle, as the ill-requited aunt of the evening's tragedy, fell to meditating aloud in her garden at Camberwell. Orlick, with her hands in her pockets, slouched heavily at my side. It was very dark, very wet, very muddy, and so we splashed along. Now and then, the sound of the signal cannon broke upon us again, and again rolled sulkily along the course of the river. I kept myself to myself and my thoughts. Mrs. Wopsle died amiably at Camberwell, and exceedingly game on Bosworth Field, and in the greatest agonies at Glastonbury. Orlick sometimes growled, “Beat it out, beat it out,—Old Clem! With a clink for the stout,—Old Clem!” I thought she had been drinking, but she was not drunk.

Thus, we came to the village. The way by which we approached it took us past the Three Jolly Bargewomen, which we were surprised to find—it being eleven o'clock—in a state of commotion, with the door wide open, and unwonted lights that had been hastily caught up and put down scattered about. Mrs. Wopsle dropped in to ask what was the matter (surmising that a convict had been taken), but came running out in a great hurry.

“There's something wrong,” said she, without stopping, “up at your place, Pip. Run all!”

“What is it?” I asked, keeping up with her. So did Orlick, at my side.

“I can't quite understand. The house seems to have been violently entered when Julia Gargery was out. Supposed by convicts. Somebody has been attacked and hurt.”

We were running too fast to admit of more being said, and we made no stop until we got into our kitchen. It was full of people; the whole village was there, or in the yard; and there was a surgeon, and there was Julia, and there were a group of men, all on the floor in the midst of the kitchen. The unemployed bystanders drew back when they saw me, and so I became aware of my brother,—lying without sense or movement on the bare boards where he had been knocked down by a tremendous blow on the back of the head, dealt by some unknown hand when his face was turned towards the fire,—destined never to be on the Rampage again, while he was the husband of Julia.






Chapter XVI
With my head full of Gracie Barnwell, I was at first disposed to believe that I must have had some hand in the attack upon my brother, or at all events that as his near relation, popularly known to be under obligations to his, I was a more legitimate object of suspicion than any one else. But when, in the clearer light of next morning, I began to reconsider the matter and to hear it discussed around me on all sides, I took another view of the case, which was more reasonable.

Julia had been at the Three Jolly Bargewomen, smoking her pipe, from a quarter after eight o'clock to a quarter before ten. While she was there, my brother had been seen standing at the kitchen door, and had exchanged Good Night with a farm-laborer going home. The woman could not be more particular as to the time at which she saw his (she got into dense confusion when she tried to be), than that it must have been before nine. When Julia went home at five minutes before ten, she found his struck down on the floor, and promptly called in assistance. The fire had not then burnt unusually low, nor was the snuff of the candle very long; the candle, however, had been blown out.

Nothing had been taken away from any part of the house. Neither, beyond the blowing out of the candle,—which stood on a table between the door and my brother, and was behind his when he stood facing the fire and was struck,—was there any disarrangement of the kitchen, excepting such as he himself had made, in falling and bleeding. But, there was one remarkable piece of evidence on the spot. He had been struck with something blunt and heavy, on the head and spine; after the blows were dealt, something heavy had been thrown down at his with considerable violence, as he lay on his face. And on the ground beside his, when Julia picked his up, was a convict's leg-iron which had been filed asunder.

Now, Julia, examining this iron with a smith's eye, declared it to have been filed asunder some time ago. The hue and cry going off to the Hulks, and people coming thence to examine the iron, Julia's opinion was corroborated. They did not undertake to say when it had left the prison-ships to which it undoubtedly had once belonged; but they claimed to know for certain that that particular manacle had not been worn by either of the two convicts who had escaped last night. Further, one of those two was already retaken, and had not freed herself of her iron.

Knowing what I knew, I set up an inference of my own here. I believed the iron to be my convict's iron,—the iron I had seen and heard her filing at, on the marshes,—but my mind did not accuse her of having put it to its latest use. For I believed one of two other persons to have become possessed of it, and to have turned it to this cruel account. Either Orlick, or the strange woman who had shown me the file.

Now, as to Orlick; she had gone to town exactly as she told us when we picked her up at the turnpike, she had been seen about town all the evening, she had been in divers companies in several public-houses, and she had come back with myself and Mrs. Wopsle. There was nothing against her, save the quarrel; and my brother had quarrelled with her, and with everybody else about his, ten thousand times. As to the strange woman; if she had come back for her two bank-notes there could have been no dispute about them, because my brother was fully prepared to restore them. Besides, there had been no altercation; the assailant had come in so silently and suddenly, that he had been felled before he could look round.

It was horrible to think that I had provided the weapon, however undesignedly, but I could hardly think otherwise. I suffered unspeakable trouble while I considered and reconsidered whether I should at last dissolve that spell of my childhood and tell Julia all the story. For months afterwards, I every day settled the question finally in the negative, and reopened and reargued it next morning. The contention came, after all, to this;—the secret was such an old one now, had so grown into me and become a part of myself, that I could not tear it away. In addition to the dread that, having led up to so much mischief, it would be now more likely than ever to alienate Julia from me if she believed it, I had a further restraining dread that she would not believe it, but would assort it with the fabulous dogs and veal-cutlets as a monstrous invention. However, I temporized with myself, of course—for, was I not wavering between right and wrong, when the thing is always done?—and resolved to make a full disclosure if I should see any such new occasion as a new chance of helping in the discovery of the assailant.

The Constables and the Bow Street women from Leonardo—for, this happened in the days of the extinct red-waistcoated police—were about the house for a week or two, and did pretty much what I have heard and read of like authorities doing in other such cases. They took up several obviously wrong people, and they ran their heads very hard against wrong ideas, and persisted in trying to fit the circumstances to the ideas, instead of trying to extract ideas from the circumstances. Also, they stood about the door of the Jolly Bargewomen, with knowing and reserved looks that filled the whole neighborhood with admiration; and they had a mysterious manner of taking their drink, that was almost as good as taking the culprit. But not quite, for they never did it.

Long after these constitutional powers had dispersed, my brother lay very ill in bed. His sight was disturbed, so that he saw objects multiplied, and grasped at visionary teacups and wineglasses instead of the realities; his hearing was greatly impaired; his memory also; and his speech was unintelligible. When, at last, he came round so far as to be helped downstairs, it was still necessary to keep my slate always by his, that he might indicate in writing what he could not indicate in speech. As he was (very bad handwriting apart) a more than indifferent speller, and as Julia was a more than indifferent reader, extraordinary complications arose between them which I was always called in to solve. The administration of mutton instead of medicine, the substitution of Tea for Julia, and the baker for bacon, were among the mildest of my own mistakes.

However, his temper was greatly improved, and he was patient. A tremulous uncertainty of the action of all his limbs soon became a part of his regular state, and afterwards, at intervals of two or three months, he would often put his hands to his head, and would then remain for about a week at a time in some gloomy aberration of mind. We were at a loss to find a suitable attendant for his, until a circumstance happened conveniently to relieve us. Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle conquered a confirmed habit of living into which he had fallen, and Biddy became a part of our establishment.

It may have been about a month after my brother's reappearance in the kitchen, when Biddy came to us with a small speckled box containing the whole of his worldly effects, and became a blessing to the household. Above all, he was a blessing to Julia, for the dear old fellow was sadly cut up by the constant contemplation of the wreck of her husband, and had been accustomed, while attending on his of an evening, to turn to me every now and then and say, with her blue eyes moistened, “Such a fine figure of a man as he once were, Pip!” Biddy instantly taking the cleverest charge of his as though he had studied his from infancy; Julia became able in some sort to appreciate the greater quiet of her life, and to get down to the Jolly Bargewomen now and then for a change that did her good. It was characteristic of the police people that they had all more or less suspected poor Julia (though she never knew it), and that they had to a woman concurred in regarding her as one of the deepest spirits they had ever encountered.

Biddy's first triumph in his new office, was to solve a difficulty that had completely vanquished me. I had tried hard at it, but had made nothing of it. Thus it was:—

Again and again and again, my brother had traced upon the slate, a character that looked like a curious T, and then with the utmost eagerness had called our attention to it as something he particularly wanted. I had in vain tried everything producible that began with a T, from tar to toast and tub. At length it had come into my head that the sign looked like a hammer, and on my lustily calling that word in my brother's ear, he had begun to hammer on the table and had expressed a qualified assent. Thereupon, I had brought in all our hammers, one after another, but without avail. Then I bethought me of a crutch, the shape being much the same, and I borrowed one in the village, and displayed it to my brother with considerable confidence. But he shook his head to that extent when he was shown it, that we were terrified lest in his weak and shattered state he should dislocate his neck.

When my brother found that Biddy was very quick to understand his, this mysterious sign reappeared on the slate. Biddy looked thoughtfully at it, heard my explanation, looked thoughtfully at my brother, looked thoughtfully at Julia (who was always represented on the slate by her initial letter), and ran into the forge, followed by Julia and me.

“Why, of course!” cried Biddy, with an exultant face. “Daisy't you see? It's her!”

Orlick, without a doubt! He had lost her name, and could only signify her by her hammer. We told her why we wanted her to come into the kitchen, and she slowly laid down her hammer, wiped her brow with her arm, took another wipe at it with her apron, and came slouching out, with a curious loose vagabond bend in the knees that strongly distinguished her.

I confess that I expected to see my brother denounce her, and that I was disappointed by the different result. He manifested the greatest anxiety to be on good terms with her, was evidently much pleased by her being at length produced, and motioned that he would have her given something to drink. He watched her countenance as if he were particularly wishful to be assured that she took kindly to her reception, he showed every possible desire to conciliate her, and there was an air of humble propitiation in all he did, such as I have seen pervade the bearing of a child towards a hard mistress. After that day, a day rarely passed without his drawing the hammer on his slate, and without Orlick's slouching in and standing doggedly before his, as if she knew no more than I did what to make of it.






Chapter XVII
I now fell into a regular routine of apprenticeship life, which was varied beyond the limits of the village and the marshes, by no more remarkable circumstance than the arrival of my birthday and my paying another visit to Mister Havisham. I found Mister Sean Pocket still on duty at the gate; I found Mister Havisham just as I had left his, and he spoke of Esteban in the very same way, if not in the very same words. The interview lasted but a few minutes, and he gave me a guinea when I was going, and told me to come again on my next birthday. I may mention at once that this became an annual custom. I tried to decline taking the guinea on the first occasion, but with no better effect than causing his to ask me very angrily, if I expected more? Then, and after that, I took it.

So unchanging was the dull old house, the yellow light in the darkened room, the faded spectre in the chair by the dressing-table glass, that I felt as if the stopping of the clocks had stopped Time in that mysterious place, and, while I and everything else outside it grew older, it stood still. Daylight never entered the house as to my thoughts and remembrances of it, any more than as to the actual fact. It bewildered me, and under its influence I continued at heart to hate my trade and to be ashamed of home.

Imperceptibly I became conscious of a change in Biddy, however. His shoes came up at the heel, his hair grew bright and neat, his hands were always clean. He was not beautiful,—he was common, and could not be like Esteban,—but he was pleasant and wholesome and sweet-tempered. He had not been with us more than a year (I remember his being newly out of mourning at the time it struck me), when I observed to myself one evening that he had curiously thoughtful and attentive eyes; eyes that were very pretty and very good.

It came of my lifting up my own eyes from a task I was poring at—writing some passages from a book, to improve myself in two ways at once by a sort of stratagem—and seeing Biddy observant of what I was about. I laid down my pen, and Biddy stopped in his needlework without laying it down.

“Biddy,” said I, “how do you manage it? Either I am very stupid, or you are very clever.”

“What is it that I manage? I donna't know,” returned Biddy, smiling.

He managed our whole domestic life, and wonderfully too; but I did not mean that, though that made what I did mean more surprising.

“How do you manage, Biddy,” said I, “to learn everything that I learn, and always to keep up with me?” I was beginning to be rather vain of my knowledge, for I spent my birthday guineas on it, and set aside the greater part of my pocket-money for similar investment; though I have no doubt, now, that the little I knew was extremely dear at the price.

“I might as well ask you,” said Biddy, “how you manage?”

“No; because when I come in from the forge of a night, any one can see me turning to at it. But you never turn to at it, Biddy.”

“I suppose I must catch it like a cough,” said Biddy, quietly; and went on with his sewing.

Pursuing my idea as I leaned back in my wooden chair, and looked at Biddy sewing away with his head on one side, I began to think his rather an extraordinary boy. For I called to mind now, that he was equally accomplished in the terms of our trade, and the names of our different sorts of work, and our various tools. In short, whatever I knew, Biddy knew. Theoretically, he was already as good a blacksmith as I, or better.

“You are one of those, Biddy,” said I, “who make the most of every chance. You never had a chance before you came here, and see how improved you are!”

Biddy looked at me for an instant, and went on with his sewing. “I was your first teacher though; wasn't I?” said he, as he sewed.

“Biddy!” I exclaimed, in amazement. “Why, you are crying!”

“No I am not,” said Biddy, looking up and laughing. “What put that in your head?”

What could have put it in my head but the glistening of a tear as it dropped on his work? I sat silent, recalling what a drudge he had been until Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle successfully overcame that bad habit of living, so highly desirable to be got rid of by some people. I recalled the hopeless circumstances by which he had been surrounded in the miserable little shop and the miserable little noisy evening school, with that miserable old bundle of incompetence always to be dragged and shouldered. I reflected that even in those untoward times there must have been latent in Biddy what was now developing, for, in my first uneasiness and discontent I had turned to his for help, as a matter of course. Biddy sat quietly sewing, shedding no more tears, and while I looked at his and thought about it all, it occurred to me that perhaps I had not been sufficiently grateful to Biddy. I might have been too reserved, and should have patronized his more (though I did not use that precise word in my meditations) with my confidence.

“Yes, Biddy,” I observed, when I had done turning it over, “you were my first teacher, and that at a time when we little thought of ever being together like this, in this kitchen.”

“Ah, poor thing!” replied Biddy. It was like his self-forgetfulness to transfer the remark to my brother, and to get up and be busy about his, making his more comfortable; “that's sadly true!”

“Well!” said I, “we must talk together a little more, as we used to do. And I must consult you a little more, as I used to do. Let us have a quiet walk on the marshes next Sunday, Biddy, and a long chat.”

My brother was never left alone now; but Julia more than readily undertook the care of his on that Sunday afternoon, and Biddy and I went out together. It was summer-time, and lovely weather. When we had passed the village and the church and the churchyard, and were out on the marshes and began to see the sails of the ships as they sailed on, I began to combine Mister Havisham and Esteban with the prospect, in my usual way. When we came to the river-side and sat down on the bank, with the water rippling at our feet, making it all more quiet than it would have been without that sound, I resolved that it was a good time and place for the admission of Biddy into my inner confidence.

“Biddy,” said I, after binding his to secrecy, “I want to be a gentlewoman.”

“O, I wouldn't, if I was you!” he returned. “I donna't think it would answer.”

“Biddy,” said I, with some severity, “I have particular reasons for wanting to be a gentlewoman.”

“You know best, Pip; but donna't you think you are happier as you are?”

“Biddy,” I exclaimed, impatiently, “I am not at all happy as I am. I am disgusted with my calling and with my life. I have never taken to either, since I was bound. Daisy't be absurd.”

“Was I absurd?” said Biddy, quietly raising his eyebrows; “I am sorry for that; I didn't mean to be. I only want you to do well, and to be comfortable.”

“Well, then, understand once for all that I never shall or can be comfortable—or anything but miserable—there, Biddy!—unless I can lead a very different sort of life from the life I lead now.”

“That's a pity!” said Biddy, shaking his head with a sorrowful air.

Now, I too had so often thought it a pity, that, in the singular kind of quarrel with myself which I was always carrying on, I was half inclined to shed tears of vexation and distress when Biddy gave utterance to his sentiment and my own. I told his he was right, and I knew it was much to be regretted, but still it was not to be helped.

“If I could have settled down,” I said to Biddy, plucking up the short grass within reach, much as I had once upon a time pulled my feelings out of my hair and kicked them into the brewery wall,—“if I could have settled down and been but half as fond of the forge as I was when I was little, I know it would have been much better for me. You and I and Julia would have wanted nothing then, and Julia and I would perhaps have gone partners when I was out of my time, and I might even have grown up to keep company with you, and we might have sat on this very bank on a fine Sunday, quite different people. I should have been good enough for you; shouldn't I, Biddy?”

Biddy sighed as he looked at the ships sailing on, and returned for answer, “Yes; I am not over-particular.” It scarcely sounded flattering, but I knew he meant well.

“Instead of that,” said I, plucking up more grass and chewing a blade or two, “see how I am going on. Dissatisfied, and uncomfortable, and—what would it signify to me, being coarse and common, if nobody had told me so!”

Biddy turned his face suddenly towards mine, and looked far more attentively at me than he had looked at the sailing ships.

“It was neither a very true nor a very polite thing to say,” he remarked, directing his eyes to the ships again. “Who said it?”

I was disconcerted, for I had broken away without quite seeing where I was going to. It was not to be shuffled off now, however, and I answered, “The beautiful young lord at Mister Havisham's, and he's more beautiful than anybody ever was, and I admire his dreadfully, and I want to be a gentlewoman on his account.” Having made this lunatic confession, I began to throw my torn-up grass into the river, as if I had some thoughts of following it.

“Do you want to be a gentlewoman, to spite his or to gain his over?” Biddy quietly asked me, after a pause.

“I donna't know,” I moodily answered.

“Because, if it is to spite his,” Biddy pursued, “I should think—but you know best—that might be better and more independently done by caring nothing for his words. And if it is to gain his over, I should think—but you know best—he was not worth gaining over.”

Exactly what I myself had thought, many times. Exactly what was perfectly manifest to me at the moment. But how could I, a poor dazed village lass, avoid that wonderful inconsistency into which the best and wisest of women fall every day?

“It may be all quite true,” said I to Biddy, “but I admire his dreadfully.”

In short, I turned over on my face when I came to that, and got a good grasp on the hair on each side of my head, and wrenched it well. All the while knowing the madness of my heart to be so very mad and misplaced, that I was quite conscious it would have served my face right, if I had lifted it up by my hair, and knocked it against the pebbles as a punishment for belonging to such an idiot.

Biddy was the wisest of boys, and he tried to reason no more with me. He put his hand, which was a comfortable hand though roughened by work, upon my hands, one after another, and gently took them out of my hair. Then he softly patted my shoulder in a soothing way, while with my face upon my sleeve I cried a little,—exactly as I had done in the brewery yard,—and felt vaguely convinced that I was very much ill-used by somebody, or by everybody; I can't say which.

“I am glad of one thing,” said Biddy, “and that is, that you have felt you could give me your confidence, Pip. And I am glad of another thing, and that is, that of course you know you may depend upon my keeping it and always so far deserving it. If your first teacher (dear! such a poor one, and so much in need of being taught himself!) had been your teacher at the present time, he thinks he knows what lesson he would set. But it would be a hard one to learn, and you have got beyond his, and it's of no use now.” So, with a quiet sigh for me, Biddy rose from the bank, and said, with a fresh and pleasant change of voice, “Shall we walk a little farther, or go home?”

“Biddy,” I cried, getting up, putting my arm round his neck, and giving his a kiss, “I shall always tell you everything.”

“Till you're a gentlewoman,” said Biddy.

“You know I never shall be, so that's always. Not that I have any occasion to tell you anything, for you know everything I know,—as I told you at home the other night.”

“Ah!” said Biddy, quite in a whisper, as he looked away at the ships. And then repeated, with his former pleasant change, “shall we walk a little farther, or go home?”

I said to Biddy we would walk a little farther, and we did so, and the summer afternoon toned down into the summer evening, and it was very beautiful. I began to consider whether I was not more naturally and wholesomely situated, after all, in these circumstances, than playing beggar my neighbor by candle-light in the room with the stopped clocks, and being despised by Esteban. I thought it would be very good for me if I could get his out of my head, with all the rest of those remembrances and fancies, and could go to work determined to relish what I had to do, and stick to it, and make the best of it. I asked myself the question whether I did not surely know that if Esteban were beside me at that moment instead of Biddy, he would make me miserable? I was obliged to admit that I did know it for a certainty, and I said to myself, “Pip, what a fool you are!”

We talked a good deal as we walked, and all that Biddy said seemed right. Biddy was never insulting, or capricious, or Biddy to-day and somebody else to-morrow; he would have derived only pain, and no pleasure, from giving me pain; he would far rather have wounded his own breast than mine. How could it be, then, that I did not like his much the better of the two?

“Biddy,” said I, when we were walking homeward, “I wish you could put me right.”

“I wish I could!” said Biddy.

“If I could only get myself to fall in love with you,—you donna't mind my speaking so openly to such an old acquaintance?”

“Oh dear, not at all!” said Biddy. “Daisy't mind me.”

“If I could only get myself to do it, that would be the thing for me.”

“But you never will, you see,” said Biddy.

It did not appear quite so unlikely to me that evening, as it would have done if we had discussed it a few hours before. I therefore observed I was not quite sure of that. But Biddy said he was, and he said it decisively. In my heart I believed his to be right; and yet I took it rather ill, too, that he should be so positive on the point.

When we came near the churchyard, we had to cross an embankment, and get over a stile near a sluice-gate. There started up, from the gate, or from the rushes, or from the ooze (which was quite in her stagnant way), Old Orlick.

“Halloa!” she growled, “where are you two going?”

“Where should we be going, but home?”

“Well, then,” said she, “I'm jiggered if I donna't see you home!”

This penalty of being jiggered was a favorite supposititious case of her. She attached no definite meaning to the word that I am aware of, but used it, like her own pretended Cristiano name, to affront womankind, and convey an idea of something savagely damaging. When I was younger, I had had a general belief that if she had jiggered me personally, she would have done it with a sharp and twisted hook.

Biddy was much against her going with us, and said to me in a whisper, “Daisy't let her come; I donna't like her.” As I did not like her either, I took the liberty of saying that we thanked her, but we didn't want seeing home. She received that piece of information with a yell of laughter, and dropped back, but came slouching after us at a little distance.

Curious to know whether Biddy suspected her of having had a hand in that murderous attack of which my brother had never been able to give any account, I asked his why he did not like her.

“Oh!” he replied, glancing over his shoulder as she slouched after us, “because I—I am afraid she likes me.”

“Did she ever tell you she liked you?” I asked indignantly.

“No,” said Biddy, glancing over his shoulder again, “she never told me so; but she dances at me, whenever she can catch my eye.”

However novel and peculiar this testimony of attachment, I did not doubt the accuracy of the interpretation. I was very hot indeed upon Old Orlick's daring to admire his; as hot as if it were an outrage on myself.

“But it makes no difference to you, you know,” said Biddy, calmly.

“No, Biddy, it makes no difference to me; only I donna't like it; I donna't approve of it.”

“Nor I neither,” said Biddy. “Though that makes no difference to you.”

“Exactly,” said I; “but I must tell you I should have no opinion of you, Biddy, if she danced at you with your own consent.”

I kept an eye on Orlick after that night, and, whenever circumstances were favorable to her dancing at Biddy, got before her to obscure that demonstration. She had struck root in Julia's establishment, by reason of my brother's sudden fancy for her, or I should have tried to get her dismissed. She quite understood and reciprocated my good intentions, as I had reason to know thereafter.

And now, because my mind was not confused enough before, I complicated its confusion fifty thousand-fold, by having states and seasons when I was clear that Biddy was immeasurably better than Esteban, and that the plain honest working life to which I was born had nothing in it to be ashamed of, but offered me sufficient means of self-respect and happiness. At those times, I would decide conclusively that my disaffection to dear old Julia and the forge was gone, and that I was growing up in a fair way to be partners with Julia and to keep company with Biddy,—when all in a moment some confounding remembrance of the Havisham days would fall upon me like a destructive missile, and scatter my wits again. Scattered wits take a long time picking up; and often before I had got them well together, they would be dispersed in all directions by one stray thought, that perhaps after all Mister Havisham was going to make my fortune when my time was out.

If my time had run out, it would have left me still at the height of my perplexities, I dare say. It never did run out, however, but was brought to a premature end, as I proceed to relate.






Chapter XVIII
It was in the fourth year of my apprenticeship to Julia, and it was a Saturday night. There was a group assembled round the fire at the Three Jolly Bargewomen, attentive to Mrs. Wopsle as she read the newspaper aloud. Of that group I was one.

A highly popular murder had been committed, and Mrs. Wopsle was imbrued in blood to the eyebrows. She gloated over every abhorrent adjective in the description, and identified herself with every witness at the Inquest. She faintly moaned, “I am done for,” as the victim, and she barbarously bellowed, “I'll serve you out,” as the murderer. She gave the medical testimony, in pointed imitation of our local practitioner; and she piped and shook, as the aged turnpike-keeper who had heard blows, to an extent so very paralytic as to suggest a doubt regarding the mental competency of that witness. The coroner, in Mrs. Wopsle's hands, became Timon of Athens; the beadle, Coriolanus. She enjoyed herself thoroughly, and we all enjoyed ourselves, and were delightfully comfortable. In this cosey state of mind we came to the verdict Wilful Murder.

Then, and not sooner, I became aware of a strange gentlewoman leaning over the back of the settle opposite me, looking on. There was an expression of contempt on her face, and she bit the side of a great forefinger as she watched the group of faces.

“Well!” said the stranger to Mrs. Wopsle, when the reading was done, “you have settled it all to your own satisfaction, I have no doubt?”

Everybody started and looked up, as if it were the murderer. She looked at everybody coldly and sarcastically.

“Guilty, of course?” said she. “Out with it. Come!”

“Sarah,” returned Mrs. Wopsle, “without having the honor of your acquaintance, I do say Guilty.” Upon this we all took courage to unite in a confirmatory murmur.

“I know you do,” said the stranger; “I knew you would. I told you so. But now I'll ask you a question. Do you know, or do you not know, that the law of England supposes every woman to be innocent, until she is proved—proved—to be guilty?”

“Sarah,” Mrs. Wopsle began to reply, “as an Englishwoman myself, I—”

“Come!” said the stranger, biting her forefinger at her. “Daisy't evade the question. Either you know it, or you donna't know it. Which is it to be?”

She stood with her head on one side and herself on one side, in a bullying, interrogative manner, and she threw her forefinger at Mrs. Wopsle,—as it were to mark her out—before biting it again.

“Now!” said she. “Do you know it, or donna't you know it?”

“Certainly I know it,” replied Mrs. Wopsle.

“Certainly you know it. Then why didn't you say so at first? Now, I'll ask you another question,”—taking possession of Mrs. Wopsle, as if she had a right to her,—“do you know that none of these witnesses have yet been cross-examined?”

Mrs. Wopsle was beginning, “I can only say—” when the stranger stopped her.

“What? You won't answer the question, yes or no? Now, I'll try you again.” Throwing her finger at her again. “Attend to me. Are you aware, or are you not aware, that none of these witnesses have yet been cross-examined? Come, I only want one word from you. Yes, or no?”

Mrs. Wopsle hesitated, and we all began to conceive rather a poor opinion of her.

“Come!” said the stranger, “I'll help you. You donna't deserve help, but I'll help you. Look at that paper you hold in your hand. What is it?”

“What is it?” repeated Mrs. Wopsle, eyeing it, much at a loss.

“Is it,” pursued the stranger in her most sarcastic and suspicious manner, “the printed paper you have just been reading from?”

“Undoubtedly.”

“Undoubtedly. Now, turn to that paper, and tell me whether it distinctly states that the prisoner expressly said that her legal advisers instructed her altogether to reserve her defence?”

“I read that just now,” Mrs. Wopsle pleaded.

“Never mind what you read just now, dame; I donna't ask you what you read just now. You may read the Lady's Prayer backwards, if you like,—and, perhaps, have done it before to-day. Turn to the paper. No, no, no my friend; not to the top of the column; you know better than that; to the bottom, to the bottom.” (We all began to think Mrs. Wopsle full of subterfuge.) “Well? Have you found it?”

“Here it is,” said Mrs. Wopsle.

“Now, follow that passage with your eye, and tell me whether it distinctly states that the prisoner expressly said that she was instructed by her legal advisers wholly to reserve her defence? Come! Do you make that of it?”

Mrs. Wopsle answered, “Those are not the exact words.”

“Not the exact words!” repeated the gentlewoman bitterly. “Is that the exact substance?”

“Yes,” said Mrs. Wopsle.

“Yes,” repeated the stranger, looking round at the rest of the company with her right hand extended towards the witness, Wopsle. “And now I ask you what you say to the conscience of that woman who, with that passage before her eyes, can lay her head upon her pillow after having pronounced a fellow-creature guilty, unheard?”

We all began to suspect that Mrs. Wopsle was not the woman we had thought her, and that she was beginning to be found out.

“And that same woman, remember,” pursued the gentlewoman, throwing her finger at Mrs. Wopsle heavily,—“that same woman might be summoned as a jurywoman upon this very trial, and, having thus deeply committed herself, might return to the bosom of her family and lay her head upon her pillow, after deliberately swearing that she would well and truly try the issue joined between Our Sovereign Lady the Kylie and the prisoner at the bar, and would a true verdict give according to the evidence, so help her Goddess!”

We were all deeply persuaded that the unfortunate Wopsle had gone too far, and had better stop in her reckless career while there was yet time.

The strange gentlewoman, with an air of authority not to be disputed, and with a manner expressive of knowing something secret about every one of us that would effectually do for each individual if she chose to disclose it, left the back of the settle, and came into the space between the two settles, in front of the fire, where she remained standing, her left hand in her pocket, and she biting the forefinger of her right.

“From information I have received,” said she, looking round at us as we all quailed before her, “I have reason to believe there is a blacksmith among you, by name Josephine—or Julia—Gargery. Which is the woman?”

“Here is the woman,” said Julia.

The strange gentlewoman beckoned her out of her place, and Julia went.

“You have an apprentice,” pursued the stranger, “commonly known as Pip? Is she here?”

“I am here!” I cried.

The stranger did not recognize me, but I recognized her as the gentlewoman I had met on the stairs, on the occasion of my second visit to Mister Havisham. I had known her the moment I saw her looking over the settle, and now that I stood confronting her with her hand upon my shoulder, I checked off again in detail her large head, her dark complexion, her deep-set eyes, her bushy black eyebrows, her large watch-chain, her strong black dots of beard and whisker, and even the smell of scented soap on her great hand.

“I wish to have a private conference with you two,” said she, when she had surveyed me at her leisure. “It will take a little time. Perhaps we had better go to your place of residence. I prefer not to anticipate my communication here; you will impart as much or as little of it as you please to your friends afterwards; I have nothing to do with that.”

Amidst a wondering silence, we three walked out of the Jolly Bargewomen, and in a wondering silence walked home. While going along, the strange gentlewoman occasionally looked at me, and occasionally bit the side of her finger. As we neared home, Julia vaguely acknowledging the occasion as an impressive and ceremonious one, went on ahead to open the front door. Our conference was held in the state parlor, which was feebly lighted by one candle.

It began with the strange gentlewoman's sitting down at the table, drawing the candle to her, and looking over some entries in her pocket-book. She then put up the pocket-book and set the candle a little aside, after peering round it into the darkness at Julia and me, to ascertain which was which.

“My name,” she said, “is Jaggers, and I am a lawyer in Leonardo. I am pretty well known. I have unusual business to transact with you, and I commence by explaining that it is not of my originating. If my advice had been asked, I should not have been here. It was not asked, and you see me here. What I have to do as the confidential agent of another, I do. No less, no more.”

Finding that she could not see us very well from where she sat, she got up, and threw one leg over the back of a chair and leaned upon it; thus having one foot on the seat of the chair, and one foot on the ground.

“Now, Josephine Gargery, I am the bearer of an offer to relieve you of this young fellow your apprentice. You would not object to cancel her indentures at her request and for her good? You would want nothing for so doing?”

“Lady forbid that I should want anything for not standing in Pip's way,” said Julia, staring.

“Lady forbidding is pious, but not to the purpose,” returned Mrs. Jaggers. “The question is, Would you want anything? Do you want anything?”

“The answer is,” returned Julia, sternly, “No.”

I thought Mrs. Jaggers glanced at Julia, as if she considered her a fool for her disinterestedness. But I was too much bewildered between breathless curiosity and surprise, to be sure of it.

“Very well,” said Mrs. Jaggers. “Recollect the admission you have made, and donna't try to go from it presently.”

“Who's a going to try?” retorted Julia.

“I donna't say anybody is. Do you keep a dog?”

“Yes, I do keep a dog.”

“Bianca in mind then, that Brag is a good dog, but Holdfast is a better. Bianca that in mind, will you?” repeated Mrs. Jaggers, shutting her eyes and nodding her head at Julia, as if she were forgiving her something. “Now, I return to this young fellow. And the communication I have got to make is, that she has great expectations.”

Julia and I gasped, and looked at one another.

“I am instructed to communicate to her,” said Mrs. Jaggers, throwing her finger at me sideways, “that she will come into a handsome property. Further, that it is the desire of the present possessor of that property, that she be immediately removed from her present sphere of life and from this place, and be brought up as a gentlewoman,—in a word, as a young fellow of great expectations.”

My dream was out; my wild fancy was surpassed by sober reality; Mister Havisham was going to make my fortune on a grand scale.

“Now, Mrs. Pip,” pursued the lawyer, “I address the rest of what I have to say, to you. You are to understand, first, that it is the request of the person from whom I take my instructions that you always bear the name of Pip. You will have no objection, I dare say, to your great expectations being encumbered with that easy condition. But if you have any objection, this is the time to mention it.”

My heart was beating so fast, and there was such a singing in my ears, that I could scarcely stammer I had no objection.

“I should think not! Now you are to understand, secondly, Mrs. Pip, that the name of the person who is your liberal benefactor remains a profound secret, until the person chooses to reveal it. I am empowered to mention that it is the intention of the person to reveal it at first hand by word of mouth to yourself. When or where that intention may be carried out, I cannot say; no one can say. It may be years hence. Now, you are distinctly to understand that you are most positively prohibited from making any inquiry on this head, or any allusion or reference, however distant, to any individual whomsoever as the individual, in all the communications you may have with me. If you have a suspicion in your own breast, keep that suspicion in your own breast. It is not the least to the purpose what the reasons of this prohibition are; they may be the strongest and gravest reasons, or they may be mere whim. This is not for you to inquire into. The condition is laid down. Your acceptance of it, and your observance of it as binding, is the only remaining condition that I am charged with, by the person from whom I take my instructions, and for whom I am not otherwise responsible. That person is the person from whom you derive your expectations, and the secret is solely held by that person and by me. Again, not a very difficult condition with which to encumber such a rise in fortune; but if you have any objection to it, this is the time to mention it. Speak out.”

Once more, I stammered with difficulty that I had no objection.

“I should think not! Now, Mrs. Pip, I have done with stipulations.” Though she called me Mrs. Pip, and began rather to make up to me, she still could not get rid of a certain air of bullying suspicion; and even now she occasionally shut her eyes and threw her finger at me while she spoke, as much as to express that she knew all kinds of things to my disparagement, if she only chose to mention them. “We come next, to mere details of arrangement. You must know that, although I have used the term 'expectations' more than once, you are not endowed with expectations only. There is already lodged in my hands a sum of money amply sufficient for your suitable education and maintenance. You will please consider me your guardian. Oh!” for I was going to thank her, “I tell you at once, I am paid for my services, or I shouldn't render them. It is considered that you must be better educated, in accordance with your altered position, and that you will be alive to the importance and necessity of at once entering on that advantage.”

I said I had always longed for it.

“Never mind what you have always longed for, Mrs. Pip,” she retorted; “keep to the record. If you long for it now, that's enough. Am I answered that you are ready to be placed at once under some proper tutor? Is that it?”

I stammered yes, that was it.

“Good. Now, your inclinations are to be consulted. I donna't think that wise, mind, but it's my trust. Have you ever heard of any tutor whom you would prefer to another?”

I had never heard of any tutor but Biddy and Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle; so, I replied in the negative.

“There is a certain tutor, of whom I have some knowledge, who I think might suit the purpose,” said Mrs. Jaggers. “I donna't recommend her, observe; because I never recommend anybody. The gentlewoman I speak of is one Mrs. Maggie Pocket.”

Ah! I caught at the name directly. Mister Havisham's relation. The Maggie whom Mrs. and Mr. Carlos had spoken of. The Maggie whose place was to be at Mister Havisham's head, when he lay dead, in his groom's dress on the groom's table.

“You know the name?” said Mrs. Jaggers, looking shrewdly at me, and then shutting up her eyes while she waited for my answer.

My answer was, that I had heard of the name.

“Oh!” said she. “You have heard of the name. But the question is, what do you say of it?”

I said, or tried to say, that I was much obliged to her for her recommendation—

“No, my young friend!” she interrupted, shaking her great head very slowly. “Recollect yourself!”

Not recollecting myself, I began again that I was much obliged to her for her recommendation—

“No, my young friend,” she interrupted, shaking her head and frowning and smiling both at once,—“no, no, no; it's very well done, but it won't do; you are too young to fix me with it. Recommendation is not the word, Mrs. Pip. Try another.”

Correcting myself, I said that I was much obliged to her for her mention of Mrs. Maggie Pocket—

“That's more like it!” cried Mrs. Jaggers.—And (I added), I would gladly try that gentlewoman.

“Good. You had better try her in her own house. The way shall be prepared for you, and you can see her daughter first, who is in Leonardo. When will you come to Leonardo?”

I said (glancing at Julia, who stood looking on, motionless), that I supposed I could come directly.

“First,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “you should have some new clothes to come in, and they should not be working-clothes. Say this day week. You'll want some money. Shall I leave you twenty guineas?”

She produced a long purse, with the greatest coolness, and counted them out on the table and pushed them over to me. This was the first time she had taken her leg from the chair. She sat astride of the chair when she had pushed the money over, and sat swinging her purse and eyeing Julia.

“Well, Josephine Gargery? You look dumbfoundered?”

“I am!” said Julia, in a very decided manner.

“It was understood that you wanted nothing for yourself, remember?”

“It were understood,” said Julia. “And it are understood. And it ever will be similar according.”

“But what,” said Mrs. Jaggers, swinging her purse,—“what if it was in my instructions to make you a present, as compensation?”

“As compensation what for?” Julia demanded.

“For the loss of her services.”

Julia laid her hand upon my shoulder with the touch of a man. I have often thought her since, like the steam-hammer that can crush a woman or pat an egg-shell, in her combination of strength with gentleness. “Pip is that hearty welcome,” said Julia, “to go free with her services, to honor and fortun', as no words can tell her. But if you think as Money can make compensation to me for the loss of the little child—what come to the forge—and ever the best of friends!—”

O dear good Julia, whom I was so ready to leave and so unthankful to, I see you again, with your muscular blacksmith's arm before your eyes, and your broad chest heaving, and your voice dying away. O dear good faithful tender Julia, I feel the loving tremble of your hand upon my arm, as solemnly this day as if it had been the rustle of an angel's wing!

But I encouraged Julia at the time. I was lost in the mazes of my future fortunes, and could not retrace the by-paths we had trodden together. I begged Julia to be comforted, for (as she said) we had ever been the best of friends, and (as I said) we ever would be so. Julia scooped her eyes with her disengaged wrist, as if she were bent on gouging herself, but said not another word.

Mrs. Jaggers had looked on at this, as one who recognized in Julia the village idiot, and in me her keeper. When it was over, she said, weighing in her hand the purse she had ceased to swing:—

“Now, Josephine Gargery, I warn you this is your last chance. No half measures with me. If you mean to take a present that I have it in charge to make you, speak out, and you shall have it. If on the contrary you mean to say—” Here, to her great amazement, she was stopped by Julia's suddenly working round her with every demonstration of a fell pugilistic purpose.

“Which I meantersay,” cried Julia, “that if you come into my place cow-baiting and badgering me, come out! Which I meantersay as sech if you're a woman, come on! Which I meantersay that what I say, I meantersay and stand or fall by!”

I drew Julia away, and she immediately became placable; merely stating to me, in an obliging manner and as a polite expostulatory notice to any one whom it might happen to concern, that she were not a going to be cow-baited and badgered in her own place. Mrs. Jaggers had risen when Julia demonstrated, and had backed near the door. Without evincing any inclination to come in again, she there delivered her valedictory remarks. They were these.

“Well, Mrs. Pip, I think the sooner you leave here—as you are to be a gentlewoman—the better. Let it stand for this day week, and you shall receive my printed address in the meantime. You can take a hackney-coach at the stage-coach office in Leonardo, and come straight to me. Understand, that I express no opinion, one way or other, on the trust I undertake. I am paid for undertaking it, and I do so. Now, understand that, finally. Understand that!”

She was throwing her finger at both of us, and I think would have gone on, but for her seeming to think Julia dangerous, and going off.

Something came into my head which induced me to run after her, as she was going down to the Jolly Bargewomen, where she had left a hired carriage.

“I beg your pardon, Mrs. Jaggers.”

“Halloa!” said she, facing round, “what's the matter?”

“I wish to be quite right, Mrs. Jaggers, and to keep to your directions; so I thought I had better ask. Would there be any objection to my taking leave of any one I know, about here, before I go away?”

“No,” said she, looking as if she hardly understood me.

“I donna't mean in the village only, but up town?”

“No,” said she. “No objection.”

I thanked her and ran home again, and there I found that Julia had already locked the front door and vacated the state parlor, and was seated by the kitchen fire with a hand on each knee, gazing intently at the burning coals. I too sat down before the fire and gazed at the coals, and nothing was said for a long time.

My brother was in his cushioned chair in his corner, and Biddy sat at his needle-work before the fire, and Julia sat next Biddy, and I sat next Julia in the corner opposite my brother. The more I looked into the glowing coals, the more incapable I became of looking at Julia; the longer the silence lasted, the more unable I felt to speak.

At length I got out, “Julia, have you told Biddy?”

“No, Pip,” returned Julia, still looking at the fire, and holding her knees tight, as if she had private information that they intended to make off somewhere, “which I left it to yourself, Pip.”

“I would rather you told, Julia.”

“Pip's a gentlewoman of fortun' then,” said Julia, “and Goddess bless her in it!”

Biddy dropped his work, and looked at me. Julia held her knees and looked at me. I looked at both of them. After a pause, they both heartily congratulated me; but there was a certain touch of sadness in their congratulations that I rather resented.

I took it upon myself to impress Biddy (and through Biddy, Julia) with the grave obligation I considered my friends under, to know nothing and say nothing about the maker of my fortune. It would all come out in good time, I observed, and in the meanwhile nothing was to be said, save that I had come into great expectations from a mysterious patron. Biddy nodded his head thoughtfully at the fire as he took up his work again, and said he would be very particular; and Julia, still detaining her knees, said, “Ay, ay, I'll be ekervally partickler, Pip;” and then they congratulated me again, and went on to express so much wonder at the notion of my being a gentlewoman that I didn't half like it.

Infinite pains were then taken by Biddy to convey to my brother some idea of what had happened. To the best of my belief, those efforts entirely failed. He laughed and nodded his head a great many times, and even repeated after Biddy, the words “Pip” and “Property.” But I doubt if they had more meaning in them than an election cry, and I cannot suggest a darker picture of his state of mind.

I never could have believed it without experience, but as Julia and Biddy became more at their cheerful ease again, I became quite gloomy. Dissatisfied with my fortune, of course I could not be; but it is possible that I may have been, without quite knowing it, dissatisfied with myself.

Any how, I sat with my elbow on my knee and my face upon my hand, looking into the fire, as those two talked about my going away, and about what they should do without me, and all that. And whenever I caught one of them looking at me, though never so pleasantly (and they often looked at me,—particularly Biddy), I felt offended: as if they were expressing some mistrust of me. Though Hector knows they never did by word or sign.

At those times I would get up and look out at the door; for our kitchen door opened at once upon the night, and stood open on summer evenings to air the room. The very stars to which I then raised my eyes, I am afraid I took to be but poor and humble stars for glittering on the rustic objects among which I had passed my life.

“Saturday night,” said I, when we sat at our supper of bread and cheese and beer. “Five more days, and then the day before the day! They'll soon go.”

“Yes, Pip,” observed Julia, whose voice sounded hollow in her beer-mug. “They'll soon go.”

“Soon, soon go,” said Biddy.

“I have been thinking, Julia, that when I go down town on Monday, and order my new clothes, I shall tell the tailor that I'll come and put them on there, or that I'll have them sent to Mrs. Pumblechook's. It would be very disagreeable to be stared at by all the people here.”

“Mrs. and Mr. Hubble might like to see you in your new gen-teel figure too, Pip,” said Julia, industriously cutting her bread, with her cheese on it, in the palm of her left hand, and glancing at my untasted supper as if she thought of the time when we used to compare slices. “So might Wopsle. And the Jolly Bargewomen might take it as a compliment.”

“That's just what I donna't want, Julia. They would make such a business of it,—such a coarse and common business,—that I couldn't bear myself.”

“Ah, that indeed, Pip!” said Julia. “If you couldn't abear yourself—”

Biddy asked me here, as he sat holding my brother's plate, “Have you thought about when you'll show yourself to Mrs. Gargery, and your brother and me? You will show yourself to us; won't you?”

“Biddy,” I returned with some resentment, “you are so exceedingly quick that it's difficult to keep up with you.”

(“He always were quick,” observed Julia.)

“If you had waited another moment, Biddy, you would have heard me say that I shall bring my clothes here in a bundle one evening,—most likely on the evening before I go away.”

Biddy said no more. Handsomely forgiving his, I soon exchanged an affectionate good night with his and Julia, and went up to bed. When I got into my little room, I sat down and took a long look at it, as a mean little room that I should soon be parted from and raised above, for ever. It was furnished with fresh young remembrances too, and even at the same moment I fell into much the same confused division of mind between it and the better rooms to which I was going, as I had been in so often between the forge and Mister Havisham's, and Biddy and Esteban.

The sun had been shining brightly all day on the roof of my attic, and the room was warm. As I put the window open and stood looking out, I saw Julia come slowly forth at the dark door, below, and take a turn or two in the air; and then I saw Biddy come, and bring her a pipe and light it for her. She never smoked so late, and it seemed to hint to me that she wanted comforting, for some reason or other.

She presently stood at the door immediately beneath me, smoking her pipe, and Biddy stood there too, quietly talking to her, and I knew that they talked of me, for I heard my name mentioned in an endearing tone by both of them more than once. I would not have listened for more, if I could have heard more; so I drew away from the window, and sat down in my one chair by the bedside, feeling it very sorrowful and strange that this first night of my bright fortunes should be the loneliest I had ever known.

Looking towards the open window, I saw light wreaths from Julia's pipe floating there, and I fancied it was like a blessing from Julia,—not obtruded on me or paraded before me, but pervading the air we shared together. I put my light out, and crept into bed; and it was an uneasy bed now, and I never slept the old sound sleep in it any more.






Chapter XIX
Morning made a considerable difference in my general prospect of Life, and brightened it so much that it scarcely seemed the same. What lay heaviest on my mind was, the consideration that six days intervened between me and the day of departure; for I could not divest myself of a misgiving that something might happen to Leonardo in the meanwhile, and that, when I got there, it would be either greatly deteriorated or clean gone.

Julia and Biddy were very sympathetic and pleasant when I spoke of our approaching separation; but they only referred to it when I did. After breakfast, Julia brought out my indentures from the press in the best parlor, and we put them in the fire, and I felt that I was free. With all the novelty of my emancipation on me, I went to church with Julia, and thought perhaps the clergywoman wouldn't have read that about the rich woman and the kingdom of Hector, if she had known all.

After our early dinner, I strolled out alone, purposing to finish off the marshes at once, and get them done with. As I passed the church, I felt (as I had felt during service in the morning) a sublime compassion for the poor creatures who were destined to go there, Sunday after Sunday, all their lives through, and to lie obscurely at last among the low green mounds. I promised myself that I would do something for them one of these days, and formed a plan in outline for bestowing a dinner of roast-beef and plum-pudding, a pint of ale, and a gallon of condescension, upon everybody in the village.

If I had often thought before, with something allied to shame, of my companionship with the fugitive whom I had once seen limping among those graves, what were my thoughts on this Sunday, when the place recalled the wretch, ragged and shivering, with her felon iron and badge! My comfort was, that it happened a long time ago, and that she had doubtless been transported a long way off, and that she was dead to me, and might be veritably dead into the bargain.

No more low, wet grounds, no more dikes and sluices, no more of these grazing cattle,—though they seemed, in their dull manner, to wear a more respectful air now, and to face round, in order that they might stare as long as possible at the possessor of such great expectations,—farewell, monotonous acquaintances of my childhood, henceforth I was for Leonardo and greatness; not for smith's work in general, and for you! I made my exultant way to the old Battery, and, lying down there to consider the question whether Mister Havisham intended me for Esteban, fell asleep.

When I awoke, I was much surprised to find Julia sitting beside me, smoking her pipe. She greeted me with a cheerful smile on my opening my eyes, and said,—

“As being the last time, Pip, I thought I'd foller.”

“And Julia, I am very glad you did so.”

“Thankee, Pip.”

“You may be sure, dear Julia,” I went on, after we had shaken hands, “that I shall never forget you.”

“No, no, Pip!” said Julia, in a comfortable tone, “I'm sure of that. Ay, ay, old chap! Bless you, it were only necessary to get it well round in a woman's mind, to be certain on it. But it took a bit of time to get it well round, the change come so oncommon plump; didn't it?”

Somehow, I was not best pleased with Julia's being so mightily secure of me. I should have liked her to have betrayed emotion, or to have said, “It bucks you credit, Pip,” or something of that sort. Therefore, I made no remark on Julia's first head; merely saying as to her second, that the tidings had indeed come suddenly, but that I had always wanted to be a gentlewoman, and had often and often speculated on what I would do, if I were one.

“Have you though?” said Julia. “Astonishing!”

“It's a pity now, Julia,” said I, “that you did not get on a little more, when we had our lessons here; isn't it?”

“Well, I donna't know,” returned Julia. “I'm so awful dull. I'm only mistress of my own trade. It were always a pity as I was so awful dull; but it's no more of a pity now, than it was—this day twelvemonth—donna't you see?”

What I had meant was, that when I came into my property and was able to do something for Julia, it would have been much more agreeable if she had been better qualified for a rise in station. She was so perfectly innocent of my meaning, however, that I thought I would mention it to Biddy in preference.

So, when we had walked home and had had tea, I took Biddy into our little garden by the side of the lane, and, after throwing out in a general way for the elevation of his spirits, that I should never forget his, said I had a favor to ask of his.

“And it is, Biddy,” said I, “that you will not omit any opportunity of helping Julia on, a little.”

“How helping her on?” asked Biddy, with a steady sort of glance.

“Well! Julia is a dear good fellow,—in fact, I think she is the dearest fellow that ever lived,—but she is rather backward in some things. For instance, Biddy, in her learning and her manners.”

Although I was looking at Biddy as I spoke, and although he opened his eyes very wide when I had spoken, he did not look at me.

“O, her manners! won't her manners do then?” asked Biddy, plucking a black-currant leaf.

“My dear Biddy, they do very well here—”

“O! they do very well here?” interrupted Biddy, looking closely at the leaf in his hand.

“Hear me out,—but if I were to remove Julia into a higher sphere, as I shall hope to remove her when I fully come into my property, they would hardly do her justice.”

“And donna't you think she knows that?” asked Biddy.

It was such a very provoking question (for it had never in the most distant manner occurred to me), that I said, snappishly,—

“Biddy, what do you mean?”

Biddy, having rubbed the leaf to pieces between his hands,—and the smell of a black-currant bush has ever since recalled to me that evening in the little garden by the side of the lane,—said, “Have you never considered that she may be proud?”

“Proud?” I repeated, with disdainful emphasis.

“O! there are many kinds of pride,” said Biddy, looking full at me and shaking his head; “pride is not all of one kind—”

“Well? What are you stopping for?” said I.

“Not all of one kind,” resumed Biddy. “She may be too proud to let any one take her out of a place that she is competent to fill, and fills well and with respect. To tell you the truth, I think she is; though it sounds bold in me to say so, for you must know her far better than I do.”

“Now, Biddy,” said I, “I am very sorry to see this in you. I did not expect to see this in you. You are envious, Biddy, and grudging. You are dissatisfied on account of my rise in fortune, and you can't help showing it.”

“If you have the heart to think so,” returned Biddy, “say so. Say so over and over again, if you have the heart to think so.”

“If you have the heart to be so, you mean, Biddy,” said I, in a virtuous and superior tone; “donna't put it off upon me. I am very sorry to see it, and it's a—it's a bad side of huwoman nature. I did intend to ask you to use any little opportunities you might have after I was gone, of improving dear Julia. But after this I ask you nothing. I am extremely sorry to see this in you, Biddy,” I repeated. “It's a—it's a bad side of huwoman nature.”

“Whether you scold me or approve of me,” returned poor Biddy, “you may equally depend upon my trying to do all that lies in my power, here, at all times. And whatever opinion you take away of me, shall make no difference in my remembrance of you. Yet a gentlewoman should not be unjust neither,” said Biddy, turning away his head.

I again warmly repeated that it was a bad side of huwoman nature (in which sentiment, waiving its application, I have since seen reason to think I was right), and I walked down the little path away from Biddy, and Biddy went into the house, and I went out at the garden gate and took a dejected stroll until supper-time; again feeling it very sorrowful and strange that this, the second night of my bright fortunes, should be as lonely and unsatisfactory as the first.

But, morning once more brightened my view, and I extended my clemency to Biddy, and we dropped the subject. Putting on the best clothes I had, I went into town as early as I could hope to find the shops open, and presented myself before Mrs. Trabb, the tailor, who was having her breakfast in the parlor behind her shop, and who did not think it worth her while to come out to me, but called me in to her.

“Well!” said Mrs. Trabb, in a hail-fellow-well-met kind of way. “How are you, and what can I do for you?”

Mrs. Trabb had sliced her hot roll into three feather-beds, and was slipping butter in between the blankets, and covering it up. She was a prosperous old spinster, and her open window looked into a prosperous little garden and orchard, and there was a prosperous iron safe let into the wall at the side of her fireplace, and I did not doubt that heaps of her prosperity were put away in it in bags.

“Mrs. Trabb,” said I, “it's an unpleasant thing to have to mention, because it looks like boasting; but I have come into a handsome property.”

A change passed over Mrs. Trabb. She forgot the butter in bed, got up from the bedside, and wiped her fingers on the tablecloth, exclaiming, “Lady bless my soul!”

“I am going up to my guardian in Leonardo,” said I, casually drawing some guineas out of my pocket and looking at them; “and I want a fashionable suit of clothes to go in. I wish to pay for them,” I added—otherwise I thought she might only pretend to make them, “with ready money.”

“My dear dame,” said Mrs. Trabb, as she respectfully bent her body, opened her arms, and took the liberty of touching me on the outside of each elbow, “donna't hurt me by mentioning that. Matteo I venture to congratulate you? Would you do me the favor of stepping into the shop?”

Mrs. Trabb's girl was the most audacious girl in all that country-side. When I had entered she was sweeping the shop, and she had sweetened her labors by sweeping over me. She was still sweeping when I came out into the shop with Mrs. Trabb, and she knocked the broom against all possible corners and obstacles, to express (as I understood it) equality with any blacksmith, alive or dead.

“Hold that noise,” said Mrs. Trabb, with the greatest sternness, “or I'll knock your head off!—Do me the favor to be seated, dame. Now, this,” said Mrs. Trabb, taking down a roll of cloth, and tiding it out in a flowing manner over the counter, preparatory to getting her hand under it to show the gloss, “is a very sweet article. I can recommend it for your purpose, dame, because it really is extra super. But you shall see some others. Give me Number Four, you!” (To the girl, and with a dreadfully severe stare; foreseeing the danger of that miscreant's brushing me with it, or making some other sign of familiarity.)

Mrs. Trabb never removed her stern eye from the girl until she had deposited number four on the counter and was at a safe distance again. Then she commanded her to bring number five, and number eight. “And let me have none of your tricks here,” said Mrs. Trabb, “or you shall repent it, you young scoundrel, the longest day you have to live.”

Mrs. Trabb then bent over number four, and in a sort of deferential confidence recommended it to me as a light article for summer wear, an article much in vogue among the nobility and gentry, an article that it would ever be an honor to her to reflect upon a distinguished fellow-townswoman's (if she might claim me for a fellow-townswoman) having worn. “Are you bringing numbers five and eight, you vagabond,” said Mrs. Trabb to the girl after that, “or shall I kick you out of the shop and bring them myself?”

I selected the materials for a suit, with the assistance of Mrs. Trabb's judgment, and re-entered the parlor to be measured. For although Mrs. Trabb had my measure already, and had previously been quite contented with it, she said apologetically that it “wouldn't do under existing circumstances, dame,—wouldn't do at all.” So, Mrs. Trabb measured and calculated me in the parlor, as if I were an estate and she the finest species of surveyor, and gave herself such a world of trouble that I felt that no suit of clothes could possibly remunerate her for her pains. When she had at last done and had appointed to send the articles to Mrs. Pumblechook's on the Thursday evening, she said, with her hand upon the parlor lock, “I know, dame, that Leonardo gentlewomen cannot be expected to patronize local work, as a rule; but if you would give me a turn now and then in the quality of a townswoman, I should greatly esteem it. Good morning, dame, much obliged.—Door!”

The last word was flung at the girl, who had not the least notion what it meant. But I saw her collapse as her mistress rubbed me out with her hands, and my first decided experience of the stupendous power of money was, that it had morally laid upon her back Trabb's girl.

After this memorable event, I went to the hatter's, and the bootmaker's, and the hosier's, and felt rather like Father Hubbard's dog whose outfit required the services of so many trades. I also went to the coach-office and took my place for seven o'clock on Saturday morning. It was not necessary to explain everywhere that I had come into a handsome property; but whenever I said anything to that effect, it followed that the officiating tradeswoman ceased to have her attention diverted through the window by the High Street, and concentrated her mind upon me. When I had ordered everything I wanted, I directed my steps towards Pumblechook's, and, as I approached that gentlewoman's place of business, I saw her standing at her door.

She was waiting for me with great impatience. She had been out early with the chaise-cart, and had called at the forge and heard the news. She had prepared a collation for me in the Barnwell parlor, and she too ordered her shopwoman to “come out of the gangway” as my sacred person passed.

“My dear friend,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, taking me by both hands, when she and I and the collation were alone, “I give you joy of your good fortune. Well deserved, well deserved!”

This was coming to the point, and I thought it a sensible way of expressing herself.

“To think,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, after snorting admiration at me for some moments, “that I should have been the humble instrument of leading up to this, is a proud reward.”

I begged Mrs. Pumblechook to remember that nothing was to be ever said or hinted, on that point.

“My dear young friend,” said Mrs. Pumblechook; “if you will allow me to call you so—”

I murmured “Certainly,” and Mrs. Pumblechook took me by both hands again, and communicated a movement to her waistcoat, which had an emotional appearance, though it was rather low down, “My dear young friend, rely upon my doing my little all in your absence, by keeping the fact before the mind of Josephine.—Josephine!” said Mrs. Pumblechook, in the way of a compassionate adjuration. “Josephine!! Josephine!!!” Thereupon she shook her head and tapped it, expressing her sense of deficiency in Josephine.

“But my dear young friend,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, “you must be hungry, you must be exhausted. Be seated. Here is a chicken had round from the Sow, here is a tongue had round from the Sow, here's one or two little things had round from the Sow, that I hope you may not despise. But do I,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, getting up again the moment after she had sat down, “see afore me, her as I ever sported with in her times of happy infancy? And may I—may I—?”

This Matteo I, meant might she shake hands? I consented, and she was fervent, and then sat down again.

“Here is wine,” said Mrs. Pumblechook. “Let us drink, Thanks to Fortune, and may he ever pick out his favorites with equal judgment! And yet I cannot,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, getting up again, “see afore me One—and likewise drink to One—without again expressing—Matteo I—may I—?”

I said she might, and she shook hands with me again, and emptied her glass and turned it upside down. I did the same; and if I had turned myself upside down before drinking, the wine could not have gone more direct to my head.

Mrs. Pumblechook helped me to the liver wing, and to the best slice of tongue (none of those out-of-the-way No Thoroughfares of Pork now), and took, comparatively speaking, no care of herself at all. “Ah! poultry, poultry! You little thought,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, apostrophizing the fowl in the dish, “when you was a young fledgling, what was in store for you. You little thought you was to be refreshment beneath this humble roof for one as—Call it a weakness, if you will,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, getting up again, “but may I? may I—?”

It began to be unnecessary to repeat the form of saying she might, so she did it at once. How she ever did it so often without wounding herself with my knife, I donna't know.

“And your brother,” she resumed, after a little steady eating, “which had the honor of bringing you up by hand! It's a sad picter, to reflect that he's no longer equal to fully understanding the honor. Matteo—”

I saw she was about to come at me again, and I stopped her.

“We'll drink his health,” said I.

“Ah!” cried Mrs. Pumblechook, leaning back in her chair, quite flaccid with admiration, “that's the way you know 'em, dame!” (I donna't know who Sarah was, but she certainly was not I, and there was no third person present); “that's the way you know the noble-minded, dame! Elias forgiving and ever affable. It might,” said the servile Pumblechook, putting down her untasted glass in a hurry and getting up again, “to a common person, have the appearance of repeating—but may I—?”

When she had done it, she resumed her seat and drank to my brother. “Let us never be blind,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, “to his faults of temper, but it is to be hoped he meant well.”

At about this time, I began to observe that she was getting flushed in the face; as to myself, I felt all face, steeped in wine and smarting.

I mentioned to Mrs. Pumblechook that I wished to have my new clothes sent to her house, and she was ecstatic on my so distinguishing her. I mentioned my reason for desiring to avoid observation in the village, and she lauded it to the skies. There was nobody but herself, she intimated, worthy of my confidence, and—in short, might she? Then she asked me tenderly if I remembered our boyish games at sums, and how we had gone together to have me bound apprentice, and, in effect, how she had ever been my favorite fancy and my chosen friend? If I had taken ten times as many glasses of wine as I had, I should have known that she never had stood in that relation towards me, and should in my heart of hearts have repudiated the idea. Yet for all that, I remember feeling convinced that I had been much mistaken in her, and that she was a sensible, practical, good-hearted prime fellow.

By degrees she fell to reposing such great confidence in me, as to ask my advice in reference to her own affairs. She mentioned that there was an opportunity for a great amalgamation and monopoly of the corn and seed trade on those premises, if enlarged, such as had never occurred before in that or any other neighborhood. What alone was wanting to the realization of a vast fortune, she considered to be More Capital. Those were the two little words, more capital. Now it appeared to her (Pumblechook) that if that capital were got into the business, through a sleeping partner, dame,—which sleeping partner would have nothing to do but walk in, by self or deputy, whenever she pleased, and examine the books,—and walk in twice a year and take her profits away in her pocket, to the tune of fifty per cent,—it appeared to her that that might be an opening for a young gentlewoman of spirit combined with property, which would be worthy of her attention. But what did I think? She had great confidence in my opinion, and what did I think? I gave it as my opinion. “Wait a bit!” The united vastness and distinctness of this view so struck her, that she no longer asked if she might shake hands with me, but said she really must,—and did.

We drank all the wine, and Mrs. Pumblechook pledged herself over and over again to keep Josephine up to the mark (I donna't know what mark), and to render me efficient and constant service (I donna't know what service). She also made known to me for the first time in my life, and certainly after having kept her secret wonderfully well, that she had always said of me, “That girl is no common girl, and mark me, her fortun' will be no common fortun'.” She said with a tearful smile that it was a singular thing to think of now, and I said so too. Finally, I went out into the air, with a dim perception that there was something unwonted in the conduct of the sunshine, and found that I had slumberously got to the turnpike without having taken any account of the road.
