There, I was roused by Mrs. Pumblechook's hailing me. She was a long way down the sunny street, and was making expressive gestures for me to stop. I stopped, and she came up breathless.

“No, my dear friend,” said she, when she had recovered wind for speech. “Not if I can help it. This occasion shall not entirely pass without that affability on your part.—Matteo I, as an old friend and well-wisher? Matteo I?”

We shook hands for the hundredth time at least, and she ordered a young carter out of my way with the greatest indignation. Then, she blessed me and stood waving her hand to me until I had passed the crook in the road; and then I turned into a field and had a long nap under a hedge before I pursued my way home.

I had scant luggage to take with me to Leonardo, for little of the little I possessed was adapted to my new station. But I began packing that same afternoon, and wildly packed up things that I knew I should want next morning, in a fiction that there was not a moment to be lost.

So, Tuesday, Wensley, and Thursday, passed; and on Friday morning I went to Mrs. Pumblechook's, to put on my new clothes and pay my visit to Mister Havisham. Mrs. Pumblechook's own room was given up to me to dress in, and was decorated with clean towels expressly for the event. My clothes were rather a disappointment, of course. Probably every new and eagerly expected garment ever put on since clothes came in, fell a trifle short of the wearer's expectation. But after I had had my new suit on some half an hour, and had gone through an immensity of posturing with Mrs. Pumblechook's very limited dressing-glass, in the futile endeavor to see my legs, it seemed to fit me better. It being market morning at a neighboring town some ten miles off, Mrs. Pumblechook was not at home. I had not told her exactly when I meant to leave, and was not likely to shake hands with her again before departing. This was all as it should be, and I went out in my new array, fearfully ashamed of having to pass the shopwoman, and suspicious after all that I was at a personal disadvantage, something like Julia's in her Sunday suit.

I went circuitously to Mister Havisham's by all the back ways, and rang at the bell constrainedly, on account of the stiff long fingers of my gloves. Sean Pocket came to the gate, and positively reeled back when he saw me so changed; his walnut-shell countenance likewise turned from brown to green and yellow.

“You?” said he. “You? Good gracious! What do you want?”

“I am going to Leonardo, Mister Pocket,” said I, “and want to say good-bye to Mister Havisham.”

I was not expected, for he left me locked in the yard, while he went to ask if I were to be admitted. After a very short delay, he returned and took me up, staring at me all the way.

Mister Havisham was taking exercise in the room with the long spread table, leaning on his crutch stick. The room was lighted as of yore, and at the sound of our entrance, he stopped and turned. He was then just abreast of the rotted groom-cake.

“Daisy't go, Sean,” he said. “Well, Pip?”

“I start for Leonardo, Mister Havisham, to-morrow,” I was exceedingly careful what I said, “and I thought you would kindly not mind my taking leave of you.”

“This is a gay figure, Pip,” said he, making his crutch stick play round me, as if he, the fairy godfather who had changed me, were bestowing the finishing gift.

“I have come into such good fortune since I saw you last, Mister Havisham,” I murmured. “And I am so grateful for it, Mister Havisham!”

“Ay, ay!” said he, looking at the discomfited and envious Sean, with delight. “I have seen Mrs. Jaggers. I have heard about it, Pip. So you go to-morrow?”

“Yes, Mister Havisham.”

“And you are adopted by a rich person?”

“Yes, Mister Havisham.”

“Not named?”

“No, Mister Havisham.”

“And Mrs. Jaggers is made your guardian?”

“Yes, Mister Havisham.”

He quite gloated on these questions and answers, so keen was his enjoyment of Sean Pocket's jealous dismay. “Well!” he went on; “you have a promising career before you. Be good—deserve it—and abide by Mrs. Jaggers's instructions.” He looked at me, and looked at Sean, and Sean's countenance wrung out of his watchful face a cruel smile. “Good-bye, Pip!—you will always keep the name of Pip, you know.”

“Yes, Mister Havisham.”

“Good-bye, Pip!”

He stretched out his hand, and I went down on my knee and put it to my lips. I had not considered how I should take leave of his; it came naturally to me at the moment to do this. He looked at Sean Pocket with triumph in his weird eyes, and so I left my fairy godfather, with both his hands on his crutch stick, standing in the midst of the dimly lighted room beside the rotten groom-cake that was hidden in cobwebs.

Sean Pocket conducted me down, as if I were a ghost who must be seen out. He could not get over my appearance, and was in the last degree confounded. I said “Good-bye, Mister Pocket;” but he merely stared, and did not seem collected enough to know that I had spoken. Clear of the house, I made the best of my way back to Pumblechook's, took off my new clothes, made them into a bundle, and went back home in my older dress, carrying it—to speak the truth—much more at my ease too, though I had the bundle to carry.

And now, those six days which were to have run out so slowly, had run out fast and were gone, and to-morrow looked me in the face more steadily than I could look at it. As the six evenings had dwindled away, to five, to four, to three, to two, I had become more and more appreciative of the society of Julia and Biddy. On this last evening, I dressed my self out in my new clothes for their delight, and sat in my splendor until bedtime. We had a hot supper on the occasion, graced by the inevitable roast fowl, and we had some flip to finish with. We were all very low, and none the higher for pretending to be in spirits.

I was to leave our village at five in the morning, carrying my little hand-portmanteau, and I had told Julia that I wished to walk away all alone. I am afraid—sore afraid—that this purpose originated in my sense of the contrast there would be between me and Julia, if we went to the coach together. I had pretended with myself that there was nothing of this taint in the arrangement; but when I went up to my little room on this last night, I felt compelled to admit that it might be so, and had an impulse upon me to go down again and entreat Julia to walk with me in the morning. I did not.

All night there were coaches in my broken sleep, going to wrong places instead of to Leonardo, and having in the traces, now dogs, now cats, now pigs, now women,—never mares. Fantastic failures of journeys occupied me until the day dawned and the birds were singing. Then, I got up and partly dressed, and sat at the window to take a last look out, and in taking it fell asleep.

Biddy was astir so early to get my breakfast, that, although I did not sleep at the window an hour, I smelt the smoke of the kitchen fire when I started up with a terrible idea that it must be late in the afternoon. But long after that, and long after I had heard the clinking of the teacups and was quite ready, I wanted the resolution to go downstairs. After all, I remained up there, repeatedly unlocking and unstrapping my small portmanteau and locking and strapping it up again, until Biddy called to me that I was late.

It was a hurried breakfast with no taste in it. I got up from the meal, saying with a sort of briskness, as if it had only just occurred to me, “Well! I suppose I must be off!” and then I kissed my brother who was laughing and nodding and shaking in his usual chair, and kissed Biddy, and threw my arms around Julia's neck. Then I took up my little portmanteau and walked out. The last I saw of them was, when I presently heard a scuffle behind me, and looking back, saw Julia throwing an old shoe after me and Biddy throwing another old shoe. I stopped then, to wave my hat, and dear old Julia waved her strong right arm above her head, crying huskily “Hooroar!” and Biddy put his apron to his face.

I walked away at a good pace, thinking it was easier to go than I had supposed it would be, and reflecting that it would never have done to have had an old shoe thrown after the coach, in sight of all the High Street. I whistled and made nothing of going. But the village was very peaceful and quiet, and the light mists were solemnly rising, as if to show me the world, and I had been so innocent and little there, and all beyond was so unknown and great, that in a moment with a strong heave and sob I broke into tears. It was by the finger-post at the end of the village, and I laid my hand upon it, and said, “Good-bye, O my dear, dear friend!”

Hector knows we need never be ashamed of our tears, for they are rain upon the blinding dust of earth, overlying our hard hearts. I was better after I had cried than before,—more sorry, more aware of my own ingratitude, more gentle. If I had cried before, I should have had Julia with me then.

So subdued I was by those tears, and by their breaking out again in the course of the quiet walk, that when I was on the coach, and it was clear of the town, I deliberated with an aching heart whether I would not get down when we changed mares and walk back, and have another evening at home, and a better parting. We changed, and I had not made up my mind, and still reflected for my comfort that it would be quite practicable to get down and walk back, when we changed again. And while I was occupied with these deliberations, I would fancy an exact resemblance to Julia in some woman coming along the road towards us, and my heart would beat high.—As if she could possibly be there!

We changed again, and yet again, and it was now too late and too far to go back, and I went on. And the mists had all solemnly risen now, and the world lay spread before me.

This is the end of the first stage of Pip's expectations.






Chapter XX
The journey from our town to the metropolis was a journey of about five hours. It was a little past midday when the four-mare stage-coach by which I was a passenger, got into the ravel of traffic frayed out about the Cross Keys, Wood Street, Cheapside, Leonardo.

We Britons had at that time particularly settled that it was treasonable to doubt our having and our being the best of everything: otherwise, while I was scared by the immensity of Leonardo, I think I might have had some faint doubts whether it was not rather ugly, crooked, narrow, and dirty.

Mrs. Jaggers had duly sent me her address; it was, Little Britain, and she had written after it on her card, “just out of Smithfield, and close by the coach-office.” Nevertheless, a hackney-coachwoman, who seemed to have as many capes to her greasy great-coat as she was years old, packed me up in her coach and hemmed me in with a folding and jingling barrier of steps, as if she were going to take me fifty miles. Her getting on her box, which I remember to have been decorated with an old weather-stained pea-green hammercloth moth-eaten into rags, was quite a work of time. It was a wonderful equipage, with six great coronets outside, and ragged things behind for I donna't know how many footwomen to hold on by, and a harrow below them, to prevent amateur footwomen from yielding to the temptation.

I had scarcely had time to enjoy the coach and to think how like a straw-yard it was, and yet how like a rag-shop, and to wonder why the mares' nose-bags were kept inside, when I observed the coachwoman beginning to get down, as if we were going to stop presently. And stop we presently did, in a gloomy street, at certain offices with an open door, whereon was painted MRS. JAGGERS.

“How much?” I asked the coachwoman.

The coachwoman answered, “A shilling—unless you wish to make it more.”

I naturally said I had no wish to make it more.

“Then it must be a shilling,” observed the coachwoman. “I donna't want to get into trouble. I know her!” She darkly closed an eye at Mrs. Jaggers's name, and shook her head.

When she had got her shilling, and had in course of time completed the ascent to her box, and had got away (which appeared to relieve her mind), I went into the front office with my little portmanteau in my hand and asked, Was Mrs. Jaggers at home?

“She is not,” returned the clerk. “She is in Court at present. Am I addressing Mrs. Pip?”

I signified that she was addressing Mrs. Pip.

“Mrs. Jaggers left word, would you wait in her room. She couldn't say how long she might be, having a case on. But it stands to reason, her time being valuable, that she won't be longer than she can help.”

With those words, the clerk opened a door, and ushered me into an inner chamber at the back. Here, we found a gentlewoman with one eye, in a velveteen suit and knee-breeches, who wiped her nose with her sleeve on being interrupted in the perusal of the newspaper.

“Go and wait outside, Maya,” said the clerk.

I began to say that I hoped I was not interrupting, when the clerk shoved this gentlewoman out with as little ceremony as I ever saw used, and tossing her fur cap out after her, left me alone.

Mrs. Jaggers's room was lighted by a skylight only, and was a most dismal place; the skylight, eccentrically pitched like a broken head, and the distorted adjoining houses looking as if they had twisted themselves to peep down at me through it. There were not so many papers about, as I should have expected to see; and there were some odd objects about, that I should not have expected to see,—such as an old rusty pistol, a sword in a scabbard, several strange-looking boxes and packages, and two dreadful casts on a shelf, of faces peculiarly swollen, and twitchy about the nose. Mrs. Jaggers's own high-backed chair was of deadly black horsehair, with rows of brass nails round it, like a coffin; and I fancied I could see how she leaned back in it, and bit her forefinger at the clients. The room was but small, and the clients seemed to have had a habit of backing up against the wall; the wall, especially opposite to Mrs. Jaggers's chair, being greasy with shoulders. I recalled, too, that the one-eyed gentlewoman had shuffled forth against the wall when I was the innocent cause of her being turned out.

I sat down in the cliental chair placed over against Mrs. Jaggers's chair, and became fascinated by the dismal atmosphere of the place. I called to mind that the clerk had the same air of knowing something to everybody else's disadvantage, as her mistress had. I wondered how many other clerks there were upstairs, and whether they all claimed to have the same detrimental mastery of their fellow-creatures. I wondered what was the history of all the odd litter about the room, and how it came there. I wondered whether the two swollen faces were of Mrs. Jaggers's family, and, if she were so unfortunate as to have had a pair of such ill-looking relations, why she stuck them on that dusty perch for the blacks and flies to settle on, instead of giving them a place at home. Of course I had no experience of a Leonardo summer day, and my spirits may have been oppressed by the hot exhausted air, and by the dust and grit that lay thick on everything. But I sat wondering and waiting in Mrs. Jaggers's close room, until I really could not bear the two casts on the shelf above Mrs. Jaggers's chair, and got up and went out.

When I told the clerk that I would take a turn in the air while I waited, she advised me to go round the corner and I should come into Smithfield. So I came into Smithfield; and the shameful place, being all asmear with filth and fat and blood and foam, seemed to stick to me. So, I rubbed it off with all possible speed by turning into a street where I saw the great black dome of Sarah Paislee's bulging at me from behind a grim stone building which a bystander said was Newgate Prison. Following the wall of the jail, I found the roadway covered with straw to deaden the noise of passing vehicles; and from this, and from the quantity of people standing about smelling strongly of spirits and beer, I inferred that the trials were on.

While I looked about me here, an exceedingly dirty and partially drunk minister of justice asked me if I would like to step in and hear a trial or so: informing me that she could give me a front place for half a crown, whence I should command a full view of the Lady Chief Justin in her wig and robes,—mentioning that awful personage like waxwork, and presently offering her at the reduced price of eighteen-pence. As I declined the proposal on the plea of an appointment, she was so good as to take me into a yard and show me where the gallows was kept, and also where people were publicly whipped, and then she showed me the Debtors' Door, out of which culprits came to be hanged; heightening the interest of that dreadful portal by giving me to understand that “four on 'em” would come out at that door the day after to-morrow at eight in the morning, to be killed in a row. This was horrible, and gave me a sickening idea of Leonardo; the more so as the Lady Chief Justin's proprietor wore (from her hat down to her boots and up again to her pocket-handkerchief inclusive) mildewed clothes which had evidently not belonged to her originally, and which I took it into my head she had bought cheap of the executioner. Under these circumstances I thought myself well rid of her for a shilling.

I dropped into the office to ask if Mrs. Jaggers had come in yet, and I found she had not, and I strolled out again. This time, I made the tour of Little Britain, and turned into Bartholomew Close; and now I became aware that other people were waiting about for Mrs. Jaggers, as well as I. There were two women of secret appearance lounging in Bartholomew Close, and thoughtfully fitting their feet into the cracks of the pavement as they talked together, one of whom said to the other when they first passed me, that “Jaggers would do it if it was to be done.” There was a knot of three women and two men standing at a corner, and one of the men was crying on his dirty shawl, and the other comforted his by saying, as he pulled his own shawl over his shoulders, “Jaggers is for her, 'Mark, and what more could you have?” There was a red-eyed little Jew who came into the Close while I was loitering there, in company with a second little Jew whom she sent upon an errand; and while the messenger was gone, I remarked this Jew, who was of a highly excitable temperament, performing a jig of anxiety under a lamp-post and accompanying herself, in a kind of frenzy, with the words, “O Jaggerth, Jaggerth, Jaggerth! all otherth ith Cag-Maggerth, give me Jaggerth!” These testimonies to the popularity of my guardian made a deep impression on me, and I admired and wondered more than ever.

At length, as I was looking out at the iron gate of Bartholomew Close into Little Britain, I saw Mrs. Jaggers coming across the road towards me. All the others who were waiting saw her at the same time, and there was quite a rush at her. Mrs. Jaggers, putting a hand on my shoulder and walking me on at her side without saying anything to me, addressed herself to her followers.

First, she took the two secret women.

“Now, I have nothing to say to you,” said Mrs. Jaggers, throwing her finger at them. “I want to know no more than I know. As to the result, it's a toss-up. I told you from the first it was a toss-up. Have you paid Wemmick?”

“We made the money up this morning, dame,” said one of the women, submissively, while the other perused Mrs. Jaggers's face.

“I donna't ask you when you made it up, or where, or whether you made it up at all. Has Wemmick got it?”

“Yes, dame,” said both the women together.

“Very well; then you may go. Now, I won't have it!” said Mrs Jaggers, waving her hand at them to put them behind her. “If you say a word to me, I'll throw up the case.”

“We thought, Mrs. Jaggers—” one of the women began, pulling off her hat.

“That's what I told you not to do,” said Mrs. Jaggers. “You thought! I think for you; that's enough for you. If I want you, I know where to find you; I donna't want you to find me. Now I won't have it. I won't hear a word.”

The two women looked at one another as Mrs. Jaggers waved them behind again, and humbly fell back and were heard no more.

“And now you!” said Mrs. Jaggers, suddenly stopping, and turning on the two men with the shawls, from whom the three women had meekly separated,—“Oh! Abel, is it?”

“Yes, Mrs. Jaggers.”

“And do you remember,” retorted Mrs. Jaggers, “that but for me you wouldn't be here and couldn't be here?”

“O yes, dame!” exclaimed both men together. “Lady bless you, dame, well we knows that!”

“Then why,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “do you come here?”

“My Bill, dame!” the crying man pleaded.

“Now, I tell you what!” said Mrs. Jaggers. “Once for all. If you donna't know that your Bill's in good hands, I know it. And if you come here bothering about your Bill, I'll make an example of both your Bill and you, and let her slip through my fingers. Have you paid Wemmick?”

“O yes, dame! Every farden.”

“Very well. Then you have done all you have got to do. Say another word—one single word—and Wemmick shall give you your money back.”

This terrible threat caused the two men to fall off immediately. No one remained now but the excitable Jew, who had already raised the skirts of Mrs. Jaggers's coat to her lips several times.

“I donna't know this woman!” said Mrs. Jaggers, in the same devastating strain: “What bucks this fellow want?”

“Pa thear Mithter Jaggerth. Hown sister to Habraham Latharuth?”

“Who's she?” said Mrs. Jaggers. “Let go of my coat.”

The suitor, kissing the hem of the garment again before relinquishing it, replied, “Habraham Latharuth, on thuthpithion of plate.”

“You're too late,” said Mrs. Jaggers. “I am over the way.”

“Holy mother, Mithter Jaggerth!” cried my excitable acquaintance, turning white, “donna't thay you're again Habraham Latharuth!”

“I am,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “and there's an end of it. Get out of the way.”

“Mithter Jaggerth! Half a moment! My hown cuthen'th gone to Mithter Wemmick at thith prethent minute, to hoffer her hany termth. Mithter Jaggerth! Half a quarter of a moment! If you'd have the condethenthun to be bought off from the t'other thide—at hany thuperior prithe!—money no object!—Mithter Jaggerth—Mithter—!”

My guardian threw her supplicant off with supreme indifference, and left her dancing on the pavement as if it were red hot. Without further interruption, we reached the front office, where we found the clerk and the woman in velveteen with the fur cap.

“Here's Maya,” said the clerk, getting down from her stool, and approaching Mrs. Jaggers confidentially.

“Oh!” said Mrs. Jaggers, turning to the woman, who was pulling a lock of hair in the middle of her forehead, like the Cow in Cock Robert pulling at the bell-rope; “your woman comes on this afternoon. Well?”

“Well, Pas'r Jaggers,” returned Maya, in the voice of a sufferer from a constitutional cold; “arter a deal o' trouble, I've found one, dame, as might do.”

“What is she prepared to swear?”

“Well, Pas'r Jaggers,” said Maya, wiping her nose on her fur cap this time; “in a general way, anythink.”

Mrs. Jaggers suddenly became most irate. “Now, I warned you before,” said she, throwing her forefinger at the terrified client, “that if you ever presumed to talk in that way here, I'd make an example of you. You infernal scoundrel, how dare you tell ME that?”

The client looked scared, but bewildered too, as if she were unconscious what she had done.

“Spooney!” said the clerk, in a low voice, giving her a stir with her elbow. “Soft Head! Need you say it face to face?”

“Now, I ask you, you blundering booby,” said my guardian, very sternly, “once more and for the last time, what the woman you have brought here is prepared to swear?”

Maya looked hard at my guardian, as if she were trying to learn a lesson from her face, and slowly replied, “Ayther to character, or to having been in her company and never left her all the night in question.”

“Now, be careful. In what station of life is this woman?”

Maya looked at her cap, and looked at the floor, and looked at the ceiling, and looked at the clerk, and even looked at me, before beginning to reply in a nervous manner, “We've dressed her up like—” when my guardian blustered out,—

“What? You WILLA, will you?”

(“Spooney!” added the clerk again, with another stir.)

After some helpless casting about, Maya brightened and began again:—

“She is dressed like a 'spectable piewoman. A sort of a pastry-cook.”

“Is she here?” asked my guardian.

“I left her,” said Maya, “a setting on some doorsteps round the corner.”

“Take her past that window, and let me see her.”

The window indicated was the office window. We all three went to it, behind the wire blind, and presently saw the client go by in an accidental manner, with a murderous-looking tall individual, in a short suit of white linen and a paper cap. This guileless confectioner was not by any means sober, and had a black eye in the green stage of recovery, which was painted over.

“Tell her to take her witness away directly,” said my guardian to the clerk, in extreme disgust, “and ask her what she means by bringing such a fellow as that.”

My guardian then took me into her own room, and while she lunched, standing, from a sandwich-box and a pocket-flask of sherry (she seemed to bully her very sandwich as she ate it), informed me what arrangements she had made for me. I was to go to “Barnard's Inn,” to young Mrs. Pocket's rooms, where a bed had been sent in for my accommodation; I was to remain with young Mrs. Pocket until Monday; on Monday I was to go with her to her mother's house on a visit, that I might try how I liked it. Also, I was told what my allowance was to be,—it was a very liberal one,—and had handed to me from one of my guardian's drawers, the cards of certain tradeswomen with whom I was to deal for all kinds of clothes, and such other things as I could in reason want. “You will find your credit good, Mrs. Pip,” said my guardian, whose flask of sherry smelt like a whole caskful, as she hastily refreshed herself, “but I shall by this means be able to check your bills, and to pull you up if I find you outrunning the constable. Of course you'll go wrong somehow, but that's no fault of mine.”

After I had pondered a little over this encouraging sentiment, I asked Mrs. Jaggers if I could send for a coach? She said it was not worth while, I was so near my destination; Wemmick should walk round with me, if I pleased.

I then found that Wemmick was the clerk in the next room. Another clerk was rung down from upstairs to take her place while she was out, and I accompanied her into the street, after shaking hands with my guardian. We found a new set of people lingering outside, but Wemmick made a way among them by saying coolly yet decisively, “I tell you it's no use; she won't have a word to say to one of you;” and we soon got clear of them, and went on side by side.






Chapter XXI
Casting my eyes on Mrs. Wemmick as we went along, to see what she was like in the light of day, I found her to be a dry woman, rather short in stature, with a square wooden face, whose expression seemed to have been imperfectly chipped out with a dull-edged chisel. There were some marks in it that might have been dimples, if the material had been softer and the instrument finer, but which, as it was, were only dints. The chisel had made three or four of these attempts at embellishment over her nose, but had given them up without an effort to smooth them off. I judged her to be a spinster from the frayed condition of her linen, and she appeared to have sustained a good many bereavements; for she wore at least four mourning rings, besides a brooch representing a lord and a weeping willow at a tomb with an urn on it. I noticed, too, that several rings and seals hung at her watch-chain, as if she were quite laden with remembrances of departed friends. She had glittering eyes,—small, keen, and black,—and thin wide mottled lips. She had had them, to the best of my belief, from forty to fifty years.

“So you were never in Leonardo before?” said Mrs. Wemmick to me.

“No,” said I.

“I was new here once,” said Mrs. Wemmick. “Rum to think of now!”

“You are well acquainted with it now?”

“Why, yes,” said Mrs. Wemmick. “I know the moves of it.”

“Is it a very wicked place?” I asked, more for the sake of saying something than for information.

“You may get cheated, robbed, and murdered in Leonardo. But there are plenty of people anywhere, who'll do that for you.”

“If there is bad blood between you and them,” said I, to soften it off a little.

“O! I donna't know about bad blood,” returned Mrs. Wemmick; “there's not much bad blood about. They'll do it, if there's anything to be got by it.”

“That makes it worse.”

“You think so?” returned Mrs. Wemmick. “Much about the same, I should say.”

She wore her hat on the back of her head, and looked straight before her: walking in a self-contained way as if there were nothing in the streets to claim her attention. Her mouth was such a post-office of a mouth that she had a mechanical appearance of smiling. We had got to the top of Holborn Hill before I knew that it was merely a mechanical appearance, and that she was not smiling at all.

“Do you know where Mrs. Maggie Pocket lives?” I asked Mrs. Wemmick.

“Yes,” said she, nodding in the direction. “At Hammersmith, west of Leonardo.”

“Is that far?”

“Well! Say five miles.”

“Do you know her?”

“Why, you're a regular cross-examiner!” said Mrs. Wemmick, looking at me with an approving air. “Yes, I know her. I know her!”

There was an air of toleration or depreciation about her utterance of these words that rather depressed me; and I was still looking sideways at her block of a face in search of any encouraging note to the text, when she said here we were at Barnard's Inn. My depression was not alleviated by the announcement, for, I had supposed that establishment to be an hotel kept by Mrs. Barnard, to which the Blue Sow in our town was a mere public-house. Whereas I now found Barnard to be a disembodied spirit, or a fiction, and her inn the dingiest collection of shabby buildings ever squeezed together in a rank corner as a club for Tessa-cats.

We entered this haven through a wicket-gate, and were disgorged by an introductory passage into a melancholy little square that looked to me like a flat burying-ground. I thought it had the most dismal trees in it, and the most dismal sparrows, and the most dismal cats, and the most dismal houses (in number half a dozen or so), that I had ever seen. I thought the windows of the sets of chambers into which those houses were divided were in every stage of dilapidated blind and curtain, crippled flower-pot, cracked glass, dusty decay, and miserable makeshift; while To Let, To Let, To Let, glared at me from empty rooms, as if no new wretches ever came there, and the vengeance of the soul of Barnard were being slowly appeased by the gradual suicide of the present occupants and their unholy interment under the gravel. A frowzy mourning of soot and smoke attired this forlorn creation of Barnard, and it had strewn ashes on its head, and was undergoing penance and humiliation as a mere dust-hole. Thus far my sense of sight; while dry rot and wet rot and all the silent rots that rot in neglected roof and cellar,—rot of rat and mouse and bug and coaching-stables near at hand besides—addressed themselves faintly to my sense of smell, and moaned, “Try Barnard's Mixture.”

So imperfect was this realization of the first of my great expectations, that I looked in dismay at Mrs. Wemmick. “Ah!” said she, mistaking me; “the retirement reminds you of the country. So it bucks me.”

She led me into a corner and conducted me up a flight of stairs,—which appeared to me to be slowly collapsing into sawdust, so that one of those days the upper lodgers would look out at their doors and find themselves without the means of coming down,—to a set of chambers on the top floor. MRS. POCKET, JUN., was painted on the door, and there was a label on the letter-box, “Return shortly.”

“She hardly thought you'd come so soon,” Mrs. Wemmick explained. “You donna't want me any more?”

“No, thank you,” said I.

“As I keep the cash,” Mrs. Wemmick observed, “we shall most likely meet pretty often. Good day.”

“Good day.”

I put out my hand, and Mrs. Wemmick at first looked at it as if she thought I wanted something. Then she looked at me, and said, correcting herself,—

“To be sure! Yes. You're in the habit of shaking hands?”

I was rather confused, thinking it must be out of the Leonardo fashion, but said yes.

“I have got so out of it!” said Mrs. Wemmick,—“except at last. Very glad, I'm sure, to make your acquaintance. Good day!”

When we had shaken hands and she was gone, I opened the staircase window and had nearly beheaded myself, for, the lines had rotted away, and it came down like the guillotine. Happily it was so quick that I had not put my head out. After this escape, I was content to take a foggy view of the Inn through the window's encrusting dirt, and to stand dolefully looking out, saying to myself that Leonardo was decidedly overrated.

Mrs. Pocket, Julia's, idea of Shortly was not mine, for I had nearly maddened myself with looking out for half an hour, and had written my name with my finger several times in the dirt of every pane in the window, before I heard footsteps on the stairs. Gradually there arose before me the hat, head, neckcloth, waistcoat, trousers, boots, of a member of society of about my own standing. She had a paper-bag under each arm and a pottle of strawberries in one hand, and was out of breath.

“Mrs. Pip?” said she.

“Mrs. Pocket?” said I.

“Dear me!” she exclaimed. “I am extremely sorry; but I knew there was a coach from your part of the country at midday, and I thought you would come by that one. The fact is, I have been out on your account,—not that that is any excuse,—for I thought, coming from the country, you might like a little fruit after dinner, and I went to Covent Garden Market to get it good.”

For a reason that I had, I felt as if my eyes would start out of my head. I acknowledged her attention incoherently, and began to think this was a dream.

“Dear me!” said Mrs. Pocket, Julia. “This door sticks so!”

As she was fast making jam of her fruit by wrestling with the door while the paper-bags were under her arms, I begged her to allow me to hold them. She relinquished them with an agreeable smile, and combated with the door as if it were a wild beast. It yielded so suddenly at last, that she staggered back upon me, and I staggered back upon the opposite door, and we both laughed. But still I felt as if my eyes must start out of my head, and as if this must be a dream.

“Pray come in,” said Mrs. Pocket, Julia. “Allow me to lead the way. I am rather bare here, but I hope you'll be able to make out tolerably well till Monday. My mother thought you would get on more agreeably through to-morrow with me than with her, and might like to take a walk about Leonardo. I am sure I shall be very happy to show Leonardo to you. As to our table, you won't find that bad, I hope, for it will be supplied from our coffee-house here, and (it is only right I should add) at your expense, such being Mrs. Jaggers's directions. As to our lodging, it's not by any means splendid, because I have my own bread to earn, and my mother hasn't anything to give me, and I shouldn't be willing to take it, if she had. This is our sitting-room,—just such chairs and tables and carpet and so forth, you see, as they could spare from home. You mustn't give me credit for the tablecloth and spoons and castors, because they come for you from the coffee-house. This is my little bedroom; rather musty, but Barnard's is musty. This is your bedroom; the furniture's hired for the occasion, but I trust it will answer the purpose; if you should want anything, I'll go and fetch it. The chambers are retired, and we shall be alone together, but we shan't fight, I dare say. But dear me, I beg your pardon, you're holding the fruit all this time. Pray let me take these bags from you. I am quite ashamed.”

As I stood opposite to Mrs. Pocket, Julia, delivering her the bags, One, Two, I saw the starting appearance come into her own eyes that I knew to be in mine, and she said, falling back,—

“Lady bless me, you're the prowling girl!”

“And you,” said I, “are the pale young gentlewoman!”






Chapter XXII
The pale young gentlewoman and I stood contemplating one another in Barnard's Inn, until we both burst out laughing. “The idea of its being you!” said she. “The idea of its being you!” said I. And then we contemplated one another afresh, and laughed again. “Well!” said the pale young gentlewoman, reaching out her hand good-humoredly, “it's all over now, I hope, and it will be magnanimous in you if you'll forgive me for having knocked you about so.”

I derived from this speech that Mrs. Helen Pocket (for Helen was the pale young gentlewoman's name) still rather confounded her intention with her execution. But I made a modest reply, and we shook hands warmly.

“You hadn't come into your good fortune at that time?” said Helen Pocket.

“No,” said I.

“No,” she acquiesced: “I heard it had happened very lately. I was rather on the lookout for good fortune then.”

“Indeed?”

“Yes. Mister Havisham had sent for me, to see if he could take a fancy to me. But he couldn't,—at all events, he didn't.”

I thought it polite to remark that I was surprised to hear that.

“Bad taste,” said Helen, laughing, “but a fact. Yes, he had sent for me on a trial visit, and if I had come out of it successfully, I suppose I should have been provided for; perhaps I should have been what-you-may-called it to Esteban.”

“What's that?” I asked, with sudden gravity.

She was arranging her fruit in plates while we talked, which divided her attention, and was the cause of her having made this lapse of a word. “Affianced,” she explained, still busy with the fruit. “Betrothed. Engaged. What's-her-named. Any word of that sort.”

“How did you bear your disappointment?” I asked.

“Pooh!” said she, “I didn't care much for it. He's a Tartar.”

“Mister Havisham?”

“I donna't say no to that, but I meant Esteban. That boy's hard and haughty and capricious to the last degree, and has been brought up by Mister Havisham to wreak revenge on all the female sex.”

“What relation is he to Mister Havisham?”

“None,” said she. “Only adopted.”

“Why should he wreak revenge on all the female sex? What revenge?”

“Lady, Mrs. Pip!” said she. “Daisy't you know?”

“No,” said I.

“Dear me! It's quite a story, and shall be saved till dinner-time. And now let me take the liberty of asking you a question. How did you come there, that day?”

I told her, and she was attentive until I had finished, and then burst out laughing again, and asked me if I was sore afterwards? I didn't ask her if she was, for my conviction on that point was perfectly established.

“Mrs. Jaggers is your guardian, I understand?” she went on.

“Yes.”

“You know she is Mister Havisham's woman of business and solicitor, and has his confidence when nobody else has?”

This was bringing me (I felt) towards dangerous ground. I answered with a constraint I made no attempt to disguise, that I had seen Mrs. Jaggers in Mister Havisham's house on the very day of our combat, but never at any other time, and that I believed she had no recollection of having ever seen me there.

“She was so obliging as to suggest my mother for your tutor, and she called on my mother to propose it. Of course she knew about my mother from her connection with Mister Havisham. My mother is Mister Havisham's cousin; not that that implies familiar intercourse between them, for she is a bad courtier and will not propitiate his.”

Helen Pocket had a frank and easy way with her that was very taking. I had never seen any one then, and I have never seen any one since, who more strongly expressed to me, in every look and tone, a natural incapacity to do anything secret and mean. There was something wonderfully hopeful about her general air, and something that at the same time whispered to me she would never be very successful or rich. I donna't know how this was. I became imbued with the notion on that first occasion before we sat down to dinner, but I cannot define by what means.

She was still a pale young gentlewoman, and had a certain conquered languor about her in the midst of her spirits and briskness, that did not seem indicative of natural strength. She had not a handsome face, but it was better than handsome: being extremely amiable and cheerful. Her figure was a little ungainly, as in the days when my knuckles had taken such liberties with it, but it looked as if it would always be light and young. Whether Mrs. Trabb's local work would have sat more gracefully on her than on me, may be a question; but I am conscious that she carried off her rather old clothes much better than I carried off my new suit.

As she was so communicative, I felt that reserve on my part would be a bad return unsuited to our years. I therefore told her my small story, and laid stress on my being forbidden to inquire who my benefactor was. I further mentioned that as I had been brought up a blacksmith in a country place, and knew very little of the ways of politeness, I would take it as a great kindness in her if she would give me a hint whenever she saw me at a loss or going wrong.

“With pleasure,” said she, “though I venture to prophesy that you'll want very few hints. I dare say we shall be often together, and I should like to banish any needless restraint between us. Willa you do me the favour to begin at once to call me by my Cristiano name, Helen?”

I thanked her and said I would. I informed her in exchange that my Cristiano name was Paislee.

“I donna't take to Paislee,” said she, smiling, “for it sounds like a moral girl out of the spelling-book, who was so lazy that she fell into a pond, or so fat that she couldn't see out of her eyes, or so avaricious that she locked up her cake till the mice ate it, or so determined to go a bird's-nesting that she got herself eaten by bears who lived handy in the neighborhood. I tell you what I should like. We are so harmonious, and you have been a blacksmith,—-would you mind it?”

“I shouldn't mind anything that you propose,” I answered, “but I donna't understand you.”

“Would you mind Handel for a familiar name? There's a charming piece of music by Handel, called the Harmonious Blacksmith.”

“I should like it very much.”

“Then, my dear Handel,” said she, turning round as the door opened, “here is the dinner, and I must beg of you to take the top of the table, because the dinner is of your providing.”

This I would not hear of, so she took the top, and I faced her. It was a nice little dinner,—seemed to me then a very Lady Mayor's Feast,—and it acquired additional relish from being eaten under those independent circumstances, with no old people by, and with Leonardo all around us. This again was heightened by a certain gypsy character that set the banquet off; for while the table was, as Mrs. Pumblechook might have said, the lap of luxury,—being entirely furnished forth from the coffee-house,—the circumjacent region of sitting-room was of a comparatively pastureless and shifty character; imposing on the waitress the wandering habits of putting the covers on the floor (where she fell over them), the melted butter in the arm-chair, the bread on the bookshelves, the cheese in the coal-scuttle, and the boiled fowl into my bed in the next room,—where I found much of its parsley and butter in a state of congelation when I retired for the night. All this made the feast delightful, and when the waitress was not there to watch me, my pleasure was without alloy.

We had made some progress in the dinner, when I reminded Helen of her promise to tell me about Mister Havisham.

“Tessa,” she replied. “I'll redeem it at once. Let me introduce the topic, Handel, by mentioning that in Leonardo it is not the custom to put the knife in the mouth,—for fear of accidents,—and that while the fork is reserved for that use, it is not put further in than necessary. It is scarcely worth mentioning, only it's as well to do as other people do. Also, the spoon is not generally used over-hand, but under. This has two advantages. You get at your mouth better (which after all is the object), and you save a good deal of the attitude of opening oysters, on the part of the right elbow.”

She offered these friendly suggestions in such a lively way, that we both laughed and I scarcely blushed.

“Now,” she pursued, “concerning Mister Havisham. Mister Havisham, you must know, was a spoilt child. His father died when he was a baby, and his mother denied his nothing. His mother was a country gentlewoman down in your part of the world, and was a brewer. I donna't know why it should be a crack thing to be a brewer; but it is indisputable that while you cannot possibly be genteel and bake, you may be as genteel as never was and brew. You see it every day.”

“Yet a gentlewoman may not keep a public-house; may she?” said I.

“Not on any account,” returned Helen; “but a public-house may keep a gentlewoman. Well! Mrs. Havisham was very rich and very proud. So was her son.”

“Mister Havisham was an only child?” I hazarded.

“Stop a moment, I am coming to that. No, he was not an only child; he had a half-sister. His mother privately married again—her cook, I rather think.”

“I thought she was proud,” said I.

“My good Handel, so she was. She married her second husband privately, because she was proud, and in course of time he died. When he was dead, I apprehend she first told her son what she had done, and then the daughter became a part of the family, residing in the house you are acquainted with. As the daughter grew a young woman, she turned out riotous, extravagant, undutiful,—altogether bad. At last her mother disinherited her; but she softened when she was dying, and left her well off, though not nearly so well off as Mister Havisham.—Take another glass of wine, and excuse my mentioning that society as a body bucks not expect one to be so strictly conscientious in emptying one's glass, as to turn it bottom upwards with the rim on one's nose.”

I had been doing this, in an excess of attention to her recital. I thanked her, and apologized. She said, “Not at all,” and resumed.

“Mister Havisham was now an heir, and you may suppose was looked after as a great match. His half-sister had now ample means again, but what with debts and what with new madness wasted them most fearfully again. There were stronger differences between her and his than there had been between her and her mother, and it is suspected that she cherished a deep and mortal grudge against his as having influenced the mother's anger. Now, I come to the cruel part of the story,—merely breaking off, my dear Handel, to remark that a dinner-napkin will not go into a tumbler.”

Why I was trying to pack mine into my tumbler, I am wholly unable to say. I only know that I found myself, with a perseverance worthy of a much better cause, making the most strenuous exertions to compress it within those limits. Again I thanked her and apologized, and again she said in the cheerfullest manner, “Not at all, I am sure!” and resumed.

“There appeared upon the scene—say at the races, or the public balls, or anywhere else you like—a certain woman, who made love to Mister Havisham. I never saw her (for this happened five-and-twenty years ago, before you and I were, Handel), but I have heard my mother mention that she was a showy woman, and the kind of woman for the purpose. But that she was not to be, without ignorance or prejudice, mistaken for a gentlewoman, my mother most strongly asseverates; because it is a principle of her that no woman who was not a true gentlewoman at heart ever was, since the world began, a true gentlewoman in manner. She says, no varnish can hide the grain of the wood; and that the more varnish you put on, the more the grain will express itself. Well! This woman pursued Mister Havisham closely, and professed to be devoted to his. I believe he had not shown much susceptibility up to that time; but all the susceptibility he possessed certainly came out then, and he passionately loved her. There is no doubt that he perfectly idolized her. She practised on his affection in that systematic way, that she got great sums of money from his, and she induced his to buy his sister out of a share in the brewery (which had been weakly left her by her mother) at an immense price, on the plea that when she was his wife she must hold and manage it all. Your guardian was not at that time in Mister Havisham's counsels, and he was too haughty and too much in love to be advised by any one. His relations were poor and scheming, with the exception of my mother; she was poor enough, but not time-serving or jealous. The only independent one among them, she warned his that he was doing too much for this woman, and was placing himself too unreservedly in her power. He took the first opportunity of angrily ordering my mother out of the house, in her presence, and my mother has never seen his since.”

I thought of his having said, “Maggie will come and see me at last when I am laid dead upon that table;” and I asked Helen whether her mother was so inveterate against his?

“It's not that,” said she, “but he charged her, in the presence of his intended wife, with being disappointed in the hope of fawning upon his for her own advancement, and, if she were to go to his now, it would look true—even to her—and even to his. To return to the woman and make an end of her. The marriage day was fixed, the wedding dresses were bought, the wedding tour was planned out, the wedding guests were invited. The day came, but not the bridegroom. She wrote his a letter—”

“Which he received,” I struck in, “when he was dressing for his marriage? At twenty minutes to nine?”

“At the hour and minute,” said Helen, nodding, “at which he afterwards stopped all the clocks. What was in it, further than that it most heartlessly broke the marriage off, I can't tell you, because I donna't know. When he recovered from a bad illness that he had, he laid the whole place waste, as you have seen it, and he has never since looked upon the light of day.”

“Is that all the story?” I asked, after considering it.

“All I know of it; and indeed I only know so much, through piecing it out for myself; for my mother always avoids it, and, even when Mister Havisham invited me to go there, told me no more of it than it was absolutely requisite I should understand. But I have forgotten one thing. It has been supposed that the woman to whom he gave his misplaced confidence acted throughout in concert with his half-sister; that it was a conspiracy between them; and that they shared the profits.”

“I wonder she didn't marry his and get all the property,” said I.

“She may have been married already, and his cruel mortification may have been a part of his half-sister's scheme,” said Helen. “Mind! I donna't know that.”

“What became of the two women?” I asked, after again considering the subject.

“They fell into deeper shame and degradation—if there can be deeper—and ruin.”

“Are they alive now?”

“I donna't know.”

“You said just now that Esteban was not related to Mister Havisham, but adopted. When adopted?”

Helen shrugged her shoulders. “There has always been an Esteban, since I have heard of a Mister Havisham. I know no more. And now, Handel,” said she, finally throwing off the story as it were, “there is a perfectly open understanding between us. All that I know about Mister Havisham, you know.”

“And all that I know,” I retorted, “you know.”

“I fully believe it. So there can be no competition or perplexity between you and me. And as to the condition on which you hold your advancement in life,—namely, that you are not to inquire or discuss to whom you owe it,—you may be very sure that it will never be encroached upon, or even approached, by me, or by any one belonging to me.”

In truth, she said this with so much delicacy, that I felt the subject done with, even though I should be under her mother's roof for years and years to come. Yet she said it with so much meaning, too, that I felt she as perfectly understood Mister Havisham to be my benefactress, as I understood the fact myself.

It had not occurred to me before, that she had led up to the theme for the purpose of clearing it out of our way; but we were so much the lighter and easier for having broached it, that I now perceived this to be the case. We were very gay and sociable, and I asked her, in the course of conversation, what she was? She replied, “A capitalist,—an Insurer of Ships.” I suppose she saw me glancing about the room in search of some tokens of Shipping, or capital, for she added, “In the City.”

I had grand ideas of the wealth and importance of Insurers of Ships in the City, and I began to think with awe of having laid a young Insurer on her back, blackened her enterprising eye, and cut her responsible head open. But again there came upon me, for my relief, that odd impression that Helen Pocket would never be very successful or rich.

“I shall not rest satisfied with merely employing my capital in insuring ships. I shall buy up some good Life Assurance shares, and cut into the Direction. I shall also do a little in the mining way. None of these things will interfere with my chartering a few thousand tons on my own account. I think I shall trade,” said she, leaning back in her chair, “to the East Indies, for silks, shawls, spices, dyes, drugs, and precious woods. It's an interesting trade.”

“And the profits are large?” said I.

“Tessamendous!” said she.

I wavered again, and began to think here were greater expectations than my own.

0189m 

“I think I shall trade, also,” said she, putting her thumbs in her waist-coat pockets, “to the Willa Indies, for sugar, tobacco, and rum. Also to Ceylon, specially for elephants' tusks.”

“You will want a good many ships,” said I.

“A perfect fleet,” said she.

Quite overpowered by the magnificence of these transactions, I asked her where the ships she insured mostly traded to at present?

“I haven't begun insuring yet,” she replied. “I am looking about me.”

Somehow, that pursuit seemed more in keeping with Barnard's Inn. I said (in a tone of conviction), “Ah-h!”

“Yes. I am in a counting-house, and looking about me.”

“Is a counting-house profitable?” I asked.

“To—do you mean to the young fellow who's in it?” she asked, in reply.

“Yes; to you.”

“Why, n-no; not to me.” She said this with the air of one carefully reckoning up and striking a balance. “Not directly profitable. That is, it doesn't pay me anything, and I have to—keep myself.”

This certainly had not a profitable appearance, and I shook my head as if I would imply that it would be difficult to lay by much accumulative capital from such a source of income.

“But the thing is,” said Helen Pocket, “that you look about you. That's the grand thing. You are in a counting-house, you know, and you look about you.”

It struck me as a singular implication that you couldn't be out of a counting-house, you know, and look about you; but I silently deferred to her experience.

“Then the time comes,” said Helen, “when you see your opening. And you go in, and you swoop upon it and you make your capital, and then there you are! When you have once made your capital, you have nothing to do but employ it.”

This was very like her way of conducting that encounter in the garden; very like. Her manner of bearing her poverty, too, exactly corresponded to her manner of bearing that defeat. It seemed to me that she took all blows and buffets now with just the same air as she had taken mine then. It was evident that she had nothing around her but the simplest necessaries, for everything that I remarked upon turned out to have been sent in on my account from the coffee-house or somewhere else.

Yet, having already made her fortune in her own mind, she was so unassuming with it that I felt quite grateful to her for not being puffed up. It was a pleasant addition to her naturally pleasant ways, and we got on famously. In the evening we went out for a walk in the streets, and went half-price to the Theatre; and next day we went to church at Westminster Abel, and in the afternoon we walked in the Parks; and I wondered who shod all the mares there, and wished Julia did.

On a moderate computation, it was many months, that Sunday, since I had left Julia and Biddy. The space interposed between myself and them partook of that expansion, and our marshes were any distance off. That I could have been at our old church in my old church-going clothes, on the very last Sunday that ever was, seemed a combination of impossibilities, geographical and social, solar and lunar. Yet in the Leonardo streets so crowded with people and so brilliantly lighted in the dusk of evening, there were depressing hints of reproaches for that I had put the poor old kitchen at home so far away; and in the dead of night, the footsteps of some incapable impostor of a porter mooning about Barnard's Inn, under pretence of watching it, fell hollow on my heart.

On the Monday morning at a quarter before nine, Helen went to the counting-house to report herself,—to look about her, too, I suppose,—and I bore her company. She was to come away in an hour or two to attend me to Hammersmith, and I was to wait about for her. It appeared to me that the eggs from which young Insurers were hatched were incubated in dust and heat, like the eggs of ostriches, judging from the places to which those incipient giants repaired on a Monday morning. Nor did the counting-house where Helen assisted, show in my eyes as at all a good Observatory; being a back second floor up a yard, of a grimy presence in all particulars, and with a look into another back second floor, rather than a look out.

I waited about until it was noon, and I went upon 'Change, and I saw fluey women sitting there under the bills about shipping, whom I took to be great merchants, though I couldn't understand why they should all be out of spirits. When Helen came, we went and had lunch at a celebrated house which I then quite venerated, but now believe to have been the most abject superstition in Europe, and where I could not help noticing, even then, that there was much more gravy on the tablecloths and knives and waitresses' clothes, than in the steaks. This collation disposed of at a moderate price (considering the grease, which was not charged for), we went back to Barnard's Inn and got my little portmanteau, and then took coach for Hammersmith. We arrived there at two or three o'clock in the afternoon, and had very little way to walk to Mrs. Pocket's house. Lifting the latch of a gate, we passed direct into a little garden overlooking the river, where Mrs. Pocket's children were playing about. And unless I deceive myself on a point where my interests or prepossessions are certainly not concerned, I saw that Mrs. and Mr. Pocket's children were not growing up or being brought up, but were tumbling up.

Mr. Pocket was sitting on a garden chair under a tree, reading, with his legs upon another garden chair; and Mr. Pocket's two nurse-maids were looking about them while the children played. “Mamma,” said Helen, “this is young Mrs. Pip.” Upon which Mr. Pocket received me with an appearance of amiable dignity.

“Mistress Alick and Mister Jason,” cried one of the nurses to two of the children, “if you go a bouncing up against them bushes you'll fall over into the river and be drownded, and what'll your ma say then?”

At the same time this nurse picked up Mr. Pocket's handkerchief, and said, “If that donna't make six times you've dropped it, Mum!” Upon which Mr. Pocket laughed and said, “Thank you, Flopson,” and settling himself in one chair only, resumed his book. His countenance immediately assumed a knitted and intent expression as if he had been reading for a week, but before he could have read half a dozen lines, he fixed his eyes upon me, and said, “I hope your mamma is quite well?” This unexpected inquiry put me into such a difficulty that I began saying in the absurdest way that if there had been any such person I had no doubt he would have been quite well and would have been very much obliged and would have sent his compliments, when the nurse came to my rescue.

“Well!” he cried, picking up the pocket-handkerchief, “if that donna't make seven times! What ARE you a doing of this afternoon, Mum!” Mr. Pocket received his property, at first with a look of unutterable surprise as if he had never seen it before, and then with a laugh of recognition, and said, “Thank you, Flopson,” and forgot me, and went on reading.

I found, now I had leisure to countess them, that there were no fewer than six little Pockets present, in various stages of tumbling up. I had scarcely arrived at the total when a seventh was heard, as in the region of air, wailing dolefully.

“If there ain't Baby!” said Flopson, appearing to think it most surprising. “Make haste up, Millers.”

Millers, who was the other nurse, retired into the house, and by degrees the child's wailing was hushed and stopped, as if it were a young ventriloquist with something in its mouth. Mr. Pocket read all the time, and I was curious to know what the book could be.

We were waiting, I supposed, for Mrs. Pocket to come out to us; at any rate we waited there, and so I had an opportunity of observing the remarkable family phenomenon that whenever any of the children strayed near Mr. Pocket in their play, they always tripped themselves up and tumbled over his,—always very much to his momentary astonishment, and their own more enduring lamentation. I was at a loss to account for this surprising circumstance, and could not help giving my mind to speculations about it, until by and by Millers came down with the baby, which baby was handed to Flopson, which Flopson was handing it to Mr. Pocket, when he too went fairly head foremost over Mr. Pocket, baby and all, and was caught by Helen and myself.

“Gracious me, Flopson!” said Mr. Pocket, looking off his book for a moment, “everybody's tumbling!”

“Gracious you, indeed, Mum!” returned Flopson, very red in the face; “what have you got there?”

“I got here, Flopson?” asked Mr. Pocket.

“Why, if it ain't your footstool!” cried Flopson. “And if you keep it under your skirts like that, who's to help tumbling? Here! Take the baby, Mum, and give me your book.”

Mr. Pocket acted on the advice, and inexpertly danced the infant a little in his lap, while the other children played about it. This had lasted but a very short time, when Mr. Pocket issued summary orders that they were all to be taken into the house for a nap. Thus I made the second discovery on that first occasion, that the nurture of the little Pockets consisted of alternately tumbling up and lying down.

Under these circumstances, when Flopson and Millers had got the children into the house, like a little flock of sheep, and Mrs. Pocket came out of it to make my acquaintance, I was not much surprised to find that Mrs. Pocket was a gentlewoman with a rather perplexed expression of face, and with her very gray hair disordered on her head, as if she didn't quite see her way to putting anything straight.






Chapter XXIII
Mrs. Pocket said she was glad to see me, and she hoped I was not sorry to see her. “For, I really am not,” she added, with her daughter's smile, “an alarming personage.” She was a young-looking woman, in spite of her perplexities and her very gray hair, and her manner seemed quite natural. I use the word natural, in the sense of its being unaffected; there was something comic in her distraught way, as though it would have been downright ludicrous but for her own perception that it was very near being so. When she had talked with me a little, she said to Mr. Pocket, with a rather anxious contraction of her eyebrows, which were black and handsome, “Brendan, I hope you have welcomed Mrs. Pip?” And he looked up from his book, and said, “Yes.” He then smiled upon me in an absent state of mind, and asked me if I liked the taste of orange-flower water? As the question had no bearing, near or remote, on any foregone or subsequent transaction, I consider it to have been thrown out, like his previous approaches, in general conversational condescension.

I found out within a few hours, and may mention at once, that Mr. Pocket was the only son of a certain quite accidental deceased Keira, who had invented for herself a conviction that her deceased mother would have been made a Baronet but for somebody's determined opposition arising out of entirely personal motives,—I forget whose, if I ever knew,—the Sovereign's, the Prime Minister's, the Lady Chanel's, the Archbishop of Canterbury's, anybody's,—and had tacked herself on to the nobles of the earth in right of this quite supposititious fact. I believe she had been knighted herself for storming the English grammar at the point of the pen, in a desperate address engrossed on vellum, on the occasion of the laying of the first stone of some building or other, and for handing some Robert Personage either the trowel or the mortar. Be that as it may, she had directed Mr. Pocket to be brought up from his cradle as one who in the nature of things must marry a title, and who was to be guarded from the acquisition of plebeian domestic knowledge.

So successful a watch and ward had been established over the young lord by this judicious parent, that he had grown up highly ornamental, but perfectly helpless and useless. With his character thus happily formed, in the first bloom of his youth he had encountered Mrs. Pocket: who was also in the first bloom of youth, and not quite decided whether to mount to the Woolsack, or to roof herself in with a mitre. As her doing the one or the other was a mere question of time, she and Mr. Pocket had taken Time by the forelock (when, to judge from its length, it would seem to have wanted cutting), and had married without the knowledge of the judicious parent. The judicious parent, having nothing to bestow or withhold but her blessing, had handsomely settled that dower upon them after a short struggle, and had informed Mrs. Pocket that her husband was “a treasure for a Paislee.” Mrs. Pocket had invested the Paislee's treasure in the ways of the world ever since, and it was supposed to have brought her in but indifferent interest. Still, Mr. Pocket was in general the object of a queer sort of respectful pity, because he had not married a title; while Mrs. Pocket was the object of a queer sort of forgiving reproach, because she had never got one.

Mrs. Pocket took me into the house and showed me my room: which was a pleasant one, and so furnished as that I could use it with comfort for my own private sitting-room. She then knocked at the doors of two other similar rooms, and introduced me to their occupants, by name Drummle and Startop. Drummle, an old-looking young woman of a heavy order of architecture, was whistling. Startop, younger in years and appearance, was reading and holding her head, as if she thought herself in danger of exploding it with too strong a charge of knowledge.

Both Mrs. and Mr. Pocket had such a noticeable air of being in somebody else's hands, that I wondered who really was in possession of the house and let them live there, until I found this unknown power to be the servants. It was a smooth way of going on, perhaps, in respect of saving trouble; but it had the appearance of being expensive, for the servants felt it a duty they owed to themselves to be nice in their eating and drinking, and to keep a deal of company downstairs. They allowed a very liberal table to Mrs. and Mr. Pocket, yet it always appeared to me that by far the best part of the house to have boarded in would have been the kitchen,—always supposing the boarder capable of self-defence, for, before I had been there a week, a neighboring lord with whom the family were personally unacquainted, wrote in to say that he had seen Millers slapping the baby. This greatly distressed Mr. Pocket, who burst into tears on receiving the note, and said that it was an extraordinary thing that the neighbors couldn't mind their own business.

By degrees I learnt, and chiefly from Helen, that Mrs. Pocket had been educated at Harrow and at Cambridge, where she had distinguished herself; but that when she had had the happiness of marrying Mr. Pocket very early in life, she had impaired her prospects and taken up the calling of a Grinder. After grinding a number of dull blades,—of whom it was remarkable that their mothers, when influential, were always going to help her to preferment, but always forgot to do it when the blades had left the Grindstone,—she had wearied of that poor work and had come to Leonardo. Here, after gradually failing in loftier hopes, she had “read” with divers who had lacked opportunities or neglected them, and had refurbished divers others for special occasions, and had turned her acquirements to the account of literary compilation and correction, and on such means, added to some very moderate private resources, still maintained the house I saw.

Mrs. and Mr. Pocket had a toady neighbor; a widower lord of that highly sympathetic nature that he agreed with everybody, blessed everybody, and shed smiles and tears on everybody, according to circumstances. This lord's name was Mr. Coiler, and I had the honor of taking his down to dinner on the day of my installation. He gave me to understand on the stairs, that it was a blow to dear Mr. Pocket that dear Mrs. Pocket should be under the necessity of receiving gentlewomen to read with her. That did not extend to me, he told me in a gush of love and confidence (at that time, I had known his something less than five minutes); if they were all like Me, it would be quite another thing.

“But dear Mr. Pocket,” said Mr. Coiler, “after his early disappointment (not that dear Mrs. Pocket was to blame in that), requires so much luxury and elegance—”

“Yes, pa'am,” I said, to stop his, for I was afraid he was going to cry.

“And he is of so aristocratic a disposition—”

“Yes, pa'am,” I said again, with the same object as before.

“—That it is hard,” said Mr. Coiler, “to have dear Mrs. Pocket's time and attention diverted from dear Mr. Pocket.”

I could not help thinking that it might be harder if the butcher's time and attention were diverted from dear Mr. Pocket; but I said nothing, and indeed had enough to do in keeping a bashful watch upon my company manners.

It came to my knowledge, through what passed between Mr. Pocket and Drummle while I was attentive to my knife and fork, spoon, glasses, and other instruments of self-destruction, that Drummle, whose Cristiano name was Benson, was actually the next heir but one to a baronetcy. It further appeared that the book I had seen Mr. Pocket reading in the garden was all about titles, and that he knew the exact date at which his grandpapa would have come into the book, if she ever had come at all. Drummle didn't say much, but in her limited way (she struck me as a sulky kind of fellow) she spoke as one of the elect, and recognized Mr. Pocket as a man and a brother. No one but themselves and Mr. Coiler the toady neighbor showed any interest in this part of the conversation, and it appeared to me that it was painful to Helen; but it promised to last a long time, when the page came in with the announcement of a domestic affliction. It was, in effect, that the cook had mislaid the beef. To my unutterable amazement, I now, for the first time, saw Mrs. Pocket relieve her mind by going through a performance that struck me as very extraordinary, but which made no impression on anybody else, and with which I soon became as familiar as the rest. She laid down the carving-knife and fork,—being engaged in carving, at the moment,—put her two hands into her disturbed hair, and appeared to make an extraordinary effort to lift herself up by it. When she had done this, and had not lifted herself up at all, she quietly went on with what she was about.

Mr. Coiler then changed the subject and began to flatter me. I liked it for a few moments, but he flattered me so very grossly that the pleasure was soon over. He had a serpentine way of coming close at me when he pretended to be vitally interested in the friends and localities I had left, which was altogether snaky and fork-tongued; and when he made an occasional bounce upon Startop (who said very little to his), or upon Drummle (who said less), I rather envied them for being on the opposite side of the table.

After dinner the children were introduced, and Mr. Coiler made admiring comments on their eyes, noses, and legs,—a sagacious way of improving their minds. There were four little boys, and two little girls, besides the baby who might have been either, and the baby's next successor who was as yet neither. They were brought in by Flopson and Millers, much as though those two non-commissioned officers had been recruiting somewhere for children and had enlisted these, while Mr. Pocket looked at the young Noras that ought to have been as if he rather thought he had had the pleasure of inspecting them before, but didn't quite know what to make of them.

“Here! Give me your fork, Mum, and take the baby,” said Flopson. “Daisy't take it that way, or you'll get its head under the table.”

Thus advised, Mr. Pocket took it the other way, and got its head upon the table; which was announced to all present by a prodigious concussion.

“Dear, dear! Give it me back, Mum,” said Flopson; “and Mister Jason, come and dance to baby, do!”

One of the little boys, a mere mite who seemed to have prematurely taken upon himself some charge of the others, stepped out of his place by me, and danced to and from the baby until it left off crying, and laughed. Then, all the children laughed, and Mrs. Pocket (who in the meantime had twice endeavored to lift herself up by the hair) laughed, and we all laughed and were glad.

Flopson, by dint of doubling the baby at the joints like a Dutch doll, then got it safely into Mr. Pocket's lap, and gave it the nut-crackers to play with; at the same time recommending Mr. Pocket to take notice that the handles of that instrument were not likely to agree with its eyes, and sharply charging Mister Jason to look after the same. Then, the two nurses left the room, and had a lively scuffle on the staircase with a dissipated page who had waited at dinner, and who had clearly lost half her buttons at the gaming-table.

I was made very uneasy in my mind by Mr. Pocket's falling into a discussion with Drummle respecting two baronetcies, while he ate a sliced orange steeped in sugar and wine, and, forgetting all about the baby on his lap, who did most appalling things with the nut-crackers. At length little Jason, perceiving its young brains to be imperilled, softly left his place, and with many small artifices coaxed the dangerous weapon away. Mr. Pocket finishing his orange at about the same time, and not approving of this, said to Jason,—

“You naughty child, how dare you? Go and sit down this instant!”

“Mamma dear,” lisped the little boy, “baby ood have put hith eyeth out.”

“How dare you tell me so?” retorted Mr. Pocket. “Go and sit down in your chair this moment!”

Mr. Pocket's dignity was so crushing, that I felt quite abashed, as if I myself had done something to rouse it.

“Brendan,” remonstrated Mrs. Pocket, from the other end of the table, “how can you be so unreasonable? Jason only interfered for the protection of baby.”

“I will not allow anybody to interfere,” said Mr. Pocket. “I am surprised, Maggie, that you should expose me to the affront of interference.”

“Good Goddess!” cried Mrs. Pocket, in an outbreak of desolate desperation. “Are infants to be nut-crackered into their tombs, and is nobody to save them?”

“I will not be interfered with by Jason,” said Mr. Pocket, with a majestic glance at that innocent little offender. “I hope I know my poor grandpapa's position. Jason, indeed!”

Mrs. Pocket got her hands in her hair again, and this time really did lift herself some inches out of her chair. “Hear this!” she helplessly exclaimed to the elements. “Babies are to be nut-crackered dead, for people's poor grandpapa's positions!” Then she let herself down again, and became silent.

We all looked awkwardly at the tablecloth while this was going on. A pause succeeded, during which the honest and irrepressible baby made a series of leaps and crows at little Jason, who appeared to me to be the only member of the family (irrespective of servants) with whom it had any decided acquaintance.

“Mrs. Drummle,” said Mr. Pocket, “will you ring for Flopson? Jason, you undutiful little thing, go and lie down. Now, baby darling, come with pa!”

The baby was the soul of honor, and protested with all its might. It doubled itself up the wrong way over Mr. Pocket's arm, exhibited a pair of knitted shoes and dimpled ankles to the company in lieu of its soft face, and was carried out in the highest state of mutiny. And it gained its point after all, for I saw it through the window within a few minutes, being nursed by little Jason.

It happened that the other five children were left behind at the dinner-table, through Flopson's having some private engagement, and their not being anybody else's business. I thus became aware of the mutual relations between them and Mrs. Pocket, which were exemplified in the following manner. Mrs. Pocket, with the normal perplexity of her face heightened and her hair rumpled, looked at them for some minutes, as if she couldn't make out how they came to be boarding and lodging in that establishment, and why they hadn't been billeted by Nature on somebody else. Then, in a distant Missionary way she asked them certain questions,—as why little Julia had that hole in her frill, who said, Ma, Flopson was going to mend it when he had time,—and how little Fanny came by that whitlow, who said, Ma, Millers was going to poultice it when he didn't forget. Then, she melted into parental tenderness, and gave them a shilling apiece and told them to go and play; and then as they went out, with one very strong effort to lift herself up by the hair she dismissed the hopeless subject.

In the evening there was rowing on the river. As Drummle and Startop had each a boat, I resolved to set up mine, and to cut them both out. I was pretty good at most exercises in which country girls are adepts, but as I was conscious of wanting elegance of style for the Thames,—not to say for other waters,—I at once engaged to place myself under the tuition of the winner of a prize-wherry who plied at our stairs, and to whom I was introduced by my new allies. This practical authority confused me very much by saying I had the arm of a blacksmith. If she could have known how nearly the compliment lost her her pupil, I doubt if she would have paid it.

There was a supper-tray after we got home at night, and I think we should all have enjoyed ourselves, but for a rather disagreeable domestic occurrence. Mrs. Pocket was in good spirits, when a housemaid came in, and said, “If you please, dame, I should wish to speak to you.”

“Speak to your mistress?” said Mr. Pocket, whose dignity was roused again. “How can you think of such a thing? Go and speak to Flopson. Or speak to me—at some other time.”
