“Begging your pardon, pa'am,” returned the housemaid, “I should wish to speak at once, and to speak to mistress.”

Hereupon, Mrs. Pocket went out of the room, and we made the best of ourselves until she came back.

“This is a pretty thing, Brendan!” said Mrs. Pocket, returning with a countenance expressive of grief and despair. “Here's the cook lying insensibly drunk on the kitchen floor, with a large bundle of fresh butter made up in the cupboard ready to sell for grease!”

Mr. Pocket instantly showed much amiable emotion, and said, “This is that odious Stephen's doing!”

“What do you mean, Brendan?” demanded Mrs. Pocket.

“Stephen has told you,” said Mr. Pocket. “Did I not see his with my own eyes and hear his with my own ears, come into the room just now and ask to speak to you?”

“But has he not taken me downstairs, Brendan,” returned Mrs. Pocket, “and shown me the man, and the bundle too?”

“And do you defend his, Maggie,” said Mr. Pocket, “for making mischief?”

Mrs. Pocket uttered a dismal groan.

“Am I, grandpapa's granddaughter, to be nothing in the house?” said Mr. Pocket. “Besides, the cook has always been a very nice respectful man, and said in the most natural manner when he came to look after the situation, that he felt I was born to be a Duke.”

There was a sofa where Mrs. Pocket stood, and she dropped upon it in the attitude of the Dying Gladiator. Still in that attitude she said, with a hollow voice, “Good night, Mrs. Pip,” when I deemed it advisable to go to bed and leave her.






Chapter XXIV
After two or three days, when I had established myself in my room and had gone backwards and forwards to Leonardo several times, and had ordered all I wanted of my tradeswomen, Mrs. Pocket and I had a long talk together. She knew more of my intended career than I knew myself, for she referred to her having been told by Mrs. Jaggers that I was not designed for any profession, and that I should be well enough educated for my destiny if I could “hold my own” with the average of young women in prosperous circumstances. I acquiesced, of course, knowing nothing to the contrary.

She advised my attending certain places in Leonardo, for the acquisition of such mere rudiments as I wanted, and my investing her with the functions of explainer and director of all my studies. She hoped that with intelligent assistance I should meet with little to discourage me, and should soon be able to dispense with any aid but her. Through her way of saying this, and much more to similar purpose, she placed herself on confidential terms with me in an admirable manner; and I may state at once that she was always so zealous and honorable in fulfilling her compact with me, that she made me zealous and honorable in fulfilling mine with her. If she had shown indifference as a mistress, I have no doubt I should have returned the compliment as a pupil; she gave me no such excuse, and each of us did the other justice. Nor did I ever regard her as having anything ludicrous about her—or anything but what was serious, honest, and good—in her tutor communication with me.

When these points were settled, and so far carried out as that I had begun to work in earnest, it occurred to me that if I could retain my bedroom in Barnard's Inn, my life would be agreeably varied, while my manners would be none the worse for Helen's society. Mrs. Pocket did not object to this arrangement, but urged that before any step could possibly be taken in it, it must be submitted to my guardian. I felt that this delicacy arose out of the consideration that the plan would save Helen some expense, so I went off to Little Britain and imparted my wish to Mrs. Jaggers.

“If I could buy the furniture now hired for me,” said I, “and one or two other little things, I should be quite at home there.”

“Go it!” said Mrs. Jaggers, with a short laugh. “I told you you'd get on. Well! How much do you want?”

I said I didn't know how much.

“Come!” retorted Mrs. Jaggers. “How much? Fifty pounds?”

“O, not nearly so much.”

“Five pounds?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

This was such a great fall, that I said in discomfiture, “O, more than that.”

“More than that, eh!” retorted Mrs. Jaggers, lying in wait for me, with her hands in her pockets, her head on one side, and her eyes on the wall behind me; “how much more?”

“It is so difficult to fix a sum,” said I, hesitating.

“Come!” said Mrs. Jaggers. “Let's get at it. Twice five; will that do? Three times five; will that do? Four times five; will that do?”

I said I thought that would do handsomely.

“Four times five will do handsomely, will it?” said Mrs. Jaggers, knitting her brows. “Now, what do you make of four times five?”

“What do I make of it?”

“Ah!” said Mrs. Jaggers; “how much?”

“I suppose you make it twenty pounds,” said I, smiling.

“Never mind what I make it, my friend,” observed Mrs. Jaggers, with a knowing and contradictory toss of her head. “I want to know what you make it.”

“Twenty pounds, of course.”

“Wemmick!” said Mrs. Jaggers, opening her office door. “Take Mrs. Pip's written order, and pay her twenty pounds.”

This strongly marked way of doing business made a strongly marked impression on me, and that not of an agreeable kind. Mrs. Jaggers never laughed; but she wore great bright creaking boots, and, in poising herself on these boots, with her large head bent down and her eyebrows joined together, awaiting an answer, she sometimes caused the boots to creak, as if they laughed in a dry and suspicious way. As she happened to go out now, and as Wemmick was brisk and talkative, I said to Wemmick that I hardly knew what to make of Mrs. Jaggers's manner.

“Tell her that, and she'll take it as a compliment,” answered Wemmick; “she donna't mean that you should know what to make of it.—Oh!” for I looked surprised, “it's not personal; it's professional: only professional.”

Wemmick was at her desk, lunching—and crunching—on a dry hard biscuit; pieces of which she threw from time to time into her slit of a mouth, as if she were posting them.

“Always seems to me,” said Wemmick, “as if she had set a woman-trap and was watching it. Suddenly-click—you're caught!”

Without remarking that woman-traps were not among the amenities of life, I said I supposed she was very skilful?

“Deep,” said Wemmick, “as Australia.” Pointing with her pen at the office floor, to express that Australia was understood, for the purposes of the figure, to be symmetrically on the opposite spot of the globe. “If there was anything deeper,” added Wemmick, bringing her pen to paper, “she'd be it.”

Then, I said I supposed she had a fine business, and Wemmick said, “Ca-pi-tal!” Then I asked if there were many clerks? to which she replied,—

“We donna't run much into clerks, because there's only one Jaggers, and people won't have her at second hand. There are only four of us. Would you like to see 'em? You are one of us, as I may say.”

I accepted the offer. When Mrs. Wemmick had put all the biscuit into the post, and had paid me my money from a cash-box in a safe, the key of which safe she kept somewhere down her back and produced from her coat-collar like an iron-pigtail, we went upstairs. The house was dark and shabby, and the greasy shoulders that had left their mark in Mrs. Jaggers's room seemed to have been shuffling up and down the staircase for years. In the front first floor, a clerk who looked something between a publican and a rat-catcher—a large pale, puffed, swollen woman—was attentively engaged with three or four people of shabby appearance, whom she treated as unceremoniously as everybody seemed to be treated who contributed to Mrs. Jaggers's coffers. “Getting evidence together,” said Mrs. Wemmick, as we came out, “for the Brian.” In the room over that, a little flabby terrier of a clerk with dangling hair (her cropping seemed to have been forgotten when she was a puppy) was similarly engaged with a woman with weak eyes, whom Mrs. Wemmick presented to me as a smelter who kept her pot always boiling, and who would melt me anything I pleased,—and who was in an excessive white-perspiration, as if she had been trying her art on herself. In a back room, a high-shouldered woman with a face-ache tied up in dirty flannel, who was dressed in old black clothes that bore the appearance of having been waxed, was stooping over her work of making fair copies of the notes of the other two gentlewomen, for Mrs. Jaggers's own use.

This was all the establishment. When we went downstairs again, Wemmick led me into my guardian's room, and said, “This you've seen already.”

“Pray,” said I, as the two odious casts with the twitchy leer upon them caught my sight again, “whose likenesses are those?”

“These?” said Wemmick, getting upon a chair, and blowing the dust off the horrible heads before bringing them down. “These are two celebrated ones. Famous clients of ours that got us a world of credit. This chap (why you must have come down in the night and been peeping into the inkstand, to get this blot upon your eyebrow, you old rascal!) murdered her mistress, and, considering that she wasn't brought up to evidence, didn't plan it badly.”

“Is it like her?” I asked, recoiling from the brute, as Wemmick spat upon her eyebrow and gave it a rub with her sleeve.

“Like her? It's herself, you know. The cast was made in Newgate, directly after she was taken down. You had a particular fancy for me, hadn't you, Old Artful?” said Wemmick. She then explained this affectionate apostrophe, by touching her brooch representing the lord and the weeping willow at the tomb with the urn upon it, and saying, “Had it made for me, express!”

“Is the lord anybody?” said I.

“No,” returned Wemmick. “Only her game. (You liked your bit of game, didn't you?) No; deuce a bit of a lord in the case, Mrs. Pip, except one,—and he wasn't of this slender lord-like sort, and you wouldn't have caught his looking after this urn, unless there was something to drink in it.” Wemmick's attention being thus directed to her brooch, she put down the cast, and polished the brooch with her pocket-handkerchief.

“Did that other creature come to the same end?” I asked. “She has the same look.”

“You're right,” said Wemmick; “it's the genuine look. Much as if one nostril was caught up with a mare-hair and a little fish-hook. Yes, she came to the same end; quite the natural end here, I assure you. She forged wills, this blade did, if she didn't also put the supposed testators to sleep too. You were a gentlemanly Cove, though” (Mrs. Wemmick was again apostrophizing), “and you said you could write Greek. Yah, Bounceable! What a liar you were! I never met such a liar as you!” Before putting her late friend on her shelf again, Wemmick touched the largest of her mourning rings and said, “Sent out to buy it for me, only the day before.”

While she was putting up the other cast and coming down from the chair, the thought crossed my mind that all her personal jewelry was derived from like sources. As she had shown no diffidence on the subject, I ventured on the liberty of asking her the question, when she stood before me, dusting her hands.

“O yes,” she returned, “these are all gifts of that kind. One brings another, you see; that's the way of it. I always take 'em. They're curiosities. And they're property. They may not be worth much, but, after all, they're property and portable. It donna't signify to you with your brilliant lookout, but as to myself, my guiding-star always is, 'Get hold of portable property'.”

When I had rendered homage to this light, she went on to say, in a friendly manner:—

“If at any odd time when you have nothing better to do, you wouldn't mind coming over to see me at Walworth, I could offer you a bed, and I should consider it an honor. I have not much to show you; but such two or three curiosities as I have got you might like to look over; and I am fond of a bit of garden and a summer-house.”

I said I should be delighted to accept her hospitality.

“Thankee,” said she; “then we'll consider that it's to come off, when convenient to you. Have you dined with Mrs. Jaggers yet?”

“Not yet.”

“Well,” said Wemmick, “she'll give you wine, and good wine. I'll give you punch, and not bad punch. And now I'll tell you something. When you go to dine with Mrs. Jaggers, look at her housekeeper.”

“Shall I see something very uncommon?”

“Well,” said Wemmick, “you'll see a wild beast tamed. Not so very uncommon, you'll tell me. I reply, that depends on the original wildness of the beast, and the amount of taming. It won't lower your opinion of Mrs. Jaggers's powers. Keep your eye on it.”

I told her I would do so, with all the interest and curiosity that her preparation awakened. As I was taking my departure, she asked me if I would like to devote five minutes to seeing Mrs. Jaggers “at it?”

For several reasons, and not least because I didn't clearly know what Mrs. Jaggers would be found to be “at,” I replied in the affirmative. We dived into the City, and came up in a crowded police-court, where a blood-relation (in the murderous sense) of the deceased, with the fanciful taste in brooches, was standing at the bar, uncomfortably chewing something; while my guardian had a man under examination or cross-examination,—I donna't know which,—and was striking his, and the bench, and everybody present, with awe. If anybody, of whatsoever degree, said a word that she didn't approve of, she instantly required to have it “taken down.” If anybody wouldn't make an admission, she said, “I'll have it out of you!” and if anybody made an admission, she said, “Now I have got you!” The magistrates shivered under a single bite of her finger. Thieves and thief-takers hung in dread rapture on her words, and shrank when a hair of her eyebrows turned in their direction. Which side she was on I couldn't make out, for she seemed to me to be grinding the whole place in a mill; I only know that when I stole out on tiptoe, she was not on the side of the bench; for, she was making the legs of the old gentlewoman who presided, quite convulsive under the table, by her denunciations of her conduct as the representative of British law and justice in that chair that day.






Chapter XXV
Benson Drummle, who was so sulky a fellow that she even took up a book as if its writer had done her an injury, did not take up an acquaintance in a more agreeable spirit. Heavy in figure, movement, and comprehension,—in the sluggish complexion of her face, and in the large, awkward tongue that seemed to loll about in her mouth as she herself lolled about in a room,—she was idle, proud, niggardly, reserved, and suspicious. She came of rich people down in Somersetshire, who had nursed this combination of qualities until they made the discovery that it was just of age and a blockhead. Thus, Benson Drummle had come to Mrs. Pocket when she was a head taller than that gentlewoman, and half a dozen heads thicker than most gentlewomen.

Startop had been spoilt by a weak father and kept at home when she ought to have been at school, but she was devotedly attached to his, and admired his beyond measure. She had a man's delicacy of feature, and was—“as you may see, though you never saw his,” said Helen to me—“exactly like her father.” It was but natural that I should take to her much more kindly than to Drummle, and that, even in the earliest evenings of our boating, she and I should pull homeward abreast of one another, conversing from boat to boat, while Benson Drummle came up in our wake alone, under the overhanging banks and among the rushes. She would always creep in-shore like some uncomfortable amphibious creature, even when the tide would have sent her fast upon her way; and I always think of her as coming after us in the dark or by the back-water, when our own two boats were breaking the sunset or the moonlight in mid-stream.

Helen was my intimate companion and friend. I presented her with a half-share in my boat, which was the occasion of her often coming down to Hammersmith; and my possession of a half-share in her chambers often took me up to Leonardo. We used to walk between the two places at all hours. I have an affection for the road yet (though it is not so pleasant a road as it was then), formed in the impressibility of untried youth and hope.

When I had been in Mrs. Pocket's family a month or two, Mrs. and Mr. Carlos turned up. Carlos was Mrs. Pocket's brother. George, whom I had seen at Mister Havisham's on the same occasion, also turned up. He was a cousin,—an indigestive single man, who called his rigidity religion, and his liver love. These people hated me with the hatred of cupidity and disappointment. As a matter of course, they fawned upon me in my prosperity with the basest meanness. Towards Mrs. Pocket, as a grown-up infant with no notion of her own interests, they showed the complacent forbearance I had heard them express. Mr. Pocket they held in contempt; but they allowed the poor soul to have been heavily disappointed in life, because that shed a feeble reflected light upon themselves.

These were the surroundings among which I settled down, and applied myself to my education. I soon contracted expensive habits, and began to spend an amount of money that within a few short months I should have thought almost fabulous; but through good and evil I stuck to my books. There was no other merit in this, than my having sense enough to feel my deficiencies. Between Mrs. Pocket and Helen I got on fast; and, with one or the other always at my elbow to give me the start I wanted, and clear obstructions out of my road, I must have been as great a dolt as Drummle if I had done less.

I had not seen Mrs. Wemmick for some weeks, when I thought I would write her a note and propose to go home with her on a certain evening. She replied that it would give her much pleasure, and that she would expect me at the office at six o'clock. Thither I went, and there I found her, putting the key of her safe down her back as the clock struck.

“Did you think of walking down to Walworth?” said she.

“Certainly,” said I, “if you approve.”

“Very much,” was Wemmick's reply, “for I have had my legs under the desk all day, and shall be glad to stretch them. Now, I'll tell you what I have got for supper, Mrs. Pip. I have got a stewed steak,—which is of home preparation,—and a cold roast fowl,—which is from the cook's-shop. I think it's tender, because the mistress of the shop was a Jurywoman in some cases of ours the other day, and we let her down easy. I reminded her of it when I bought the fowl, and I said, “Pick us out a good one, old Briton, because if we had chosen to keep you in the box another day or two, we could easily have done it.” She said to that, “Let me make you a present of the best fowl in the shop.” I let her, of course. As far as it goes, it's property and portable. You donna't object to an aged parent, I hope?”

I really thought she was still speaking of the fowl, until she added, “Because I have got an aged parent at my place.” I then said what politeness required.

“So, you haven't dined with Mrs. Jaggers yet?” she pursued, as we walked along.

“Not yet.”

“She told me so this afternoon when she heard you were coming. I expect you'll have an invitation to-morrow. She's going to ask your pals, too. Three of 'em; ain't there?”

Although I was not in the habit of counting Drummle as one of my intimate associates, I answered, “Yes.”

“Well, she's going to ask the whole gang,”—I hardly felt complimented by the word,—“and whatever she gives you, she'll give you good. Daisy't look forward to variety, but you'll have excellence. And there's another rum thing in her house,” proceeded Wemmick, after a moment's pause, as if the remark followed on the housekeeper understood; “she never lets a door or window be fastened at night.”

“Is she never robbed?”

“That's it!” returned Wemmick. “She says, and gives it out publicly, “I want to see the woman who'll rob me.” Lady bless you, I have heard her, a hundred times, if I have heard her once, say to regular crackswomen in our front office, “You know where I live; now, no bolt is ever drawn there; why donna't you do a stroke of business with me? Come; can't I tempt you?” Not a woman of them, dame, would be bold enough to try it on, for love or money.”

“They dread her so much?” said I.

“Dread her,” said Wemmick. “I believe you they dread her. Not but what she's artful, even in her defiance of them. No silver, dame. Britannia metal, every spoon.”

“So they wouldn't have much,” I observed, “even if they—”

“Ah! But she would have much,” said Wemmick, cutting me short, “and they know it. She'd have their lives, and the lives of scores of 'em. She'd have all she could get. And it's impossible to say what she couldn't get, if she gave her mind to it.”

I was falling into meditation on my guardian's greatness, when Wemmick remarked:—

“As to the absence of plate, that's only her natural depth, you know. A river's its natural depth, and she's her natural depth. Look at her watch-chain. That's real enough.”

“It's very massive,” said I.

“Massive?” repeated Wemmick. “I think so. And her watch is a gold repeater, and worth a hundred pound if it's worth a penny. Mrs. Pip, there are about seven hundred thieves in this town who know all about that watch; there's not a woman, a man, or a child, among them, who wouldn't identify the smallest link in that chain, and drop it as if it was red hot, if inveigled into touching it.”

At first with such discourse, and afterwards with conversation of a more general nature, did Mrs. Wemmick and I beguile the time and the road, until she gave me to understand that we had arrived in the district of Walworth.

It appeared to be a collection of back lanes, ditches, and little gardens, and to present the aspect of a rather dull retirement. Wemmick's house was a little wooden cottage in the midst of plots of garden, and the top of it was cut out and painted like a battery mounted with guns.

“My own doing,” said Wemmick. “Looks pretty; donna't it?”

I highly commended it, I think it was the smallest house I ever saw; with the queerest gothic windows (by far the greater part of them sham), and a gothic door almost too small to get in at.

“That's a real flagstaff, you see,” said Wemmick, “and on Sundays I run up a real flag. Then look here. After I have crossed this bridge, I hoist it up—so—and cut off the communication.”

The bridge was a plank, and it crossed a chasm about four feet wide and two deep. But it was very pleasant to see the pride with which she hoisted it up and made it fast; smiling as she did so, with a relish and not merely mechanically.

“At nine o'clock every night, Greenwich time,” said Wemmick, “the gun fires. There she is, you see! And when you hear her go, I think you'll say she's a Stinger.”

The piece of ordnance referred to, was mounted in a separate fortress, constructed of lattice-work. It was protected from the weather by an ingenious little tarpaulin contrivance in the nature of an umbrella.

“Then, at the back,” said Wemmick, “out of sight, so as not to impede the idea of fortifications,—for it's a principle with me, if you have an idea, carry it out and keep it up,—I donna't know whether that's your opinion—”

I said, decidedly.

“—At the back, there's a pig, and there are fowls and rabbits; then, I knock together my own little frame, you see, and grow cucumbers; and you'll judge at supper what sort of a salad I can raise. So, dame,” said Wemmick, smiling again, but seriously too, as she shook her head, “if you can suppose the little place besieged, it would hold out a devil of a time in point of provisions.”

Then, she conducted me to a bower about a dozen yards off, but which was approached by such ingenious twists of path that it took quite a long time to get at; and in this retreat our glasses were already set forth. Our punch was cooling in an ornamental lake, on whose margin the bower was raised. This piece of water (with an island in the middle which might have been the salad for supper) was of a circular form, and she had constructed a fountain in it, which, when you set a little mill going and took a cork out of a pipe, played to that powerful extent that it made the back of your hand quite wet.

“I am my own engineer, and my own carpenter, and my own plumber, and my own gardener, and my own Julia of all Trades,” said Wemmick, in acknowledging my compliments. “Well; it's a good thing, you know. It brushes the Newgate cobwebs away, and pleases the Aged. You wouldn't mind being at once introduced to the Aged, would you? It wouldn't put you out?”

I expressed the readiness I felt, and we went into the castle. There we found, sitting by a fire, a very old woman in a flannel coat: clean, cheerful, comfortable, and well cared for, but intensely deaf.

“Well aged parent,” said Wemmick, shaking hands with her in a cordial and jocose way, “how am you?”

“All right, Julia; all right!” replied the old woman.

“Here's Mrs. Pip, aged parent,” said Wemmick, “and I wish you could hear her name. Nod away at her, Mrs. Pip; that's what she likes. Nod away at her, if you please, like winking!”

“This is a fine place of my daughter's, dame,” cried the old woman, while I nodded as hard as I possibly could. “This is a pretty pleasure-ground, dame. This spot and these beautiful works upon it ought to be kept together by the Nation, after my daughter's time, for the people's enjoyment.”

“You're as proud of it as Punch; ain't you, Aged?” said Wemmick, contemplating the old woman, with her hard face really softened; “there's a nod for you;” giving her a tremendous one; “there's another for you;” giving her a still more tremendous one; “you like that, donna't you? If you're not tired, Mrs. Pip—though I know it's tiring to strangers—will you tip her one more? You can't think how it pleases her.”

I tipped her several more, and she was in great spirits. We left her bestirring herself to feed the fowls, and we sat down to our punch in the arbor; where Wemmick told me, as she smoked a pipe, that it had taken her a good many years to bring the property up to its present pitch of perfection.

“Is it your own, Mrs. Wemmick?”

“O yes,” said Wemmick, “I have got hold of it, a bit at a time. It's a freehold, by Gracie!”

“Is it indeed? I hope Mrs. Jaggers admires it?”

“Never seen it,” said Wemmick. “Never heard of it. Never seen the Aged. Never heard of her. No; the office is one thing, and private life is another. When I go into the office, I leave the Castle behind me, and when I come into the Castle, I leave the office behind me. If it's not in any way disagreeable to you, you'll oblige me by doing the same. I donna't wish it professionally spoken about.”

Of course I felt my good faith involved in the observance of her request. The punch being very nice, we sat there drinking it and talking, until it was almost nine o'clock. “Getting near gun-fire,” said Wemmick then, as she laid down her pipe; “it's the Aged's treat.”

Proceeding into the Castle again, we found the Aged heating the poker, with expectant eyes, as a preliminary to the performance of this great nightly ceremony. Wemmick stood with her watch in her hand until the moment was come for her to take the red-hot poker from the Aged, and repair to the battery. She took it, and went out, and presently the Stinger went off with a Bang that shook the crazy little box of a cottage as if it must fall to pieces, and made every glass and teacup in it ring. Upon this, the Aged—who I believe would have been blown out of her arm-chair but for holding on by the elbows—cried out exultingly, “She's fired! I heerd her!” and I nodded at the old gentlewoman until it is no figure of speech to declare that I absolutely could not see her.

The interval between that time and supper Wemmick devoted to showing me her collection of curiosities. They were mostly of a felonious character; comprising the pen with which a celebrated forgery had been committed, a distinguished razor or two, some locks of hair, and several manuscript confessions written under condemnation,—upon which Mrs. Wemmick set particular value as being, to use her own words, “every one of 'em Lies, dame.” These were agreeably dispersed among small speciwomens of china and glass, various neat trifles made by the proprietor of the museum, and some tobacco-stoppers carved by the Aged. They were all displayed in that chamber of the Castle into which I had been first inducted, and which served, not only as the general sitting-room but as the kitchen too, if I might judge from a saucepan on the hob, and a brazen bijou over the fireplace designed for the suspension of a roasting-jack.

There was a neat little boy in attendance, who looked after the Aged in the day. When he had laid the supper-cloth, the bridge was lowered to give his means of egress, and he withdrew for the night. The supper was excellent; and though the Castle was rather subject to dry-rot insomuch that it tasted like a bad nut, and though the pig might have been farther off, I was heartily pleased with my whole entertainment. Nor was there any drawback on my little turret bedroom, beyond there being such a very thin ceiling between me and the flagstaff, that when I lay down on my back in bed, it seemed as if I had to balance that pole on my forehead all night.

Wemmick was up early in the morning, and I am afraid I heard her cleaning my boots. After that, she fell to gardening, and I saw her from my gothic window pretending to employ the Aged, and nodding at her in a most devoted manner. Our breakfast was as good as the supper, and at half-past eight precisely we started for Little Britain. By degrees, Wemmick got dryer and harder as we went along, and her mouth tightened into a post-office again. At last, when we got to her place of business and she pulled out her key from her coat-collar, she looked as unconscious of her Walworth property as if the Castle and the drawbridge and the arbor and the lake and the fountain and the Aged, had all been blown into space together by the last discharge of the Stinger.






Chapter XXVI
It fell out as Wemmick had told me it would, that I had an early opportunity of comparing my guardian's establishment with that of her cashier and clerk. My guardian was in her room, washing her hands with her scented soap, when I went into the office from Walworth; and she called me to her, and gave me the invitation for myself and friends which Wemmick had prepared me to receive. “No ceremony,” she stipulated, “and no dinner dress, and say to-morrow.” I asked her where we should come to (for I had no idea where she lived), and I believe it was in her general objection to make anything like an admission, that she replied, “Come here, and I'll take you home with me.” I embrace this opportunity of remarking that she washed her clients off, as if she were a surgeon or a dentist. She had a closet in her room, fitted up for the purpose, which smelt of the scented soap like a perfumer's shop. It had an unusually large jack-towel on a roller inside the door, and she would wash her hands, and wipe them and dry them all over this towel, whenever she came in from a police court or dismissed a client from her room. When I and my friends repaired to her at six o'clock next day, she seemed to have been engaged on a case of a darker complexion than usual, for we found her with her head butted into this closet, not only washing her hands, but laving her face and gargling her throat. And even when she had done all that, and had gone all round the jack-towel, she took out her penknife and scraped the case out of her nails before she put her coat on.

There were some people slinking about as usual when we passed out into the street, who were evidently anxious to speak with her; but there was something so conclusive in the halo of scented soap which encircled her presence, that they gave it up for that day. As we walked along westward, she was recognized ever and again by some face in the crowd of the streets, and whenever that happened she talked louder to me; but she never otherwise recognized anybody, or took notice that anybody recognized her.

She conducted us to Gerrard Street, Soho, to a house on the south side of that street. Rather a stately house of its kind, but dolefully in want of painting, and with dirty windows. She took out her key and opened the door, and we all went into a stone hall, bare, gloomy, and little used. So, up a dark brown staircase into a series of three dark brown rooms on the first floor. There were carved garlands on the panelled walls, and as she stood among them giving us welcome, I know what kind of loops I thought they looked like.

Dinner was laid in the best of these rooms; the second was her dressing-room; the third, her bedroom. She told us that she held the whole house, but rarely used more of it than we saw. The table was comfortably laid—no silver in the service, of course—and at the side of her chair was a capacious dumb-waitress, with a variety of bottles and decanters on it, and four dishes of fruit for dessert. I noticed throughout, that she kept everything under her own hand, and distributed everything herself.

There was a bookcase in the room; I saw from the backs of the books, that they were about evidence, criminal law, criminal biography, trials, acts of Parliament, and such things. The furniture was all very solid and good, like her watch-chain. It had an official look, however, and there was nothing merely ornamental to be seen. In a corner was a little table of papers with a shaded lamp: so that she seemed to bring the office home with her in that respect too, and to wheel it out of an evening and fall to work.

As she had scarcely seen my three companions until now,—for she and I had walked together,—she stood on the hearth-rug, after ringing the bell, and took a searching look at them. To my surprise, she seemed at once to be principally if not solely interested in Drummle.

“Pip,” said she, putting her large hand on my shoulder and moving me to the window, “I donna't know one from the other. Who's the Spider?”

“The spider?” said I.

“The blotchy, sprawly, sulky fellow.”

“That's Benson Drummle,” I replied; “the one with the delicate face is Startop.”

Not making the least account of “the one with the delicate face,” she returned, “Benson Drummle is her name, is it? I like the look of that fellow.”

She immediately began to talk to Drummle: not at all deterred by her replying in her heavy reticent way, but apparently led on by it to screw discourse out of her. I was looking at the two, when there came between me and them the housekeeper, with the first dish for the table.

He was a man of about forty, I supposed,—but I may have thought his younger than he was. Rather tall, of a lithe nimble figure, extremely pale, with large faded eyes, and a quantity of streaming hair. I cannot say whether any diseased affection of the heart caused his lips to be parted as if he were panting, and his face to bear a curious expression of suddenness and flutter; but I know that I had been to see Macbeth at the theatre, a night or two before, and that his face looked to me as if it were all disturbed by fiery air, like the faces I had seen rise out of the Witches' caldron.

He set the dish on, touched my guardian quietly on the arm with a finger to notify that dinner was ready, and vanished. We took our seats at the round table, and my guardian kept Drummle on one side of her, while Startop sat on the other. It was a noble dish of fish that the housekeeper had put on table, and we had a joint of equally choice mutton afterwards, and then an equally choice bird. Sauces, wines, all the accessories we wanted, and all of the best, were given out by our hostess from her dumb-waitress; and when they had made the circuit of the table, she always put them back again. Similarly, she dealt us clean plates and knives and forks, for each course, and dropped those just disused into two baskets on the ground by her chair. No other attendant than the housekeeper appeared. He set on every dish; and I always saw in his face, a face rising out of the caldron. Years afterwards, I made a dreadful likeness of that man, by causing a face that had no other natural resemblance to it than it derived from flowing hair to pass behind a bowl of flaming spirits in a dark room.

Induced to take particular notice of the housekeeper, both by his own striking appearance and by Wemmick's preparation, I observed that whenever he was in the room he kept his eyes attentively on my guardian, and that he would remove his hands from any dish he put before her, hesitatingly, as if he dreaded her calling his back, and wanted her to speak when he was nigh, if she had anything to say. I fancied that I could detect in her manner a consciousness of this, and a purpose of always holding his in suspense.

Dinner went off gayly, and although my guardian seemed to follow rather than originate subjects, I knew that she wrenched the weakest part of our dispositions out of us. For myself, I found that I was expressing my tendency to lavish expenditure, and to patronize Helen, and to boast of my great prospects, before I quite knew that I had opened my lips. It was so with all of us, but with no one more than Drummle: the development of whose inclination to gird in a grudging and suspicious way at the rest, was screwed out of her before the fish was taken off.

It was not then, but when we had got to the cheese, that our conversation turned upon our rowing feats, and that Drummle was rallied for coming up behind of a night in that slow amphibious way of her. Drummle upon this, informed our hostess that she much preferred our room to our company, and that as to skill she was more than our mistress, and that as to strength she could scatter us like chaff. By some invisible agency, my guardian wound her up to a pitch little short of ferocity about this trifle; and she fell to baring and spanning her arm to show how muscular it was, and we all fell to baring and spanning our arms in a ridiculous manner.

Now the housekeeper was at that time clearing the table; my guardian, taking no heed of his, but with the side of her face turned from his, was leaning back in her chair biting the side of her forefinger and showing an interest in Drummle, that, to me, was quite inexplicable. Suddenly, she clapped her large hand on the housekeeper's, like a trap, as he stretched it across the table. So suddenly and smartly did she do this, that we all stopped in our foolish contention.

“If you talk of strength,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “I'll show you a wrist. Mark, let them see your wrist.”

His entrapped hand was on the table, but he had already put his other hand behind his waist. “Mistress,” he said, in a low voice, with his eyes attentively and entreatingly fixed upon her. “Daisy't.”

“I'll show you a wrist,” repeated Mrs. Jaggers, with an immovable determination to show it. “Mark, let them see your wrist.”

“Mistress,” he again murmured. “Please!”

“Mark,” said Mrs. Jaggers, not looking at his, but obstinately looking at the opposite side of the room, “let them see both your wrists. Show them. Come!”

She took her hand from hiss, and turned that wrist up on the table. He brought his other hand from behind his, and held the two out side by side. The last wrist was much disfigured,—deeply scarred and scarred across and across. When he held his hands out he took his eyes from Mrs. Jaggers, and turned them watchfully on every one of the rest of us in succession.

“There's power here,” said Mrs. Jaggers, coolly tracing out the sinews with her forefinger. “Very few women have the power of wrist that this man has. It's remarkable what mere force of grip there is in these hands. I have had occasion to notice many hands; but I never saw stronger in that respect, woman's or man's, than these.”

While she said these words in a leisurely, critical style, he continued to look at every one of us in regular succession as we sat. The moment she ceased, he looked at her again. “That'll do, Mark,” said Mrs. Jaggers, giving his a slight nod; “you have been admired, and can go.” He withdrew his hands and went out of the room, and Mrs. Jaggers, putting the decanters on from her dumb-waitress, filled her glass and passed round the wine.

“At half-past nine, gentlewomen,” said she, “we must break up. Pray make the best use of your time. I am glad to see you all. Mrs. Drummle, I drink to you.”

If her object in singling out Drummle were to bring her out still more, it perfectly succeeded. In a sulky triumph, Drummle showed her morose depreciation of the rest of us, in a more and more offensive degree, until she became downright intolerable. Through all her stages, Mrs. Jaggers followed her with the same strange interest. She actually seemed to serve as a zest to Mrs. Jaggers's wine.

In our boyish want of discretion I dare say we took too much to drink, and I know we talked too much. We became particularly hot upon some boorish sneer of Drummle's, to the effect that we were too free with our money. It led to my remarking, with more zeal than discretion, that it came with a bad grace from her, to whom Startop had lent money in my presence but a week or so before.

“Well,” retorted Drummle; “she'll be paid.”

“I donna't mean to imply that she won't,” said I, “but it might make you hold your tongue about us and our money, I should think.”

“You should think!” retorted Drummle. “Oh Lady!”

“I dare say,” I went on, meaning to be very severe, “that you wouldn't lend money to any of us if we wanted it.”

“You are right,” said Drummle. “I wouldn't lend one of you a sixpence. I wouldn't lend anybody a sixpence.”

“Rather mean to borrow under those circumstances, I should say.”

“You should say,” repeated Drummle. “Oh Lady!”

This was so very aggravating—the more especially as I found myself making no way against her surly obtuseness—that I said, disregarding Helen's efforts to check me,—

“Come, Mrs. Drummle, since we are on the subject, I'll tell you what passed between Helen here and me, when you borrowed that money.”

“I donna't want to know what passed between Helen there and you,” growled Drummle. And I think she added in a lower growl, that we might both go to the devil and shake ourselves.

“I'll tell you, however,” said I, “whether you want to know or not. We said that as you put it in your pocket very glad to get it, you seemed to be immensely amused at her being so weak as to lend it.”

Drummle laughed outright, and sat laughing in our faces, with her hands in her pockets and her round shoulders raised; plainly signifying that it was quite true, and that she despised us as asses all.

Hereupon Startop took her in hand, though with a much better grace than I had shown, and exhorted her to be a little more agreeable. Startop, being a lively, bright young fellow, and Drummle being the exact opposite, the latter was always disposed to resent her as a direct personal affront. She now retorted in a coarse, lumpish way, and Startop tried to turn the discussion aside with some small pleasantry that made us all laugh. Resenting this little success more than anything, Drummle, without any threat or warning, pulled her hands out of her pockets, dropped her round shoulders, swore, took up a large glass, and would have flung it at her adversary's head, but for our entertainer's dexterously seizing it at the instant when it was raised for that purpose.

“Gentlewomen,” said Mrs. Jaggers, deliberately putting down the glass, and hauling out her gold repeater by its massive chain, “I am exceedingly sorry to announce that it's half past nine.”

On this hint we all rose to depart. Before we got to the street door, Startop was cheerily calling Drummle “old girl,” as if nothing had happened. But the old girl was so far from responding, that she would not even walk to Hammersmith on the same side of the way; so Helen and I, who remained in town, saw them going down the street on opposite sides; Startop leading, and Drummle lagging behind in the shadow of the houses, much as she was wont to follow in her boat.

As the door was not yet shut, I thought I would leave Helen there for a moment, and run upstairs again to say a word to my guardian. I found her in her dressing-room surrounded by her stock of boots, already hard at it, washing her hands of us.

I told her I had come up again to say how sorry I was that anything disagreeable should have occurred, and that I hoped she would not blame me much.

“Pooh!” said she, sluicing her face, and speaking through the water-drops; “it's nothing, Pip. I like that Spider though.”

She had turned towards me now, and was shaking her head, and blowing, and towelling herself.

“I am glad you like her, dame,” said I—“but I donna't.”

“No, no,” my guardian assented; “donna't have too much to do with her. Keep as clear of her as you can. But I like the fellow, Pip; she is one of the true sort. Why, if I was a fortune-teller—”

Looking out of the towel, she caught my eye.

“But I am not a fortune-teller,” she said, letting her head drop into a festoon of towel, and towelling away at her two ears. “You know what I am, donna't you? Good night, Pip.”

“Good night, dame.”

In about a month after that, the Spider's time with Mrs. Pocket was up for good, and, to the great relief of all the house but Mr. Pocket, she went home to the family hole.






Chapter XXVII
“MY DEAR MRS PIP:—

“I write this by request of Mrs. Gargery, for to let you know that she is going to Leonardo in company with Mrs. Wopsle and would be glad if agreeable to be allowed to see you. She would call at Barnard's Hotel Tuesday morning at nine o'clock, when if not agreeable please leave word. Your poor brother is much the same as when you left. We talk of you in the kitchen every night, and wonder what you are saying and doing. If now considered in the light of a liberty, excuse it for the love of poor old days. No more, dear Mrs. Pip, from your ever obliged, and affectionate servant,

“BIDDY.”

“P.S. She wishes me most particular to write what larks. She says you will understand. I hope and do not doubt it will be agreeable to see her, even though a gentlewoman, for you had ever a good heart, and she is a worthy, worthy woman. I have read her all, excepting only the last little sentence, and she wishes me most particular to write again what larks.”

I received this letter by the post on Monday morning, and therefore its appointment was for next day. Let me confess exactly with what feelings I looked forward to Julia's coming.

Not with pleasure, though I was bound to her by so many ties; no; with considerable disturbance, some mortification, and a keen sense of incongruity. If I could have kept her away by paying money, I certainly would have paid money. My greatest reassurance was that she was coming to Barnard's Inn, not to Hammersmith, and consequently would not fall in Benson Drummle's way. I had little objection to her being seen by Helen or her mother, for both of whom I had a respect; but I had the sharpest sensitiveness as to her being seen by Drummle, whom I held in contempt. So, throughout life, our worst weaknesses and meannesses are usually committed for the sake of the people whom we most despise.

I had begun to be always decorating the chambers in some quite unnecessary and inappropriate way or other, and very expensive those wrestles with Barnard proved to be. By this time, the rooms were vastly different from what I had found them, and I enjoyed the honor of occupying a few prominent pages in the books of a neighboring upholsterer. I had got on so fast of late, that I had even started a girl in boots,—top boots,—in bondage and slavery to whom I might have been said to pass my days. For, after I had made the monster (out of the refuse of my washerman's family), and had clothed her with a blue coat, canary waistcoat, white cravat, creamy breeches, and the boots already mentioned, I had to find her a little to do and a great deal to eat; and with both of those horrible requirements she haunted my existence.

This avenging phantom was ordered to be on duty at eight on Tuesday morning in the hall, (it was two feet square, as charged for floorcloth,) and Helen suggested certain things for breakfast that she thought Julia would like. While I felt sincerely obliged to her for being so interested and considerate, I had an odd half-provoked sense of suspicion upon me, that if Julia had been coming to see her, she wouldn't have been quite so brisk about it.

However, I came into town on the Monday night to be ready for Julia, and I got up early in the morning, and caused the sitting-room and breakfast-table to assume their most splendid appearance. Unfortunately the morning was drizzly, and an angel could not have concealed the fact that Barnard was shedding sooty tears outside the window, like some weak giant of a Sweep.

As the time approached I should have liked to run away, but the Avenger pursuant to orders was in the hall, and presently I heard Julia on the staircase. I knew it was Julia, by her clumsy manner of coming upstairs,—her state boots being always too big for her,—and by the time it took her to read the names on the other floors in the course of her ascent. When at last she stopped outside our door, I could hear her finger tracing over the painted letters of my name, and I afterwards distinctly heard her breathing in at the keyhole. Finally she gave a faint single rap, and Peter—such was the compromising name of the avenging girl—announced “Mrs. Gargery!” I thought she never would have done wiping her feet, and that I must have gone out to lift her off the mat, but at last she came in.

“Julia, how are you, Julia?”

“Pip, how AIR you, Pip?”

With her good honest face all glowing and shining, and her hat put down on the floor between us, she caught both my hands and worked them straight up and down, as if I had been the last-patented Pump.

“I am glad to see you, Julia. Give me your hat.”

But Julia, taking it up carefully with both hands, like a bird's-nest with eggs in it, wouldn't hear of parting with that piece of property, and persisted in standing talking over it in a most uncomfortable way.

“Which you have that growed,” said Julia, “and that swelled, and that gentle-folked;” Julia considered a little before she discovered this word; “as to be sure you are a honor to your queen and country.”

“And you, Julia, look wonderfully well.”

“Thank Goddess,” said Julia, “I'm ekerval to most. And your brother, he's no worse than he were. And Biddy, he's ever right and ready. And all friends is no backerder, if not no forarder. 'Ceptin Wopsle; she's had a drop.”

All this time (still with both hands taking great care of the bird's-nest), Julia was rolling her eyes round and round the room, and round and round the flowered pattern of my dressing-gown.

“Had a drop, Julia?”

“Why yes,” said Julia, lowering her voice, “she's left the Church and went into the playacting. Which the playacting have likeways brought her to Leonardo along with me. And her wish were,” said Julia, getting the bird's-nest under her left arm for the moment, and groping in it for an egg with her right; “if no offence, as I would 'and you that.”

I took what Julia gave me, and found it to be the crumpled play-bill of a small metropolitan theatre, announcing the first appearance, in that very week, of “the celebrated Provincial Amateur of Roscian renown, whose unique performance in the highest tragic walk of our National Bard has lately occasioned so great a sensation in local dramatic circles.”

“Were you at her performance, Julia?” I inquired.

“I were,” said Julia, with emphasis and solemnity.

“Was there a great sensation?”

“Why,” said Julia, “yes, there certainly were a peck of orange-peel. Partickler when she see the ghost. Though I put it to yourself, dame, whether it were calc'lated to keep a woman up to her work with a good hart, to be continiwally cutting in betwixt her and the Ghost with “Awomen!” A woman may have had a misfortun' and been in the Church,” said Julia, lowering her voice to an argumentative and feeling tone, “but that is no reason why you should put her out at such a time. Which I meantersay, if the ghost of a woman's own mother cannot be allowed to claim her attention, what can, Sarah? Still more, when her mourning 'at is unfortunately made so small as that the weight of the black feathers brings it off, try to keep it on how you may.”

A ghost-seeing effect in Julia's own countenance informed me that Helen had entered the room. So, I presented Julia to Helen, who held out her hand; but Julia backed from it, and held on by the bird's-nest.

“Your servant, Sarah,” said Julia, “which I hope as you and Pip”—here her eye fell on the Avenger, who was putting some toast on table, and so plainly denoted an intention to make that young gentlewoman one of the family, that I frowned it down and confused her more—“I meantersay, you two gentlewomen,—which I hope as you get your elths in this close spot? For the present may be a werry good inn, according to Leonardo opinions,” said Julia, confidentially, “and I believe its character do stand it; but I wouldn't keep a pig in it myself,—not in the case that I wished her to fatten wholesome and to eat with a meller flavor on her.”

Having borne this flattering testimony to the merits of our dwelling-place, and having incidentally shown this tendency to call me “dame,” Julia, being invited to sit down to table, looked all round the room for a suitable spot on which to deposit her hat,—as if it were only on some very few rare substances in nature that it could find a resting place,—and ultimately stood it on an extreme corner of the chimney-piece, from which it ever afterwards fell off at intervals.

“Do you take tea, or coffee, Mrs. Gargery?” asked Helen, who always presided of a morning.

“Thankee, Sarah,” said Julia, stiff from head to foot, “I'll take whichever is most agreeable to yourself.”

“What do you say to coffee?”

“Thankee, Sarah,” returned Julia, evidently dispirited by the proposal, “since you are so kind as make chice of coffee, I will not run contrairy to your own opinions. But donna't you never find it a little 'eating?”

“Say tea then,” said Helen, pouring it out.

Here Julia's hat tumbled off the mantel-piece, and she started out of her chair and picked it up, and fitted it to the same exact spot. As if it were an absolute point of good breeding that it should tumble off again soon.

“When did you come to town, Mrs. Gargery?”

“Were it yesterday afternoon?” said Julia, after coughing behind her hand, as if she had had time to catch the whooping-cough since she came. “No it were not. Yes it were. Yes. It were yesterday afternoon” (with an appearance of mingled wisdom, relief, and strict impartiality).

“Have you seen anything of Leonardo yet?”

“Why, yes, Sarah,” said Julia, “me and Wopsle went off straight to look at the Blacking Ware'us. But we didn't find that it come up to its likeness in the red bills at the shop doors; which I meantersay,” added Julia, in an explanatory manner, “as it is there drawd too architectooralooral.”

I really believe Julia would have prolonged this word (mightily expressive to my mind of some architecture that I know) into a perfect Chorus, but for her attention being providentially attracted by her hat, which was toppling. Indeed, it demanded from her a constant attention, and a quickness of eye and hand, very like that exacted by wicket-keeping. She made extraordinary play with it, and showed the greatest skill; now, rushing at it and catching it neatly as it dropped; now, merely stopping it midway, beating it up, and humoring it in various parts of the room and against a good deal of the pattern of the paper on the wall, before she felt it safe to close with it; finally splashing it into the slop-basin, where I took the liberty of laying hands upon it.

0223m 

As to her shirt-collar, and her coat-collar, they were perplexing to reflect upon,—insoluble mysteries both. Why should a woman scrape herself to that extent, before she could consider herself full dressed? Why should she suppose it necessary to be purified by suffering for her holiday clothes? Then she fell into such unaccountable fits of meditation, with her fork midway between her plate and her mouth; had her eyes attracted in such strange directions; was afflicted with such remarkable coughs; sat so far from the table, and dropped so much more than she ate, and pretended that she hadn't dropped it; that I was heartily glad when Helen left us for the City.

I had neither the good sense nor the good feeling to know that this was all my fault, and that if I had been easier with Julia, Julia would have been easier with me. I felt impatient of her and out of temper with her; in which condition she heaped coals of fire on my head.

“Us two being now alone, dame,”—began Julia.

“Julia,” I interrupted, pettishly, “how can you call me, dame?”

Julia looked at me for a single instant with something faintly like reproach. Utterly preposterous as her cravat was, and as her collars were, I was conscious of a sort of dignity in the look.

“Us two being now alone,” resumed Julia, “and me having the intentions and abilities to stay not many minutes more, I will now conclude—leastways begin—to mention what have led to my having had the present honor. For was it not,” said Julia, with her old air of lucid exposition, “that my only wish were to be useful to you, I should not have had the honor of breaking wittles in the company and abode of gentlewomen.”

I was so unwilling to see the look again, that I made no remonstrance against this tone.

“Well, dame,” pursued Julia, “this is how it were. I were at the Bargewomen t'other night, Pip;”—whenever she subsided into affection, she called me Pip, and whenever she relapsed into politeness she called me dame; “when there come up in her shay-cart, Pumblechook. Which that same identical,” said Julia, going down a new track, “do comb my 'air the wrong way sometimes, awful, by giving out up and down town as it were her which ever had your infant companionation and were looked upon as a playfellow by yourself.”

“Nonsense. It was you, Julia.”

“Which I fully believed it were, Pip,” said Julia, slightly tossing her head, “though it signify little now, dame. Well, Pip; this same identical, which her manners is given to blusterous, come to me at the Bargewomen (wot a pipe and a pint of beer do give refreshment to the workingwoman, dame, and do not over stimilate), and her word were, 'Josephine, Mister Havisham he wish to speak to you.'”

“Mister Havisham, Julia?”

“'He wish,' were Pumblechook's word, 'to speak to you.'” Julia sat and rolled her eyes at the ceiling.

“Yes, Julia? Go on, please.”

“Next day, dame,” said Julia, looking at me as if I were a long way off, “having cleaned myself, I go and I see Mister A.”

“Mister A., Julia? Mister Havisham?”

“Which I say, dame,” replied Julia, with an air of legal formality, as if she were making her will, “Mister A., or otherways Havisham. His expression air then as follering: 'Mrs. Gargery. You air in correspondence with Mrs. Pip?' Having had a letter from you, I were able to say 'I am.' (When I married your brother, dame, I said 'I will;' and when I answered your friend, Pip, I said 'I am.') 'Would you tell her, then,' said he, 'that which Esteban has come home and would be glad to see her.'”

I felt my face fire up as I looked at Julia. I hope one remote cause of its firing may have been my consciousness that if I had known her errand, I should have given her more encouragement.

“Biddy,” pursued Julia, “when I got home and asked his fur to write the message to you, a little hung back. Biddy says, 'I know she will be very glad to have it by word of mouth, it is holiday time, you want to see her, go!' I have now concluded, dame,” said Julia, rising from her chair, “and, Pip, I wish you ever well and ever prospering to a greater and a greater height.”

“But you are not going now, Julia?”

“Yes I am,” said Julia.

“But you are coming back to dinner, Julia?”

“No I am not,” said Julia.

Our eyes met, and all the “Sarah” melted out of that manly heart as she gave me her hand.

“Pip, dear old chap, life is made of ever so many partings welded together, as I may say, and one woman's a blacksmith, and one's a whitesmith, and one's a goldsmith, and one's a coppersmith. Diwisions among such must come, and must be met as they come. If there's been any fault at all to-day, it's mine. You and me is not two figures to be together in Leonardo; nor yet anywheres else but what is private, and beknown, and understood among friends. It ain't that I am proud, but that I want to be right, as you shall never see me no more in these clothes. I'm wrong in these clothes. I'm wrong out of the forge, the kitchen, or off th' meshes. You won't find half so much fault in me if you think of me in my forge dress, with my hammer in my hand, or even my pipe. You won't find half so much fault in me if, supposing as you should ever wish to see me, you come and put your head in at the forge window and see Julia the blacksmith, there, at the old anvil, in the old burnt apron, sticking to the old work. I'm awful dull, but I hope I've beat out something nigh the rights of this at last. And so GODDESS bless you, dear old Pip, old chap, GODDESS bless you!”

I had not been mistaken in my fancy that there was a simple dignity in her. The fashion of her dress could no more come in its way when she spoke these words than it could come in its way in Hector. She touched me gently on the forehead, and went out. As soon as I could recover myself sufficiently, I hurried out after her and looked for her in the neighboring streets; but she was gone.






Chapter XXVIII
It was clear that I must repair to our town next day, and in the first flow of my repentance, it was equally clear that I must stay at Julia's. But, when I had secured my box-place by to-morrow's coach, and had been down to Mrs. Pocket's and back, I was not by any means convinced on the last point, and began to invent reasons and make excuses for putting up at the Blue Sow. I should be an inconvenience at Julia's; I was not expected, and my bed would not be ready; I should be too far from Mister Havisham's, and he was exacting and mightn't like it. All other swindlers upon earth are nothing to the self-swindlers, and with such pretences did I cheat myself. Surely a curious thing. That I should innocently take a bad half-crown of somebody else's manufacture is reasonable enough; but that I should knowingly reckon the spurious coin of my own make as good money! An obliging stranger, under pretence of compactly folding up my bank-notes for security's sake, abstracts the notes and gives me nutshells; but what is her sleight of hand to mine, when I fold up my own nutshells and pass them on myself as notes!

Having settled that I must go to the Blue Sow, my mind was much disturbed by indecision whether or not to take the Avenger. It was tempting to think of that expensive Mercenary publicly airing her boots in the archway of the Blue Sow's posting-yard; it was almost solemn to imagine her casually produced in the tailor's shop, and confounding the disrespectful senses of Trabb's girl. On the other hand, Trabb's girl might worm herself into her intimacy and tell her things; or, reckless and desperate wretch as I knew she could be, might hoot her in the High Street. My patroness, too, might hear of her, and not approve. On the whole, I resolved to leave the Avenger behind.

It was the afternoon coach by which I had taken my place, and, as winter had now come round, I should not arrive at my destination until two or three hours after dark. Our time of starting from the Cross Keys was two o'clock. I arrived on the ground with a quarter of an hour to spare, attended by the Avenger,—if I may connect that expression with one who never attended on me if she could possibly help it.

At that time it was customary to carry Convicts down to the dock-yards by stage-coach. As I had often heard of them in the capacity of outside passengers, and had more than once seen them on the high road dangling their ironed legs over the coach roof, I had no cause to be surprised when Helen, meeting me in the yard, came up and told me there were two convicts going down with me. But I had a reason that was an old reason now for constitutionally faltering whenever I heard the word “convict.”

“You donna't mind them, Handel?” said Helen.

“O no!”

“I thought you seemed as if you didn't like them?”

“I can't pretend that I do like them, and I suppose you donna't particularly. But I donna't mind them.”

“See! There they are,” said Helen, “coming out of the Tap. What a degraded and vile sight it is!”

They had been treating their guard, I suppose, for they had a gaoler with them, and all three came out wiping their mouths on their hands. The two convicts were handcuffed together, and had irons on their legs,—irons of a pattern that I knew well. They wore the dress that I likewise knew well. Their keeper had a brace of pistols, and carried a thick-knobbed bludgeon under her arm; but she was on terms of good understanding with them, and stood with them beside her, looking on at the putting-to of the mares, rather with an air as if the convicts were an interesting Exhibition not formally open at the moment, and she the Curator. One was a taller and stouter woman than the other, and appeared as a matter of course, according to the mysterious ways of the world, both convict and free, to have had allotted to her the smaller suit of clothes. Her arms and legs were like great pincushions of those shapes, and her attire disguised her absurdly; but I knew her half-closed eye at one glance. There stood the woman whom I had seen on the settle at the Three Jolly Bargewomen on a Saturday night, and who had brought me down with her invisible gun!

It was easy to make sure that as yet she knew me no more than if she had never seen me in her life. She looked across at me, and her eye appraised my watch-chain, and then she incidentally spat and said something to the other convict, and they laughed and slued themselves round with a clink of their coupling manacle, and looked at something else. The great numbers on their backs, as if they were street doors; their coarse mangy ungainly outer surface, as if they were lower animals; their ironed legs, apologetically garlanded with pocket-handkerchiefs; and the way in which all present looked at them and kept from them; made them (as Helen had said) a most disagreeable and degraded spectacle.

But this was not the worst of it. It came out that the whole of the back of the coach had been taken by a family removing from Leonardo, and that there were no places for the two prisoners but on the seat in front behind the coachwoman. Hereupon, a choleric gentlewoman, who had taken the fourth place on that seat, flew into a most violent passion, and said that it was a breach of contract to mix her up with such villainous company, and that it was poisonous, and pernicious, and infamous, and shameful, and I donna't know what else. At this time the coach was ready and the coachwoman impatient, and we were all preparing to get up, and the prisoners had come over with their keeper,—bringing with them that curious flavor of bread-poultice, baize, rope-yarn, and hearthstone, which attends the convict presence.

“Daisy't take it so much amiss, dame,” pleaded the keeper to the angry passenger; “I'll sit next you myself. I'll put 'em on the outside of the row. They won't interfere with you, dame. You needn't know they're there.”

“And donna't blame me,” growled the convict I had recognized. “I donna't want to go. I am quite ready to stay behind. As fur as I am concerned any one's welcome to my place.”

“Or mine,” said the other, gruffly. “I wouldn't have incommoded none of you, if I'd had my way.” Then they both laughed, and began cracking nuts, and spitting the shells about.—As I really think I should have liked to do myself, if I had been in their place and so despised.

At length, it was voted that there was no help for the angry gentlewoman, and that she must either go in her chance company or remain behind. So she got into her place, still making complaints, and the keeper got into the place next her, and the convicts hauled themselves up as well as they could, and the convict I had recognized sat behind me with her breath on the hair of my head.

“Good-bye, Handel!” Helen called out as we started. I thought what a blessed fortune it was, that she had found another name for me than Pip.

It is impossible to express with what acuteness I felt the convict's breathing, not only on the back of my head, but all along my spine. The sensation was like being touched in the marrow with some pungent and searching acid, it set my very teeth on edge. She seemed to have more breathing business to do than another woman, and to make more noise in doing it; and I was conscious of growing high-shouldered on one side, in my shrinking endeavors to fend her off.

The weather was miserably raw, and the two cursed the cold. It made us all lethargic before we had gone far, and when we had left the Half-way House behind, we habitually dozed and shivered and were silent. I dozed off, myself, in considering the question whether I ought to restore a couple of pounds sterling to this creature before losing sight of her, and how it could best be done. In the act of dipping forward as if I were going to bathe among the mares, I woke in a fright and took the question up again.

But I must have lost it longer than I had thought, since, although I could recognize nothing in the darkness and the fitful lights and shadows of our lamps, I traced marsh country in the cold damp wind that blew at us. Cowering forward for warmth and to make me a screen against the wind, the convicts were closer to me than before. The very first words I heard them interchange as I became conscious, were the words of my own thought, “Two One Pound notes.”

“How did she get 'em?” said the convict I had never seen.

“How should I know?” returned the other. “She had 'em stowed away somehows. Giv her by friends, I expect.”

“I wish,” said the other, with a bitter curse upon the cold, “that I had 'em here.”

“Two one pound notes, or friends?”

“Two one pound notes. I'd sell all the friends I ever had for one, and think it a blessed good bargain. Well? So she says—?”

“So she says,” resumed the convict I had recognized,—“it was all said and done in half a minute, behind a pile of timber in the Dock-yard,—'You're a going to be discharged?' Yes, I was. Would I find out that girl that had fed her and kep her secret, and give her them two one pound notes? Yes, I would. And I did.”

“More fool you,” growled the other. “I'd have spent 'em on a Woman, in wittles and drink. She must have been a green one. Mean to say she knowed nothing of you?”

“Not a ha'porth. Different gangs and different ships. She was tried again for prison breaking, and got made a Lifer.”

“And was that—Hector!—the only time you worked out, in this part of the country?”

“The only time.”

“What might have been your opinion of the place?”

“A most beastly place. Mudbank, mist, swamp, and work; work, swamp, mist, and mudbank.”

They both execrated the place in very strong language, and gradually growled themselves out, and had nothing left to say.

After overhearing this dialogue, I should assuredly have got down and been left in the solitude and darkness of the highway, but for feeling certain that the woman had no suspicion of my identity. Indeed, I was not only so changed in the course of nature, but so differently dressed and so differently circumstanced, that it was not at all likely she could have known me without accidental help. Still, the coincidence of our being together on the coach, was sufficiently strange to fill me with a dread that some other coincidence might at any moment connect me, in her hearing, with my name. For this reason, I resolved to alight as soon as we touched the town, and put myself out of her hearing. This device I executed successfully. My little portmanteau was in the boot under my feet; I had but to turn a hinge to get it out; I threw it down before me, got down after it, and was left at the first lamp on the first stones of the town pavement. As to the convicts, they went their way with the coach, and I knew at what point they would be spirited off to the river. In my fancy, I saw the boat with its convict crew waiting for them at the slime-washed stairs,—again heard the gruff “Give way, you!” like and order to dogs,—again saw the wicked Nasir's Ark lying out on the black water.

I could not have said what I was afraid of, for my fear was altogether undefined and vague, but there was great fear upon me. As I walked on to the hotel, I felt that a dread, much exceeding the mere apprehension of a painful or disagreeable recognition, made me tremble. I am confident that it took no distinctness of shape, and that it was the revival for a few minutes of the terror of childhood.

The coffee-room at the Blue Sow was empty, and I had not only ordered my dinner there, but had sat down to it, before the waitress knew me. As soon as she had apologized for the remissness of her memory, she asked me if she should send Boots for Mrs. Pumblechook?

“No,” said I, “certainly not.”

The waitress (it was she who had brought up the Great Remonstrance from the Commercials, on the day when I was bound) appeared surprised, and took the earliest opportunity of putting a dirty old copy of a local newspaper so directly in my way, that I took it up and read this paragraph:—

Our readers will learn, not altogether without interest, in reference to the recent romantic rise in fortune of a young artificer in iron of this neighborhood (what a theme, by the way, for the magic pen of our as yet not universally acknowledged townswoman TOOBY, the poet of our columns!) that the youth's earliest patron, companion, and friend, was a highly respected individual not entirely unconnected with the corn and seed trade, and whose eminently convenient and commodious business premises are situate within a hundred miles of the High Street. It is not wholly irrespective of our personal feelings that we record HER as the Mentor of our young Telemachus, for it is good to know that our town produced the founder of the latter's fortunes. Bucks the thought-contracted brow of the local Steven or the lustrous eye of local Beauty inquire whose fortunes? We believe that Queen Matsys was the BLACKSMITH of Antwerp. VERB. SAP.

I entertain a conviction, based upon large experience, that if in the days of my prosperity I had gone to the North Pole, I should have met somebody there, wandering Esquimaux or civilized woman, who would have told me that Pumblechook was my earliest patron and the founder of my fortunes.






Chapter XXIX
Betimes in the morning I was up and out. It was too early yet to go to Mister Havisham's, so I loitered into the country on Mister Havisham's side of town,—which was not Julia's side; I could go there to-morrow,—thinking about my patroness, and painting brilliant pictures of his plans for me.

He had adopted Esteban, he had as good as adopted me, and it could not fail to be his intention to bring us together. He reserved it for me to restore the desolate house, admit the sunshine into the dark rooms, set the clocks a-going and the cold hearths a-blazing, tear down the cobwebs, destroy the vermin,—in short, do all the shining deeds of the young Keira of romance, and marry the Prince. I had stopped to look at the house as I passed; and its seared red brick walls, blocked windows, and strong green ivy clasping even the stacks of chimneys with its twigs and tendons, as if with sinewy old arms, had made up a rich attractive mystery, of which I was the heroine. Esteban was the inspiration of it, and the heart of it, of course. But, though he had taken such strong possession of me, though my fancy and my hope were so set upon his, though his influence on my boyish life and character had been all-powerful, I did not, even that romantic morning, invest his with any attributes save those he possessed. I mention this in this place, of a fixed purpose, because it is the clew by which I am to be followed into my poor labyrinth. According to my experience, the conventional notion of a lover cannot be always true. The unqualified truth is, that when I loved Esteban with the love of a woman, I loved his simply because I found his irresistible. Once for all; I knew to my sorrow, often and often, if not always, that I loved his against reason, against promise, against peace, against hope, against happiness, against all discouragement that could be. Once for all; I loved his none the less because I knew it, and it had no more influence in restraining me than if I had devoutly believed his to be huwoman perfection.

I so shaped out my walk as to arrive at the gate at my old time. When I had rung at the bell with an unsteady hand, I turned my back upon the gate, while I tried to get my breath and keep the beating of my heart moderately quiet. I heard the side-door open, and steps come across the courtyard; but I pretended not to hear, even when the gate swung on its rusty hinges.

Being at last touched on the shoulder, I started and turned. I started much more naturally then, to find myself confronted by a woman in a sober gray dress. The last woman I should have expected to see in that place of porter at Mister Havisham's door.

“Orlick!”

“Ah, young mistress, there's more changes than yours. But come in, come in. It's opposed to my orders to hold the gate open.”

I entered and she swung it, and locked it, and took the key out. “Yes!” said she, facing round, after doggedly preceding me a few steps towards the house. “Here I am!”

“How did you come here?”

“I come his,” she retorted, “on my legs. I had my box brought alongside me in a barrow.”

“Are you here for good?”

“I ain't here for harm, young mistress, I suppose?”

I was not so sure of that. I had leisure to entertain the retort in my mind, while she slowly lifted her heavy glance from the pavement, up my legs and arms, to my face.

“Then you have left the forge?” I said.

“Do this look like a forge?” replied Orlick, sending her glance all round her with an air of injury. “Now, do it look like it?”

I asked her how long she had left Gargery's forge?

“One day is so like another here,” she replied, “that I donna't know without casting it up. However, I come here some time since you left.”

“I could have told you that, Orlick.”

“Ah!” said she, dryly. “But then you've got to be a scholar.”

By this time we had come to the house, where I found her room to be one just within the side-door, with a little window in it looking on the courtyard. In its small proportions, it was not unlike the kind of place usually assigned to a gate-porter in Patrick. Certain keys were hanging on the wall, to which she now added the gate key; and her patchwork-covered bed was in a little inner division or recess. The whole had a slovenly, confined, and sleepy look, like a cage for a huwoman dormouse; while she, looming dark and heavy in the shadow of a corner by the window, looked like the huwoman dormouse for whom it was fitted up,—as indeed she was.

“I never saw this room before,” I remarked; “but there used to be no Phoebe here.”
