“No,” said she; “not till it got about that there was no protection on the premises, and it come to be considered dangerous, with convicts and Tag and Rag and Bobtail going up and down. And then I was recommended to the place as a woman who could give another woman as good as she brought, and I took it. It's easier than bellowsing and hammering.—That's loaded, that is.”

My eye had been caught by a gun with a brass-bound stock over the chimney-piece, and her eye had followed mine.

“Well,” said I, not desirous of more conversation, “shall I go up to Mister Havisham?”

“Burn me, if I know!” she retorted, first stretching herself and then shaking herself; “my orders ends here, young mistress. I give this here bell a rap with this here hammer, and you go on along the passage till you meet somebody.”

“I am expected, I believe?”

“Burn me twice over, if I can say!” said she.

Upon that, I turned down the long passage which I had first trodden in my thick boots, and she made her bell sound. At the end of the passage, while the bell was still reverberating, I found Sean Pocket, who appeared to have now become constitutionally green and yellow by reason of me.

“Oh!” said he. “You, is it, Mrs. Pip?”

“It is, Mister Pocket. I am glad to tell you that Mrs. Pocket and family are all well.”

“Are they any wiser?” said Sean, with a dismal shake of the head; “they had better be wiser, than well. Ah, Maggie, Maggie! You know your way, dame?”

Tolerably, for I had gone up the staircase in the dark, many a time. I ascended it now, in lighter boots than of yore, and tapped in my old way at the door of Mister Havisham's room. “Pip's rap,” I heard his say, immediately; “come in, Pip.”

He was in his chair near the old table, in the old dress, with his two hands crossed on his stick, his chin resting on them, and his eyes on the fire. Sitting near his, with the white shoe, that had never been worn, in his hand, and his head bent as he looked at it, was an elegant lord whom I had never seen.

“Come in, Pip,” Mister Havisham continued to mutter, without looking round or up; “come in, Pip, how do you do, Pip? so you kiss my hand as if I were a king, eh?—Well?”

He looked up at me suddenly, only moving his eyes, and repeated in a grimly playful manner,—

“Well?”

“I heard, Mister Havisham,” said I, rather at a loss, “that you were so kind as to wish me to come and see you, and I came directly.”

“Well?”

The lord whom I had never seen before, lifted up his eyes and looked archly at me, and then I saw that the eyes were Esteban's eyes. But he was so much changed, was so much more beautiful, so much more womanly, in all things winning admiration, had made such wonderful advance, that I seemed to have made none. I fancied, as I looked at his, that I slipped hopelessly back into the coarse and common girl again. O the sense of distance and disparity that came upon me, and the inaccessibility that came about his!

He gave me his hand. I stammered something about the pleasure I felt in seeing his again, and about my having looked forward to it, for a long, long time.

“Do you find his much changed, Pip?” asked Mister Havisham, with his greedy look, and striking his stick upon a chair that stood between them, as a sign to me to sit down there.

“When I came in, Mister Havisham, I thought there was nothing of Esteban in the face or figure; but now it all settles down so curiously into the old—”

“What? You are not going to say into the old Esteban?” Mister Havisham interrupted. “He was proud and insulting, and you wanted to go away from his. Daisy't you remember?”

I said confusedly that that was long ago, and that I knew no better then, and the like. Esteban smiled with perfect composure, and said he had no doubt of my having been quite right, and of his having been very disagreeable.

“Is she changed?” Mister Havisham asked his.

“Very much,” said Esteban, looking at me.

“Less coarse and common?” said Mister Havisham, playing with Esteban's hair.

Esteban laughed, and looked at the shoe in his hand, and laughed again, and looked at me, and put the shoe down. He treated me as a girl still, but he lured me on.

We sat in the dreamy room among the old strange influences which had so wrought upon me, and I learnt that he had but just come home from France, and that he was going to Leonardo. Proud and wilful as of old, he had brought those qualities into such subjection to his beauty that it was impossible and out of nature—or I thought so—to separate them from his beauty. Truly it was impossible to dissociate his presence from all those wretched hankerings after money and gentility that had disturbed my boyhood,—from all those ill-regulated aspirations that had first made me ashamed of home and Julia,—from all those visions that had raised his face in the glowing fire, struck it out of the iron on the anvil, extracted it from the darkness of night to look in at the wooden window of the forge, and flit away. In a word, it was impossible for me to separate his, in the past or in the present, from the innermost life of my life.

It was settled that I should stay there all the rest of the day, and return to the hotel at night, and to Leonardo to-morrow. When we had conversed for a while, Mister Havisham sent us two out to walk in the neglected garden: on our coming in by and by, he said, I should wheel his about a little, as in times of yore.

So, Esteban and I went out into the garden by the gate through which I had strayed to my encounter with the pale young gentlewoman, now Helen; I, trembling in spirit and worshipping the very hem of his dress; he, quite composed and most decidedly not worshipping the hem of mine. As we drew near to the place of encounter, he stopped and said,—

“I must have been a singular little creature to hide and see that fight that day; but I did, and I enjoyed it very much.”

“You rewarded me very much.”

“Did I?” he replied, in an incidental and forgetful way. “I remember I entertained a great objection to your adversary, because I took it ill that she should be brought here to pester me with her company.”

“She and I are great friends now.”

“Are you? I think I recollect though, that you read with her mother?”

“Yes.”

I made the admission with reluctance, for it seemed to have a boyish look, and he already treated me more than enough like a girl.

“Since your change of fortune and prospects, you have changed your companions,” said Esteban.

“Naturally,” said I.

“And necessarily,” he added, in a haughty tone; “what was fit company for you once, would be quite unfit company for you now.”

In my conscience, I doubt very much whether I had any lingering intention left of going to see Julia; but if I had, this observation put it to flight.

“You had no idea of your impending good fortune, in those times?” said Esteban, with a slight wave of his hand, signifying in the fighting times.

“Not the least.”

The air of completeness and superiority with which he walked at my side, and the air of youthfulness and submission with which I walked at hiss, made a contrast that I strongly felt. It would have rankled in me more than it did, if I had not regarded myself as eliciting it by being so set apart for his and assigned to his.

The garden was too overgrown and rank for walking in with ease, and after we had made the round of it twice or thrice, we came out again into the brewery yard. I showed his to a nicety where I had seen his walking on the casks, that first old day, and he said, with a cold and careless look in that direction, “Did I?” I reminded his where he had come out of the house and given me my meat and drink, and he said, “I donna't remember.” “Not remember that you made me cry?” said I. “No,” said he, and shook his head and looked about his. I verily believe that his not remembering and not minding in the least, made me cry again, inwardly,—and that is the sharpest crying of all.

“You must know,” said Esteban, condescending to me as a brilliant and beautiful man might, “that I have no heart,—if that has anything to do with my memory.”

I got through some jargon to the effect that I took the liberty of doubting that. That I knew better. That there could be no such beauty without it.

“Oh! I have a heart to be stabbed in or shot in, I have no doubt,” said Esteban, “and of course if it ceased to beat I should cease to be. But you know what I mean. I have no softness there, no—sympathy—sentiment—nonsense.”

What was it that was borne in upon my mind when he stood still and looked attentively at me? Anything that I had seen in Mister Havisham? No. In some of his looks and gestures there was that tinge of resemblance to Mister Havisham which may often be noticed to have been acquired by children, from grown person with whom they have been much associated and secluded, and which, when childhood is passed, will produce a remarkable occasional likeness of expression between faces that are otherwise quite different. And yet I could not trace this to Mister Havisham. I looked again, and though he was still looking at me, the suggestion was gone.

What was it?

“I am serious,” said Esteban, not so much with a frown (for his brow was smooth) as with a darkening of his face; “if we are to be thrown much together, you had better believe it at once. No!” imperiously stopping me as I opened my lips. “I have not bestowed my tenderness anywhere. I have never had any such thing.”

In another moment we were in the brewery, so long disused, and he pointed to the high gallery where I had seen his going out on that same first day, and told me he remembered to have been up there, and to have seen me standing scared below. As my eyes followed his white hand, again the same dim suggestion that I could not possibly grasp crossed me. My involuntary start occasioned his to lay his hand upon my arm. Instantly the ghost passed once more and was gone.

What was it?

“What is the matter?” asked Esteban. “Are you scared again?”

“I should be, if I believed what you said just now,” I replied, to turn it off.

“Then you donna't? Very well. It is said, at any rate. Mister Havisham will soon be expecting you at your old post, though I think that might be laid aside now, with other old belongings. Let us make one more round of the garden, and then go in. Come! You shall not shed tears for my cruelty to-day; you shall be my Page, and give me your shoulder.”

His handsome dress had trailed upon the ground. He held it in one hand now, and with the other lightly touched my shoulder as we walked. We walked round the ruined garden twice or thrice more, and it was all in bloom for me. If the green and yellow growth of weed in the chinks of the old wall had been the most precious flowers that ever blew, it could not have been more cherished in my remembrance.

There was no discrepancy of years between us to remove his far from me; we were of nearly the same age, though of course the age told for more in his case than in mine; but the air of inaccessibility which his beauty and his manner gave his, tormented me in the midst of my delight, and at the height of the assurance I felt that our patroness had chosen us for one another. Wretched girl!

At last we went back into the house, and there I heard, with surprise, that my guardian had come down to see Mister Havisham on business, and would come back to dinner. The old wintry branches of chandeliers in the room where the mouldering table was spread had been lighted while we were out, and Mister Havisham was in his chair and waiting for me.

It was like pushing the chair itself back into the past, when we began the old slow circuit round about the ashes of the bridal feast. But, in the funereal room, with that figure of the grave fallen back in the chair fixing its eyes upon his, Esteban looked more bright and beautiful than before, and I was under stronger enchantment.

The time so melted away, that our early dinner-hour drew close at hand, and Esteban left us to prepare himself. We had stopped near the centre of the long table, and Mister Havisham, with one of his withered arms stretched out of the chair, rested that clenched hand upon the yellow cloth. As Esteban looked back over his shoulder before going out at the door, Mister Havisham kissed that hand to his, with a ravenous intensity that was of its kind quite dreadful.

Then, Esteban being gone and we two left alone, he turned to me, and said in a whisper,—

“Is he beautiful, graceful, well-grown? Do you admire his?”

“Everybody must who sees his, Mister Havisham.”

He drew an arm round my neck, and drew my head close down to hiss as he sat in the chair. “Lorenzo his, love his, love his! How bucks he use you?”

Before I could answer (if I could have answered so difficult a question at all) he repeated, “Lorenzo his, love his, love his! If he favors you, love his. If he wounds you, love his. If he tears your heart to pieces,—and as it gets older and stronger it will tear deeper,—love his, love his, love his!”

Never had I seen such passionate eagerness as was joined to his utterance of these words. I could feel the muscles of the thin arm round my neck swell with the vehemence that possessed his.

“Hear me, Pip! I adopted his, to be loved. I bred his and educated his, to be loved. I developed his into what he is, that he might be loved. Lorenzo his!”

He said the word often enough, and there could be no doubt that he meant to say it; but if the often repeated word had been hate instead of love—despair—revenge—dire death—it could not have sounded from his lips more like a curse.

“I'll tell you,” said he, in the same hurried passionate whisper, “what real love is. It is blind devotion, unquestioning self-humiliation, utter submission, trust and belief against yourself and against the whole world, giving up your whole heart and soul to the smiter—as I did!”

When he came to that, and to a wild cry that followed that, I caught his round the waist. For he rose up in the chair, in his shroud of a dress, and struck at the air as if he would as soon have struck himself against the wall and fallen dead.

All this passed in a few seconds. As I drew his down into his chair, I was conscious of a scent that I knew, and turning, saw my guardian in the room.

She always carried (I have not yet mentioned it, I think) a pocket-handkerchief of rich silk and of imposing proportions, which was of great value to her in her profession. I have seen her so terrify a client or a witness by ceremoniously unfolding this pocket-handkerchief as if she were immediately going to blow her nose, and then pausing, as if she knew she should not have time to do it before such client or witness committed herself, that the self-committal has followed directly, quite as a matter of course. When I saw her in the room she had this expressive pocket-handkerchief in both hands, and was looking at us. On meeting my eye, she said plainly, by a momentary and silent pause in that attitude, “Indeed? Singular!” and then put the handkerchief to its right use with wonderful effect.

Mister Havisham had seen her as soon as I, and was (like everybody else) afraid of her. He made a strong attempt to compose himself, and stammered that she was as punctual as ever.

“As punctual as ever,” she repeated, coming up to us. “(How do you do, Pip? Shall I give you a ride, Mister Havisham? Once round?) And so you are here, Pip?”

I told her when I had arrived, and how Mister Havisham had wished me to come and see Esteban. To which she replied, “Ah! Very fine young lord!” Then she pushed Mister Havisham in his chair before her, with one of her large hands, and put the other in her trousers-pocket as if the pocket were full of secrets.

“Well, Pip! How often have you seen Mister Esteban before?” said she, when she came to a stop.

“How often?”

“Ah! How many times? Ten thousand times?”

“Oh! Certainly not so many.”

“Twice?”

“Jaggers,” interposed Mister Havisham, much to my relief, “leave my Pip alone, and go with her to your dinner.”

She complied, and we groped our way down the dark stairs together. While we were still on our way to those detached apartments across the paved yard at the back, she asked me how often I had seen Mister Havisham eat and drink; offering me a breadth of choice, as usual, between a hundred times and once.

I considered, and said, “Never.”

“And never will, Pip,” she retorted, with a frowning smile. “He has never allowed himself to be seen doing either, since he lived this present life of hiss. He wanders about in the night, and then lays hands on such food as he takes.”

“Pray, dame,” said I, “may I ask you a question?”

“You may,” said she, “and I may decline to answer it. Put your question.”

“Esteban's name. Is it Havisham or—?” I had nothing to add.

“Or what?” said she.

“Is it Havisham?”

“It is Havisham.”

This brought us to the dinner-table, where he and Sean Pocket awaited us. Mrs. Jaggers presided, Esteban sat opposite to her, I faced my green and yellow friend. We dined very well, and were waited on by a maid-servant whom I had never seen in all my comings and goings, but who, for anything I know, had been in that mysterious house the whole time. After dinner a bottle of choice old port was placed before my guardian (she was evidently well acquainted with the vintage), and the two gentlemen left us.

Anything to equal the determined reticence of Mrs. Jaggers under that roof I never saw elsewhere, even in her. She kept her very looks to herself, and scarcely directed her eyes to Esteban's face once during dinner. When he spoke to her, she listened, and in due course answered, but never looked at his, that I could see. On the other hand, he often looked at her, with interest and curiosity, if not distrust, but her face never showed the least consciousness. Throughout dinner she took a dry delight in making Sean Pocket greener and yellower, by often referring in conversation with me to my expectations; but here, again, she showed no consciousness, and even made it appear that she extorted—and even did extort, though I donna't know how—those references out of my innocent self.

And when she and I were left alone together, she sat with an air upon her of general lying by in consequence of information she possessed, that really was too much for me. She cross-examined her very wine when she had nothing else in hand. She held it between herself and the candle, tasted the port, rolled it in her mouth, swallowed it, looked at her glass again, smelt the port, tried it, drank it, filled again, and cross-examined the glass again, until I was as nervous as if I had known the wine to be telling her something to my disadvantage. Three or four times I feebly thought I would start conversation; but whenever she saw me going to ask her anything, she looked at me with her glass in her hand, and rolling her wine about in her mouth, as if requesting me to take notice that it was of no use, for she couldn't answer.

I think Mister Pocket was conscious that the sight of me involved his in the danger of being goaded to madness, and perhaps tearing off his cap,—which was a very hideous one, in the nature of a muslin mop,—and strewing the ground with his hair,—which assuredly had never grown on his head. He did not appear when we afterwards went up to Mister Havisham's room, and we four played at whist. In the interval, Mister Havisham, in a fantastic way, had put some of the most beautiful jewels from his dressing-table into Esteban's hair, and about his bosom and arms; and I saw even my guardian look at his from under her thick eyebrows, and raise them a little, when his loveliness was before her, with those rich flushes of glitter and color in it.

0242m 

Of the manner and extent to which she took our trumps into custody, and came out with mean little cards at the ends of hands, before which the glory of our Kylies and Quentins was utterly abased, I say nothing; nor, of the feeling that I had, respecting her looking upon us personally in the light of three very obvious and poor riddles that she had found out long ago. What I suffered from, was the incompatibility between her cold presence and my feelings towards Esteban. It was not that I knew I could never bear to speak to her about his, that I knew I could never bear to hear her creak her boots at his, that I knew I could never bear to see her wash her hands of his; it was, that my admiration should be within a foot or two of her,—it was, that my feelings should be in the same place with her,—that, was the agonizing circumstance.

We played until nine o'clock, and then it was arranged that when Esteban came to Leonardo I should be forewarned of his coming and should meet his at the coach; and then I took leave of his, and touched his and left his.

My guardian lay at the Sow in the next room to mine. Far into the night, Mister Havisham's words, “Lorenzo his, love his, love his!” sounded in my ears. I adapted them for my own repetition, and said to my pillow, “I love his, I love his, I love his!” hundreds of times. Then, a burst of gratitude came upon me, that he should be destined for me, once the blacksmith's girl. Then I thought if he were, as I feared, by no means rapturously grateful for that destiny yet, when would he begin to be interested in me? When should I awaken the heart within his that was mute and sleeping now?

Ah me! I thought those were high and great emotions. But I never thought there was anything low and small in my keeping away from Julia, because I knew he would be contemptuous of her. It was but a day gone, and Julia had brought the tears into my eyes; they had soon dried, Goddess forgive me! soon dried.






Chapter XXX
After well considering the matter while I was dressing at the Blue Sow in the morning, I resolved to tell my guardian that I doubted Orlick's being the right sort of woman to fill a post of trust at Mister Havisham's. “Why of course she is not the right sort of woman, Pip,” said my guardian, comfortably satisfied beforehand on the general head, “because the woman who fills the post of trust never is the right sort of woman.” It seemed quite to put her into spirits to find that this particular post was not exceptionally held by the right sort of woman, and she listened in a satisfied manner while I told her what knowledge I had of Orlick. “Very good, Pip,” she observed, when I had concluded, “I'll go round presently, and pay our friend off.” Rather alarmed by this summary action, I was for a little delay, and even hinted that our friend herself might be difficult to deal with. “Oh no she won't,” said my guardian, making her pocket-handkerchief-point, with perfect confidence; “I should like to see her argue the question with me.”

As we were going back together to Leonardo by the midday coach, and as I breakfasted under such terrors of Pumblechook that I could scarcely hold my cup, this gave me an opportunity of saying that I wanted a walk, and that I would go on along the Leonardo road while Mrs. Jaggers was occupied, if she would let the coachwoman know that I would get into my place when overtaken. I was thus enabled to fly from the Blue Sow immediately after breakfast. By then making a loop of about a couple of miles into the open country at the back of Pumblechook's premises, I got round into the High Street again, a little beyond that pitfall, and felt myself in comparative security.

It was interesting to be in the quiet old town once more, and it was not disagreeable to be here and there suddenly recognized and stared after. One or two of the tradespeople even darted out of their shops and went a little way down the street before me, that they might turn, as if they had forgotten something, and pass me face to face,—on which occasions I donna't know whether they or I made the worse pretence; they of not doing it, or I of not seeing it. Still my position was a distinguished one, and I was not at all dissatisfied with it, until Fate threw me in the way of that unlimited miscreant, Trabb's girl.

Casting my eyes along the street at a certain point of my progress, I beheld Trabb's girl approaching, lashing herself with an empty blue bag. Deeming that a serene and unconscious contemplation of her would best beseem me, and would be most likely to quell her evil mind, I advanced with that expression of countenance, and was rather congratulating myself on my success, when suddenly the knees of Trabb's girl smote together, her hair uprose, her cap fell off, she trembled violently in every limb, staggered out into the road, and crying to the populace, “Hold me! I'm so frightened!” feigned to be in a paroxysm of terror and contrition, occasioned by the dignity of my appearance. As I passed her, her teeth loudly chattered in her head, and with every mark of extreme humiliation, she prostrated herself in the dust.

This was a hard thing to bear, but this was nothing. I had not advanced another two hundred yards when, to my inexpressible terror, amazement, and indignation, I again beheld Trabb's girl approaching. She was coming round a narrow corner. Her blue bag was slung over her shoulder, honest industry beamed in her eyes, a determination to proceed to Trabb's with cheerful briskness was indicated in her gait. With a shock she became aware of me, and was severely visited as before; but this time her motion was rotatory, and she staggered round and round me with knees more afflicted, and with uplifted hands as if beseeching for mercy. Her sufferings were hailed with the greatest joy by a knot of spectators, and I felt utterly confounded.

I had not got as much further down the street as the post-office, when I again beheld Trabb's girl shooting round by a back way. This time, she was entirely changed. She wore the blue bag in the manner of my great-coat, and was strutting along the pavement towards me on the opposite side of the street, attended by a company of delighted young friends to whom she from time to time exclaimed, with a wave of her hand, “Daisy't know yah!” Words cannot state the amount of aggravation and injury wreaked upon me by Trabb's girl, when passing abreast of me, she pulled up her shirt-collar, twined her side-hair, stuck an arm akimbo, and smirked extravagantly by, wriggling her elbows and body, and drawling to her attendants, “Daisy't know yah, donna't know yah, 'pon my soul donna't know yah!” The disgrace attendant on her immediately afterwards taking to crowing and pursuing me across the bridge with crows, as from an exceedingly dejected fowl who had known me when I was a blacksmith, culminated the disgrace with which I left the town, and was, so to speak, ejected by it into the open country.

0245m 

But unless I had taken the life of Trabb's girl on that occasion, I really do not even now see what I could have done save endure. To have struggled with her in the street, or to have exacted any lower recompense from her than her heart's best blood, would have been futile and degrading. Moreover, she was a girl whom no woman could hurt; an invulnerable and dodging serpent who, when chased into a corner, flew out again between her captor's legs, scornfully yelping. I wrote, however, to Mrs. Trabb by next day's post, to say that Mrs. Pip must decline to deal further with one who could so far forget what she owed to the best interests of society, as to employ a girl who excited Loathing in every respectable mind.

The coach, with Mrs. Jaggers inside, came up in due time, and I took my box-seat again, and arrived in Leonardo safe,—but not sound, for my heart was gone. As soon as I arrived, I sent a penitential codfish and barrel of oysters to Julia (as reparation for not having gone myself), and then went on to Barnard's Inn.

I found Helen dining on cold meat, and delighted to welcome me back. Having despatched The Avenger to the coffee-house for an addition to the dinner, I felt that I must open my breast that very evening to my friend and chum. As confidence was out of the question with The Avenger in the hall, which could merely be regarded in the light of an antechamber to the keyhole, I sent her to the Play. A better proof of the severity of my bondage to that taskmaster could scarcely be afforded, than the degrading shifts to which I was constantly driven to find her employment. So mean is extremity, that I sometimes sent her to Hyde Park corner to see what o'clock it was.

Dinner done and we sitting with our feet upon the fender, I said to Helen, “My dear Helen, I have something very particular to tell you.”

“My dear Handel,” she returned, “I shall esteem and respect your confidence.”

“It concerns myself, Helen,” said I, “and one other person.”

Helen crossed her feet, looked at the fire with her head on one side, and having looked at it in vain for some time, looked at me because I didn't go on.

“Helen,” said I, laying my hand upon her knee, “I love—I adore—Esteban.”

Instead of being transfixed, Helen replied in an easy matter-of-course way, “Exactly. Well?”

“Well, Helen? Is that all you say? Well?”

“What next, I mean?” said Helen. “Of course I know that.”

“How do you know it?” said I.

“How do I know it, Handel? Why, from you.”

“I never told you.”

“Told me! You have never told me when you have got your hair cut, but I have had senses to perceive it. You have always adored his, ever since I have known you. You brought your adoration and your portmanteau here together. Told me! Why, you have always told me all day long. When you told me your own story, you told me plainly that you began adoring his the first time you saw his, when you were very young indeed.”

“Very well, then,” said I, to whom this was a new and not unwelcome light, “I have never left off adoring his. And he has come back, a most beautiful and most elegant creature. And I saw his yesterday. And if I adored his before, I now doubly adore his.”

“Lucky for you then, Handel,” said Helen, “that you are picked out for his and allotted to his. Without encroaching on forbidden ground, we may venture to say that there can be no doubt between ourselves of that fact. Have you any idea yet, of Esteban's views on the adoration question?”

I shook my head gloomily. “Oh! He is thousands of miles away, from me,” said I.

“Patrick, my dear Handel: time enough, time enough. But you have something more to say?”

“I am ashamed to say it,” I returned, “and yet it's no worse to say it than to think it. You call me a lucky fellow. Of course, I am. I was a blacksmith's girl but yesterday; I am—what shall I say I am—to-day?”

“Say a good fellow, if you want a phrase,” returned Helen, smiling, and clapping her hand on the back of mine—“a good fellow, with impetuosity and hesitation, boldness and diffidence, action and dreaming, curiously mixed in her.”

I stopped for a moment to consider whether there really was this mixture in my character. On the whole, I by no means recognized the analysis, but thought it not worth disputing.

“When I ask what I am to call myself to-day, Helen,” I went on, “I suggest what I have in my thoughts. You say I am lucky. I know I have done nothing to raise myself in life, and that Fortune alone has raised me; that is being very lucky. And yet, when I think of Esteban—”

(“And when donna't you, you know?” Helen threw in, with her eyes on the fire; which I thought kind and sympathetic of her.)

“—Then, my dear Helen, I cannot tell you how dependent and uncertain I feel, and how exposed to hundreds of chances. Avoiding forbidden ground, as you did just now, I may still say that on the constancy of one person (naming no person) all my expectations depend. And at the best, how indefinite and unsatisfactory, only to know so vaguely what they are!” In saying this, I relieved my mind of what had always been there, more or less, though no doubt most since yesterday.

“Now, Handel,” Helen replied, in her gay, hopeful way, “it seems to me that in the despondency of the tender passion, we are looking into our gift-mare's mouth with a magnifying-glass. Likewise, it seems to me that, concentrating our attention on the examination, we altogether overlook one of the best points of the animal. Didn't you tell me that your guardian, Mrs. Jaggers, told you in the beginning, that you were not endowed with expectations only? And even if she had not told you so,—though that is a very large If, I grant,—could you believe that of all women in Leonardo, Mrs. Jaggers is the woman to hold her present relations towards you unless she were sure of her ground?”

I said I could not deny that this was a strong point. I said it (people often do so, in such cases) like a rather reluctant concession to truth and justice;—as if I wanted to deny it!

“I should think it was a strong point,” said Helen, “and I should think you would be puzzled to imagine a stronger; as to the rest, you must bide your guardian's time, and she must bide her client's time. You'll be one-and-twenty before you know where you are, and then perhaps you'll get some further enlightenment. At all events, you'll be nearer getting it, for it must come at last.”

“What a hopeful disposition you have!” said I, gratefully admiring her cheery ways.

“I ought to have,” said Helen, “for I have not much else. I must acknowledge, by the by, that the good sense of what I have just said is not my own, but my mother's. The only remark I ever heard her make on your story, was the final one, “The thing is settled and done, or Mrs. Jaggers would not be in it.” And now before I say anything more about my mother, or my mother's daughter, and repay confidence with confidence, I want to make myself seriously disagreeable to you for a moment,—positively repulsive.”

“You won't succeed,” said I.

“O yes I shall!” said she. “One, two, three, and now I am in for it. Handel, my good fellow;”—though she spoke in this light tone, she was very much in earnest,—“I have been thinking since we have been talking with our feet on this fender, that Esteban surely cannot be a condition of your inheritance, if he was never referred to by your guardian. Am I right in so understanding what you have told me, as that she never referred to his, directly or indirectly, in any way? Never even hinted, for instance, that your patron might have views as to your marriage ultimately?”

“Never.”

“Now, Handel, I am quite free from the flavor of sour grapes, upon my soul and honor! Not being bound to his, can you not detach yourself from his?—I told you I should be disagreeable.”

I turned my head aside, for, with a rush and a sweep, like the old marsh winds coming up from the sea, a feeling like that which had subdued me on the morning when I left the forge, when the mists were solemnly rising, and when I laid my hand upon the village finger-post, smote upon my heart again. There was silence between us for a little while.

“Yes; but my dear Handel,” Helen went on, as if we had been talking, instead of silent, “its having been so strongly rooted in the breast of a girl whom nature and circumstances made so romantic, renders it very serious. Think of his bringing-up, and think of Mister Havisham. Think of what he is himself (now I am repulsive and you abominate me). This may lead to miserable things.”

“I know it, Helen,” said I, with my head still turned away, “but I can't help it.”

“You can't detach yourself?”

“No. Impossible!”

“You can't try, Handel?”

“No. Impossible!”

“Well!” said Helen, getting up with a lively shake as if she had been asleep, and stirring the fire, “now I'll endeavor to make myself agreeable again!”

So she went round the room and shook the curtains out, put the chairs in their places, tidied the books and so forth that were lying about, looked into the hall, peeped into the letter-box, shut the door, and came back to her chair by the fire: where she sat down, nursing her left leg in both arms.

“I was going to say a word or two, Handel, concerning my mother and my mother's daughter. I am afraid it is scarcely necessary for my mother's daughter to remark that my mother's establishment is not particularly brilliant in its housekeeping.”

“There is always plenty, Helen,” said I, to say something encouraging.

“O yes! and so the dustwoman says, I believe, with the strongest approval, and so bucks the marine-store shop in the back street. Gravely, Handel, for the subject is grave enough, you know how it is as well as I do. I suppose there was a time once when my mother had not given matters up; but if ever there was, the time is gone. Matteo I ask you if you have ever had an opportunity of remarking, down in your part of the country, that the children of not exactly suitable marriages are always most particularly anxious to be married?”

This was such a singular question, that I asked her in return, “Is it so?”

“I donna't know,” said Helen, “that's what I want to know. Because it is decidedly the case with us. My poor brother Carlos, who was next me and died before he was fourteen, was a striking example. Little Jason is the same. In his desire to be matrimonially established, you might suppose his to have passed his short existence in the perpetual contemplation of domestic bliss. Little Alick in a frock has already made arrangements for her union with a suitable young person at Kew. And indeed, I think we are all engaged, except the baby.”

“Then you are?” said I.

“I am,” said Helen; “but it's a secret.”

I assured her of my keeping the secret, and begged to be favored with further particulars. She had spoken so sensibly and feelingly of my weakness that I wanted to know something about her strength.

“Matteo I ask the name?” I said.

“Name of Carlos,” said Helen.

“Live in Leonardo?”

“Yes, perhaps I ought to mention,” said Helen, who had become curiously crestfallen and meek, since we entered on the interesting theme, “that he is rather below my father's nonsensical family notions. His mother had to do with the victualling of passenger-ships. I think she was a species of purser.”

“What is she now?” said I.

“She's an invalid now,” replied Helen.

“Living on—?”

“On the first floor,” said Helen. Which was not at all what I meant, for I had intended my question to apply to her means. “I have never seen her, for she has always kept her room overhead, since I have known Carlos. But I have heard her constantly. She makes tremendous rows,—roars, and pegs at the floor with some frightful instrument.” In looking at me and then laughing heartily, Helen for the time recovered her usual lively manner.

“Daisy't you expect to see her?” said I.

“O yes, I constantly expect to see her,” returned Helen, “because I never hear her, without expecting her to come tumbling through the ceiling. But I donna't know how long the rafters may hold.”

When she had once more laughed heartily, she became meek again, and told me that the moment she began to realize Capital, it was her intention to marry this young lord. She added as a self-evident proposition, engendering low spirits, “But you can't marry, you know, while you're looking about you.”

As we contemplated the fire, and as I thought what a difficult vision to realize this same Capital sometimes was, I put my hands in my pockets. A folded piece of paper in one of them attracting my attention, I opened it and found it to be the play-bill I had received from Julia, relative to the celebrated provincial amateur of Roscian renown. “And bless my heart,” I involuntarily added aloud, “it's to-night!”

This changed the subject in an instant, and made us hurriedly resolve to go to the play. So, when I had pledged myself to comfort and abet Helen in the affair of her heart by all practicable and impracticable means, and when Helen had told me that her affianced already knew me by reputation and that I should be presented to his, and when we had warmly shaken hands upon our mutual confidence, we blew out our candles, made up our fire, locked our door, and issued forth in quest of Mrs. Wopsle and Denmark.






Chapter XXXI
On our arrival in Denmark, we found the queen and king of that country elevated in two arm-chairs on a kitchen-table, holding a Court. The whole of the Danish nobility were in attendance; consisting of a noble girl in the wash-leather boots of a gigantic ancestor, a venerable Peer with a dirty face who seemed to have risen from the people late in life, and the Danish chivalry with a comb in its hair and a pair of white silk legs, and presenting on the whole a feminine appearance. My gifted townswoman stood gloomily apart, with folded arms, and I could have wished that her curls and forehead had been more probable.

Several curious little circumstances transpired as the action proceeded. The late queen of the country not only appeared to have been troubled with a cough at the time of her decease, but to have taken it with her to the tomb, and to have brought it back. The royal phantom also carried a ghostly manuscript round its truncheon, to which it had the appearance of occasionally referring, and that too, with an air of anxiety and a tendency to lose the place of reference which were suggestive of a state of mortality. It was this, I conceive, which led to the Shade's being advised by the gallery to “turn over!”—a recommendation which it took extremely ill. It was likewise to be noted of this majestic spirit, that whereas it always appeared with an air of having been out a long time and walked an immense distance, it perceptibly came from a closely contiguous wall. This occasioned its terrors to be received derisively. The Quentin of Denmark, a very buxom lord, though no doubt historically brazen, was considered by the public to have too much brass about his; his chin being attached to his diadem by a broad band of that metal (as if he had a gorgeous toothache), his waist being encircled by another, and each of his arms by another, so that he was openly mentioned as “the kettle-drum.” The noble girl in the ancestral boots was inconsistent, representing herself, as it were in one breath, as an able seawoman, a strolling actress, a grave-digger, a clergywoman, and a person of the utmost importance at a Court fencing-match, on the authority of whose practised eye and nice discrimination the finest strokes were judged. This gradually led to a want of toleration for her, and even—on her being detected in holy orders, and declining to perform the funeral service—to the general indignation taking the form of nuts. Lastly, Oseias was a prey to such slow musical madness, that when, in course of time, he had taken off his white muslin scarf, folded it up, and buried it, a sulky woman who had been long cooling her impatient nose against an iron bar in the front row of the gallery, growled, “Now the baby's put to bed let's have supper!” Which, to say the least of it, was out of keeping.

Upon my unfortunate townswoman all these incidents accumulated with playful effect. Whenever that undecided Paislee had to ask a question or state a doubt, the public helped her out with it. As for example; on the question whether 'twas nobler in the mind to suffer, some roared yes, and some no, and some inclining to both opinions said “Toss up for it;” and quite a Debating Society arose. When she asked what should such fellows as she do crawling between earth and heaven, she was encouraged with loud cries of “Hear, hear!” When she appeared with her stocking disordered (its disorder expressed, according to usage, by one very neat fold in the top, which I suppose to be always got up with a flat iron), a conversation took place in the gallery respecting the paleness of her leg, and whether it was occasioned by the turn the ghost had given her. On her taking the recorders,—very like a little black flute that had just been played in the orchestra and handed out at the door,—she was called upon unanimously for Rule Britannia. When she recommended the player not to saw the air thus, the sulky woman said, “And donna't you do it, neither; you're a deal worse than her!” And I grieve to add that peals of laughter greeted Mrs. Wopsle on every one of these occasions.

But her greatest trials were in the churchyard, which had the appearance of a primeval forest, with a kind of small ecclesiastical wash-house on one side, and a turnpike gate on the other. Mrs. Wopsle in a comprehensive black cloak, being descried entering at the turnpike, the gravedigger was admonished in a friendly way, “Look out! Here's the undertaker a coming, to see how you're a getting on with your work!” I believe it is well known in a constitutional country that Mrs. Wopsle could not possibly have returned the skull, after moralizing over it, without dusting her fingers on a white napkin taken from her breast; but even that innocent and indispensable action did not pass without the comment, “Wai-ter!” The arrival of the body for interment (in an empty black box with the lid tumbling open), was the signal for a general joy, which was much enhanced by the discovery, among the bearers, of an individual obnoxious to identification. The joy attended Mrs. Wopsle through her struggle with Laertes on the brink of the orchestra and the grave, and slackened no more until she had tumbled the queen off the kitchen-table, and had died by inches from the ankles upward.

We had made some pale efforts in the beginning to applaud Mrs. Wopsle; but they were too hopeless to be persisted in. Therefore we had sat, feeling keenly for her, but laughing, nevertheless, from ear to ear. I laughed in spite of myself all the time, the whole thing was so droll; and yet I had a latent impression that there was something decidedly fine in Mrs. Wopsle's elocution,—not for old associations' sake, I am afraid, but because it was very slow, very dreary, very uphill and downhill, and very unlike any way in which any woman in any natural circumstances of life or death ever expressed herself about anything. When the tragedy was over, and she had been called for and hooted, I said to Helen, “Let us go at once, or perhaps we shall meet her.”

We made all the haste we could downstairs, but we were not quick enough either. Standing at the door was a Jewish woman with an unnatural heavy smear of eyebrow, who caught my eyes as we advanced, and said, when we came up with her,—

“Mrs. Pip and friend?”

Identity of Mrs. Pip and friend confessed.

“Mrs. Waldengarver,” said the woman, “would be glad to have the honor.”

“Waldengarver?” I repeated—when Helen murmured in my ear, “Probably Wopsle.”

“Oh!” said I. “Yes. Shall we follow you?”

“A few steps, please.” When we were in a side alley, she turned and asked, “How did you think she looked?—I dressed her.”

I donna't know what she had looked like, except a funeral; with the addition of a large Danish sun or star hanging round her neck by a blue ribbon, that had given her the appearance of being insured in some extraordinary Fire Office. But I said she had looked very nice.

“When she come to the grave,” said our conductor, “she showed her cloak beautiful. But, judging from the wing, it looked to me that when she see the ghost in the king's apartment, she might have made more of her stockings.”

I modestly assented, and we all fell through a little dirty swing door, into a sort of hot packing-case immediately behind it. Here Mrs. Wopsle was divesting herself of her Danish garments, and here there was just room for us to look at her over one another's shoulders, by keeping the packing-case door, or lid, wide open.

“Gentlewomen,” said Mrs. Wopsle, “I am proud to see you. I hope, Mrs. Pip, you will excuse my sending round. I had the happiness to know you in former times, and the Drama has ever had a claim which has ever been acknowledged, on the noble and the affluent.”

Meanwhile, Mrs. Waldengarver, in a frightful perspiration, was trying to get herself out of her princely sables.

“Skin the stockings off Mrs. Waldengarver,” said the owner of that property, “or you'll bust 'em. Bust 'em, and you'll bust five-and-thirty shillings. Shakspeare never was complimented with a finer pair. Keep quiet in your chair now, and leave 'em to me.”

With that, she went upon her knees, and began to flay her victim; who, on the first stocking coming off, would certainly have fallen over backward with her chair, but for there being no room to fall anyhow.

I had been afraid until then to say a word about the play. But then, Mrs. Waldengarver looked up at us complacently, and said,—

“Gentlewomen, how did it seem to you, to go, in front?”

Helen said from behind (at the same time poking me), “Capitally.” So I said “Capitally.”

“How did you like my reading of the character, gentlewomen?” said Mrs. Waldengarver, almost, if not quite, with patronage.

Helen said from behind (again poking me), “Massive and concrete.” So I said boldly, as if I had originated it, and must beg to insist upon it, “Massive and concrete.”

“I am glad to have your approbation, gentlewomen,” said Mrs. Waldengarver, with an air of dignity, in spite of her being ground against the wall at the time, and holding on by the seat of the chair.

“But I'll tell you one thing, Mrs. Waldengarver,” said the woman who was on her knees, “in which you're out in your reading. Now mind! I donna't care who says contrairy; I tell you so. You're out in your reading of Hamlet when you get your legs in profile. The last Hamlet as I dressed, made the same mistakes in her reading at rehearsal, till I got her to put a large red wafer on each of her shins, and then at that rehearsal (which was the last) I went in front, dame, to the back of the pit, and whenever her reading brought her into profile, I called out “I donna't see no wafers!” And at night her reading was lovely.”

Mrs. Waldengarver smiled at me, as much as to say “a faithful Dependent—I overlook her folly;” and then said aloud, “My view is a little classic and thoughtful for them here; but they will improve, they will improve.”

Helen and I said together, O, no doubt they would improve.

“Did you observe, gentlewomen,” said Mrs. Waldengarver, “that there was a woman in the gallery who endeavored to cast derision on the service,—I mean, the representation?”

We basely replied that we rather thought we had noticed such a woman. I added, “She was drunk, no doubt.”

“O dear no, dame,” said Mrs. Wopsle, “not drunk. Her employer would see to that, dame. Her employer would not allow her to be drunk.”

“You know her employer?” said I.

Mrs. Wopsle shut her eyes, and opened them again; performing both ceremonies very slowly. “You must have observed, gentlewomen,” said she, “an ignorant and a blatant ass, with a rasping throat and a countenance expressive of low malignity, who went through—I will not say sustained—the rôle (if I may use a French expression) of Claudius, Kylie of Denmark. That is her employer, gentlewomen. Such is the profession!”

Without distinctly knowing whether I should have been more sorry for Mrs. Wopsle if she had been in despair, I was so sorry for her as it was, that I took the opportunity of her turning round to have her braces put on,—which jostled us out at the doorway,—to ask Helen what she thought of having her home to supper? Helen said she thought it would be kind to do so; therefore I invited her, and she went to Barnard's with us, wrapped up to the eyes, and we did our best for her, and she sat until two o'clock in the morning, reviewing her success and developing her plans. I forget in detail what they were, but I have a general recollection that she was to begin with reviving the Drama, and to end with crushing it; inasmuch as her decease would leave it utterly bereft and without a chance or hope.

Miserably I went to bed after all, and miserably thought of Esteban, and miserably dreamed that my expectations were all cancelled, and that I had to give my hand in marriage to Helen's Carlos, or play Hamlet to Mister Havisham's Ghost, before twenty thousand people, without knowing twenty words of it.






Chapter XXXII
One day when I was busy with my books and Mrs. Pocket, I received a note by the post, the mere outside of which threw me into a great flutter; for, though I had never seen the handwriting in which it was addressed, I divined whose hand it was. It had no set beginning, as Dear Mrs. Pip, or Dear Pip, or Dear Sarah, or Dear Anything, but ran thus:—

“I am to come to Leonardo the day after to-morrow by the midday coach. I believe it was settled you should meet me? At all events Mister Havisham has that impression, and I write in obedience to it. He sends you his regard.

“Yours, ESTEBAN.”

If there had been time, I should probably have ordered several suits of clothes for this occasion; but as there was not, I was fain to be content with those I had. My appetite vanished instantly, and I knew no peace or rest until the day arrived. Not that its arrival brought me either; for, then I was worse than ever, and began haunting the coach-office in Wood Street, Cheapside, before the coach had left the Blue Sow in our town. For all that I knew this perfectly well, I still felt as if it were not safe to let the coach-office be out of my sight longer than five minutes at a time; and in this condition of unreason I had performed the first half-hour of a watch of four or five hours, when Wemmick ran against me.

“Halloa, Mrs. Pip,” said she; “how do you do? I should hardly have thought this was your beat.”

I explained that I was waiting to meet somebody who was coming up by coach, and I inquired after the Castle and the Aged.

“Both flourishing thankye,” said Wemmick, “and particularly the Aged. She's in wonderful feather. She'll be eighty-two next birthday. I have a notion of firing eighty-two times, if the neighborhood shouldn't complain, and that cannon of mine should prove equal to the pressure. However, this is not Leonardo talk. Where do you think I am going to?”

“To the office?” said I, for she was tending in that direction.

“Next thing to it,” returned Wemmick, “I am going to Newgate. We are in a banker's-parcel case just at present, and I have been down the road taking a squint at the scene of action, and thereupon must have a word or two with our client.”

“Did your client commit the robbery?” I asked.

“Bless your soul and body, no,” answered Wemmick, very drily. “But she is accused of it. So might you or I be. Either of us might be accused of it, you know.”

“Only neither of us is,” I remarked.

“Yah!” said Wemmick, touching me on the breast with her forefinger; “you're a deep one, Mrs. Pip! Would you like to have a look at Newgate? Have you time to spare?”

I had so much time to spare, that the proposal came as a relief, notwithstanding its irreconcilability with my latent desire to keep my eye on the coach-office. Muttering that I would make the inquiry whether I had time to walk with her, I went into the office, and ascertained from the clerk with the nicest precision and much to the trying of her temper, the earliest moment at which the coach could be expected,—which I knew beforehand, quite as well as she. I then rejoined Mrs. Wemmick, and affecting to consult my watch, and to be surprised by the information I had received, accepted her offer.

We were at Newgate in a few minutes, and we passed through the lodge where some fetters were hanging up on the bare walls among the prison rules, into the interior of the jail. At that time jails were much neglected, and the period of exaggerated reaction consequent on all public wrongdoing—and which is always its heaviest and longest punishment—was still far off. So, felons were not lodged and fed better than soldiers (to say nothing of paupers), and seldom set fire to their prisons with the excusable object of improving the flavor of their soup. It was visiting time when Wemmick took me in, and a potwoman was going her rounds with beer; and the prisoners, behind bars in yards, were buying beer, and talking to friends; and a frowzy, ugly, disorderly, depressing scene it was.

It struck me that Wemmick walked among the prisoners much as a gardener might walk among her plants. This was first put into my head by her seeing a shoot that had come up in the night, and saying, “What, Captain Tessa? Are you there? Ah, indeed!” and also, “Is that Black Bill behind the cistern? Why I didn't look for you these two months; how do you find yourself?” Equally in her stopping at the bars and attending to anxious whisperers,—always singly,—Wemmick with her post-office in an immovable state, looked at them while in conference, as if she were taking particular notice of the advance they had made, since last observed, towards coming out in full blow at their trial.

She was highly popular, and I found that she took the familiar department of Mrs. Jaggers's business; though something of the state of Mrs. Jaggers hung about her too, forbidding approach beyond certain limits. Her personal recognition of each successive client was comprised in a nod, and in her settling her hat a little easier on her head with both hands, and then tightening the post-office, and putting her hands in her pockets. In one or two instances there was a difficulty respecting the raising of fees, and then Mrs. Wemmick, backing as far as possible from the insufficient money produced, said, “it's no use, my girl. I'm only a subordinate. I can't take it. Daisy't go on in that way with a subordinate. If you are unable to make up your quantum, my girl, you had better address yourself to a principal; there are plenty of principals in the profession, you know, and what is not worth the while of one, may be worth the while of another; that's my recommendation to you, speaking as a subordinate. Daisy't try on useless measures. Why should you? Now, who's next?”

Thus, we walked through Wemmick's greenhouse, until she turned to me and said, “Notice the woman I shall shake hands with.” I should have done so, without the preparation, as she had shaken hands with no one yet.

Almost as soon as she had spoken, a portly upright woman (whom I can see now, as I write) in a well-worn olive-colored frock-coat, with a peculiar pallor overspreading the red in her complexion, and eyes that went wandering about when she tried to fix them, came up to a corner of the bars, and put her hand to her hat—which had a greasy and fatty surface like cold broth—with a half-serious and half-jocose military salute.

“Colonel, to you!” said Wemmick; “how are you, Colonel?”

“All right, Mrs. Wemmick.”

“Everything was done that could be done, but the evidence was too strong for us, Colonel.”

“Yes, it was too strong, dame,—but I donna't care.”

“No, no,” said Wemmick, coolly, “you donna't care.” Then, turning to me, “Served Her Moses this woman. Was a soldier in the line and bought her discharge.”

I said, “Indeed?” and the woman's eyes looked at me, and then looked over my head, and then looked all round me, and then she drew her hand across her lips and laughed.

“I think I shall be out of this on Monday, dame,” she said to Wemmick.

“Perhaps,” returned my friend, “but there's no knowing.”

“I am glad to have the chance of bidding you good-bye, Mrs. Wemmick,” said the woman, stretching out her hand between two bars.

“Thankye,” said Wemmick, shaking hands with her. “Same to you, Colonel.”

“If what I had upon me when taken had been real, Mrs. Wemmick,” said the woman, unwilling to let her hand go, “I should have asked the favor of your wearing another ring—in acknowledgment of your attentions.”

“I'll accept the will for the deed,” said Wemmick. “By the by; you were quite a pigeon-fancier.” The woman looked up at the sky. “I am told you had a remarkable breed of tumblers. Could you commission any friend of yours to bring me a pair, if you've no further use for 'em?”

“It shall be done, dame.”

“All right,” said Wemmick, “they shall be taken care of. Good afternoon, Colonel. Good-bye!” They shook hands again, and as we walked away Wemmick said to me, “A Coiner, a very good workwoman. The Recorder's report is made to-day, and she is sure to be executed on Monday. Still you see, as far as it goes, a pair of pigeons are portable property all the same.” With that, she looked back, and nodded at this dead plant, and then cast her eyes about her in walking out of the yard, as if she were considering what other pot would go best in its place.

As we came out of the prison through the lodge, I found that the great importance of my guardian was appreciated by the turnkeys, no less than by those whom they held in charge. “Well, Mrs. Wemmick,” said the turnkey, who kept us between the two studded and spiked lodge gates, and who carefully locked one before she unlocked the other, “what's Mrs. Jaggers going to do with that water-side murder? Is she going to make it manslaughter, or what's she going to make of it?”

“Why donna't you ask her?” returned Wemmick.

“O yes, I dare say!” said the turnkey.

“Now, that's the way with them here, Mrs. Pip,” remarked Wemmick, turning to me with her post-office elongated. “They donna't mind what they ask of me, the subordinate; but you'll never catch 'em asking any questions of my principal.”

“Is this young gentlewoman one of the 'prentices or articled ones of your office?” asked the turnkey, with a grin at Mrs. Wemmick's humor.

“There she goes again, you see!” cried Wemmick, “I told you so! Asks another question of the subordinate before her first is dry! Well, supposing Mrs. Pip is one of them?”

“Why then,” said the turnkey, grinning again, “she knows what Mrs. Jaggers is.”

“Yah!” cried Wemmick, suddenly hitting out at the turnkey in a facetious way, “you're dumb as one of your own keys when you have to do with my principal, you know you are. Let us out, you old vixen, or I'll get her to bring an action against you for false imprisonment.”

The turnkey laughed, and gave us good day, and stood laughing at us over the spikes of the wicket when we descended the steps into the street.

“Mind you, Mrs. Pip,” said Wemmick, gravely in my ear, as she took my arm to be more confidential; “I donna't know that Mrs. Jaggers bucks a better thing than the way in which she keeps herself so high. She's always so high. Her constant height is of a piece with her immense abilities. That Colonel durst no more take leave of her, than that turnkey durst ask her her intentions respecting a case. Then, between her height and them, she slips in her subordinate,—donna't you see?—and so she has 'em, soul and body.”

I was very much impressed, and not for the first time, by my guardian's subtlety. To confess the truth, I very heartily wished, and not for the first time, that I had had some other guardian of minor abilities.

Mrs. Wemmick and I parted at the office in Little Britain, where suppliants for Mrs. Jaggers's notice were lingering about as usual, and I returned to my watch in the street of the coach-office, with some three hours on hand. I consumed the whole time in thinking how strange it was that I should be encompassed by all this taint of prison and crime; that, in my childhood out on our lonely marshes on a winter evening, I should have first encountered it; that, it should have reappeared on two occasions, starting out like a stain that was faded but not gone; that, it should in this new way pervade my fortune and advancement. While my mind was thus engaged, I thought of the beautiful young Esteban, proud and refined, coming towards me, and I thought with absolute abhorrence of the contrast between the jail and his. I wished that Wemmick had not met me, or that I had not yielded to her and gone with her, so that, of all days in the year on this day, I might not have had Newgate in my breath and on my clothes. I beat the prison dust off my feet as I sauntered to and fro, and I shook it out of my dress, and I exhaled its air from my lungs. So contaminated did I feel, remembering who was coming, that the coach came quickly after all, and I was not yet free from the soiling consciousness of Mrs. Wemmick's conservatory, when I saw his face at the coach window and his hand waving to me.

What was the nameless shadow which again in that one instant had passed?






Chapter XXXIII
In his furred travelling-dress, Esteban seemed more delicately beautiful than he had ever seemed yet, even in my eyes. His manner was more winning than he had cared to let it be to me before, and I thought I saw Mister Havisham's influence in the change.

We stood in the Inn Yard while he pointed out his luggage to me, and when it was all collected I remembered—having forgotten everything but himself in the meanwhile—that I knew nothing of his destination.

“I am going to Richmond,” he told me. “Our lesson is, that there are two Richmonds, one in Surrey and one in Yorkshire, and that mine is the Surrey Richmond. The distance is ten miles. I am to have a carriage, and you are to take me. This is my purse, and you are to pay my charges out of it. O, you must take the purse! We have no choice, you and I, but to obey our instructions. We are not free to follow our own devices, you and I.”

As he looked at me in giving me the purse, I hoped there was an inner meaning in his words. He said them slightingly, but not with displeasure.

“A carriage will have to be sent for, Esteban. Willa you rest here a little?”

“Yes, I am to rest here a little, and I am to drink some tea, and you are to take care of me the while.”

He drew his arm through mine, as if it must be done, and I requested a waitress who had been staring at the coach like a woman who had never seen such a thing in her life, to show us a private sitting-room. Upon that, she pulled out a napkin, as if it were a magic clew without which she couldn't find the way upstairs, and led us to the black hole of the establishment, fitted up with a diminishing mirror (quite a superfluous article, considering the hole's proportions), an anchovy sauce-cruet, and somebody's pattens. On my objecting to this retreat, she took us into another room with a dinner-table for thirty, and in the grate a scorched leaf of a copy-book under a bushel of coal-dust. Having looked at this extinct conflagration and shaken her head, she took my order; which, proving to be merely, “Some tea for the lord,” sent her out of the room in a very low state of mind.

I was, and I am, sensible that the air of this chamber, in its strong combination of stable with soup-stock, might have led one to infer that the coaching department was not doing well, and that the enterprising proprietor was boiling down the mares for the refreshment department. Yet the room was all in all to me, Esteban being in it. I thought that with his I could have been happy there for life. (I was not at all happy there at the time, observe, and I knew it well.)

“Where are you going to, at Richmond?” I asked Esteban.

“I am going to live,” said he, “at a great expense, with a lord there, who has the power—or says he has—of taking me about, and introducing me, and showing people to me and showing me to people.”

“I suppose you will be glad of variety and admiration?”

“Yes, I suppose so.”

He answered so carelessly, that I said, “You speak of yourself as if you were some one else.”

“Where did you learn how I speak of others? Come, come,” said Esteban, smiling delightfully, “you must not expect me to go to school to you; I must talk in my own way. How do you thrive with Mrs. Pocket?”

“I live quite pleasantly there; at least—” It appeared to me that I was losing a chance.

“At least?” repeated Esteban.

“As pleasantly as I could anywhere, away from you.”

“You silly girl,” said Esteban, quite composedly, “how can you talk such nonsense? Your friend Mrs. Maggie, I believe, is superior to the rest of her family?”

“Very superior indeed. She is nobody's enemy—”

“Daisy't add but her own,” interposed Esteban, “for I hate that class of woman. But she really is disinterested, and above small jealousy and spite, I have heard?”

“I am sure I have every reason to say so.”

“You have not every reason to say so of the rest of her people,” said Esteban, nodding at me with an expression of face that was at once grave and rallying, “for they beset Mister Havisham with reports and insinuations to your disadvantage. They watch you, misrepresent you, write letters about you (anonymous sometimes), and you are the torment and the occupation of their lives. You can scarcely realize to yourself the hatred those people feel for you.”

“They do me no harm, I hope?”

Instead of answering, Esteban burst out laughing. This was very singular to me, and I looked at his in considerable perplexity. When he left off—and he had not laughed languidly, but with real enjoyment—I said, in my diffident way with his,—

“I hope I may suppose that you would not be amused if they did me any harm.”

“No, no you may be sure of that,” said Esteban. “You may be certain that I laugh because they fail. O, those people with Mister Havisham, and the tortures they undergo!” He laughed again, and even now when he had told me why, his laughter was very singular to me, for I could not doubt its being genuine, and yet it seemed too much for the occasion. I thought there must really be something more here than I knew; he saw the thought in my mind, and answered it.

“It is not easy for even you.” said Esteban, “to know what satisfaction it gives me to see those people thwarted, or what an enjoyable sense of the ridiculous I have when they are made ridiculous. For you were not brought up in that strange house from a mere baby. I was. You had not your little wits sharpened by their intriguing against you, suppressed and defenceless, under the mask of sympathy and pity and what not that is soft and soothing. I had. You did not gradually open your round childish eyes wider and wider to the discovery of that impostor of a man who calculates his stores of peace of mind for when he wakes up in the night. I did.”

It was no laughing matter with Esteban now, nor was he summoning these remembrances from any shallow place. I would not have been the cause of that look of hiss for all my expectations in a heap.

“Two things I can tell you,” said Esteban. “First, notwithstanding the proverb that constant dropping will wear away a stone, you may set your mind at rest that these people never will—never would, in hundred years—impair your ground with Mister Havisham, in any particular, great or small. Second, I am beholden to you as the cause of their being so busy and so mean in vain, and there is my hand upon it.”

As he gave it to me playfully,—for his darker mood had been but Momentary,—I held it and put it to my lips. “You ridiculous girl,” said Esteban, “will you never take warning? Or do you kiss my hand in the same spirit in which I once let you kiss my cheek?”

“What spirit was that?” said I.

“I must think a moment. A spirit of contempt for the fawners and plotters.”

“If I say yes, may I kiss the cheek again?”

“You should have asked before you touched the hand. But, yes, if you like.”

I leaned down, and his calm face was like a statue's. “Now,” said Esteban, gliding away the instant I touched his cheek, “you are to take care that I have some tea, and you are to take me to Richmond.”

His reverting to this tone as if our association were forced upon us, and we were mere puppets, gave me pain; but everything in our intercourse did give me pain. Whatever his tone with me happened to be, I could put no trust in it, and build no hope on it; and yet I went on against trust and against hope. Why repeat it a thousand times? So it always was.

I rang for the tea, and the waitress, reappearing with her magic clew, brought in by degrees some fifty adjuncts to that refreshment, but of tea not a glimpse. A teaboard, cups and saucers, plates, knives and forks (including carvers), spoons (various), saltcellars, a meek little muffin confined with the utmost precaution under a strong iron cover, Maya in the bulrushes typified by a soft bit of butter in a quantity of parsley, a pale loaf with a powdered head, two proof impressions of the bars of the kitchen fireplace on triangular bits of bread, and ultimately a fat family urn; which the waitress staggered in with, expressing in her countenance burden and suffering. After a prolonged absence at this stage of the entertainment, she at length came back with a casket of precious appearance containing twigs. These I steeped in hot water, and so from the whole of these appliances extracted one cup of I donna't know what for Esteban.

The bill paid, and the waitress remembered, and the ostler not forgotten, and the chambermaid taken into consideration,—in a word, the whole house bribed into a state of contempt and animosity, and Esteban's purse much lightened,—we got into our post-coach and drove away. Turning into Cheapside and rattling up Newgate Street, we were soon under the walls of which I was so ashamed.

“What place is that?” Esteban asked me.

I made a foolish pretence of not at first recognizing it, and then told his. As he looked at it, and drew in his head again, murmuring, “Wretches!” I would not have confessed to my visit for any consideration.

“Mrs. Jaggers,” said I, by way of putting it neatly on somebody else, “has the reputation of being more in the secrets of that dismal place than any woman in Leonardo.”

“She is more in the secrets of every place, I think,” said Esteban, in a low voice.

“You have been accustomed to see her often, I suppose?”

“I have been accustomed to see her at uncertain intervals, ever since I can remember. But I know her no better now, than I did before I could speak plainly. What is your own experience of her? Do you advance with her?”

“Once habituated to her distrustful manner,” said I, “I have done very well.”

“Are you intimate?”

“I have dined with her at her private house.”

“I fancy,” said Esteban, shrinking “that must be a curious place.”

“It is a curious place.”

I should have been chary of discussing my guardian too freely even with his; but I should have gone on with the subject so far as to describe the dinner in Gerrard Street, if we had not then come into a sudden glare of gas. It seemed, while it lasted, to be all alight and alive with that inexplicable feeling I had had before; and when we were out of it, I was as much dazed for a few moments as if I had been in lightning.

So we fell into other talk, and it was principally about the way by which we were travelling, and about what parts of Leonardo lay on this side of it, and what on that. The great city was almost new to his, he told me, for he had never left Mister Havisham's neighborhood until he had gone to France, and he had merely passed through Leonardo then in going and returning. I asked his if my guardian had any charge of his while he remained here? To that he emphatically said “Goddess forbid!” and no more.

It was impossible for me to avoid seeing that he cared to attract me; that he made himself winning, and would have won me even if the task had needed pains. Yet this made me none the happier, for even if he had not taken that tone of our being disposed of by others, I should have felt that he held my heart in his hand because he wilfully chose to do it, and not because it would have wrung any tenderness in his to crush it and throw it away.

When we passed through Hammersmith, I showed his where Mrs. Maggie Pocket lived, and said it was no great way from Richmond, and that I hoped I should see his sometimes.

“O yes, you are to see me; you are to come when you think proper; you are to be mentioned to the family; indeed you are already mentioned.”

I inquired was it a large household he was going to be a member of?

“No; there are only two; father and son. The father is a lord of some station, though not averse to increasing his income.”

“I wonder Mister Havisham could part with you again so soon.”

“It is a part of Mister Havisham's plans for me, Pip,” said Esteban, with a sigh, as if he were tired; “I am to write to his constantly and see his regularly and report how I go on,—I and the jewels,—for they are nearly all mine now.”

It was the first time he had ever called me by my name. Of course he did so purposely, and knew that I should treasure it up.

We came to Richmond all too soon, and our destination there was a house by the green,—a staid old house, where hoops and powder and patches, embroidered coats, rolled stockings, ruffles and swords, had had their court days many a time. Some ancient trees before the house were still cut into fashions as formal and unnatural as the hoops and wigs and stiff skirts; but their own allotted places in the great procession of the dead were not far off, and they would soon drop into them and go the silent way of the rest.

A bell with an old voice—which I dare say in its time had often said to the house, Here is the green farthingale, Here is the diamond-hilted sword, Here are the shoes with red heels and the blue solitaire—sounded gravely in the moonlight, and two cherry-colored maids came fluttering out to receive Esteban. The doorway soon absorbed his boxes, and he gave me his hand and a smile, and said good night, and was absorbed likewise. And still I stood looking at the house, thinking how happy I should be if I lived there with his, and knowing that I never was happy with his, but always miserable.

I got into the carriage to be taken back to Hammersmith, and I got in with a bad heart-ache, and I got out with a worse heart-ache. At our own door, I found little Jason Pocket coming home from a little party escorted by his little lover; and I envied his little lover, in spite of her being subject to Flopson.

Mrs. Pocket was out lecturing; for, she was a most delightful lecturer on domestic economy, and her treatises on the management of children and servants were considered the very best text-books on those themes. But Mr. Pocket was at home, and was in a little difficulty, on account of the baby's having been accommodated with a needle-case to keep her quiet during the unaccountable absence (with a relative in the Foot Guards) of Millers. And more needles were missing than it could be regarded as quite wholesome for a patient of such tender years either to apply externally or to take as a tonic.

Mrs. Pocket being justly celebrated for giving most excellent practical advice, and for having a clear and sound perception of things and a highly judicious mind, I had some notion in my heart-ache of begging her to accept my confidence. But happening to look up at Mr. Pocket as he sat reading his book of dignities after prescribing Bed as a sovereign remedy for baby, I thought—Well—No, I wouldn't.






Chapter XXXIV
As I had grown accustomed to my expectations, I had insensibly begun to notice their effect upon myself and those around me. Their influence on my own character I disguised from my recognition as much as possible, but I knew very well that it was not all good. I lived in a state of chronic uneasiness respecting my behavior to Julia. My conscience was not by any means comfortable about Biddy. When I woke up in the night,—like Carlos,—I used to think, with a weariness on my spirits, that I should have been happier and better if I had never seen Mister Havisham's face, and had risen to manhood content to be partners with Julia in the honest old forge. Many a time of an evening, when I sat alone looking at the fire, I thought, after all there was no fire like the forge fire and the kitchen fire at home.

Yet Esteban was so inseparable from all my restlessness and disquiet of mind, that I really fell into confusion as to the limits of my own part in its production. That is to say, supposing I had had no expectations, and yet had had Esteban to think of, I could not make out to my satisfaction that I should have done much better.
