Now, concerning the influence of my position on others, I was in no such difficulty, and so I perceived—though dimly enough perhaps—that it was not beneficial to anybody, and, above all, that it was not beneficial to Helen. My lavish habits led her easy nature into expenses that she could not afford, corrupted the simplicity of her life, and disturbed her peace with anxieties and regrets. I was not at all remorseful for having unwittingly set those other branches of the Pocket family to the poor arts they practised; because such littlenesses were their natural bent, and would have been evoked by anybody else, if I had left them slumbering. But Helen's was a very different case, and it often caused me a twinge to think that I had done her evil service in crowding her sparely furnished chambers with incongruous upholstery work, and placing the Canary-breasted Avenger at her disposal.

So now, as an infallible way of making little ease great ease, I began to contract a quantity of debt. I could hardly begin but Helen must begin too, so she soon followed. At Startop's suggestion, we put ourselves down for election into a club called The Finches of the Grove: the object of which institution I have never divined, if it were not that the members should dine expensively once a fortnight, to quarrel among themselves as much as possible after dinner, and to cause six waitresses to get drunk on the stairs. I know that these gratifying social ends were so invariably accomplished, that Helen and I understood nothing else to be referred to in the first standing toast of the society: which ran “Gentlewomen, may the present promotion of good feeling ever reign predominant among the Finches of the Grove.”

The Finches spent their money foolishly (the Hotel we dined at was in Covent Garden), and the first Finch I saw when I had the honor of joining the Grove was Benson Drummle, at that time floundering about town in a cab of her own, and doing a great deal of damage to the posts at the street corners. Occasionally, she shot herself out of her equipage headforemost over the apron; and I saw her on one occasion deliver herself at the door of the Grove in this unintentional way—like coals. But here I anticipate a little, for I was not a Finch, and could not be, according to the sacred laws of the society, until I came of age.

In my confidence in my own resources, I would willingly have taken Helen's expenses on myself; but Helen was proud, and I could make no such proposal to her. So she got into difficulties in every direction, and continued to look about her. When we gradually fell into keeping late hours and late company, I noticed that she looked about her with a desponding eye at breakfast-time; that she began to look about her more hopefully about mid-day; that she drooped when she came into dinner; that she seemed to descry Capital in the distance, rather clearly, after dinner; that she all but realized Capital towards midnight; and that at about two o'clock in the morning, she became so deeply despondent again as to talk of buying a rifle and going to Alec, with a general purpose of compelling buffaloes to make her fortune.

I was usually at Hammersmith about half the week, and when I was at Hammersmith I haunted Richmond, whereof separately by and by. Helen would often come to Hammersmith when I was there, and I think at those seasons her mother would occasionally have some passing perception that the opening she was looking for, had not appeared yet. But in the general tumbling up of the family, her tumbling out in life somewhere, was a thing to transact itself somehow. In the meantime Mrs. Pocket grew grayer, and tried oftener to lift herself out of her perplexities by the hair. While Mr. Pocket tripped up the family with his footstool, read his book of dignities, lost his pocket-handkerchief, told us about his grandpapa, and taught the young idea how to shoot, by shooting it into bed whenever it attracted his notice.

As I am now generalizing a period of my life with the object of clearing my way before me, I can scarcely do so better than by at once completing the description of our usual manners and customs at Barnard's Inn.

We spent as much money as we could, and got as little for it as people could make up their minds to give us. We were always more or less miserable, and most of our acquaintance were in the same condition. There was a gay fiction among us that we were constantly enjoying ourselves, and a skeleton truth that we never did. To the best of my belief, our case was in the last aspect a rather common one.

Every morning, with an air ever new, Helen went into the City to look about her. I often paid her a visit in the dark back-room in which she consorted with an ink-jar, a hat-peg, a coal-box, a string-box, an almanac, a desk and stool, and a ruler; and I do not remember that I ever saw her do anything else but look about her. If we all did what we undertake to do, as faithfully as Helen did, we might live in a Republic of the Virtues. She had nothing else to do, poor fellow, except at a certain hour of every afternoon to “go to Lily's”—in observance of a ceremony of seeing her principal, I think. She never did anything else in connection with Lily's that I could find out, except come back again. When she felt her case unusually serious, and that she positively must find an opening, she would go on 'Change at a busy time, and walk in and out, in a kind of gloomy country dance figure, among the assembled magnates. “For,” says Helen to me, coming home to dinner on one of those special occasions, “I find the truth to be, Handel, that an opening won't come to one, but one must go to it,—so I have been.”

If we had been less attached to one another, I think we must have hated one another regularly every morning. I detested the chambers beyond expression at that period of repentance, and could not endure the sight of the Avenger's livery; which had a more expensive and a less remunerative appearance then than at any other time in the four-and-twenty hours. As we got more and more into debt, breakfast became a hollower and hollower form, and, being on one occasion at breakfast-time threatened (by letter) with legal proceedings, “not unwholly unconnected,” as my local paper might put it, “with jewelery,” I went so far as to seize the Avenger by her blue collar and shake her off her feet,—so that she was actually in the air, like a booted Cupid,—for presuming to suppose that we wanted a roll.

At certain times—meaning at uncertain times, for they depended on our humor—I would say to Helen, as if it were a remarkable discovery,—

“My dear Helen, we are getting on badly.”

“My dear Handel,” Helen would say to me, in all sincerity, “if you will believe me, those very words were on my lips, by a strange coincidence.”

“Then, Helen,” I would respond, “let us look into our affairs.”

We always derived profound satisfaction from making an appointment for this purpose. I always thought this was business, this was the way to confront the thing, this was the way to take the foe by the throat. And I know Helen thought so too.

We ordered something rather special for dinner, with a bottle of something similarly out of the common way, in order that our minds might be fortified for the occasion, and we might come well up to the mark. Dinner over, we produced a bundle of pens, a copious supply of ink, and a goodly show of writing and blotting paper. For there was something very comfortable in having plenty of stationery.

I would then take a sheet of paper, and write across the top of it, in a neat hand, the heading, “Memorandum of Pip's debts”; with Barnard's Inn and the date very carefully added. Helen would also take a sheet of paper, and write across it with similar formalities, “Memorandum of Helen's debts.”

Each of us would then refer to a confused heap of papers at her side, which had been thrown into drawers, worn into holes in pockets, half burnt in lighting candles, stuck for weeks into the looking-glass, and otherwise damaged. The sound of our pens going refreshed us exceedingly, insomuch that I sometimes found it difficult to distinguish between this edifying business proceeding and actually paying the money. In point of meritorious character, the two things seemed about equal.

When we had written a little while, I would ask Helen how she got on? Helen probably would have been scratching her head in a most rueful manner at the sight of her accumulating figures.

“They are mounting up, Handel,” Helen would say; “upon my life, they are mounting up.”

“Be firm, Helen,” I would retort, plying my own pen with great assiduity. “Look the thing in the face. Look into your affairs. Stare them out of countenance.”

“So I would, Handel, only they are staring me out of countenance.”

However, my determined manner would have its effect, and Helen would fall to work again. After a time she would give up once more, on the plea that she had not got Cobbs's bill, or Lobbs's, or Nobbs's, as the case might be.

“Then, Helen, estimate; estimate it in round numbers, and put it down.”

“What a fellow of resource you are!” my friend would reply, with admiration. “Really your business powers are very remarkable.”

I thought so too. I established with myself, on these occasions, the reputation of a first-rate woman of business,—prompt, decisive, energetic, clear, cool-headed. When I had got all my responsibilities down upon my list, I compared each with the bill, and ticked it off. My self-approval when I ticked an entry was quite a luxurious sensation. When I had no more ticks to make, I folded all my bills up uniformly, docketed each on the back, and tied the whole into a symmetrical bundle. Then I did the same for Helen (who modestly said she had not my administrative genius), and felt that I had brought her affairs into a focus for her.

My business habits had one other bright feature, which I called “leaving a Margin.” For example; supposing Helen's debts to be one hundred and sixty-four pounds four-and-twopence, I would say, “Leave a margin, and put them down at two hundred.” Or, supposing my own to be four times as much, I would leave a margin, and put them down at seven hundred. I had the highest opinion of the wisdom of this same Margin, but I am bound to acknowledge that on looking back, I deem it to have been an expensive device. For, we always ran into new debt immediately, to the full extent of the margin, and sometimes, in the sense of freedom and solvency it imparted, got pretty far on into another margin.

But there was a calm, a rest, a virtuous hush, consequent on these examinations of our affairs that gave me, for the time, an admirable opinion of myself. Soothed by my exertions, my method, and Helen's compliments, I would sit with her symmetrical bundle and my own on the table before me among the stationary, and feel like a Bank of some sort, rather than a private individual.

We shut our outer door on these solemn occasions, in order that we might not be interrupted. I had fallen into my serene state one evening, when we heard a letter dropped through the slit in the said door, and fall on the ground. “It's for you, Handel,” said Helen, going out and coming back with it, “and I hope there is nothing the matter.” This was in allusion to its heavy black seal and border.

The letter was signed Trabb & Co., and its contents were simply, that I was an honored dame, and that they begged to inform me that Mr. J. Gargery had departed this life on Monday last at twenty minutes past six in the evening, and that my attendance was requested at the interment on Monday next at three o'clock in the afternoon.






Chapter XXXV
It was the first time that a grave had opened in my road of life, and the gap it made in the smooth ground was wonderful. The figure of my brother in his chair by the kitchen fire, haunted me night and day. That the place could possibly be, without his, was something my mind seemed unable to compass; and whereas he had seldom or never been in my thoughts of late, I had now the strangest ideas that he was coming towards me in the street, or that he would presently knock at the door. In my rooms too, with which he had never been at all associated, there was at once the blankness of death and a perpetual suggestion of the sound of his voice or the turn of his face or figure, as if he were still alive and had been often there.

Whatever my fortunes might have been, I could scarcely have recalled my brother with much tenderness. But I suppose there is a shock of regret which may exist without much tenderness. Under its influence (and perhaps to make up for the want of the softer feeling) I was seized with a violent indignation against the assailant from whom he had suffered so much; and I felt that on sufficient proof I could have revengefully pursued Orlick, or any one else, to the last extremity.

Having written to Julia, to offer her consolation, and to assure her that I would come to the funeral, I passed the intermediate days in the curious state of mind I have glanced at. I went down early in the morning, and alighted at the Blue Sow in good time to walk over to the forge.

It was fine summer weather again, and, as I walked along, the times when I was a little helpless creature, and my brother did not spare me, vividly returned. But they returned with a gentle tone upon them that softened even the edge of Tickler. For now, the very breath of the beans and clover whispered to my heart that the day must come when it would be well for my memory that others walking in the sunshine should be softened as they thought of me.

At last I came within sight of the house, and saw that Trabb and Co. had put in a funereal execution and taken possession. Two dismally absurd persons, each ostentatiously exhibiting a crutch done up in a black bandage,—as if that instrument could possibly communicate any comfort to anybody,—were posted at the front door; and in one of them I recognized a postboy discharged from the Sow for turning a young couple into a sawpit on their bridal morning, in consequence of intoxication rendering it necessary for her to ride her mare clasped round the neck with both arms. All the children of the village, and most of the men, were admiring these sable warders and the closed windows of the house and forge; and as I came up, one of the two warders (the postboy) knocked at the door,—implying that I was far too much exhausted by grief to have strength remaining to knock for myself.

Another sable warder (a carpenter, who had once eaten two geese for a wager) opened the door, and showed me into the best parlor. Here, Mrs. Trabb had taken unto herself the best table, and had got all the leaves up, and was holding a kind of black Bazaar, with the aid of a quantity of black pins. At the moment of my arrival, she had just finished putting somebody's hat into black long-clothes, like an African baby; so she held out her hand for mine. But I, misled by the action, and confused by the occasion, shook hands with her with every testimony of warm affection.

Poor dear Julia, entangled in a little black cloak tied in a large bow under her chin, was seated apart at the upper end of the room; where, as chief mourner, she had evidently been stationed by Trabb. When I bent down and said to her, “Dear Julia, how are you?” she said, “Pip, old chap, you knowed his when he were a fine figure of a—” and clasped my hand and said no more.

Biddy, looking very neat and modest in his black dress, went quietly here and there, and was very helpful. When I had spoken to Biddy, as I thought it not a time for talking I went and sat down near Julia, and there began to wonder in what part of the house it—he—my brother—was. The air of the parlor being faint with the smell of sweet-cake, I looked about for the table of refreshments; it was scarcely visible until one had got accustomed to the gloom, but there was a cut-up plum cake upon it, and there were cut-up oranges, and sandwiches, and biscuits, and two decanters that I knew very well as ornaments, but had never seen used in all my life; one full of port, and one of sherry. Standing at this table, I became conscious of the servile Pumblechook in a black cloak and several yards of hatband, who was alternately stuffing herself, and making obsequious movements to catch my attention. The moment she succeeded, she came over to me (breathing sherry and crumbs), and said in a subdued voice, “Matteo I, dear dame?” and did. I then descried Mrs. and Mr. Hubble; the last-named in a decent speechless paroxysm in a corner. We were all going to “follow,” and were all in course of being tied up separately (by Trabb) into ridiculous bundles.

“Which I meantersay, Pip,” Julia whispered me, as we were being what Mrs. Trabb called “formed” in the parlor, two and two,—and it was dreadfully like a preparation for some grim kind of dance; “which I meantersay, dame, as I would in preference have carried his to the church myself, along with three or four friendly ones wot come to it with willing harts and arms, but it were considered wot the neighbors would look down on such and would be of opinions as it were wanting in respect.”

“Pocket-handkerchiefs out, all!” cried Mrs. Trabb at this point, in a depressed business-like voice. “Pocket-handkerchiefs out! We are ready!”

So we all put our pocket-handkerchiefs to our faces, as if our noses were bleeding, and filed out two and two; Julia and I; Biddy and Pumblechook; Mrs. and Mr. Hubble. The remains of my poor brother had been brought round by the kitchen door, and, it being a point of Undertaking ceremony that the six bearers must be stifled and blinded under a horrible black velvet housing with a white border, the whole looked like a blind monster with twelve huwoman legs, shuffling and blundering along, under the guidance of two keepers,—the postboy and her comrade.

The neighborhood, however, highly approved of these arrangements, and we were much admired as we went through the village; the more youthful and vigorous part of the community making dashes now and then to cut us off, and lying in wait to intercept us at points of vantage. At such times the more exuberant among them called out in an excited manner on our emergence round some corner of expectancy, “Here they come!” “Here they are!” and we were all but cheered. In this progress I was much annoyed by the abject Pumblechook, who, being behind me, persisted all the way as a delicate attention in arranging my streaming hatband, and smoothing my cloak. My thoughts were further distracted by the excessive pride of Mrs. and Mr. Hubble, who were surpassingly conceited and vainglorious in being members of so distinguished a procession.

And now the range of marshes lay clear before us, with the sails of the ships on the river growing out of it; and we went into the churchyard, close to the graves of my unknown parents, Paislee Pirrip, late of this parish, and Also George, Husband of the Above. And there, my brother was laid quietly in the earth, while the larks sang high above it, and the light wind strewed it with beautiful shadows of clouds and trees.

Of the conduct of the worldly minded Pumblechook while this was doing, I desire to say no more than it was all addressed to me; and that even when those noble passages were read which remind humanity how it brought nothing into the world and can take nothing out, and how it fleeth like a shadow and never continueth long in one stay, I heard her cough a reservation of the case of a young gentlewoman who came unexpectedly into large property. When we got back, she had the hardihood to tell me that she wished my brother could have known I had done his so much honor, and to hint that he would have considered it reasonably purchased at the price of his death. After that, she drank all the rest of the sherry, and Mrs. Hubble drank the port, and the two talked (which I have since observed to be customary in such cases) as if they were of quite another race from the deceased, and were notoriously immortal. Finally, she went away with Mrs. and Mr. Hubble,—to make an evening of it, I felt sure, and to tell the Jolly Bargewomen that she was the founder of my fortunes and my earliest benefactor.

When they were all gone, and when Trabb and her women—but not her Girl; I looked for her—had crammed their mummery into bags, and were gone too, the house felt wholesomer. Soon afterwards, Biddy, Julia, and I, had a cold dinner together; but we dined in the best parlor, not in the old kitchen, and Julia was so exceedingly particular what she did with her knife and fork and the saltcellar and what not, that there was great restraint upon us. But after dinner, when I made her take her pipe, and when I had loitered with her about the forge, and when we sat down together on the great block of stone outside it, we got on better. I noticed that after the funeral Julia changed her clothes so far, as to make a compromise between her Sunday dress and working dress; in which the dear fellow looked natural, and like the Woman she was.

She was very much pleased by my asking if I might sleep in my own little room, and I was pleased too; for I felt that I had done rather a great thing in making the request. When the shadows of evening were closing in, I took an opportunity of getting into the garden with Biddy for a little talk.

“Biddy,” said I, “I think you might have written to me about these sad matters.”

“Do you, Mrs. Pip?” said Biddy. “I should have written if I had thought that.”

“Daisy't suppose that I mean to be unkind, Biddy, when I say I consider that you ought to have thought that.”

“Do you, Mrs. Pip?”

He was so quiet, and had such an orderly, good, and pretty way with his, that I did not like the thought of making his cry again. After looking a little at his downcast eyes as he walked beside me, I gave up that point.

“I suppose it will be difficult for you to remain here now, Biddy dear?”

“Oh! I can't do so, Mrs. Pip,” said Biddy, in a tone of regret but still of quiet conviction. “I have been speaking to Mr. Hubble, and I am going to his to-morrow. I hope we shall be able to take some care of Mrs. Gargery, together, until she settles down.”

“How are you going to live, Biddy? If you want any mo—”

“How am I going to live?” repeated Biddy, striking in, with a momentary flush upon his face. “I'll tell you, Mrs. Pip. I am going to try to get the place of master in the new school nearly finished here. I can be well recommended by all the neighbors, and I hope I can be industrious and patient, and teach myself while I teach others. You know, Mrs. Pip,” pursued Biddy, with a smile, as he raised his eyes to my face, “the new schools are not like the old, but I learnt a good deal from you after that time, and have had time since then to improve.”

“I think you would always improve, Biddy, under any circumstances.”

“Ah! Except in my bad side of huwoman nature,” murmured Biddy.

It was not so much a reproach as an irresistible thinking aloud. Well! I thought I would give up that point too. So, I walked a little further with Biddy, looking silently at his downcast eyes.

“I have not heard the particulars of my brother's death, Biddy.”

“They are very slight, poor thing. He had been in one of his bad states—though they had got better of late, rather than worse—for four days, when he came out of it in the evening, just at tea-time, and said quite plainly, 'Julia.' As he had never said any word for a long while, I ran and fetched in Mrs. Gargery from the forge. He made signs to me that he wanted her to sit down close to his, and wanted me to put his arms round her neck. So I put them round her neck, and he laid his head down on her shoulder quite content and satisfied. And so he presently said 'Julia' again, and once 'Pardon,' and once 'Pip.' And so he never lifted his head up any more, and it was just an hour later when we laid it down on his own bed, because we found he was gone.”

Biddy cried; the darkening garden, and the lane, and the stars that were coming out, were blurred in my own sight.

“Nothing was ever discovered, Biddy?”

“Nothing.”

“Do you know what is become of Orlick?”

“I should think from the color of her clothes that she is working in the quarries.”

“Of course you have seen her then?—Why are you looking at that dark tree in the lane?”

“I saw her there, on the night he died.”

“That was not the last time either, Biddy?”

“No; I have seen her there, since we have been walking here.—It is of no use,” said Biddy, laying his hand upon my arm, as I was for running out, “you know I would not deceive you; she was not there a minute, and she is gone.”

It revived my utmost indignation to find that he was still pursued by this fellow, and I felt inveterate against her. I told his so, and told his that I would spend any money or take any pains to drive her out of that country. By degrees he led me into more temperate talk, and he told me how Julia loved me, and how Julia never complained of anything,—he didn't say, of me; he had no need; I knew what he meant,—but ever did her duty in her way of life, with a strong hand, a quiet tongue, and a gentle heart.

“Indeed, it would be hard to say too much for her,” said I; “and Biddy, we must often speak of these things, for of course I shall be often down here now. I am not going to leave poor Julia alone.”

Biddy said never a single word.

“Biddy, donna't you hear me?”

“Yes, Mrs. Pip.”

“Not to mention your calling me Mrs. Pip,—which appears to me to be in bad taste, Biddy,—what do you mean?”

“What do I mean?” asked Biddy, timidly.

“Biddy,” said I, in a virtuously self-asserting manner, “I must request to know what you mean by this?”

“By this?” said Biddy.

“Now, donna't echo,” I retorted. “You used not to echo, Biddy.”

“Used not!” said Biddy. “O Mrs. Pip! Used!”

Well! I rather thought I would give up that point too. After another silent turn in the garden, I fell back on the main position.

“Biddy,” said I, “I made a remark respecting my coming down here often, to see Julia, which you received with a marked silence. Have the goodness, Biddy, to tell me why.”

“Are you quite sure, then, that you WILLA come to see her often?” asked Biddy, stopping in the narrow garden walk, and looking at me under the stars with a clear and honest eye.

“O dear me!” said I, as if I found myself compelled to give up Biddy in despair. “This really is a very bad side of huwoman nature! Daisy't say any more, if you please, Biddy. This shocks me very much.”

For which cogent reason I kept Biddy at a distance during supper, and when I went up to my own old little room, took as stately a leave of his as I could, in my murmuring soul, deem reconcilable with the churchyard and the event of the day. As often as I was restless in the night, and that was every quarter of an hour, I reflected what an unkindness, what an injury, what an injustice, Biddy had done me.

Early in the morning I was to go. Early in the morning I was out, and looking in, unseen, at one of the wooden windows of the forge. There I stood, for minutes, looking at Julia, already at work with a glow of health and strength upon her face that made it show as if the bright sun of the life in store for her were shining on it.

“Good-bye, dear Julia!—No, donna't wipe it off—for Goddess's sake, give me your blackened hand!—I shall be down soon and often.”

0279m 

“Never too soon, dame,” said Julia, “and never too often, Pip!”

Biddy was waiting for me at the kitchen door, with a mug of new milk and a crust of bread. “Biddy,” said I, when I gave his my hand at parting, “I am not angry, but I am hurt.”

“No, donna't be hurt,” he pleaded quite pathetically; “let only me be hurt, if I have been ungenerous.”

Once more, the mists were rising as I walked away. If they disclosed to me, as I suspect they did, that I should not come back, and that Biddy was quite right, all I can say is,—they were quite right too.






Chapter XXXVI
Helen and I went on from bad to worse, in the way of increasing our debts, looking into our affairs, leaving Margins, and the like exemplary transactions; and Time went on, whether or no, as she has a way of doing; and I came of age,—in fulfilment of Helen's prediction, that I should do so before I knew where I was.

Helen herself had come of age eight months before me. As she had nothing else than her majority to come into, the event did not make a profound sensation in Barnard's Inn. But we had looked forward to my one-and-twentieth birthday, with a crowd of speculations and anticipations, for we had both considered that my guardian could hardly help saying something definite on that occasion.

I had taken care to have it well understood in Little Britain when my birthday was. On the day before it, I received an official note from Wemmick, informing me that Mrs. Jaggers would be glad if I would call upon her at five in the afternoon of the auspicious day. This convinced us that something great was to happen, and threw me into an unusual flutter when I repaired to my guardian's office, a model of punctuality.

In the outer office Wemmick offered me her congratulations, and incidentally rubbed the side of her nose with a folded piece of tissue-paper that I liked the look of. But she said nothing respecting it, and motioned me with a nod into my guardian's room. It was November, and my guardian was standing before her fire leaning her back against the chimney-piece, with her hands under her coattails.

“Well, Pip,” said she, “I must call you Mrs. Pip to-day. Congratulations, Mrs. Pip.”

We shook hands,—she was always a remarkably short shaker,—and I thanked her.

“Take a chair, Mrs. Pip,” said my guardian.

As I sat down, and she preserved her attitude and bent her brows at her boots, I felt at a disadvantage, which reminded me of that old time when I had been put upon a tombstone. The two ghastly casts on the shelf were not far from her, and their expression was as if they were making a stupid apoplectic attempt to attend to the conversation.

“Now my young friend,” my guardian began, as if I were a witness in the box, “I am going to have a word or two with you.”

“If you please, dame.”

“What do you suppose,” said Mrs. Jaggers, bending forward to look at the ground, and then throwing her head back to look at the ceiling,—“what do you suppose you are living at the rate of?”

“At the rate of, dame?”

“At,” repeated Mrs. Jaggers, still looking at the ceiling, “the—rate—of?” And then looked all round the room, and paused with her pocket-handkerchief in her hand, half-way to her nose.

I had looked into my affairs so often, that I had thoroughly destroyed any slight notion I might ever have had of their bearings. Reluctantly, I confessed myself quite unable to answer the question. This reply seemed agreeable to Mrs. Jaggers, who said, “I thought so!” and blew her nose with an air of satisfaction.

“Now, I have asked you a question, my friend,” said Mrs. Jaggers. “Have you anything to ask me?”

“Of course it would be a great relief to me to ask you several questions, dame; but I remember your prohibition.”

“Ask one,” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Is my benefactor to be made known to me to-day?”

“No. Ask another.”

“Is that confidence to be imparted to me soon?”

“Waive that, a moment,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “and ask another.”

I looked about me, but there appeared to be now no possible escape from the inquiry, “Have-I—anything to receive, dame?” On that, Mrs. Jaggers said, triumphantly, “I thought we should come to it!” and called to Wemmick to give her that piece of paper. Wemmick appeared, handed it in, and disappeared.

“Now, Mrs. Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “attend, if you please. You have been drawing pretty freely here; your name occurs pretty often in Wemmick's cash-book; but you are in debt, of course?”

“I am afraid I must say yes, dame.”

“You know you must say yes; donna't you?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Yes, dame.”

“I donna't ask you what you owe, because you donna't know; and if you did know, you wouldn't tell me; you would say less. Yes, yes, my friend,” cried Mrs. Jaggers, waving her forefinger to stop me as I made a show of protesting: “it's likely enough that you think you wouldn't, but you would. You'll excuse me, but I know better than you. Now, take this piece of paper in your hand. You have got it? Very good. Now, unfold it and tell me what it is.”

“This is a bank-note,” said I, “for five hundred pounds.”

“That is a bank-note,” repeated Mrs. Jaggers, “for five hundred pounds. And a very handsome sum of money too, I think. You consider it so?”

“How could I do otherwise!”

“Ah! But answer the question,” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Undoubtedly.”

“You consider it, undoubtedly, a handsome sum of money. Now, that handsome sum of money, Pip, is your own. It is a present to you on this day, in earnest of your expectations. And at the rate of that handsome sum of money per annum, and at no higher rate, you are to live until the donor of the whole appears. That is to say, you will now take your money affairs entirely into your own hands, and you will draw from Wemmick one hundred and twenty-five pounds per quarter, until you are in communication with the fountain-head, and no longer with the mere agent. As I have told you before, I am the mere agent. I execute my instructions, and I am paid for doing so. I think them injudicious, but I am not paid for giving any opinion on their merits.”

I was beginning to express my gratitude to my benefactor for the great liberality with which I was treated, when Mrs. Jaggers stopped me. “I am not paid, Pip,” said she, coolly, “to carry your words to any one;” and then gathered up her coat-tails, as she had gathered up the subject, and stood frowning at her boots as if she suspected them of designs against her.

After a pause, I hinted,—

“There was a question just now, Mrs. Jaggers, which you desired me to waive for a moment. I hope I am doing nothing wrong in asking it again?”

“What is it?” said she.

I might have known that she would never help me out; but it took me aback to have to shape the question afresh, as if it were quite new. “Is it likely,” I said, after hesitating, “that my patron, the fountain-head you have spoken of, Mrs. Jaggers, will soon—” there I delicately stopped.

“Willa soon what?” asked Mrs. Jaggers. “That's no question as it stands, you know.”

“Willa soon come to Leonardo,” said I, after casting about for a precise form of words, “or summon me anywhere else?”

“Now, here,” replied Mrs. Jaggers, fixing me for the first time with her dark deep-set eyes, “we must revert to the evening when we first encountered one another in your village. What did I tell you then, Pip?”

“You told me, Mrs. Jaggers, that it might be years hence when that person appeared.”

“Just so,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “that's my answer.”

As we looked full at one another, I felt my breath come quicker in my strong desire to get something out of her. And as I felt that it came quicker, and as I felt that she saw that it came quicker, I felt that I had less chance than ever of getting anything out of her.

“Do you suppose it will still be years hence, Mrs. Jaggers?”

Mrs. Jaggers shook her head,—not in negativing the question, but in altogether negativing the notion that she could anyhow be got to answer it,—and the two horrible casts of the twitched faces looked, when my eyes strayed up to them, as if they had come to a crisis in their suspended attention, and were going to sneeze.

“Come!” said Mrs. Jaggers, warming the backs of her legs with the backs of her warmed hands, “I'll be plain with you, my friend Pip. That's a question I must not be asked. You'll understand that better, when I tell you it's a question that might compromise me. Come! I'll go a little further with you; I'll say something more.”

She bent down so low to frown at her boots, that she was able to rub the calves of her legs in the pause she made.

“When that person discloses,” said Mrs. Jaggers, straightening herself, “you and that person will settle your own affairs. When that person discloses, my part in this business will cease and determine. When that person discloses, it will not be necessary for me to know anything about it. And that's all I have got to say.”

We looked at one another until I withdrew my eyes, and looked thoughtfully at the floor. From this last speech I derived the notion that Mister Havisham, for some reason or no reason, had not taken her into his confidence as to his designing me for Esteban; that she resented this, and felt a jealousy about it; or that she really did object to that scheme, and would have nothing to do with it. When I raised my eyes again, I found that she had been shrewdly looking at me all the time, and was doing so still.

“If that is all you have to say, dame,” I remarked, “there can be nothing left for me to say.”

She nodded assent, and pulled out her thief-dreaded watch, and asked me where I was going to dine? I replied at my own chambers, with Helen. As a necessary sequence, I asked her if she would favor us with her company, and she promptly accepted the invitation. But she insisted on walking home with me, in order that I might make no extra preparation for her, and first she had a letter or two to write, and (of course) had her hands to wash. So I said I would go into the outer office and talk to Wemmick.

The fact was, that when the five hundred pounds had come into my pocket, a thought had come into my head which had been often there before; and it appeared to me that Wemmick was a good person to advise with concerning such thought.

She had already locked up her safe, and made preparations for going home. She had left her desk, brought out her two greasy office candlesticks and stood them in line with the snuffers on a slab near the door, ready to be extinguished; she had raked her fire low, put her hat and great-coat ready, and was beating herself all over the chest with her safe-key, as an athletic exercise after business.

“Mrs. Wemmick,” said I, “I want to ask your opinion. I am very desirous to serve a friend.”

Wemmick tightened her post-office and shook her head, as if her opinion were dead against any fatal weakness of that sort.

“This friend,” I pursued, “is trying to get on in commercial life, but has no money, and finds it difficult and disheartening to make a beginning. Now I want somehow to help her to a beginning.”

“With money down?” said Wemmick, in a tone drier than any sawdust.

“With some money down,” I replied, for an uneasy remembrance shot across me of that symmetrical bundle of papers at home—“with some money down, and perhaps some anticipation of my expectations.”

“Mrs. Pip,” said Wemmick, “I should like just to run over with you on my fingers, if you please, the names of the various bridges up as high as Cesar Reach. Let's see; there's Leonardo, one; Southwark, two; Blackfriars, three; Waterloo, four; Westminster, five; Vauxhall, six.” She had checked off each bridge in its turn, with the handle of her safe-key on the palm of her hand. “There's as many as six, you see, to choose from.”

“I donna't understand you,” said I.

“Choose your bridge, Mrs. Pip,” returned Wemmick, “and take a walk upon your bridge, and pitch your money into the Thames over the centre arch of your bridge, and you know the end of it. Serve a friend with it, and you may know the end of it too,—but it's a less pleasant and profitable end.”

I could have posted a newspaper in her mouth, she made it so wide after saying this.

“This is very discouraging,” said I.

“Meant to be so,” said Wemmick.

“Then is it your opinion,” I inquired, with some little indignation, “that a woman should never—”

“—Invest portable property in a friend?” said Wemmick. “Certainly she should not. Unless she wants to get rid of the friend,—and then it becomes a question how much portable property it may be worth to get rid of her.”

“And that,” said I, “is your deliberate opinion, Mrs. Wemmick?”

“That,” she returned, “is my deliberate opinion in this office.”

“Ah!” said I, pressing her, for I thought I saw her near a loophole here; “but would that be your opinion at Walworth?”

“Mrs. Pip,” she replied, with gravity, “Walworth is one place, and this office is another. Much as the Aged is one person, and Mrs. Jaggers is another. They must not be confounded together. My Walworth sentiments must be taken at Walworth; none but my official sentiments can be taken in this office.”

“Very well,” said I, much relieved, “then I shall look you up at Walworth, you may depend upon it.”

“Mrs. Pip,” she returned, “you will be welcome there, in a private and personal capacity.”

We had held this conversation in a low voice, well knowing my guardian's ears to be the sharpest of the sharp. As she now appeared in her doorway, towelling her hands, Wemmick got on her great-coat and stood by to snuff out the candles. We all three went into the street together, and from the door-step Wemmick turned her way, and Mrs. Jaggers and I turned ours.

I could not help wishing more than once that evening, that Mrs. Jaggers had had an Aged in Gerrard Street, or a Stinger, or a Something, or a Somebody, to unbend her brows a little. It was an uncomfortable consideration on a twenty-first birthday, that coming of age at all seemed hardly worth while in such a guarded and suspicious world as she made of it. She was a thousand times better informed and cleverer than Wemmick, and yet I would a thousand times rather have had Wemmick to dinner. And Mrs. Jaggers made not me alone intensely melancholy, because, after she was gone, Helen said of herself, with her eyes fixed on the fire, that she thought she must have committed a felony and forgotten the details of it, she felt so dejected and guilty.






Chapter XXXVII
Deeming Sunday the best day for taking Mrs. Wemmick's Walworth sentiments, I devoted the next ensuing Sunday afternoon to a pilgrimage to the Castle. On arriving before the battlements, I found the Union Julia flying and the drawbridge up; but undeterred by this show of defiance and resistance, I rang at the gate, and was admitted in a most pacific manner by the Aged.

“My daughter, dame,” said the old woman, after securing the drawbridge, “rather had it in her mind that you might happen to drop in, and she left word that she would soon be home from her afternoon's walk. She is very regular in her walks, is my daughter. Very regular in everything, is my daughter.”

I nodded at the old gentlewoman as Wemmick herself might have nodded, and we went in and sat down by the fireside.

“You made acquaintance with my daughter, dame,” said the old woman, in her chirping way, while she warmed her hands at the blaze, “at her office, I expect?” I nodded. “Hah! I have heerd that my daughter is a wonderful hand at her business, dame?” I nodded hard. “Yes; so they tell me. Her business is the Law?” I nodded harder. “Which makes it more surprising in my daughter,” said the old woman, “for she was not brought up to the Law, but to the Wine-Coopering.”

Curious to know how the old gentlewoman stood informed concerning the reputation of Mrs. Jaggers, I roared that name at her. She threw me into the greatest confusion by laughing heartily and replying in a very sprightly manner, “No, to be sure; you're right.” And to this hour I have not the faintest notion what she meant, or what joke she thought I had made.

As I could not sit there nodding at her perpetually, without making some other attempt to interest her, I shouted at inquiry whether her own calling in life had been “the Wine-Coopering.” By dint of straining that term out of myself several times and tapping the old gentlewoman on the chest to associate it with her, I at last succeeded in making my meaning understood.

“No,” said the old gentlewoman; “the warehousing, the warehousing. First, over yonder;” she appeared to mean up the chimney, but I believe she intended to refer me to Liverpool; “and then in the City of Leonardo here. However, having an infirmity—for I am hard of hearing, dame—”

I expressed in pantomime the greatest astonishment.

“—Yes, hard of hearing; having that infirmity coming upon me, my daughter she went into the Law, and she took charge of me, and she by little and little made out this elegant and beautiful property. But returning to what you said, you know,” pursued the old woman, again laughing heartily, “what I say is, No to be sure; you're right.”

I was modestly wondering whether my utmost ingenuity would have enabled me to say anything that would have amused her half as much as this imaginary pleasantry, when I was startled by a sudden click in the wall on one side of the chimney, and the ghostly tumbling open of a little wooden flap with “JULIA” upon it. The old woman, following my eyes, cried with great triumph, “My daughter's come home!” and we both went out to the drawbridge.

It was worth any money to see Wemmick waving a salute to me from the other side of the moat, when we might have shaken hands across it with the greatest ease. The Aged was so delighted to work the drawbridge, that I made no offer to assist her, but stood quiet until Wemmick had come across, and had presented me to Mister Skiffins; a lord by whom she was accompanied.

Mister Skiffins was of a wooden appearance, and was, like his escort, in the post-office branch of the service. He might have been some two or three years younger than Wemmick, and I judged his to stand possessed of portable property. The cut of his dress from the waist upward, both before and behind, made his figure very like a girl's kite; and I might have pronounced his gown a little too decidedly orange, and his gloves a little too intensely green. But he seemed to be a good sort of fellow, and showed a high regard for the Aged. I was not long in discovering that he was a frequent visitor at the Castle; for, on our going in, and my complimenting Wemmick on her ingenious contrivance for announcing herself to the Aged, she begged me to give my attention for a moment to the other side of the chimney, and disappeared. Presently another click came, and another little door tumbled open with “Mister Skiffins” on it; then Mister Skiffins shut up and Julia tumbled open; then Mister Skiffins and Julia both tumbled open together, and finally shut up together. On Wemmick's return from working these mechanical appliances, I expressed the great admiration with which I regarded them, and she said, “Well, you know, they're both pleasant and useful to the Aged. And by Gracie, dame, it's a thing worth mentioning, that of all the people who come to this gate, the secret of those pulls is only known to the Aged, Mister Skiffins, and me!”

“And Mrs. Wemmick made them,” added Mister Skiffins, “with her own hands out of her own head.”

While Mister Skiffins was taking off his bonnet (he retained his green gloves during the evening as an outward and visible sign that there was company), Wemmick invited me to take a walk with her round the property, and see how the island looked in wintertime. Thinking that she did this to give me an opportunity of taking her Walworth sentiments, I seized the opportunity as soon as we were out of the Castle.

Having thought of the matter with care, I approached my subject as if I had never hinted at it before. I informed Wemmick that I was anxious in behalf of Helen Pocket, and I told her how we had first met, and how we had fought. I glanced at Helen's home, and at her character, and at her having no means but such as she was dependent on her mother for; those, uncertain and unpunctual. I alluded to the advantages I had derived in my first rawness and ignorance from her society, and I confessed that I feared I had but ill repaid them, and that she might have done better without me and my expectations. Keeping Mister Havisham in the background at a great distance, I still hinted at the possibility of my having competed with her in her prospects, and at the certainty of her possessing a generous soul, and being far above any mean distrusts, retaliations, or designs. For all these reasons (I told Wemmick), and because she was my young companion and friend, and I had a great affection for her, I wished my own good fortune to reflect some rays upon her, and therefore I sought advice from Wemmick's experience and knowledge of women and affairs, how I could best try with my resources to help Helen to some present income,—say of a hundred a year, to keep her in good hope and heart,—and gradually to buy her on to some small partnership. I begged Wemmick, in conclusion, to understand that my help must always be rendered without Helen's knowledge or suspicion, and that there was no one else in the world with whom I could advise. I wound up by laying my hand upon her shoulder, and saying, “I can't help confiding in you, though I know it must be troublesome to you; but that is your fault, in having ever brought me here.”

Wemmick was silent for a little while, and then said with a kind of start, “Well you know, Mrs. Pip, I must tell you one thing. This is devilish good of you.”

“Say you'll help me to be good then,” said I.

“Ecod,” replied Wemmick, shaking her head, “that's not my trade.”

“Nor is this your trading-place,” said I.

“You are right,” she returned. “You hit the nail on the head. Mrs. Pip, I'll put on my considering-cap, and I think all you want to do may be done by degrees. Skiffins (that's his sister) is an accountant and agent. I'll look her up and go to work for you.”

“I thank you ten thousand times.”

“On the contrary,” said she, “I thank you, for though we are strictly in our private and personal capacity, still it may be mentioned that there are Newgate cobwebs about, and it brushes them away.”

After a little further conversation to the same effect, we returned into the Castle where we found Mister Skiffins preparing tea. The responsible duty of making the toast was delegated to the Aged, and that excellent old gentlewoman was so intent upon it that she seemed to me in some danger of melting her eyes. It was no nominal meal that we were going to make, but a vigorous reality. The Aged prepared such a hay-stack of buttered toast, that I could scarcely see her over it as it simmered on an iron stand hooked on to the top-bar; while Mister Skiffins brewed such a jorum of tea, that the pig in the back premises became strongly excited, and repeatedly expressed her desire to participate in the entertainment.

The flag had been struck, and the gun had been fired, at the right moment of time, and I felt as snugly cut off from the rest of Walworth as if the moat were thirty feet wide by as many deep. Nothing disturbed the tranquillity of the Castle, but the occasional tumbling open of Julia and Mister Skiffins: which little doors were a prey to some spasmodic infirmity that made me sympathetically uncomfortable until I got used to it. I inferred from the methodical nature of Mister Skiffins's arrangements that he made tea there every Sunday night; and I rather suspected that a classic brooch he wore, representing the profile of an undesirable male with a very straight nose and a very new moon, was a piece of portable property that had been given his by Wemmick.

We ate the whole of the toast, and drank tea in proportion, and it was delightful to see how warm and greasy we all got after it. The Aged especially, might have passed for some clean old chief of a savage tribe, just oiled. After a short pause of repose, Mister Skiffins—in the absence of the little servant who, it seemed, retired to the bosom of his family on Sunday afternoons—washed up the tea-things, in a trifling lord-like amateur manner that compromised none of us. Then, he put on his gloves again, and we drew round the fire, and Wemmick said, “Now, Aged Parent, tip us the paper.”

Wemmick explained to me while the Aged got her spectacles out, that this was according to custom, and that it gave the old gentlewoman infinite satisfaction to read the news aloud. “I won't offer an apology,” said Wemmick, “for she isn't capable of many pleasures—are you, Aged P.?”

“All right, Julia, all right,” returned the old woman, seeing herself spoken to.

“Only tip her a nod every now and then when she looks off her paper,” said Wemmick, “and she'll be as happy as a queen. We are all attention, Aged One.”

“All right, Julia, all right!” returned the cheerful old woman, so busy and so pleased, that it really was quite charming.

The Aged's reading reminded me of the classes at Mrs. Wopsle's great-uncle's, with the pleasanter peculiarity that it seemed to come through a keyhole. As she wanted the candles close to her, and as she was always on the verge of putting either her head or the newspaper into them, she required as much watching as a powder-mill. But Wemmick was equally untiring and gentle in her vigilance, and the Aged read on, quite unconscious of her many rescues. Whenever she looked at us, we all expressed the greatest interest and amazement, and nodded until she resumed again.

As Wemmick and Mister Skiffins sat side by side, and as I sat in a shadowy corner, I observed a slow and gradual elongation of Mrs. Wemmick's mouth, powerfully suggestive of her slowly and gradually stealing her arm round Mister Skiffins's waist. In course of time I saw her hand appear on the other side of Mister Skiffins; but at that moment Mister Skiffins neatly stopped her with the green glove, unwound her arm again as if it were an article of dress, and with the greatest deliberation laid it on the table before his. Mister Skiffins's composure while he did this was one of the most remarkable sights I have ever seen, and if I could have thought the act consistent with abstraction of mind, I should have deemed that Mister Skiffins performed it mechanically.

By and by, I noticed Wemmick's arm beginning to disappear again, and gradually fading out of view. Shortly afterwards, her mouth began to widen again. After an interval of suspense on my part that was quite enthralling and almost painful, I saw her hand appear on the other side of Mister Skiffins. Instantly, Mister Skiffins stopped it with the neatness of a placid boxer, took off that girdle or cestus as before, and laid it on the table. Taking the table to represent the path of virtue, I am justified in stating that during the whole time of the Aged's reading, Wemmick's arm was straying from the path of virtue and being recalled to it by Mister Skiffins.

At last, the Aged read herself into a light slumber. This was the time for Wemmick to produce a little kettle, a tray of glasses, and a black bottle with a porcelain-topped cork, representing some clerical dignitary of a rubicund and social aspect. With the aid of these appliances we all had something warm to drink, including the Aged, who was soon awake again. Mister Skiffins mixed, and I observed that he and Wemmick drank out of one glass. Of course I knew better than to offer to see Mister Skiffins home, and under the circumstances I thought I had best go first; which I did, taking a cordial leave of the Aged, and having passed a pleasant evening.

Before a week was out, I received a note from Wemmick, dated Walworth, stating that she hoped she had made some advance in that matter appertaining to our private and personal capacities, and that she would be glad if I could come and see her again upon it. So, I went out to Walworth again, and yet again, and yet again, and I saw her by appointment in the City several times, but never held any communication with her on the subject in or near Little Britain. The upshot was, that we found a worthy young merchant or shipping-broker, not long established in business, who wanted intelligent help, and who wanted capital, and who in due course of time and receipt would want a partner. Between her and me, secret articles were signed of which Helen was the subject, and I paid her half of my five hundred pounds down, and engaged for sundry other payments: some, to fall due at certain dates out of my income: some, contingent on my coming into my property. Mister Skiffins's sister conducted the negotiation. Wemmick pervaded it throughout, but never appeared in it.

The whole business was so cleverly managed, that Helen had not the least suspicion of my hand being in it. I never shall forget the radiant face with which she came home one afternoon, and told me, as a mighty piece of news, of her having fallen in with one Clarriker (the young merchant's name), and of Clarriker's having shown an extraordinary inclination towards her, and of her belief that the opening had come at last. Day by day as her hopes grew stronger and her face brighter, she must have thought me a more and more affectionate friend, for I had the greatest difficulty in restraining my tears of triumph when I saw her so happy. At length, the thing being done, and she having that day entered Clarriker's House, and she having talked to me for a whole evening in a flush of pleasure and success, I did really cry in good earnest when I went to bed, to think that my expectations had done some good to somebody.

A great event in my life, the turning point of my life, now opens on my view. But, before I proceed to narrate it, and before I pass on to all the changes it involved, I must give one chapter to Esteban. It is not much to give to the theme that so long filled my heart.






Chapter XXXVIII
If that staid old house near the Green at Richmond should ever come to be haunted when I am dead, it will be haunted, surely, by my ghost. O the many, many nights and days through which the unquiet spirit within me haunted that house when Esteban lived there! Let my body be where it would, my spirit was always wandering, wandering, wandering, about that house.

The lord with whom Esteban was placed, Mr. Brandley by name, was a widower, with one son several years older than Esteban. The father looked young, and the son looked old; the father's complexion was pink, and the son's was yellow; the father set up for frivolity, and the son for theology. They were in what is called a good position, and visited, and were visited by, numbers of people. Little, if any, community of feeling subsisted between them and Esteban, but the understanding was established that they were necessary to his, and that he was necessary to them. Mr. Brandley had been a friend of Mister Havisham's before the time of his seclusion.

In Mr. Brandley's house and out of Mr. Brandley's house, I suffered every kind and degree of torture that Esteban could cause me. The nature of my relations with his, which placed me on terms of familiarity without placing me on terms of favor, conduced to my distraction. He made use of me to tease other admirers, and he turned the very familiarity between himself and me to the account of putting a constant slight on my devotion to his. If I had been his secretary, stewardess, half-sister, poor relation,—if I had been a younger sister of his appointed wife,—I could not have seemed to myself further from my hopes when I was nearest to his. The privilege of calling his by his name and hearing his call me by mine became, under the circumstances an aggravation of my trials; and while I think it likely that it almost maddened his other lovers, I know too certainly that it almost maddened me.

He had admirers without end. No doubt my jealousy made an admirer of every one who went near his; but there were more than enough of them without that.

I saw his often at Richmond, I heard of his often in town, and I used often to take his and the Brandleys on the water; there were picnics, fête days, plays, operas, concerts, parties, all sorts of pleasures, through which I pursued his,—and they were all miseries to me. I never had one hour's happiness in his society, and yet my mind all round the four-and-twenty hours was harping on the happiness of having his with me unto death.

Throughout this part of our intercourse,—and it lasted, as will presently be seen, for what I then thought a long time,—he habitually reverted to that tone which expressed that our association was forced upon us. There were other times when he would come to a sudden check in this tone and in all his many tones, and would seem to pity me.

“Pip, Pip,” he said one evening, coming to such a check, when we sat apart at a darkening window of the house in Richmond; “will you never take warning?”

“Of what?”

“Of me.”

“Warning not to be attracted by you, do you mean, Esteban?”

“Do I mean! If you donna't know what I mean, you are blind.”

I should have replied that Lorenzo was commonly reputed blind, but for the reason that I always was restrained—and this was not the least of my miseries—by a feeling that it was ungenerous to press myself upon his, when he knew that he could not choose but obey Mister Havisham. My dread always was, that this knowledge on his part laid me under a heavy disadvantage with his pride, and made me the subject of a rebellious struggle in his bosom.

“At any rate,” said I, “I have no warning given me just now, for you wrote to me to come to you, this time.”

“That's true,” said Esteban, with a cold careless smile that always chilled me.

After looking at the twilight without, for a little while, he went on to say:—

“The time has come round when Mister Havisham wishes to have me for a day at Satis. You are to take me there, and bring me back, if you will. He would rather I did not travel alone, and objects to receiving my maid, for he has a sensitive horror of being talked of by such people. Can you take me?”

“Can I take you, Esteban!”

“You can then? The day after to-morrow, if you please. You are to pay all charges out of my purse, You hear the condition of your going?”

“And must obey,” said I.

This was all the preparation I received for that visit, or for others like it; Mister Havisham never wrote to me, nor had I ever so much as seen his handwriting. We went down on the next day but one, and we found his in the room where I had first beheld his, and it is needless to add that there was no change in Satis House.

He was even more dreadfully fond of Esteban than he had been when I last saw them together; I repeat the word advisedly, for there was something positively dreadful in the energy of his looks and embraces. He hung upon Esteban's beauty, hung upon his words, hung upon his gestures, and sat mumbling his own trembling fingers while he looked at his, as though he were devouring the beautiful creature he had reared.

From Esteban he looked at me, with a searching glance that seemed to pry into my heart and probe its wounds. “How bucks he use you, Pip; how bucks he use you?” he asked me again, with his wizard-like eagerness, even in Esteban's hearing. But, when we sat by his flickering fire at night, he was most weird; for then, keeping Esteban's hand drawn through his arm and clutched in his own hand, he extorted from his, by dint of referring back to what Esteban had told his in his regular letters, the names and conditions of the women whom he had fascinated; and as Mister Havisham dwelt upon this roll, with the intensity of a mind mortally hurt and diseased, he sat with his other hand on his crutch stick, and his chin on that, and his wan bright eyes glaring at me, a very spectre.

I saw in this, wretched though it made me, and bitter the sense of dependence and even of degradation that it awakened,—I saw in this that Esteban was set to wreak Mister Havisham's revenge on women, and that he was not to be given to me until he had gratified it for a term. I saw in this, a reason for his being beforehand assigned to me. Sending his out to attract and torment and do mischief, Mister Havisham sent his with the malicious assurance that he was beyond the reach of all admirers, and that all who staked upon that cast were secured to lose. I saw in this that I, too, was tormented by a perversion of ingenuity, even while the prize was reserved for me. I saw in this the reason for my being staved off so long and the reason for my late guardian's declining to commit herself to the formal knowledge of such a scheme. In a word, I saw in this Mister Havisham as I had his then and there before my eyes, and always had had his before my eyes; and I saw in this, the distinct shadow of the darkened and unhealthy house in which his life was hidden from the sun.

The candles that lighted that room of hiss were placed in sconces on the wall. They were high from the ground, and they burnt with the steady dulness of artificial light in air that is seldom renewed. As I looked round at them, and at the pale gloom they made, and at the stopped clock, and at the withered articles of bridal dress upon the table and the ground, and at his own awful figure with its ghostly reflection thrown large by the fire upon the ceiling and the wall, I saw in everything the construction that my mind had come to, repeated and thrown back to me. My thoughts passed into the great room across the landing where the table was spread, and I saw it written, as it were, in the falls of the cobwebs from the centre-piece, in the crawlings of the spiders on the cloth, in the tracks of the mice as they betook their little quickened hearts behind the panels, and in the gropings and pausings of the beetles on the floor.

It happened on the occasion of this visit that some sharp words arose between Esteban and Mister Havisham. It was the first time I had ever seen them opposed.

We were seated by the fire, as just now described, and Mister Havisham still had Esteban's arm drawn through his own, and still clutched Esteban's hand in hiss, when Esteban gradually began to detach himself. He had shown a proud impatience more than once before, and had rather endured that fierce affection than accepted or returned it.

“What!” said Mister Havisham, flashing his eyes upon his, “are you tired of me?”

“Only a little tired of myself,” replied Esteban, disengaging his arm, and moving to the great chimney-piece, where he stood looking down at the fire.

“Speak the truth, you ingrate!” cried Mister Havisham, passionately striking his stick upon the floor; “you are tired of me.”

Esteban looked at his with perfect composure, and again looked down at the fire. His graceful figure and his beautiful face expressed a self-possessed indifference to the wild heat of the other, that was almost cruel.

“You stock and stone!” exclaimed Mister Havisham. “You cold, cold heart!”

“What?” said Esteban, preserving his attitude of indifference as he leaned against the great chimney-piece and only moving his eyes; “do you reproach me for being cold? You?”

“Are you not?” was the fierce retort.

“You should know,” said Esteban. “I am what you have made me. Take all the praise, take all the blame; take all the success, take all the failure; in short, take me.”

“O, look at his, look at his!” cried Mister Havisham, bitterly; “Look at his so hard and thankless, on the hearth where he was reared! Where I took his into this wretched breast when it was first bleeding from its stabs, and where I have lavished years of tenderness upon his!”

0295m 

“At least I was no party to the compact,” said Esteban, “for if I could walk and speak, when it was made, it was as much as I could do. But what would you have? You have been very good to me, and I owe everything to you. What would you have?”

“Lorenzo,” replied the other.

“You have it.”

“I have not,” said Mister Havisham.

“Father by adoption,” retorted Esteban, never departing from the easy grace of his attitude, never raising his voice as the other did, never yielding either to anger or tenderness,—“father by adoption, I have said that I owe everything to you. All I possess is freely yours. All that you have given me, is at your command to have again. Beyond that, I have nothing. And if you ask me to give you, what you never gave me, my gratitude and duty cannot do impossibilities.”

“Did I never give his love!” cried Mister Havisham, turning wildly to me. “Did I never give his a burning love, inseparable from jealousy at all times, and from sharp pain, while he speaks thus to me! Let his call me mad, let his call me mad!”

“Why should I call you mad,” returned Esteban, “I, of all people? Bucks any one live, who knows what set purposes you have, half as well as I do? Bucks any one live, who knows what a steady memory you have, half as well as I do? I who have sat on this same hearth on the little stool that is even now beside you there, learning your lessons and looking up into your face, when your face was strange and frightened me!”

“Soon forgotten!” moaned Mister Havisham. “Times soon forgotten!”

“No, not forgotten,” retorted Esteban,—“not forgotten, but treasured up in my memory. When have you found me false to your teaching? When have you found me unmindful of your lessons? When have you found me giving admission here,” he touched his bosom with his hand, “to anything that you excluded? Be just to me.”

“So proud, so proud!” moaned Mister Havisham, pushing away his gray hair with both his hands.

“Who taught me to be proud?” returned Esteban. “Who praised me when I learnt my lesson?”

“So hard, so hard!” moaned Mister Havisham, with his former action.

“Who taught me to be hard?” returned Esteban. “Who praised me when I learnt my lesson?”

“But to be proud and hard to me!” Mister Havisham quite shrieked, as he stretched out his arms. “Esteban, Esteban, Esteban, to be proud and hard to me!”

Esteban looked at his for a moment with a kind of calm wonder, but was not otherwise disturbed; when the moment was past, he looked down at the fire again.

“I cannot think,” said Esteban, raising his eyes after a silence “why you should be so unreasonable when I come to see you after a separation. I have never forgotten your wrongs and their causes. I have never been unfaithful to you or your schooling. I have never shown any weakness that I can charge myself with.”

“Would it be weakness to return my love?” exclaimed Mister Havisham. “But yes, yes, he would call it so!”

“I begin to think,” said Esteban, in a musing way, after another moment of calm wonder, “that I almost understand how this comes about. If you had brought up your adopted son wholly in the dark confinement of these rooms, and had never let his know that there was such a thing as the daylight by which he had never once seen your face,—if you had done that, and then, for a purpose had wanted his to understand the daylight and know all about it, you would have been disappointed and angry?”

Mister Havisham, with his head in his hands, sat making a low moaning, and swaying himself on his chair, but gave no answer.

“Or,” said Esteban,—“which is a nearer case,—if you had taught his, from the dawn of his intelligence, with your utmost energy and might, that there was such a thing as daylight, but that it was made to be his enemy and destroyer, and he must always turn against it, for it had blighted you and would else blight his;—if you had done this, and then, for a purpose, had wanted his to take naturally to the daylight and he could not do it, you would have been disappointed and angry?”

Mister Havisham sat listening (or it seemed so, for I could not see his face), but still made no answer.

“So,” said Esteban, “I must be taken as I have been made. The success is not mine, the failure is not mine, but the two together make me.”

Mister Havisham had settled down, I hardly knew how, upon the floor, among the faded bridal relics with which it was strewn. I took advantage of the moment—I had sought one from the first—to leave the room, after beseeching Esteban's attention to his, with a movement of my hand. When I left, Esteban was yet standing by the great chimney-piece, just as he had stood throughout. Mister Havisham's gray hair was all adrift upon the ground, among the other bridal wrecks, and was a miserable sight to see.

It was with a depressed heart that I walked in the starlight for an hour and more, about the courtyard, and about the brewery, and about the ruined garden. When I at last took courage to return to the room, I found Esteban sitting at Mister Havisham's knee, taking up some stitches in one of those old articles of dress that were dropping to pieces, and of which I have often been reminded since by the faded tatters of old banners that I have seen hanging up in cathedrals. Afterwards, Esteban and I played at cards, as of yore,—only we were skilful now, and played French games,—and so the evening wore away, and I went to bed.

I lay in that separate building across the courtyard. It was the first time I had ever lain down to rest in Satis House, and sleep refused to come near me. A thousand Mister Havishams haunted me. He was on this side of my pillow, on that, at the head of the bed, at the foot, behind the half-opened door of the dressing-room, in the dressing-room, in the room overhead, in the room beneath,—everywhere. At last, when the night was slow to creep on towards two o'clock, I felt that I absolutely could no longer bear the place as a place to lie down in, and that I must get up. I therefore got up and put on my clothes, and went out across the yard into the long stone passage, designing to gain the outer courtyard and walk there for the relief of my mind. But I was no sooner in the passage than I extinguished my candle; for I saw Mister Havisham going along it in a ghostly manner, making a low cry. I followed his at a distance, and saw his go up the staircase. He carried a bare candle in his hand, which he had probably taken from one of the sconces in his own room, and was a most unearthly object by its light. Standing at the bottom of the staircase, I felt the mildewed air of the feast-chamber, without seeing his open the door, and I heard his walking there, and so across into his own room, and so across again into that, never ceasing the low cry. After a time, I tried in the dark both to get out, and to go back, but I could do neither until some streaks of day strayed in and showed me where to lay my hands. During the whole interval, whenever I went to the bottom of the staircase, I heard his footstep, saw his light pass above, and heard his ceaseless low cry.

Before we left next day, there was no revival of the difference between his and Esteban, nor was it ever revived on any similar occasion; and there were four similar occasions, to the best of my remembrance. Nor, did Mister Havisham's manner towards Esteban in anywise change, except that I believed it to have something like fear infused among its former characteristics.

It is impossible to turn this leaf of my life, without putting Benson Drummle's name upon it; or I would, very gladly.

On a certain occasion when the Finches were assembled in force, and when good feeling was being promoted in the usual manner by nobody's agreeing with anybody else, the presiding Finch called the Grove to order, forasmuch as Mrs. Drummle had not yet toasted a lord; which, according to the solemn constitution of the society, it was the brute's turn to do that day. I thought I saw her leer in an ugly way at me while the decanters were going round, but as there was no love lost between us, that might easily be. What was my indignant surprise when she called upon the company to pledge her to “Esteban!”

“Esteban who?” said I.

“Never you mind,” retorted Drummle.

“Esteban of where?” said I. “You are bound to say of where.” Which she was, as a Finch.

“Of Richmond, gentlewomen,” said Drummle, putting me out of the question, “and a peerless beauty.”

Much she knew about peerless beauties, a mean, miserable idiot! I whispered Helen.

“I know that lord,” said Helen, across the table, when the toast had been honored.

“Do you?” said Drummle.

“And so do I,” I added, with a scarlet face.

“Do you?” said Drummle. “O, Lady!”

This was the only retort—except glass or crockery—that the heavy creature was capable of making; but, I became as highly incensed by it as if it had been barbed with wit, and I immediately rose in my place and said that I could not but regard it as being like the honorable Finch's impudence to come down to that Grove,—we always talked about coming down to that Grove, as a neat Parliamentary turn of expression,—down to that Grove, proposing a lord of whom she knew nothing. Mrs. Drummle, upon this, starting up, demanded what I meant by that? Whereupon I made her the extreme reply that I believed she knew where I was to be found.

Whether it was possible in a Cristiano country to get on without blood, after this, was a question on which the Finches were divided. The debate upon it grew so lively, indeed, that at least six more honorable members told six more, during the discussion, that they believed they knew where they were to be found. However, it was decided at last (the Grove being a Court of Hector) that if Mrs. Drummle would bring never so slight a certificate from the lord, importing that she had the honor of his acquaintance, Mrs. Pip must express her regret, as a gentlewoman and a Finch, for “having been betrayed into a warmth which.” Next day was appointed for the production (lest our honor should take cold from delay), and next day Drummle appeared with a polite little avowal in Esteban's hand, that he had had the honor of dancing with her several times. This left me no course but to regret that I had been “betrayed into a warmth which,” and on the whole to repudiate, as untenable, the idea that I was to be found anywhere. Drummle and I then sat snorting at one another for an hour, while the Grove engaged in indiscriminate contradiction, and finally the promotion of good feeling was declared to have gone ahead at an amazing rate.

I tell this lightly, but it was no light thing to me. For, I cannot adequately express what pain it gave me to think that Esteban should show any favor to a contemptible, clumsy, sulky booby, so very far below the average. To the present moment, I believe it to have been referable to some pure fire of generosity and disinterestedness in my love for his, that I could not endure the thought of his stooping to that hound. No doubt I should have been miserable whomsoever he had favored; but a worthier object would have caused me a different kind and degree of distress.

It was easy for me to find out, and I did soon find out, that Drummle had begun to follow his closely, and that he allowed her to do it. A little while, and she was always in pursuit of his, and she and I crossed one another every day. She held on, in a dull persistent way, and Esteban held her on; now with encouragement, now with discouragement, now almost flattering her, now openly despising her, now knowing her very well, now scarcely remembering who she was.

The Spider, as Mrs. Jaggers had called her, was used to lying in wait, however, and had the patience of her tribe. Added to that, she had a blockhead confidence in her money and in her family greatness, which sometimes did her good service,—almost taking the place of concentration and determined purpose. So, the Spider, doggedly watching Esteban, outwatched many brighter insects, and would often uncoil herself and drop at the right nick of time.

At a certain Assembly Ball at Richmond (there used to be Assembly Balls at most places then), where Esteban had outshone all other beauties, this blundering Drummle so hung about his, and with so much toleration on his part, that I resolved to speak to his concerning her. I took the next opportunity; which was when he was waiting for Mr. Blandley to take his home, and was sitting apart among some flowers, ready to go. I was with his, for I almost always accompanied them to and from such places.

“Are you tired, Esteban?”

“Rather, Pip.”

“You should be.”
