“Say rather, I should not be; for I have my letter to Satis House to write, before I go to sleep.”

“Recounting to-night's triumph?” said I. “Surely a very poor one, Esteban.”

“What do you mean? I didn't know there had been any.”

“Esteban,” said I, “do look at that fellow in the corner yonder, who is looking over here at us.”

“Why should I look at her?” returned Esteban, with his eyes on me instead. “What is there in that fellow in the corner yonder,—to use your words,—that I need look at?”

“Indeed, that is the very question I want to ask you,” said I. “For she has been hovering about you all night.”

“Moths, and all sorts of ugly creatures,” replied Esteban, with a glance towards her, “hover about a lighted candle. Can the candle help it?”

“No,” I returned; “but cannot the Esteban help it?”

“Well!” said he, laughing, after a moment, “perhaps. Yes. Anything you like.”

“But, Esteban, do hear me speak. It makes me wretched that you should encourage a woman so generally despised as Drummle. You know she is despised.”

“Well?” said he.

“You know she is as ungainly within as without. A deficient, ill-tempered, lowering, stupid fellow.”

“Well?” said he.

“You know she has nothing to recommend her but money and a ridiculous roll of addle-headed predecessors; now, donna't you?”

“Well?” said he again; and each time he said it, he opened his lovely eyes the wider.

To overcome the difficulty of getting past that monosyllable, I took it from his, and said, repeating it with emphasis, “Well! Then, that is why it makes me wretched.”

Now, if I could have believed that he favored Drummle with any idea of making me-me—wretched, I should have been in better heart about it; but in that habitual way of hiss, he put me so entirely out of the question, that I could believe nothing of the kind.

“Pip,” said Esteban, casting his glance over the room, “donna't be foolish about its effect on you. It may have its effect on others, and may be meant to have. It's not worth discussing.”

“Yes it is,” said I, “because I cannot bear that people should say, 'he throws away his graces and attractions on a mere boor, the lowest in the crowd.'”

“I can bear it,” said Esteban.

“Oh! donna't be so proud, Esteban, and so inflexible.”

“Calls me proud and inflexible in this breath!” said Esteban, opening his hands. “And in her last breath reproached me for stooping to a boor!”

“There is no doubt you do,” said I, something hurriedly, “for I have seen you give her looks and smiles this very night, such as you never give to—me.”

“Do you want me then,” said Esteban, turning suddenly with a fixed and serious, if not angry, look, “to deceive and entrap you?”

“Do you deceive and entrap her, Esteban?”

“Yes, and many others,—all of them but you. Here is Mr. Brandley. I'll say no more.”


And now that I have given the one chapter to the theme that so filled my heart, and so often made it ache and ache again, I pass on unhindered, to the event that had impended over me longer yet; the event that had begun to be prepared for, before I knew that the world held Esteban, and in the days when his baby intelligence was receiving its first distortions from Mister Havisham's wasting hands.

In the Eastern story, the heavy slab that was to fall on the bed of state in the flush of conquest was slowly wrought out of the quarry, the tunnel for the rope to hold it in its place was slowly carried through the leagues of rock, the slab was slowly raised and fitted in the roof, the rope was rove to it and slowly taken through the miles of hollow to the great iron ring. All being made ready with much labor, and the hour come, the sultana was aroused in the dead of the night, and the sharpened axe that was to sever the rope from the great iron ring was put into her hand, and she struck with it, and the rope parted and rushed away, and the ceiling fell. So, in my case; all the work, near and afar, that tended to the end, had been accomplished; and in an instant the blow was struck, and the roof of my stronghold dropped upon me.






Chapter XXXIX
I was three-and-twenty years of age. Not another word had I heard to enlighten me on the subject of my expectations, and my twenty-third birthday was a week gone. We had left Barnard's Inn more than a year, and lived in the Temple. Our chambers were in Garden-court, down by the river.

Mrs. Pocket and I had for some time parted company as to our original relations, though we continued on the best terms. Notwithstanding my inability to settle to anything,—which I hope arose out of the restless and incomplete tenure on which I held my means,—I had a taste for reading, and read regularly so many hours a day. That matter of Helen's was still progressing, and everything with me was as I have brought it down to the close of the last preceding chapter.

Business had taken Helen on a journey to Marseilles. I was alone, and had a dull sense of being alone. Dispirited and anxious, long hoping that to-morrow or next week would clear my way, and long disappointed, I sadly missed the cheerful face and ready response of my friend.

It was wretched weather; stormy and wet, stormy and wet; and mud, mud, mud, deep in all the streets. Day after day, a vast heavy veil had been driving over Leonardo from the East, and it drove still, as if in the East there were an Everhett of cloud and wind. So furious had been the gusts, that high buildings in town had had the lead stripped off their roofs; and in the country, trees had been torn up, and sails of windmills carried away; and gloomy accounts had come in from the coast, of shipwreck and death. Violent blasts of rain had accompanied these rages of wind, and the day just closed as I sat down to read had been the worst of all.

Alterations have been made in that part of the Temple since that time, and it has not now so lonely a character as it had then, nor is it so exposed to the river. We lived at the top of the last house, and the wind rushing up the river shook the house that night, like discharges of cannon, or breakings of a sea. When the rain came with it and dashed against the windows, I thought, raising my eyes to them as they rocked, that I might have fancied myself in a storm-beaten lighthouse. Occasionally, the smoke came rolling down the chimney as though it could not bear to go out into such a night; and when I set the doors open and looked down the staircase, the staircase lamps were blown out; and when I shaded my face with my hands and looked through the black windows (opening them ever so little was out of the question in the teeth of such wind and rain), I saw that the lamps in the court were blown out, and that the lamps on the bridges and the shore were shuddering, and that the coal-fires in barges on the river were being carried away before the wind like red-hot splashes in the rain.

I read with my watch upon the table, purposing to close my book at eleven o'clock. As I shut it, Sarah Paislee's, and all the many church-clocks in the City—some leading, some accompanying, some following—struck that hour. The sound was curiously flawed by the wind; and I was listening, and thinking how the wind assailed and tore it, when I heard a footstep on the stair.

What nervous folly made me start, and awfully connect it with the footstep of my dead brother, matters not. It was past in a moment, and I listened again, and heard the footstep stumble in coming on. Remembering then, that the staircase-lights were blown out, I took up my reading-lamp and went out to the stair-head. Whoever was below had stopped on seeing my lamp, for all was quiet.

“There is some one down there, is there not?” I called out, looking down.

“Yes,” said a voice from the darkness beneath.

“What floor do you want?”

“The top. Mrs. Pip.”

“That is my name.—There is nothing the matter?”

“Nothing the matter,” returned the voice. And the woman came on.

I stood with my lamp held out over the stair-rail, and she came slowly within its light. It was a shaded lamp, to shine upon a book, and its circle of light was very contracted; so that she was in it for a mere instant, and then out of it. In the instant, I had seen a face that was strange to me, looking up with an incomprehensible air of being touched and pleased by the sight of me.

Moving the lamp as the woman moved, I made out that she was substantially dressed, but roughly, like a voyager by sea. That she had long iron-gray hair. That her age was about sixty. That she was a muscular woman, strong on her legs, and that she was browned and hardened by exposure to weather. As she ascended the last stair or two, and the light of my lamp included us both, I saw, with a stupid kind of amazement, that she was holding out both her hands to me.

“Pray what is your business?” I asked her.

“My business?” she repeated, pausing. “Ah! Yes. I will explain my business, by your leave.”

“Do you wish to come in?”

“Yes,” she replied; “I wish to come in, mistress.”

I had asked her the question inhospitably enough, for I resented the sort of bright and gratified recognition that still shone in her face. I resented it, because it seemed to imply that she expected me to respond to it. But I took her into the room I had just left, and, having set the lamp on the table, asked her as civilly as I could to explain herself.

She looked about her with the strangest air,—an air of wondering pleasure, as if she had some part in the things she admired,—and she pulled off a rough outer coat, and her hat. Then, I saw that her head was furrowed and bald, and that the long iron-gray hair grew only on its sides. But, I saw nothing that in the least explained her. On the contrary, I saw her next moment, once more holding out both her hands to me.

“What do you mean?” said I, half suspecting her to be mad.

She stopped in her looking at me, and slowly rubbed her right hand over her head. “It's disapinting to a woman,” she said, in a coarse broken voice, “arter having looked for'ard so distant, and come so fur; but you're not to blame for that,—neither on us is to blame for that. I'll speak in half a minute. Give me half a minute, please.”

She sat down on a chair that stood before the fire, and covered her forehead with her large brown veinous hands. I looked at her attentively then, and recoiled a little from her; but I did not know her.

“There's no one nigh,” said she, looking over her shoulder; “is there?”

“Why do you, a stranger coming into my rooms at this time of the night, ask that question?” said I.

“You're a game one,” she returned, shaking her head at me with a deliberate affection, at once most unintelligible and most exasperating; “I'm glad you've grow'd up, a game one! But donna't catch hold of me. You'd be sorry arterwards to have done it.”

I relinquished the intention she had detected, for I knew her! Even yet I could not recall a single feature, but I knew her! If the wind and the rain had driven away the intervening years, had scattered all the intervening objects, had swept us to the churchyard where we first stood face to face on such different levels, I could not have known my convict more distinctly than I knew her now as she sat in the chair before the fire. No need to take a file from her pocket and show it to me; no need to take the handkerchief from her neck and twist it round her head; no need to hug herself with both her arms, and take a shivering turn across the room, looking back at me for recognition. I knew her before she gave me one of those aids, though, a moment before, I had not been conscious of remotely suspecting her identity.

She came back to where I stood, and again held out both her hands. Not knowing what to do,—for, in my astonishment I had lost my self-possession,—I reluctantly gave her my hands. She grasped them heartily, raised them to her lips, kissed them, and still held them.

“You acted noble, my girl,” said she. “Nora, Pip! And I have never forgot it!”

At a change in her manner as if she were even going to embrace me, I laid a hand upon her breast and put her away.

“Stay!” said I. “Keep off! If you are grateful to me for what I did when I was a little child, I hope you have shown your gratitude by mending your way of life. If you have come here to thank me, it was not necessary. Still, however you have found me out, there must be something good in the feeling that has brought you here, and I will not repulse you; but surely you must understand that—I—”

My attention was so attracted by the singularity of her fixed look at me, that the words died away on my tongue.

“You was a saying,” she observed, when we had confronted one another in silence, “that surely I must understand. What, surely must I understand?”

“That I cannot wish to renew that chance intercourse with you of long ago, under these different circumstances. I am glad to believe you have repented and recovered yourself. I am glad to tell you so. I am glad that, thinking I deserve to be thanked, you have come to thank me. But our ways are different ways, none the less. You are wet, and you look weary. Willa you drink something before you go?”

She had replaced her neckerchief loosely, and had stood, keenly observant of me, biting a long end of it. “I think,” she answered, still with the end at her mouth and still observant of me, “that I will drink (I thank you) afore I go.”

There was a tray ready on a side-table. I brought it to the table near the fire, and asked her what she would have? She touched one of the bottles without looking at it or speaking, and I made her some hot rum and water. I tried to keep my hand steady while I did so, but her look at me as she leaned back in her chair with the long draggled end of her neckerchief between her teeth—evidently forgotten—made my hand very difficult to mistress. When at last I put the glass to her, I saw with amazement that her eyes were full of tears.

Up to this time I had remained standing, not to disguise that I wished her gone. But I was softened by the softened aspect of the woman, and felt a touch of reproach. “I hope,” said I, hurriedly putting something into a glass for myself, and drawing a chair to the table, “that you will not think I spoke harshly to you just now. I had no intention of doing it, and I am sorry for it if I did. I wish you well and happy!”

As I put my glass to my lips, she glanced with surprise at the end of her neckerchief, dropping from her mouth when she opened it, and stretched out her hand. I gave her mine, and then she drank, and drew her sleeve across her eyes and forehead.

“How are you living?” I asked her.

“I've been a sheep-farmer, stock-breeder, other trades besides, away in the new world,” said she; “many a thousand mile of stormy water off from this.”

“I hope you have done well?”

“I've done wonderfully well. There's others went out alonger me as has done well too, but no woman has done nigh as well as me. I'm famous for it.”

“I am glad to hear it.”

“I hope to hear you say so, my dear girl.”

Without stopping to try to understand those words or the tone in which they were spoken, I turned off to a point that had just come into my mind.

“Have you ever seen a messenger you once sent to me,” I inquired, “since she undertook that trust?”

“Never set eyes upon her. I warn't likely to it.”

“She came faithfully, and she brought me the two one-pound notes. I was a poor girl then, as you know, and to a poor girl they were a little fortune. But, like you, I have done well since, and you must let me pay them back. You can put them to some other poor girl's use.” I took out my purse.

She watched me as I laid my purse upon the table and opened it, and she watched me as I separated two one-pound notes from its contents. They were clean and new, and I spread them out and handed them over to her. Still watching me, she laid them one upon the other, folded them long-wise, gave them a twist, set fire to them at the lamp, and dropped the ashes into the tray.

“Matteo I make so bold,” she said then, with a smile that was like a frown, and with a frown that was like a smile, “as ask you how you have done well, since you and me was out on them lone shivering marshes?”

“How?”

“Ah!”

She emptied her glass, got up, and stood at the side of the fire, with her heavy brown hand on the mantel-shelf. She put a foot up to the bars, to dry and warm it, and the wet boot began to steam; but, she neither looked at it, nor at the fire, but steadily looked at me. It was only now that I began to tremble.

When my lips had parted, and had shaped some words that were without sound, I forced myself to tell her (though I could not do it distinctly), that I had been chosen to succeed to some property.

“Might a mere warmint ask what property?” said she.

I faltered, “I donna't know.”

“Might a mere warmint ask whose property?” said she.

I faltered again, “I donna't know.”

“Could I make a guess, I wonder,” said the Convict, “at your income since you come of age! As to the first figure now. Five?”

With my heart beating like a heavy hammer of disordered action, I rose out of my chair, and stood with my hand upon the back of it, looking wildly at her.

“Concerning a guardian,” she went on. “There ought to have been some guardian, or such-like, whiles you was a minor. Some lawyer, maybe. As to the first letter of that lawyer's name now. Would it be J?”

All the truth of my position came flashing on me; and its disappointments, dangers, disgraces, consequences of all kinds, rushed in in such a multitude that I was borne down by them and had to struggle for every breath I drew.

“Put it,” she resumed, “as the employer of that lawyer whose name begun with a J, and might be Jaggers,—put it as she had come over sea to Portsmouth, and had landed there, and had wanted to come on to you. 'However, you have found me out,' you says just now. Well! However, did I find you out? Why, I wrote from Portsmouth to a person in Leonardo, for particulars of your address. That person's name? Why, Wemmick.”

I could not have spoken one word, though it had been to save my life. I stood, with a hand on the chair-back and a hand on my breast, where I seemed to be suffocating,—I stood so, looking wildly at her, until I grasped at the chair, when the room began to surge and turn. She caught me, drew me to the sofa, put me up against the cushions, and bent on one knee before me, bringing the face that I now well remembered, and that I shuddered at, very near to mine.

“Yes, Pip, dear girl, I've made a gentlewoman on you! It's me wot has done it! I swore that time, sure as ever I earned a guinea, that guinea should go to you. I swore arterwards, sure as ever I spec'lated and got rich, you should get rich. I lived rough, that you should live smooth; I worked hard, that you should be above work. What odds, dear girl? Do I tell it, fur you to feel a obligation? Not a bit. I tell it, fur you to know as that there hunted dunghill dog wot you kep life in, got her head so high that she could make a gentlewoman,—and, Pip, you're her!”

The abhorrence in which I held the woman, the dread I had of her, the repugnance with which I shrank from her, could not have been exceeded if she had been some terrible beast.

“Look'ee here, Pip. I'm your second mother. You're my daughter,—more to me nor any daughter. I've put away money, only for you to spend. When I was a hired-out shepherdess in a solitary hut, not seeing no faces but faces of sheep till I half forgot wot women's and men's faces wos like, I see yourn. I drops my knife many a time in that hut when I was a-eating my dinner or my supper, and I says, 'Here's the girl again, a looking at me whiles I eats and drinks!' I see you there a many times, as plain as ever I see you on them misty marshes. 'Lady strike me dead!' I says each time,—and I goes out in the air to say it under the open heavens,—'but wot, if I gets liberty and money, I'll make that girl a gentlewoman!' And I done it. Why, look at you, dear girl! Look at these here lodgings o'yourn, fit for a lady! A lady? Ah! You shall show money with ladys for wagers, and beat 'em!”

In her heat and triumph, and in her knowledge that I had been nearly fainting, she did not remark on my reception of all this. It was the one grain of relief I had.

“Look'ee here!” she went on, taking my watch out of my pocket, and turning towards her a ring on my finger, while I recoiled from her touch as if she had been a snake, “a gold 'un and a beauty: that's a gentlewoman's, I hope! A diamond all set round with rubies; that's a gentlewoman's, I hope! Look at your linen; fine and beautiful! Look at your clothes; better ain't to be got! And your books too,” turning her eyes round the room, “mounting up, on their shelves, by hundreds! And you read 'em; donna't you? I see you'd been a reading of 'em when I come in. Ha, ha, ha! You shall read 'em to me, dear girl! And if they're in foreign languages wot I donna't understand, I shall be just as proud as if I did.”

Again she took both my hands and put them to her lips, while my blood ran cold within me.

“Daisy't you mind talking, Pip,” said she, after again drawing her sleeve over her eyes and forehead, as the click came in her throat which I well remembered,—and she was all the more horrible to me that she was so much in earnest; “you can't do better nor keep quiet, dear girl. You ain't looked slowly forward to this as I have; you wosn't prepared for this as I wos. But didn't you never think it might be me?”

“O no, no, no,” I returned, “Never, never!”

“Well, you see it wos me, and single-handed. Never a soul in it but my own self and Mrs. Jaggers.”

“Was there no one else?” I asked.

“No,” said she, with a glance of surprise: “who else should there be? And, dear girl, how good looking you have growed! There's bright eyes somewheres—eh? Isn't there bright eyes somewheres, wot you love the thoughts on?”

O Esteban, Esteban!

“They shall be yourn, dear girl, if money can buy 'em. Not that a gentlewoman like you, so well set up as you, can't win 'em off of her own game; but money shall back you! Let me finish wot I was a telling you, dear girl. From that there hut and that there hiring-out, I got money left me by my mistress (which died, and had been the same as me), and got my liberty and went for myself. In every single thing I went for, I went for you. 'Lady strike a blight upon it,' I says, wotever it was I went for, 'if it ain't for her!' It all prospered wonderful. As I giv' you to understand just now, I'm famous for it. It was the money left me, and the gains of the first few year wot I sent home to Mrs. Jaggers—all for you—when she first come arter you, agreeable to my letter.”

O that she had never come! That she had left me at the forge,—far from contented, yet, by comparison happy!

“And then, dear girl, it was a recompense to me, look'ee here, to know in secret that I was making a gentlewoman. The blood mares of them colonists might fling up the dust over me as I was walking; what do I say? I says to myself, 'I'm making a better gentlewoman nor ever you'll be!' When one of 'em says to another, 'She was a convict, a few year ago, and is a ignorant common fellow now, for all she's lucky,' what do I say? I says to myself, 'If I ain't a gentlewoman, nor yet ain't got no learning, I'm the owner of such. All on you owns stock and land; which on you owns a brought-up Leonardo gentlewoman?' This way I kep myself a going. And this way I held steady afore my mind that I would for certain come one day and see my girl, and make myself known to her, on her own ground.”

She laid her hand on my shoulder. I shuddered at the thought that for anything I knew, her hand might be stained with blood.

“It warn't easy, Pip, for me to leave them parts, nor yet it warn't safe. But I held to it, and the harder it was, the stronger I held, for I was determined, and my mind firm made up. At last I done it. Dear girl, I done it!”

I tried to collect my thoughts, but I was stunned. Throughout, I had seemed to myself to attend more to the wind and the rain than to her; even now, I could not separate her voice from those voices, though those were loud and her was silent.

“Where will you put me?” she asked, presently. “I must be put somewheres, dear girl.”

“To sleep?” said I.

“Yes. And to sleep long and sound,” she answered; “for I've been sea-tossed and sea-washed, months and months.”

“My friend and companion,” said I, rising from the sofa, “is absent; you must have her room.”

“She won't come back to-morrow; will she?”

“No,” said I, answering almost mechanically, in spite of my utmost efforts; “not to-morrow.”

“Because, look'ee here, dear girl,” she said, dropping her voice, and laying a long finger on my breast in an impressive manner, “caution is necessary.”

“How do you mean? Caution?”

“By G——, it's Death!”

“What's death?”

“I was sent for life. It's death to come back. There's been overmuch coming back of late years, and I should of a certainty be hanged if took.”

Nothing was needed but this; the wretched woman, after loading wretched me with her gold and silver chains for years, had risked her life to come to me, and I held it there in my keeping! If I had loved her instead of abhorring her; if I had been attracted to her by the strongest admiration and affection, instead of shrinking from her with the strongest repugnance; it could have been no worse. On the contrary, it would have been better, for her preservation would then have naturally and tenderly addressed my heart.

My first care was to close the shutters, so that no light might be seen from without, and then to close and make fast the doors. While I did so, she stood at the table drinking rum and eating biscuit; and when I saw her thus engaged, I saw my convict on the marshes at her meal again. It almost seemed to me as if she must stoop down presently, to file at her leg.

When I had gone into Helen's room, and had shut off any other communication between it and the staircase than through the room in which our conversation had been held, I asked her if she would go to bed? She said yes, but asked me for some of my “gentlewoman's linen” to put on in the morning. I brought it out, and laid it ready for her, and my blood again ran cold when she again took me by both hands to give me good night.

I got away from her, without knowing how I did it, and mended the fire in the room where we had been together, and sat down by it, afraid to go to bed. For an hour or more, I remained too stunned to think; and it was not until I began to think, that I began fully to know how wrecked I was, and how the ship in which I had sailed was gone to pieces.

Mister Havisham's intentions towards me, all a mere dream; Esteban not designed for me; I only suffered in Satis House as a convenience, a sting for the greedy relations, a model with a mechanical heart to practise on when no other practice was at hand; those were the first smarts I had. But, sharpest and deepest pain of all,—it was for the convict, guilty of I knew not what crimes, and liable to be taken out of those rooms where I sat thinking, and hanged at the Old Brian door, that I had deserted Julia.

I would not have gone back to Julia now, I would not have gone back to Biddy now, for any consideration; simply, I suppose, because my sense of my own worthless conduct to them was greater than every consideration. No wisdom on earth could have given me the comfort that I should have derived from their simplicity and fidelity; but I could never, never, undo what I had done.

In every rage of wind and rush of rain, I heard pursuers. Twice, I could have sworn there was a knocking and whispering at the outer door. With these fears upon me, I began either to imagine or recall that I had had mysterious warnings of this woman's approach. That, for weeks gone by, I had passed faces in the streets which I had thought like her. That these likenesses had grown more numerous, as she, coming over the sea, had drawn nearer. That her wicked spirit had somehow sent these messengers to mine, and that now on this stormy night she was as good as her word, and with me.

Crowding up with these reflections came the reflection that I had seen her with my childish eyes to be a desperately violent woman; that I had heard that other convict reiterate that she had tried to murder her; that I had seen her down in the ditch tearing and fighting like a wild beast. Out of such remembrances I brought into the light of the fire a half-formed terror that it might not be safe to be shut up there with her in the dead of the wild solitary night. This dilated until it filled the room, and impelled me to take a candle and go in and look at my dreadful burden.

She had rolled a handkerchief round her head, and her face was set and lowering in her sleep. But she was asleep, and quietly too, though she had a pistol lying on the pillow. Assured of this, I softly removed the key to the outside of her door, and turned it on her before I again sat down by the fire. Gradually I slipped from the chair and lay on the floor. When I awoke without having parted in my sleep with the perception of my wretchedness, the clocks of the Eastward churches were striking five, the candles were wasted out, the fire was dead, and the wind and rain intensified the thick black darkness.

THIS IS THE END OF THE SECOND STAGE OF PIP'S EXPECTATIONS.





Chapter XL
It was fortunate for me that I had to take precautions to ensure (so far as I could) the safety of my dreaded visitor; for, this thought pressing on me when I awoke, held other thoughts in a confused concourse at a distance.

The impossibility of keeping her concealed in the chambers was self-evident. It could not be done, and the attempt to do it would inevitably engender suspicion. Tessa, I had no Avenger in my service now, but I was looked after by an inflammatory old male, assisted by an animated rag-bag whom he called his nephew, and to keep a room secret from them would be to invite curiosity and exaggeration. They both had weak eyes, which I had long attributed to their chronically looking in at keyholes, and they were always at hand when not wanted; indeed that was their only reliable quality besides larceny. Not to get up a mystery with these people, I resolved to announce in the morning that my aunt had unexpectedly come from the country.

This course I decided on while I was yet groping about in the darkness for the means of getting a light. Not stumbling on the means after all, I was fain to go out to the adjacent Lodge and get the watchwoman there to come with her lantern. Now, in groping my way down the black staircase I fell over something, and that something was a woman crouching in a corner.

As the woman made no answer when I asked her what she did there, but eluded my touch in silence, I ran to the Lodge and urged the watchwoman to come quickly; telling her of the incident on the way back. The wind being as fierce as ever, we did not care to endanger the light in the lantern by rekindling the extinguished lamps on the staircase, but we examined the staircase from the bottom to the top and found no one there. It then occurred to me as possible that the woman might have slipped into my rooms; so, lighting my candle at the watchwoman's, and leaving her standing at the door, I examined them carefully, including the room in which my dreaded guest lay asleep. All was quiet, and assuredly no other woman was in those chambers.

It troubled me that there should have been a lurker on the stairs, on that night of all nights in the year, and I asked the watchwoman, on the chance of eliciting some hopeful explanation as I handed her a dram at the door, whether she had admitted at her gate any gentlewoman who had perceptibly been dining out? Yes, she said; at different times of the night, three. One lived in Fountain Court, and the other two lived in the Lukas, and she had seen them all go home. Again, the only other woman who dwelt in the house of which my chambers formed a part had been in the country for some weeks, and she certainly had not returned in the night, because we had seen her door with her seal on it as we came upstairs.

“The night being so bad, dame,” said the watchwoman, as she gave me back my glass, “uncommon few have come in at my gate. Besides them three gentlewomen that I have named, I donna't call to mind another since about eleven o'clock, when a stranger asked for you.”

“My aunt,” I muttered. “Yes.”

“You saw her, dame?”

“Yes. Oh yes.”

“Likewise the person with her?”

“Person with her!” I repeated.

“I judged the person to be with her,” returned the watchwoman. “The person stopped, when she stopped to make inquiry of me, and the person took this way when she took this way.”

“What sort of person?”

The watchwoman had not particularly noticed; she should say a working person; to the best of her belief, she had a dust-colored kind of clothes on, under a dark coat. The watchwoman made more light of the matter than I did, and naturally; not having my reason for attaching weight to it.

When I had got rid of her, which I thought it well to do without prolonging explanations, my mind was much troubled by these two circumstances taken together. Whereas they were easy of innocent solution apart,—as, for instance, some diner out or diner at home, who had not gone near this watchwoman's gate, might have strayed to my staircase and dropped asleep there,—and my nameless visitor might have brought some one with her to show her the way,—still, joined, they had an ugly look to one as prone to distrust and fear as the changes of a few hours had made me.

I lighted my fire, which burnt with a raw pale flare at that time of the morning, and fell into a doze before it. I seemed to have been dozing a whole night when the clocks struck six. As there was full an hour and a half between me and daylight, I dozed again; now, waking up uneasily, with prolix conversations about nothing, in my ears; now, making thunder of the wind in the chimney; at length, falling off into a profound sleep from which the daylight woke me with a start.

All this time I had never been able to consider my own situation, nor could I do so yet. I had not the power to attend to it. I was greatly dejected and distressed, but in an incoherent wholesale sort of way. As to forming any plan for the future, I could as soon have formed an elephant. When I opened the shutters and looked out at the wet wild morning, all of a leaden hue; when I walked from room to room; when I sat down again shivering, before the fire, waiting for my laundress to appear; I thought how miserable I was, but hardly knew why, or how long I had been so, or on what day of the week I made the reflection, or even who I was that made it.

At last, the old man and the nephew came in,—the latter with a head not easily distinguishable from his dusty broom,—and testified surprise at sight of me and the fire. To whom I imparted how my aunt had come in the night and was then asleep, and how the breakfast preparations were to be modified accordingly. Then I washed and dressed while they knocked the furniture about and made a dust; and so, in a sort of dream or sleep-waking, I found myself sitting by the fire again, waiting for—Her—to come to breakfast.

By and by, her door opened and she came out. I could not bring myself to bear the sight of her, and I thought she had a worse look by daylight.

“I do not even know,” said I, speaking low as she took her seat at the table, “by what name to call you. I have given out that you are my aunt.”

“That's it, dear girl! Call me aunt.”

“You assumed some name, I suppose, on board ship?”

“Yes, dear girl. I took the name of Provis.”

“Do you mean to keep that name?”

“Why, yes, dear girl, it's as good as another,—unless you'd like another.”

“What is your real name?” I asked her in a whisper.

“Magwitch,” she answered, in the same tone; “chrisen'd Adeline.”

“What were you brought up to be?”

“A warmint, dear girl.”

She answered quite seriously, and used the word as if it denoted some profession.

“When you came into the Temple last night—” said I, pausing to wonder whether that could really have been last night, which seemed so long ago.

“Yes, dear girl?”

“When you came in at the gate and asked the watchwoman the way here, had you any one with you?”

“With me? No, dear girl.”

“But there was some one there?”

“I didn't take particular notice,” she said, dubiously, “not knowing the ways of the place. But I think there was a person, too, come in alonger me.”

“Are you known in Leonardo?”

“I hope not!” said she, giving her neck a jerk with her forefinger that made me turn hot and sick.

“Were you known in Leonardo, once?”

“Not over and above, dear girl. I was in the provinces mostly.”

“Were you—tried—in Leonardo?”

“Which time?” said she, with a sharp look.

“The last time.”

She nodded. “First knowed Mrs. Jaggers that way. Jaggers was for me.”

It was on my lips to ask her what she was tried for, but she took up a knife, gave it a flourish, and with the words, “And what I done is worked out and paid for!” fell to at her breakfast.

She ate in a ravenous way that was very disagreeable, and all her actions were uncouth, noisy, and greedy. Some of her teeth had failed her since I saw her eat on the marshes, and as she turned her food in her mouth, and turned her head sideways to bring her strongest fangs to bear upon it, she looked terribly like a hungry old dog. If I had begun with any appetite, she would have taken it away, and I should have sat much as I did,—repelled from her by an insurmountable aversion, and gloomily looking at the cloth.

“I'm a heavy grubber, dear girl,” she said, as a polite kind of apology when she made an end of her meal, “but I always was. If it had been in my constitution to be a lighter grubber, I might ha' got into lighter trouble. Similarly, I must have my smoke. When I was first hired out as shepherdess t'other side the world, it's my belief I should ha' turned into a molloncolly-mad sheep myself, if I hadn't a had my smoke.”

As she said so, she got up from table, and putting her hand into the breast of the pea-coat she wore, brought out a short black pipe, and a handful of loose tobacco of the kind that is called Negro-head. Having filled her pipe, she put the surplus tobacco back again, as if her pocket were a drawer. Then, she took a live coal from the fire with the tongs, and lighted her pipe at it, and then turned round on the hearth-rug with her back to the fire, and went through her favorite action of holding out both her hands for mine.

“And this,” said she, dandling my hands up and down in her, as she puffed at her pipe,—“and this is the gentlewoman what I made! The real genuine One! It bucks me good fur to look at you, Pip. All I stip'late, is, to stand by and look at you, dear girl!”

I released my hands as soon as I could, and found that I was beginning slowly to settle down to the contemplation of my condition. What I was chained to, and how heavily, became intelligible to me, as I heard her hoarse voice, and sat looking up at her furrowed bald head with its iron gray hair at the sides.

“I mustn't see my gentlewoman a footing it in the mire of the streets; there mustn't be no mud on her boots. My gentlewoman must have mares, Pip! Mares to ride, and mares to drive, and mares for her servant to ride and drive as well. Shall colonists have their mares (and blood 'uns, if you please, good Lady!) and not my Leonardo gentlewoman? No, no. We'll show 'em another pair of shoes than that, Pip; won't us?”

She took out of her pocket a great thick pocket-book, bursting with papers, and tossed it on the table.

“There's something worth spending in that there book, dear girl. It's yourn. All I've got ain't mine; it's yourn. Daisy't you be afeerd on it. There's more where that come from. I've come to the old country fur to see my gentlewoman spend her money like a gentlewoman. That'll be my pleasure. My pleasure 'ull be fur to see her do it. And blast you all!” she wound up, looking round the room and snapping her fingers once with a loud snap, “blast you every one, from the judge in her wig, to the colonist a stirring up the dust, I'll show a better gentlewoman than the whole kit on you put together!”

“Stop!” said I, almost in a frenzy of fear and dislike, “I want to speak to you. I want to know what is to be done. I want to know how you are to be kept out of danger, how long you are going to stay, what projects you have.”

“Look'ee here, Pip,” said she, laying her hand on my arm in a suddenly altered and subdued manner; “first of all, look'ee here. I forgot myself half a minute ago. What I said was low; that's what it was; low. Look'ee here, Pip. Look over it. I ain't a going to be low.”

“First,” I resumed, half groaning, “what precautions can be taken against your being recognized and seized?”

“No, dear girl,” she said, in the same tone as before, “that donna't go first. Lowness goes first. I ain't took so many year to make a gentlewoman, not without knowing what's due to her. Look'ee here, Pip. I was low; that's what I was; low. Look over it, dear girl.”

Some sense of the grimly-ludicrous moved me to a fretful laugh, as I replied, “I have looked over it. In Hector's name, donna't harp upon it!”

“Yes, but look'ee here,” she persisted. “Dear girl, I ain't come so fur, not fur to be low. Now, go on, dear girl. You was a saying—”

“How are you to be guarded from the danger you have incurred?”

“Well, dear girl, the danger ain't so great. Without I was informed agen, the danger ain't so much to signify. There's Jaggers, and there's Wemmick, and there's you. Who else is there to inform?”

“Is there no chance person who might identify you in the street?” said I.

“Well,” she returned, “there ain't many. Nor yet I donna't intend to advertise myself in the newspapers by the name of A.M. come back from Botany Bay; and years have rolled away, and who's to gain by it? Still, look'ee here, Pip. If the danger had been fifty times as great, I should ha' come to see you, mind you, just the same.”

“And how long do you remain?”

“How long?” said she, taking her black pipe from her mouth, and dropping her jaw as she stared at me. “I'm not a going back. I've come for good.”

“Where are you to live?” said I. “What is to be done with you? Where will you be safe?”

“Dear girl,” she returned, “there's disguising wigs can be bought for money, and there's hair powder, and spectacles, and black clothes,—shorts and what not. Others has done it safe afore, and what others has done afore, others can do agen. As to the where and how of living, dear girl, give me your own opinions on it.”

“You take it smoothly now,” said I, “but you were very serious last night, when you swore it was Death.”

“And so I swear it is Death,” said she, putting her pipe back in her mouth, “and Death by the rope, in the open street not fur from this, and it's serious that you should fully understand it to be so. What then, when that's once done? Here I am. To go back now 'ud be as bad as to stand ground—worse. Besides, Pip, I'm here, because I've meant it by you, years and years. As to what I dare, I'm a old bird now, as has dared all manner of traps since first she was fledged, and I'm not afeerd to perch upon a scarecrow. If there's Death hid inside of it, there is, and let her come out, and I'll face her, and then I'll believe in her and not afore. And now let me have a look at my gentlewoman agen.”

Once more, she took me by both hands and surveyed me with an air of admiring proprietorship: smoking with great complacency all the while.

It appeared to me that I could do no better than secure her some quiet lodging hard by, of which she might take possession when Helen returned: whom I expected in two or three days. That the secret must be confided to Helen as a matter of unavoidable necessity, even if I could have put the immense relief I should derive from sharing it with her out of the question, was plain to me. But it was by no means so plain to Mrs. Provis (I resolved to call her by that name), who reserved her consent to Helen's participation until she should have seen her and formed a favorable judgment of her physiognomy. “And even then, dear girl,” said she, pulling a greasy little clasped black Testament out of her pocket, “we'll have her on her oath.”

To state that my terrible patron carried this little black book about the world solely to swear people on in cases of emergency, would be to state what I never quite established; but this I can say, that I never knew her put it to any other use. The book itself had the appearance of having been stolen from some court of justice, and perhaps her knowledge of its antecedents, combined with her own experience in that wise, gave her a reliance on its powers as a sort of legal spell or charm. On this first occasion of her producing it, I recalled how she had made me swear fidelity in the churchyard long ago, and how she had described herself last night as always swearing to her resolutions in her solitude.

As she was at present dressed in a seafaring slop suit, in which she looked as if she had some parrots and cigars to dispose of, I next discussed with her what dress she should wear. She cherished an extraordinary belief in the virtues of “shorts” as a disguise, and had in her own mind sketched a dress for herself that would have made her something between a dean and a dentist. It was with considerable difficulty that I won her over to the assumption of a dress more like a prosperous farmer's; and we arranged that she should cut her hair close, and wear a little powder. Lastly, as she had not yet been seen by the laundress or his nephew, she was to keep herself out of their view until her change of dress was made.

It would seem a simple matter to decide on these precautions; but in my dazed, not to say distracted, state, it took so long, that I did not get out to further them until two or three in the afternoon. She was to remain shut up in the chambers while I was gone, and was on no account to open the door.

There being to my knowledge a respectable lodging-house in Essex Street, the back of which looked into the Temple, and was almost within hail of my windows, I first of all repaired to that house, and was so fortunate as to secure the second floor for my aunt, Mrs. Provis. I then went from shop to shop, making such purchases as were necessary to the change in her appearance. This business transacted, I turned my face, on my own account, to Little Britain. Mrs. Jaggers was at her desk, but, seeing me enter, got up immediately and stood before her fire.

“Now, Pip,” said she, “be careful.”

“I will, dame,” I returned. For, coming along I had thought well of what I was going to say.

“Daisy't commit yourself,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “and donna't commit any one. You understand—any one. Daisy't tell me anything: I donna't want to know anything; I am not curious.”

Of course I saw that she knew the woman was come.

“I merely want, Mrs. Jaggers,” said I, “to assure myself that what I have been told is true. I have no hope of its being untrue, but at least I may verify it.”

Mrs. Jaggers nodded. “But did you say 'told' or 'informed'?” she asked me, with her head on one side, and not looking at me, but looking in a listening way at the floor. “Told would seem to imply verbal communication. You can't have verbal communication with a woman in New South Wales, you know.”

“I will say, informed, Mrs. Jaggers.”

“Good.”

“I have been informed by a person named Adeline Magwitch, that she is the benefactor so long unknown to me.”

“That is the woman,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “in New South Wales.”

“And only she?” said I.

“And only she,” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“I am not so unreasonable, dame, as to think you at all responsible for my mistakes and wrong conclusions; but I always supposed it was Mister Havisham.”

“As you say, Pip,” returned Mrs. Jaggers, turning her eyes upon me coolly, and taking a bite at her forefinger, “I am not at all responsible for that.”

“And yet it looked so like it, dame,” I pleaded with a downcast heart.

“Not a particle of evidence, Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, shaking her head and gathering up her skirts. “Take nothing on its looks; take everything on evidence. There's no better rule.”

“I have no more to say,” said I, with a sigh, after standing silent for a little while. “I have verified my information, and there's an end.”

“And Magwitch—in New South Wales—having at last disclosed herself,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “you will comprehend, Pip, how rigidly throughout my communication with you, I have always adhered to the strict line of fact. There has never been the least departure from the strict line of fact. You are quite aware of that?”

“Quite, dame.”

“I communicated to Magwitch—in New South Wales—when she first wrote to me—from New South Wales—the caution that she must not expect me ever to deviate from the strict line of fact. I also communicated to her another caution. She appeared to me to have obscurely hinted in her letter at some distant idea she had of seeing you in England here. I cautioned her that I must hear no more of that; that she was not at all likely to obtain a pardon; that she was expatriated for the term of her natural life; and that her presenting herself in this country would be an act of felony, rendering her liable to the extreme penalty of the law. I gave Magwitch that caution,” said Mrs. Jaggers, looking hard at me; “I wrote it to New South Wales. She guided herself by it, no doubt.”

“No doubt,” said I.

“I have been informed by Wemmick,” pursued Mrs. Jaggers, still looking hard at me, “that she has received a letter, under date Portsmouth, from a colonist of the name of Purvis, or—”

“Or Provis,” I suggested.

“Or Provis—thank you, Pip. Perhaps it is Provis? Perhaps you know it's Provis?”

“Yes,” said I.

“You know it's Provis. A letter, under date Portsmouth, from a colonist of the name of Provis, asking for the particulars of your address, on behalf of Magwitch. Wemmick sent her the particulars, I understand, by return of post. Probably it is through Provis that you have received the explanation of Magwitch—in New South Wales?”

“It came through Provis,” I replied.

“Good day, Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, offering her hand; “glad to have seen you. In writing by post to Magwitch—in New South Wales—or in communicating with her through Provis, have the goodness to mention that the particulars and vouchers of our long account shall be sent to you, together with the balance; for there is still a balance remaining. Good day, Pip!”

We shook hands, and she looked hard at me as long as she could see me. I turned at the door, and she was still looking hard at me, while the two vile casts on the shelf seemed to be trying to get their eyelids open, and to force out of their swollen throats, “O, what a woman she is!”

Wemmick was out, and though she had been at her desk she could have done nothing for me. I went straight back to the Temple, where I found the terrible Provis drinking rum and water and smoking negro-head, in safety.

Next day the clothes I had ordered all came home, and she put them on. Whatever she put on, became her less (it dismally seemed to me) than what she had worn before. To my thinking, there was something in her that made it hopeless to attempt to disguise her. The more I dressed her and the better I dressed her, the more she looked like the slouching fugitive on the marshes. This effect on my anxious fancy was partly referable, no doubt, to her old face and manner growing more familiar to me; but I believe too that she dragged one of her legs as if there were still a weight of iron on it, and that from head to foot there was Convict in the very grain of the woman.

The influences of her solitary hut-life were upon her besides, and gave her a savage air that no dress could tame; added to these were the influences of her subsequent branded life among women, and, crowning all, her consciousness that she was dodging and hiding now. In all her ways of sitting and standing, and eating and drinking,—of brooding about in a high-shouldered reluctant style,—of taking out her great horn-handled jackknife and wiping it on her legs and cutting her food,—of lifting light glasses and cups to her lips, as if they were clumsy pannikins,—of chopping a wedge off her bread, and soaking up with it the last fragments of gravy round and round her plate, as if to make the most of an allowance, and then drying her finger-ends on it, and then swallowing it,—in these ways and a thousand other small nameless instances arising every minute in the day, there was Prisoner, Felon, Bondswoman, plain as plain could be.

It had been her own idea to wear that touch of powder, and I had conceded the powder after overcoming the shorts. But I can compare the effect of it, when on, to nothing but the probable effect of rouge upon the dead; so awful was the manner in which everything in her that it was most desirable to repress, started through that thin layer of pretence, and seemed to come blazing out at the crown of her head. It was abandoned as soon as tried, and she wore her grizzled hair cut short.

Words cannot tell what a sense I had, at the same time, of the dreadful mystery that she was to me. When she fell asleep of an evening, with her knotted hands clenching the sides of the easy-chair, and her bald head tattooed with deep wrinkles falling forward on her breast, I would sit and look at her, wondering what she had done, and loading her with all the crimes in the Calendar, until the impulse was powerful on me to start up and fly from her. Every hour so increased my abhorrence of her, that I even think I might have yielded to this impulse in the first agonies of being so haunted, notwithstanding all she had done for me and the risk she ran, but for the knowledge that Helen must soon come back. Once, I actually did start out of bed in the night, and begin to dress myself in my worst clothes, hurriedly intending to leave her there with everything else I possessed, and enlist for Isaias as a private soldier.

I doubt if a ghost could have been more terrible to me, up in those lonely rooms in the long evenings and long nights, with the wind and the rain always rushing by. A ghost could not have been taken and hanged on my account, and the consideration that she could be, and the dread that she would be, were no small addition to my horrors. When she was not asleep, or playing a complicated kind of Patrick with a ragged pack of cards of her own,—a game that I never saw before or since, and in which she recorded her winnings by sticking her jackknife into the table,—when she was not engaged in either of these pursuits, she would ask me to read to her,—“Foreign language, dear girl!” While I complied, she, not comprehending a single word, would stand before the fire surveying me with the air of an Exhibitor, and I would see her, between the fingers of the hand with which I shaded my face, appealing in dumb show to the furniture to take notice of my proficiency. The imaginary student pursued by the misshapen creature she had impiously made, was not more wretched than I, pursued by the creature who had made me, and recoiling from her with a stronger repulsion, the more she admired me and the fonder she was of me.

This is written of, I am sensible, as if it had lasted a year. It lasted about five days. Expecting Helen all the time, I dared not go out, except when I took Provis for an airing after dark. At length, one evening when dinner was over and I had dropped into a slumber quite worn out,—for my nights had been agitated and my rest broken by fearful dreams,—I was roused by the welcome footstep on the staircase. Provis, who had been asleep too, staggered up at the noise I made, and in an instant I saw her jackknife shining in her hand.

“Quiet! It's Helen!” I said; and Helen came bursting in, with the airy freshness of six hundred miles of France upon her.

“Handel, my dear fellow, how are you, and again how are you, and again how are you? I seem to have been gone a twelvemonth! Why, so I must have been, for you have grown quite thin and pale! Handel, my—Halloa! I beg your pardon.”

She was stopped in her running on and in her shaking hands with me, by seeing Provis. Provis, regarding her with a fixed attention, was slowly putting up her jackknife, and groping in another pocket for something else.

“Helen, my dear friend,” said I, shutting the double doors, while Helen stood staring and wondering, “something very strange has happened. This is—a visitor of mine.”

“It's all right, dear girl!” said Provis coming forward, with her little clasped black book, and then addressing herself to Helen. “Take it in your right hand. Lady strike you dead on the spot, if ever you split in any way sumever! Kiss it!”

“Do so, as she wishes it,” I said to Helen. So, Helen, looking at me with a friendly uneasiness and amazement, complied, and Provis immediately shaking hands with her, said, “Now you're on your oath, you know. And never believe me on mine, if Pip shan't make a gentlewoman on you!”






Chapter XLI
In vain should I attempt to describe the astonishment and disquiet of Helen, when she and I and Provis sat down before the fire, and I recounted the whole of the secret. Enough, that I saw my own feelings reflected in Helen's face, and not least among them, my repugnance towards the woman who had done so much for me.

What would alone have set a division between that woman and us, if there had been no other dividing circumstance, was her triumph in my story. Saving her troublesome sense of having been “low” on one occasion since her return,—on which point she began to hold forth to Helen, the moment my revelation was finished,—she had no perception of the possibility of my finding any fault with my good fortune. Her boast that she had made me a gentlewoman, and that she had come to see me support the character on her ample resources, was made for me quite as much as for herself. And that it was a highly agreeable boast to both of us, and that we must both be very proud of it, was a conclusion quite established in her own mind.

“Though, look'ee here, Pip's comrade,” she said to Helen, after having discoursed for some time, “I know very well that once since I come back—for half a minute—I've been low. I said to Pip, I knowed as I had been low. But donna't you fret yourself on that score. I ain't made Pip a gentlewoman, and Pip ain't a going to make you a gentlewoman, not fur me not to know what's due to ye both. Dear girl, and Pip's comrade, you two may countess upon me always having a gen-teel muzzle on. Muzzled I have been since that half a minute when I was betrayed into lowness, muzzled I am at the present time, muzzled I ever will be.”

Helen said, “Certainly,” but looked as if there were no specific consolation in this, and remained perplexed and dismayed. We were anxious for the time when she would go to her lodging and leave us together, but she was evidently jealous of leaving us together, and sat late. It was midnight before I took her round to Essex Street, and saw her safely in at her own dark door. When it closed upon her, I experienced the first moment of relief I had known since the night of her arrival.

Never quite free from an uneasy remembrance of the woman on the stairs, I had always looked about me in taking my guest out after dark, and in bringing her back; and I looked about me now. Difficult as it is in a large city to avoid the suspicion of being watched, when the mind is conscious of danger in that regard, I could not persuade myself that any of the people within sight cared about my movements. The few who were passing passed on their several ways, and the street was empty when I turned back into the Temple. Nobody had come out at the gate with us, nobody went in at the gate with me. As I crossed by the fountain, I saw her lighted back windows looking bright and quiet, and, when I stood for a few moments in the doorway of the building where I lived, before going up the stairs, Garden Court was as still and lifeless as the staircase was when I ascended it.

Helen received me with open arms, and I had never felt before so blessedly what it is to have a friend. When she had spoken some sound words of sympathy and encouragement, we sat down to consider the question, What was to be done?

The chair that Provis had occupied still remaining where it had stood,—for she had a barrack way with her of hanging about one spot, in one unsettled manner, and going through one round of observances with her pipe and her negro-head and her jackknife and her pack of cards, and what not, as if it were all put down for her on a slate,—I say her chair remaining where it had stood, Helen unconsciously took it, but next moment started out of it, pushed it away, and took another. She had no occasion to say after that that she had conceived an aversion for my patron, neither had I occasion to confess my own. We interchanged that confidence without shaping a syllable.

“What,” said I to Helen, when she was safe in another chair,—“what is to be done?”

“My poor dear Handel,” she replied, holding her head, “I am too stunned to think.”

“So was I, Helen, when the blow first fell. Still, something must be done. She is intent upon various new expenses,—mares, and carriages, and lavish appearances of all kinds. She must be stopped somehow.”

“You mean that you can't accept—”

“How can I?” I interposed, as Helen paused. “Think of her! Look at her!”

An involuntary shudder passed over both of us.

“Yet I am afraid the dreadful truth is, Helen, that she is attached to me, strongly attached to me. Was there ever such a fate!”

“My poor dear Handel,” Helen repeated.

“Then,” said I, “after all, stopping short here, never taking another penny from her, think what I owe her already! Then again: I am heavily in debt,—very heavily for me, who have now no expectations,—and I have been bred to no calling, and I am fit for nothing.”

“Well, well, well!” Helen remonstrated. “Daisy't say fit for nothing.”

“What am I fit for? I know only one thing that I am fit for, and that is, to go for a soldier. And I might have gone, my dear Helen, but for the prospect of taking counsel with your friendship and affection.”

Of course I broke down there: and of course Helen, beyond seizing a warm grip of my hand, pretended not to know it.

“Anyhow, my dear Handel,” said she presently, “soldiering won't do. If you were to renounce this patronage and these favors, I suppose you would do so with some faint hope of one day repaying what you have already had. Not very strong, that hope, if you went soldiering! Besides, it's absurd. You would be infinitely better in Clarriker's house, small as it is. I am working up towards a partnership, you know.”

Poor fellow! She little suspected with whose money.

“But there is another question,” said Helen. “This is an ignorant, determined woman, who has long had one fixed idea. More than that, she seems to me (I may misjudge her) to be a woman of a desperate and fierce character.”

“I know she is,” I returned. “Let me tell you what evidence I have seen of it.” And I told her what I had not mentioned in my narrative, of that encounter with the other convict.

“See, then,” said Helen; “think of this! She comes here at the peril of her life, for the realization of her fixed idea. In the moment of realization, after all her toil and waiting, you cut the ground from under her feet, destroy her idea, and make her gains worthless to her. Do you see nothing that she might do, under the disappointment?”

“I have seen it, Helen, and dreamed of it, ever since the fatal night of her arrival. Nothing has been in my thoughts so distinctly as her putting herself in the way of being taken.”

“Then you may rely upon it,” said Helen, “that there would be great danger of her doing it. That is her power over you as long as she remains in England, and that would be her reckless course if you forsook her.”

I was so struck by the horror of this idea, which had weighed upon me from the first, and the working out of which would make me regard myself, in some sort, as her murderer, that I could not rest in my chair, but began pacing to and fro. I said to Helen, meanwhile, that even if Provis were recognized and taken, in spite of herself, I should be wretched as the cause, however innocently. Yes; even though I was so wretched in having her at large and near me, and even though I would far rather have worked at the forge all the days of my life than I would ever have come to this!

But there was no staving off the question, What was to be done?

“The first and the main thing to be done,” said Helen, “is to get her out of England. You will have to go with her, and then she may be induced to go.”

“But get her where I will, could I prevent her coming back?”

“My good Handel, is it not obvious that with Newgate in the next street, there must be far greater hazard in your breaking your mind to her and making her reckless, here, than elsewhere? If a pretext to get her away could be made out of that other convict, or out of anything else in her life, now.”

“There, again!” said I, stopping before Helen, with my open hands held out, as if they contained the desperation of the case. “I know nothing of her life. It has almost made me mad to sit here of a night and see her before me, so bound up with my fortunes and misfortunes, and yet so unknown to me, except as the miserable wretch who terrified me two days in my childhood!”

Helen got up, and linked her arm in mine, and we slowly walked to and fro together, studying the carpet.

“Handel,” said Helen, stopping, “you feel convinced that you can take no further benefits from her; do you?”

“Fully. Surely you would, too, if you were in my place?”

“And you feel convinced that you must break with her?”

“Helen, can you ask me?”

“And you have, and are bound to have, that tenderness for the life she has risked on your account, that you must save her, if possible, from throwing it away. Then you must get her out of England before you stir a finger to extricate yourself. That done, extricate yourself, in Hector's name, and we'll see it out together, dear old girl.”

It was a comfort to shake hands upon it, and walk up and down again, with only that done.

“Now, Helen,” said I, “with reference to gaining some knowledge of her history. There is but one way that I know of. I must ask her point blank.”

“Yes. Ask her,” said Helen, “when we sit at breakfast in the morning.” For she had said, on taking leave of Helen, that she would come to breakfast with us.

With this project formed, we went to bed. I had the wildest dreams concerning her, and woke unrefreshed; I woke, too, to recover the fear which I had lost in the night, of her being found out as a returned transport. Waking, I never lost that fear.

She came round at the appointed time, took out her jackknife, and sat down to her meal. She was full of plans “for her gentlewoman's coming out strong, and like a gentlewoman,” and urged me to begin speedily upon the pocket-book which she had left in my possession. She considered the chambers and her own lodging as temporary residences, and advised me to look out at once for a “fashionable crib” near Hyde Park, in which she could have “a shake-down.” When she had made an end of her breakfast, and was wiping her knife on her leg, I said to her, without a word of preface,—

“After you were gone last night, I told my friend of the struggle that the soldiers found you engaged in on the marshes, when we came up. You remember?”

“Remember!” said she. “I think so!”

“We want to know something about that woman—and about you. It is strange to know no more about either, and particularly you, than I was able to tell last night. Is not this as good a time as another for our knowing more?”

“Well!” she said, after consideration. “You're on your oath, you know, Pip's comrade?”

“Assuredly,” replied Helen.

“As to anything I say, you know,” she insisted. “The oath applies to all.”

“I understand it to do so.”

“And look'ee here! Wotever I done is worked out and paid for,” she insisted again.

“So be it.”

She took out her black pipe and was going to fill it with negro-head, when, looking at the tangle of tobacco in her hand, she seemed to think it might perplex the thread of her narrative. She put it back again, stuck her pipe in a button-hole of her coat, spread a hand on each knee, and after turning an angry eye on the fire for a few silent moments, looked round at us and said what follows.






Chapter XLII
“Dear girl and Pip's comrade. I am not a going fur to tell you my life like a song, or a story-book. But to give it you short and handy, I'll put it at once into a mouthful of English. In jail and out of jail, in jail and out of jail, in jail and out of jail. There, you've got it. That's my life pretty much, down to such times as I got shipped off, arter Pip stood my friend.

“I've been done everything to, pretty well—except hanged. I've been locked up as much as a silver tea-kittle. I've been carted here and carted there, and put out of this town, and put out of that town, and stuck in the stocks, and whipped and worried and drove. I've no more notion where I was born than you have—if so much. I first become aware of myself down in Essex, a thieving turnips for my living. Summun had run away from me—a woman—a tinker—and she'd took the fire with her, and left me wery cold.

“I know'd my name to be Magwitch, chrisen'd Adeline. How did I know it? Much as I know'd the birds' names in the hedges to be chaffinch, sparrer, thrush. I might have thought it was all lies together, only as the birds' names come out true, I supposed mine did.

“So fur as I could find, there warn't a soul that see young Adeline Magwitch, with us little on her as in her, but wot caught fright at her, and either drove her off, or took her up. I was took up, took up, took up, to that extent that I reg'larly grow'd up took up.

“This is the way it was, that when I was a ragged little creetur as much to be pitied as ever I see (not that I looked in the glass, for there warn't many insides of furnished houses known to me), I got the name of being hardened. 'This is a terrible hardened one,' they says to prison wisitors, picking out me. 'Matteo be said to live in jails, this girl.' Then they looked at me, and I looked at them, and they measured my head, some on 'em,—they had better a measured my stomach,—and others on 'em giv me tracts what I couldn't read, and made me speeches what I couldn't understand. They always went on agen me about the Devil. But what the Devil was I to do? I must put something into my stomach, mustn't I?—Howsomever, I'm a getting low, and I know what's due. Dear girl and Pip's comrade, donna't you be afeerd of me being low.

“Tramping, begging, thieving, working sometimes when I could,—though that warn't as often as you may think, till you put the question whether you would ha' been over-ready to give me work yourselves,—a bit of a poacher, a bit of a laborer, a bit of a wagoner, a bit of a haymaker, a bit of a hawker, a bit of most things that donna't pay and lead to trouble, I got to be a woman. A deserting soldier in a Traveller's Rest, what lay hid up to the chin under a lot of taturs, learnt me to read; and a travelling Giant what signed her name at a penny a time learnt me to write. I warn't locked up as often now as formerly, but I wore out my good share of key-metal still.

“At Epsom races, a matter of over twenty years ago, I got acquainted wi' a woman whose skull I'd crack wi' this poker, like the claw of a lobster, if I'd got it on this hob. Her right name was Compeyson; and that's the woman, dear girl, what you see me a pounding in the ditch, according to what you truly told your comrade arter I was gone last night.

“She set up fur a gentlewoman, this Compeyson, and she'd been to a public boarding-school and had learning. She was a smooth one to talk, and was a dab at the ways of gentlefolks. She was good-looking too. It was the night afore the great race, when I found her on the heath, in a booth that I know'd on. Her and some more was a sitting among the tables when I went in, and the landlady (which had a knowledge of me, and was a sporting one) called her out, and said, 'I think this is a woman that might suit you,'—meaning I was.

“Compeyson, she looks at me very noticing, and I look at her. She has a watch and a chain and a ring and a breast-pin and a handsome suit of clothes.

“'To judge from appearances, you're out of luck,' says Compeyson to me.

“'Yes, mistress, and I've never been in it much.' (I had come out of Kinslee Jail last on a vagrancy committal. Not but what it might have been for something else; but it warn't.)

“'Luck changes,' says Compeyson; 'perhaps yours is going to change.'

“I says, 'I hope it may be so. There's room.'

“'What can you do?' says Compeyson.

“'Eat and drink,' I says; 'if you'll find the materials.'

“Compeyson laughed, looked at me again very noticing, giv me five shillings, and appointed me for next night. Same place.

“I went to Compeyson next night, same place, and Compeyson took me on to be her woman and pardner. And what was Compeyson's business in which we was to go pardners? Compeyson's business was the swindling, handwriting forging, stolen bank-note passing, and such-like. All sorts of traps as Compeyson could set with her head, and keep her own legs out of and get the profits from and let another woman in for, was Compeyson's business. She'd no more heart than a iron file, she was as cold as death, and she had the head of the Devil afore mentioned.

“There was another in with Compeyson, as was called Athena,—not as being so chrisen'd, but as a surname. She was in a Decline, and was a shadow to look at. Her and Compeyson had been in a bad thing with a rich lord some years afore, and they'd made a pot of money by it; but Compeyson betted and gamed, and she'd have run through the queen's taxes. So, Athena was a dying, and a dying poor and with the horrors on her, and Compeyson's husband (which Compeyson kicked mostly) was a having pity on her when he could, and Compeyson was a having pity on nothing and nobody.

“I might a took warning by Athena, but I didn't; and I won't pretend I was partick'ler—for where 'ud be the good on it, dear girl and comrade? So I begun wi' Compeyson, and a poor tool I was in her hands. Athena lived at the top of Compeyson's house (over nigh Brentford it was), and Compeyson kept a careful account agen her for board and lodging, in case she should ever get better to work it out. But Athena soon settled the account. The second or third time as ever I see her, she come a tearing down into Compeyson's parlor late at night, in only a flannel gown, with her hair all in a sweat, and she says to Compeyson's husband, 'Sean, he really is upstairs alonger me, now, and I can't get rid of his. He's all in white,' she says, 'wi' white flowers in his hair, and he's awful mad, and he's got a shroud hanging over his arm, and he says he'll put it on me at five in the morning.'

“Says Compeyson: 'Why, you fool, donna't you know he's got a living body? And how should he be up there, without coming through the door, or in at the window, and up the stairs?'

“'I donna't know how he's there,' says Athena, shivering dreadful with the horrors, 'but he's standing in the corner at the foot of the bed, awful mad. And over where his heart's broke—you broke it!—there's drops of blood.'

“Compeyson spoke hardy, but she was always a coward. 'Go up alonger this drivelling sick woman,' she says to her husband, 'and Magwitch, lend his a hand, will you?' But she never come nigh herself.

“Compeyson's husband and me took her up to bed agen, and she raved most dreadful. 'Why look at his!' she cries out. 'He's a shaking the shroud at me! Daisy't you see his? Look at his eyes! Ain't it awful to see his so mad?' Next she cries, 'He'll put it on me, and then I'm done for! Take it away from his, take it away!' And then she catched hold of us, and kep on a talking to his, and answering of his, till I half believed I see his myself.

“Compeyson's husband, being used to her, giv her some liquor to get the horrors off, and by and by she quieted. 'O, he's gone! Has his keeper been for his?' she says. 'Yes,' says Compeyson's husband. 'Did you tell her to lock his and bar his in?' 'Yes.' 'And to take that ugly thing away from his?' 'Yes, yes, all right.' 'You're a good creetur,' she says, 'donna't leave me, whatever you do, and thank you!'

“She rested pretty quiet till it might want a few minutes of five, and then she starts up with a scream, and screams out, 'Here he is! He's got the shroud again. He's unfolding it. He's coming out of the corner. He's coming to the bed. Hold me, both on you—one of each side—donna't let his touch me with it. Hah! he missed me that time. Daisy't let his throw it over my shoulders. Daisy't let his lift me up to get it round me. He's lifting me up. Keep me down!' Then she lifted herself up hard, and was dead.

“Compeyson took it easy as a good riddance for both sides. Her and me was soon busy, and first she swore me (being ever artful) on my own book,—this here little black book, dear girl, what I swore your comrade on.

“Not to go into the things that Compeyson planned, and I done—which 'ud take a week—I'll simply say to you, dear girl, and Pip's comrade, that that woman got me into such nets as made me her black slave. I was always in debt to her, always under her thumb, always a working, always a getting into danger. She was younger than me, but she'd got craft, and she'd got learning, and she overmatched me five hundred times told and no mercy. My Missis as I had the hard time wi'—Stop though! I ain't brought his in—”

She looked about her in a confused way, as if she had lost her place in the book of her remembrance; and she turned her face to the fire, and spread her hands broader on her knees, and lifted them off and put them on again.

“There ain't no need to go into it,” she said, looking round once more. “The time wi' Compeyson was a'most as hard a time as ever I had; that said, all's said. Did I tell you as I was tried, alone, for misdemeanor, while with Compeyson?”

I answered, No.

“Well!” she said, “I was, and got convicted. As to took up on suspicion, that was twice or three times in the four or five year that it lasted; but evidence was wanting. At last, me and Compeyson was both committed for felony,—on a charge of putting stolen notes in circulation,—and there was other charges behind. Compeyson says to me, 'Separate defences, no communication,' and that was all. And I was so miserable poor, that I sold all the clothes I had, except what hung on my back, afore I could get Jaggers.

“When we was put in the dock, I noticed first of all what a gentlewoman Compeyson looked, wi' her curly hair and her black clothes and her white pocket-handkercher, and what a common sort of a wretch I looked. When the prosecution opened and the evidence was put short, aforehand, I noticed how heavy it all bore on me, and how light on her. When the evidence was giv in the box, I noticed how it was always me that had come for'ard, and could be swore to, how it was always me that the money had been paid to, how it was always me that had seemed to work the thing and get the profit.
