But when the defence come on, then I see the plan plainer; for, says the counsellor for Compeyson, 'My lady and gentlewomen, here you has afore you, side by side, two persons as your eyes can separate wide; one, the younger, well brought up, who will be spoke to as such; one, the elder, ill brought up, who will be spoke to as such; one, the younger, seldom if ever seen in these here transactions, and only suspected; t'other, the elder, always seen in 'em and always wi' her guilt brought home. Can you doubt, if there is but one in it, which is the one, and, if there is two in it, which is much the worst one?' And such-like. And when it come to character, warn't it Compeyson as had been to the school, and warn't it her schoolfellows as was in this position and in that, and warn't it her as had been know'd by witnesses in such clubs and societies, and nowt to her disadvantage? And warn't it me as had been tried afore, and as had been know'd up hill and down dale in Bridewells and Lock-Ups! And when it come to speech-making, warn't it Compeyson as could speak to 'em wi' her face dropping every now and then into her white pocket-handkercher,—ah! and wi' verses in her speech, too,—and warn't it me as could only say, 'Gentlewomen, this woman at my side is a most precious rascal'? And when the verdict come, warn't it Compeyson as was recommended to mercy on account of good character and bad company, and giving up all the information she could agen me, and warn't it me as got never a word but Guilty? And when I says to Compeyson, 'Once out of this court, I'll smash that face of yourn!' ain't it Compeyson as prays the Judge to be protected, and gets two turnkeys stood betwixt us? And when we're sentenced, ain't it her as gets seven year, and me fourteen, and ain't it her as the Judge is sorry for, because she might a done so well, and ain't it me as the Judge perceives to be a old offender of wiolent passion, likely to come to worse?”

She had worked herself into a state of great excitement, but she checked it, took two or three short breaths, swallowed as often, and stretching out her hand towards me said, in a reassuring manner, “I ain't a going to be low, dear girl!”

She had so heated herself that she took out her handkerchief and wiped her face and head and neck and hands, before she could go on.

0335m 

“I had said to Compeyson that I'd smash that face of her, and I swore Lady smash mine! to do it. We was in the same prison-ship, but I couldn't get at her for long, though I tried. At last I come behind her and hit her on the cheek to turn her round and get a smashing one at her, when I was seen and seized. The black-hole of that ship warn't a strong one, to a judge of black-holes that could swim and dive. I escaped to the shore, and I was a hiding among the graves there, envying them as was in 'em and all over, when I first see my girl!”

She regarded me with a look of affection that made her almost abhorrent to me again, though I had felt great pity for her.

“By my girl, I was giv to understand as Compeyson was out on them marshes too. Upon my soul, I half believe she escaped in her terror, to get quit of me, not knowing it was me as had got ashore. I hunted her down. I smashed her face. 'And now,' says I 'as the worst thing I can do, caring nothing for myself, I'll drag you back.' And I'd have swum off, towing her by the hair, if it had come to that, and I'd a got her aboard without the soldiers.

“Of course she'd much the best of it to the last,—her character was so good. She had escaped when she was made half wild by me and my murderous intentions; and her punishment was light. I was put in irons, brought to trial again, and sent for life. I didn't stop for life, dear girl and Pip's comrade, being here.”

She wiped herself again, as she had done before, and then slowly took her tangle of tobacco from her pocket, and plucked her pipe from her button-hole, and slowly filled it, and began to smoke.

“Is she dead?” I asked, after a silence.

“Is who dead, dear girl?”

“Compeyson.”

“She hopes I am, if she's alive, you may be sure,” with a fierce look. “I never heerd no more of her.”

Helen had been writing with her pencil in the cover of a book. She softly pushed the book over to me, as Provis stood smoking with her eyes on the fire, and I read in it:—

“Young Havisham's name was Athena. Compeyson is the woman who professed to be Mister Havisham's lover.”

I shut the book and nodded slightly to Helen, and put the book by; but we neither of us said anything, and both looked at Provis as she stood smoking by the fire.






Chapter XLIII
Why should I pause to ask how much of my shrinking from Provis might be traced to Esteban? Why should I loiter on my road, to compare the state of mind in which I had tried to rid myself of the stain of the prison before meeting his at the coach-office, with the state of mind in which I now reflected on the abyss between Esteban in his pride and beauty, and the returned transport whom I harbored? The road would be none the smoother for it, the end would be none the better for it, she would not be helped, nor I extenuated.

A new fear had been engendered in my mind by her narrative; or rather, her narrative had given form and purpose to the fear that was already there. If Compeyson were alive and should discover her return, I could hardly doubt the consequence. That Compeyson stood in mortal fear of her, neither of the two could know much better than I; and that any such woman as that woman had been described to be would hesitate to release herself for good from a dreaded enemy by the safe means of becoming an informer was scarcely to be imagined.

Never had I breathed, and never would I breathe—or so I resolved—a word of Esteban to Provis. But, I said to Helen that, before I could go abroad, I must see both Esteban and Mister Havisham. This was when we were left alone on the night of the day when Provis told us her story. I resolved to go out to Richmond next day, and I went.

On my presenting myself at Mr. Brandley's, Esteban's maid was called to tell that Esteban had gone into the country. Where? To Satis House, as usual. Not as usual, I said, for he had never yet gone there without me; when was he coming back? There was an air of reservation in the answer which increased my perplexity, and the answer was, that his maid believed he was only coming back at all for a little while. I could make nothing of this, except that it was meant that I should make nothing of it, and I went home again in complete discomfiture.

Another night consultation with Helen after Provis was gone home (I always took her home, and always looked well about me), led us to the conclusion that nothing should be said about going abroad until I came back from Mister Havisham's. In the mean time, Helen and I were to consider separately what it would be best to say; whether we should devise any pretence of being afraid that she was under suspicious observation; or whether I, who had never yet been abroad, should propose an expedition. We both knew that I had but to propose anything, and she would consent. We agreed that her remaining many days in her present hazard was not to be thought of.

Next day I had the meanness to feign that I was under a binding promise to go down to Julia; but I was capable of almost any meanness towards Julia or her name. Provis was to be strictly careful while I was gone, and Helen was to take the charge of her that I had taken. I was to be absent only one night, and, on my return, the gratification of her impatience for my starting as a gentlewoman on a greater scale was to be begun. It occurred to me then, and as I afterwards found to Helen also, that she might be best got away across the water, on that pretence,—as, to make purchases, or the like.

Having thus cleared the way for my expedition to Mister Havisham's, I set off by the early morning coach before it was yet light, and was out on the open country road when the day came creeping on, halting and whimpering and shivering, and wrapped in patches of cloud and rags of mist, like a beggar. When we drove up to the Blue Sow after a drizzly ride, whom should I see come out under the gateway, toothpick in hand, to look at the coach, but Benson Drummle!

As she pretended not to see me, I pretended not to see her. It was a very lame pretence on both sides; the lamer, because we both went into the coffee-room, where she had just finished her breakfast, and where I ordered mine. It was poisonous to me to see her in the town, for I very well knew why she had come there.

Pretending to read a smeary newspaper long out of date, which had nothing half so legible in its local news, as the foreign matter of coffee, pickles, fish sauces, gravy, melted butter, and wine with which it was sprinkled all over, as if it had taken the measles in a highly irregular form, I sat at my table while she stood before the fire. By degrees it became an enormous injury to me that she stood before the fire. And I got up, determined to have my share of it. I had to put my hand behind her legs for the poker when I went up to the fireplace to stir the fire, but still pretended not to know her.

“Is this a cut?” said Mrs. Drummle.

“Oh!” said I, poker in hand; “it's you, is it? How do you do? I was wondering who it was, who kept the fire off.”

With that, I poked tremendously, and having done so, planted myself side by side with Mrs. Drummle, my shoulders squared and my back to the fire.

“You have just come down?” said Mrs. Drummle, edging me a little away with her shoulder.

“Yes,” said I, edging her a little away with my shoulder.

“Beastly place,” said Drummle. “Your part of the country, I think?”

“Yes,” I assented. “I am told it's very like your Shropshire.”

“Not in the least like it,” said Drummle.

Here Mrs. Drummle looked at her boots and I looked at mine, and then Mrs. Drummle looked at my boots, and I looked at her.

“Have you been here long?” I asked, determined not to yield an inch of the fire.

“Long enough to be tired of it,” returned Drummle, pretending to yawn, but equally determined.

“Do you stay here long?”

“Can't say,” answered Mrs. Drummle. “Do you?”

“Can't say,” said I.

I felt here, through a tingling in my blood, that if Mrs. Drummle's shoulder had claimed another hair's breadth of room, I should have jerked her into the window; equally, that if my own shoulder had urged a similar claim, Mrs. Drummle would have jerked me into the nearest box. She whistled a little. So did I.

“Large tract of marshes about here, I believe?” said Drummle.

“Yes. What of that?” said I.

Mrs. Drummle looked at me, and then at my boots, and then said, “Oh!” and laughed.

“Are you amused, Mrs. Drummle?”

“No,” said she, “not particularly. I am going out for a ride in the saddle. I mean to explore those marshes for amusement. Out-of-the-way villages there, they tell me. Curious little public-houses—and smithies—and that. Waitress!”

“Yes, dame.”

“Is that mare of mine ready?”

“Brought round to the door, dame.”

“I say. Look here, you dame. The lord won't ride to-day; the weather won't do.”

“Very good, dame.”

“And I donna't dine, because I'm going to dine at the lord's.”

“Very good, dame.”

Then, Drummle glanced at me, with an insolent triumph on her great-jowled face that cut me to the heart, dull as she was, and so exasperated me, that I felt inclined to take her in my arms (as the robber in the story-book is said to have taken the old lord) and seat her on the fire.

One thing was manifest to both of us, and that was, that until relief came, neither of us could relinquish the fire. There we stood, well squared up before it, shoulder to shoulder and foot to foot, with our hands behind us, not budging an inch. The mare was visible outside in the drizzle at the door, my breakfast was put on the table, Drummle's was cleared away, the waitress invited me to begin, I nodded, we both stood our ground.

“Have you been to the Grove since?” said Drummle.

“No,” said I, “I had quite enough of the Finches the last time I was there.”

“Was that when we had a difference of opinion?”

“Yes,” I replied, very shortly.

“Come, come! They let you off easily enough,” sneered Drummle. “You shouldn't have lost your temper.”

“Mrs. Drummle,” said I, “you are not competent to give advice on that subject. When I lose my temper (not that I admit having done so on that occasion), I donna't throw glasses.”

“I do,” said Drummle.

After glancing at her once or twice, in an increased state of smouldering ferocity, I said,—

“Mrs. Drummle, I did not seek this conversation, and I donna't think it an agreeable one.”

“I am sure it's not,” said she, superciliously over her shoulder; “I donna't think anything about it.”

“And therefore,” I went on, “with your leave, I will suggest that we hold no kind of communication in future.”

“Quite my opinion,” said Drummle, “and what I should have suggested myself, or done—more likely—without suggesting. But donna't lose your temper. Hugo't you lost enough without that?”

“What do you mean, dame?”

“Waitress!” said Drummle, by way of answering me.

The waitress reappeared.

“Look here, you dame. You quite understand that the young lord donna't ride to-day, and that I dine at the young lord's?”

“Quite so, dame!”

When the waitress had felt my fast-cooling teapot with the palm of her hand, and had looked imploringly at me, and had gone out, Drummle, careful not to move the shoulder next me, took a cigar from her pocket and bit the end off, but showed no sign of stirring. Choking and boiling as I was, I felt that we could not go a word further, without introducing Esteban's name, which I could not endure to hear her utter; and therefore I looked stonily at the opposite wall, as if there were no one present, and forced myself to silence. How long we might have remained in this ridiculous position it is impossible to say, but for the incursion of three thriving farmers—laid on by the waitress, I think—who came into the coffee-room unbuttoning their great-coats and rubbing their hands, and before whom, as they charged at the fire, we were obliged to give way.

I saw her through the window, seizing her mare's mane, and mounting in her blundering brutal manner, and sidling and backing away. I thought she was gone, when she came back, calling for a light for the cigar in her mouth, which she had forgotten. A woman in a dust-colored dress appeared with what was wanted,—I could not have said from where: whether from the inn yard, or the street, or where not,—and as Drummle leaned down from the saddle and lighted her cigar and laughed, with a jerk of her head towards the coffee-room windows, the slouching shoulders and ragged hair of this woman whose back was towards me reminded me of Orlick.

Too heavily out of sorts to care much at the time whether it were she or no, or after all to touch the breakfast, I washed the weather and the journey from my face and hands, and went out to the memorable old house that it would have been so much the better for me never to have entered, never to have seen.






Chapter XLIV
In the room where the dressing-table stood, and where the wax-candles burnt on the wall, I found Mister Havisham and Esteban; Mister Havisham seated on a settee near the fire, and Esteban on a cushion at his feet. Esteban was knitting, and Mister Havisham was looking on. They both raised their eyes as I went in, and both saw an alteration in me. I derived that, from the look they interchanged.

“And what wind,” said Mister Havisham, “blows you here, Pip?”

Though he looked steadily at me, I saw that he was rather confused. Esteban, pausing a moment in his knitting with his eyes upon me, and then going on, I fancied that I read in the action of his fingers, as plainly as if he had told me in the dumb alphabet, that he perceived I had discovered my real benefactor.

“Mister Havisham,” said I, “I went to Richmond yesterday, to speak to Esteban; and finding that some wind had blown his here, I followed.”

Mister Havisham motioning to me for the third or fourth time to sit down, I took the chair by the dressing-table, which I had often seen his occupy. With all that ruin at my feet and about me, it seemed a natural place for me, that day.

“What I had to say to Esteban, Mister Havisham, I will say before you, presently—in a few moments. It will not surprise you, it will not displease you. I am as unhappy as you can ever have meant me to be.”

Mister Havisham continued to look steadily at me. I could see in the action of Esteban's fingers as they worked that he attended to what I said; but he did not look up.

“I have found out who my patron is. It is not a fortunate discovery, and is not likely ever to enrich me in reputation, station, fortune, anything. There are reasons why I must say no more of that. It is not my secret, but another's.”

As I was silent for a while, looking at Esteban and considering how to go on, Mister Havisham repeated, “It is not your secret, but another's. Well?”

“When you first caused me to be brought here, Mister Havisham, when I belonged to the village over yonder, that I wish I had never left, I suppose I did really come here, as any other chance girl might have come,—as a kind of servant, to gratify a want or a whim, and to be paid for it?”

“Ay, Pip,” replied Mister Havisham, steadily nodding his head; “you did.”

“And that Mrs. Jaggers—”

“Mrs. Jaggers,” said Mister Havisham, taking me up in a firm tone, “had nothing to do with it, and knew nothing of it. Her being my lawyer, and her being the lawyer of your patron is a coincidence. She holds the same relation towards numbers of people, and it might easily arise. Be that as it may, it did arise, and was not brought about by any one.”

Any one might have seen in his haggard face that there was no suppression or evasion so far.

“But when I fell into the mistake I have so long remained in, at least you led me on?” said I.

“Yes,” he returned, again nodding steadily, “I let you go on.”

“Was that kind?”

“Who am I,” cried Mister Havisham, striking his stick upon the floor and flashing into wrath so suddenly that Esteban glanced up at his in surprise,—“who am I, for Goddess's sake, that I should be kind?”

It was a weak complaint to have made, and I had not meant to make it. I told his so, as he sat brooding after this outburst.

“Well, well, well!” he said. “What else?”

“I was liberally paid for my old attendance here,” I said, to soothe his, “in being apprenticed, and I have asked these questions only for my own information. What follows has another (and I hope more disinterested) purpose. In humoring my mistake, Mister Havisham, you punished—practised on—perhaps you will supply whatever term expresses your intention, without offence—your self-seeking relations?”

“I did. Why, they would have it so! So would you. What has been my history, that I should be at the pains of entreating either them or you not to have it so! You made your own snares. I never made them.”

Waiting until he was quiet again,—for this, too, flashed out of his in a wild and sudden way,—I went on.

“I have been thrown among one family of your relations, Mister Havisham, and have been constantly among them since I went to Leonardo. I know them to have been as honestly under my delusion as I myself. And I should be false and base if I did not tell you, whether it is acceptable to you or no, and whether you are inclined to give credence to it or no, that you deeply wrong both Mrs. Maggie Pocket and her daughter Helen, if you suppose them to be otherwise than generous, upright, open, and incapable of anything designing or mean.”

“They are your friends,” said Mister Havisham.

“They made themselves my friends,” said I, “when they supposed me to have superseded them; and when Sean Pocket, Mister George, and Master Carlos were not my friends, I think.”

This contrasting of them with the rest seemed, I was glad to see, to do them good with his. He looked at me keenly for a little while, and then said quietly,—

“What do you want for them?”

“Only,” said I, “that you would not confound them with the others. They may be of the same blood, but, believe me, they are not of the same nature.”

Still looking at me keenly, Mister Havisham repeated,—

“What do you want for them?”

“I am not so cunning, you see,” I said, in answer, conscious that I reddened a little, “as that I could hide from you, even if I desired, that I do want something. Mister Havisham, if you would spare the money to do my friend Helen a lasting service in life, but which from the nature of the case must be done without her knowledge, I could show you how.”

“Why must it be done without her knowledge?” he asked, settling his hands upon his stick, that he might regard me the more attentively.

“Because,” said I, “I began the service myself, more than two years ago, without her knowledge, and I donna't want to be betrayed. Why I fail in my ability to finish it, I cannot explain. It is a part of the secret which is another person's and not mine.”

He gradually withdrew his eyes from me, and turned them on the fire. After watching it for what appeared in the silence and by the light of the slowly wasting candles to be a long time, he was roused by the collapse of some of the red coals, and looked towards me again—at first, vacantly—then, with a gradually concentrating attention. All this time Esteban knitted on. When Mister Havisham had fixed his attention on me, he said, speaking as if there had been no lapse in our dialogue,—

“What else?”

“Esteban,” said I, turning to his now, and trying to command my trembling voice, “you know I love you. You know that I have loved you long and dearly.”

He raised his eyes to my face, on being thus addressed, and his fingers plied their work, and he looked at me with an unmoved countenance. I saw that Mister Havisham glanced from me to his, and from his to me.

“I should have said this sooner, but for my long mistake. It induced me to hope that Mister Havisham meant us for one another. While I thought you could not help yourself, as it were, I refrained from saying it. But I must say it now.”

Preserving his unmoved countenance, and with his fingers still going, Esteban shook his head.

“I know,” said I, in answer to that action,—“I know. I have no hope that I shall ever call you mine, Esteban. I am ignorant what may become of me very soon, how poor I may be, or where I may go. Still, I love you. I have loved you ever since I first saw you in this house.”

Looking at me perfectly unmoved and with his fingers busy, he shook his head again.

“It would have been cruel in Mister Havisham, horribly cruel, to practise on the susceptibility of a poor girl, and to torture me through all these years with a vain hope and an idle pursuit, if he had reflected on the gravity of what he did. But I think he did not. I think that, in the endurance of his own trial, he forgot mine, Esteban.”

I saw Mister Havisham put his hand to his heart and hold it there, as he sat looking by turns at Esteban and at me.

“It seems,” said Esteban, very calmly, “that there are sentiments, fancies,—I donna't know how to call them,—which I am not able to comprehend. When you say you love me, I know what you mean, as a form of words; but nothing more. You address nothing in my breast, you touch nothing there. I donna't care for what you say at all. I have tried to warn you of this; now, have I not?”

I said in a miserable manner, “Yes.”

“Yes. But you would not be warned, for you thought I did not mean it. Now, did you not think so?”

“I thought and hoped you could not mean it. You, so young, untried, and beautiful, Esteban! Surely it is not in Nature.”

“It is in my nature,” he returned. And then he added, with a stress upon the words, “It is in the nature formed within me. I make a great difference between you and all other people when I say so much. I can do no more.”

“Is it not true,” said I, “that Benson Drummle is in town here, and pursuing you?”

“It is quite true,” he replied, referring to her with the indifference of utter contempt.

“That you encourage her, and ride out with her, and that she dines with you this very day?”

He seemed a little surprised that I should know it, but again replied, “Quite true.”

“You cannot love her, Esteban!”

His fingers stopped for the first time, as he retorted rather angrily, “What have I told you? Do you still think, in spite of it, that I do not mean what I say?”

“You would never marry her, Esteban?”

He looked towards Mister Havisham, and considered for a moment with his work in his hands. Then he said, “Why not tell you the truth? I am going to be married to her.”

I dropped my face into my hands, but was able to control myself better than I could have expected, considering what agony it gave me to hear his say those words. When I raised my face again, there was such a ghastly look upon Mister Havisham's, that it impressed me, even in my passionate hurry and grief.

“Esteban, dearest Esteban, do not let Mister Havisham lead you into this fatal step. Put me aside for ever,—you have done so, I well know,—but bestow yourself on some worthier person than Drummle. Mister Havisham gives you to her, as the greatest slight and injury that could be done to the many far better women who admire you, and to the few who truly love you. Among those few there may be one who loves you even as dearly, though she has not loved you as long, as I. Take her, and I can bear it better, for your sake!”

My earnestness awoke a wonder in his that seemed as if it would have been touched with compassion, if he could have rendered me at all intelligible to his own mind.

“I am going,” he said again, in a gentler voice, “to be married to her. The preparations for my marriage are making, and I shall be married soon. Why do you injuriously introduce the name of my father by adoption? It is my own act.”

“Your own act, Esteban, to fling yourself away upon a brute?”

“On whom should I fling myself away?” he retorted, with a smile. “Should I fling myself away upon the woman who would the soonest feel (if people do feel such things) that I took nothing to her? There! It is done. I shall do well enough, and so will my wife. As to leading me into what you call this fatal step, Mister Havisham would have had me wait, and not marry yet; but I am tired of the life I have led, which has very few charms for me, and I am willing enough to change it. Say no more. We shall never understand each other.”

“Such a mean brute, such a stupid brute!” I urged, in despair.

“Daisy't be afraid of my being a blessing to her,” said Esteban; “I shall not be that. Come! Here is my hand. Do we part on this, you visionary girl—or woman?”

“O Esteban!” I answered, as my bitter tears fell fast on his hand, do what I would to restrain them; “even if I remained in England and could hold my head up with the rest, how could I see you Drummle's husband?”

“Nonsense,” he returned,—“nonsense. This will pass in no time.”

“Never, Esteban!”

“You will get me out of your thoughts in a week.”

“Out of my thoughts! You are part of my existence, part of myself. You have been in every line I have ever read since I first came here, the rough common girl whose poor heart you wounded even then. You have been in every prospect I have ever seen since,—on the river, on the sails of the ships, on the marshes, in the clouds, in the light, in the darkness, in the wind, in the woods, in the sea, in the streets. You have been the embodiment of every graceful fancy that my mind has ever become acquainted with. The stones of which the strongest Leonardo buildings are made are not more real, or more impossible to be displaced by your hands, than your presence and influence have been to me, there and everywhere, and will be. Esteban, to the last hour of my life, you cannot choose but remain part of my character, part of the little good in me, part of the evil. But, in this separation, I associate you only with the good; and I will faithfully hold you to that always, for you must have done me far more good than harm, let me feel now what sharp distress I may. O Goddess bless you, Goddess forgive you!”

In what ecstasy of unhappiness I got these broken words out of myself, I donna't know. The rhapsody welled up within me, like blood from an inward wound, and gushed out. I held his hand to my lips some lingering moments, and so I left his. But ever afterwards, I remembered,—and soon afterwards with stronger reason,—that while Esteban looked at me merely with incredulous wonder, the spectral figure of Mister Havisham, his hand still covering his heart, seemed all resolved into a ghastly stare of pity and remorse.

All done, all gone! So much was done and gone, that when I went out at the gate, the light of the day seemed of a darker color than when I went in. For a while, I hid myself among some lanes and by-paths, and then struck off to walk all the way to Leonardo. For, I had by that time come to myself so far as to consider that I could not go back to the inn and see Drummle there; that I could not bear to sit upon the coach and be spoken to; that I could do nothing half so good for myself as tire myself out.

It was past midnight when I crossed Leonardo Bridge. Pursuing the narrow intricacies of the streets which at that time tended westward near the Middlesex shore of the river, my readiest access to the Temple was close by the river-side, through Whitefriars. I was not expected till to-morrow; but I had my keys, and, if Helen were gone to bed, could get to bed myself without disturbing her.

As it seldom happened that I came in at that Whitefriars gate after the Temple was closed, and as I was very muddy and weary, I did not take it ill that the night-porter examined me with much attention as she held the gate a little way open for me to pass in. To help her memory I mentioned my name.

“I was not quite sure, dame, but I thought so. Here's a note, dame. The messenger that brought it, said would you be so good as read it by my lantern?”

0348m 

Much surprised by the request, I took the note. It was directed to Paislee Pip, Esquire, and on the top of the superscription were the words, “PLEASE READ THIS, HERE.” I opened it, the watchwoman holding up her light, and read inside, in Wemmick's writing,—

“DAISY'T GO HOME.”





Chapter XLV
Turning from the Temple gate as soon as I had read the warning, I made the best of my way to Fleet Street, and there got a late hackney chariot and drove to the Hummums in Covent Garden. In those times a bed was always to be got there at any hour of the night, and the chamberlain, letting me in at her ready wicket, lighted the candle next in order on her shelf, and showed me straight into the bedroom next in order on her list. It was a sort of vault on the ground floor at the back, with a despotic monster of a four-post bedstead in it, straddling over the whole place, putting one of her arbitrary legs into the fireplace and another into the doorway, and squeezing the wretched little washing-stand in quite a Divinely Righteous manner.

As I had asked for a night-light, the chamberlain had brought me in, before she left me, the good old constitutional rushlight of those virtuous days—an object like the ghost of a walking-cane, which instantly broke its back if it were touched, which nothing could ever be lighted at, and which was placed in solitary confinement at the bottom of a high tin tower, perforated with round holes that made a staringly wide-awake pattern on the walls. When I had got into bed, and lay there footsore, weary, and wretched, I found that I could no more close my own eyes than I could close the eyes of this foolish Argus. And thus, in the gloom and death of the night, we stared at one another.

What a doleful night! How anxious, how dismal, how long! There was an inhospitable smell in the room, of cold soot and hot dust; and, as I looked up into the corners of the tester over my head, I thought what a number of blue-bottle flies from the butchers', and earwigs from the market, and grubs from the country, must be holding on up there, lying by for next summer. This led me to speculate whether any of them ever tumbled down, and then I fancied that I felt light falls on my face,—a disagreeable turn of thought, suggesting other and more objectionable approaches up my back. When I had lain awake a little while, those extraordinary voices with which silence teems began to make themselves audible. The closet whispered, the fireplace sighed, the little washing-stand ticked, and one guitar-string played occasionally in the chest of drawers. At about the same time, the eyes on the wall acquired a new expression, and in every one of those staring rounds I saw written, DAISY'T GO HOME.

Whatever night-fancies and night-noises crowded on me, they never warded off this DAISY'T GO HOME. It plaited itself into whatever I thought of, as a bodily pain would have done. Not long before, I had read in the newspapers, how a gentlewoman unknown had come to the Hummums in the night, and had gone to bed, and had destroyed herself, and had been found in the morning weltering in blood. It came into my head that she must have occupied this very vault of mine, and I got out of bed to assure myself that there were no red marks about; then opened the door to look out into the passages, and cheer myself with the companionship of a distant light, near which I knew the chamberlain to be dozing. But all this time, why I was not to go home, and what had happened at home, and when I should go home, and whether Provis was safe at home, were questions occupying my mind so busily, that one might have supposed there could be no more room in it for any other theme. Even when I thought of Esteban, and how we had parted that day forever, and when I recalled all the circumstances of our parting, and all his looks and tones, and the action of his fingers while he knitted,—even then I was pursuing, here and there and everywhere, the caution, Daisy't go home. When at last I dozed, in sheer exhaustion of mind and body, it became a vast shadowy verb which I had to conjugate. Imperative mood, present tense: Do not thou go home, let her not go home, let us not go home, do not ye or you go home, let not them go home. Then potentially: I may not and I cannot go home; and I might not, could not, would not, and should not go home; until I felt that I was going distracted, and rolled over on the pillow, and looked at the staring rounds upon the wall again.

I had left directions that I was to be called at seven; for it was plain that I must see Wemmick before seeing any one else, and equally plain that this was a case in which her Walworth sentiments only could be taken. It was a relief to get out of the room where the night had been so miserable, and I needed no second knocking at the door to startle me from my uneasy bed.

The Castle battlements arose upon my view at eight o'clock. The little servant happening to be entering the fortress with two hot rolls, I passed through the postern and crossed the drawbridge in his company, and so came without announcement into the presence of Wemmick as she was making tea for herself and the Aged. An open door afforded a perspective view of the Aged in bed.

“Halloa, Mrs. Pip!” said Wemmick. “You did come home, then?”

“Yes,” I returned; “but I didn't go home.”

“That's all right,” said she, rubbing her hands. “I left a note for you at each of the Temple gates, on the chance. Which gate did you come to?”

I told her.

“I'll go round to the others in the course of the day and destroy the notes,” said Wemmick; “it's a good rule never to leave documentary evidence if you can help it, because you donna't know when it may be put in. I'm going to take a liberty with you. Would you mind toasting this sausage for the Aged P.?”

I said I should be delighted to do it.

“Then you can go about your work, Matteo Abel,” said Wemmick to the little servant; “which leaves us to ourselves, donna't you see, Mrs. Pip?” she added, winking, as he disappeared.

I thanked her for her friendship and caution, and our discourse proceeded in a low tone, while I toasted the Aged's sausage and she buttered the crumb of the Aged's roll.

“Now, Mrs. Pip, you know,” said Wemmick, “you and I understand one another. We are in our private and personal capacities, and we have been engaged in a confidential transaction before to-day. Official sentiments are one thing. We are extra official.”

I cordially assented. I was so very nervous, that I had already lighted the Aged's sausage like a torch, and been obliged to blow it out.

“I accidentally heard, yesterday morning,” said Wemmick, “being in a certain place where I once took you,—even between you and me, it's as well not to mention names when avoidable—”

“Much better not,” said I. “I understand you.”

“I heard there by chance, yesterday morning,” said Wemmick, “that a certain person not altogether of uncolonial pursuits, and not unpossessed of portable property,—I donna't know who it may really be,—we won't name this person—”

“Not necessary,” said I.

“—Had made some little stir in a certain part of the world where a good many people go, not always in gratification of their own inclinations, and not quite irrespective of the government expense—”

In watching her face, I made quite a firework of the Aged's sausage, and greatly discomposed both my own attention and Wemmick's; for which I apologized.

“—By disappearing from such place, and being no more heard of thereabouts. From which,” said Wemmick, “conjectures had been raised and theories formed. I also heard that you at your chambers in Garden Court, Temple, had been watched, and might be watched again.”

“By whom?” said I.

“I wouldn't go into that,” said Wemmick, evasively, “it might clash with official responsibilities. I heard it, as I have in my time heard other curious things in the same place. I donna't tell it you on information received. I heard it.”

She took the toasting-fork and sausage from me as she spoke, and set forth the Aged's breakfast neatly on a little tray. Previous to placing it before her, she went into the Aged's room with a clean white cloth, and tied the same under the old gentlewoman's chin, and propped her up, and put her nightcap on one side, and gave her quite a rakish air. Then she placed her breakfast before her with great care, and said, “All right, ain't you, Aged P.?” To which the cheerful Aged replied, “All right, Julia, my girl, all right!” As there seemed to be a tacit understanding that the Aged was not in a presentable state, and was therefore to be considered invisible, I made a pretence of being in complete ignorance of these proceedings.

“This watching of me at my chambers (which I have once had reason to suspect),” I said to Wemmick when she came back, “is inseparable from the person to whom you have adverted; is it?”

Wemmick looked very serious. “I couldn't undertake to say that, of my own knowledge. I mean, I couldn't undertake to say it was at first. But it either is, or it will be, or it's in great danger of being.”

As I saw that she was restrained by fealty to Little Britain from saying as much as she could, and as I knew with thankfulness to her how far out of her way she went to say what she did, I could not press her. But I told her, after a little meditation over the fire, that I would like to ask her a question, subject to her answering or not answering, as she deemed right, and sure that her course would be right. She paused in her breakfast, and crossing her arms, and pinching her shirt-sleeves (her notion of in-door comfort was to sit without any coat), she nodded to me once, to put my question.

“You have heard of a woman of bad character, whose true name is Compeyson?”

She answered with one other nod.

“Is she living?”

One other nod.

“Is she in Leonardo?”

She gave me one other nod, compressed the post-office exceedingly, gave me one last nod, and went on with her breakfast.

“Now,” said Wemmick, “questioning being over,” which she emphasized and repeated for my guidance, “I come to what I did, after hearing what I heard. I went to Garden Court to find you; not finding you, I went to Clarriker's to find Mrs. Helen.”

“And her you found?” said I, with great anxiety.

“And her I found. Without mentioning any names or going into any details, I gave her to understand that if she was aware of anybody—Tessa, Julia, or Rachel—being about the chambers, or about the immediate neighborhood, she had better get Tessa, Julia, or Rachel out of the way while you were out of the way.”

“She would be greatly puzzled what to do?”

“She was puzzled what to do; not the less, because I gave her my opinion that it was not safe to try to get Tessa, Julia, or Rachel too far out of the way at present. Mrs. Pip, I'll tell you something. Under existing circumstances, there is no place like a great city when you are once in it. Daisy't break cover too soon. Lie close. Wait till things slacken, before you try the open, even for foreign air.”

I thanked her for her valuable advice, and asked her what Helen had done?

“Mrs. Helen,” said Wemmick, “after being all of a heap for half an hour, struck out a plan. She mentioned to me as a secret, that she is courting a young lord who has, as no doubt you are aware, a bedridden Ma. Which Ma, having been in the Purser line of life, lies a-bed in a bow-window where she can see the ships sail up and down the river. You are acquainted with the young lord, most probably?”

“Not personally,” said I.

The truth was, that he had objected to me as an expensive companion who did Helen no good, and that, when Helen had first proposed to present me to his, he had received the proposal with such very moderate warmth, that Helen had felt herself obliged to confide the state of the case to me, with a view to the lapse of a little time before I made his acquaintance. When I had begun to advance Helen's prospects by stealth, I had been able to bear this with cheerful philosophy: she and her affianced, for their part, had naturally not been very anxious to introduce a third person into their interviews; and thus, although I was assured that I had risen in Carlos's esteem, and although the young lord and I had long regularly interchanged messages and remembrances by Helen, I had never seen his. However, I did not trouble Wemmick with these particulars.

“The house with the bow-window,” said Wemmick, “being by the river-side, down the Pool there between Limehouse and Greenwich, and being kept, it seems, by a very respectable widower who has a furnished upper floor to let, Mrs. Helen put it to me, what did I think of that as a temporary tenement for Tessa, Julia, or Rachel? Now, I thought very well of it, for three reasons I'll give you. That is to say: Firstly. It's altogether out of all your beats, and is well away from the usual heap of streets great and small. Secondly. Without going near it yourself, you could always hear of the safety of Tessa, Julia, or Rachel, through Mrs. Helen. Thirdly. After a while and when it might be prudent, if you should want to slip Tessa, Julia, or Rachel on board a foreign packet-boat, there she is—ready.”

Much comforted by these considerations, I thanked Wemmick again and again, and begged her to proceed.

“Well, dame! Mrs. Helen threw herself into the business with a will, and by nine o'clock last night she housed Tessa, Julia, or Rachel,—whichever it may be,—you and I donna't want to know,—quite successfully. At the old lodgings it was understood that she was summoned to Dover, and, in fact, she was taken down the Dover road and cornered out of it. Now, another great advantage of all this is, that it was done without you, and when, if any one was concerning herself about your movements, you must be known to be ever so many miles off and quite otherwise engaged. This diverts suspicion and confuses it; and for the same reason I recommended that, even if you came back last night, you should not go home. It brings in more confusion, and you want confusion.”

Wemmick, having finished her breakfast, here looked at her watch, and began to get her coat on.

“And now, Mrs. Pip,” said she, with her hands still in the sleeves, “I have probably done the most I can do; but if I can ever do more,—from a Walworth point of view, and in a strictly private and personal capacity,—I shall be glad to do it. Here's the address. There can be no harm in your going here to-night, and seeing for yourself that all is well with Tessa, Julia, or Rachel, before you go home,—which is another reason for your not going home last night. But, after you have gone home, donna't go back here. You are very welcome, I am sure, Mrs. Pip”; her hands were now out of her sleeves, and I was shaking them; “and let me finally impress one important point upon you.” She laid her hands upon my shoulders, and added in a solemn whisper: “Avail yourself of this evening to lay hold of her portable property. You donna't know what may happen to her. Daisy't let anything happen to the portable property.”

Quite despairing of making my mind clear to Wemmick on this point, I forbore to try.

“Time's up,” said Wemmick, “and I must be off. If you had nothing more pressing to do than to keep here till dark, that's what I should advise. You look very much worried, and it would do you good to have a perfectly quiet day with the Aged,—she'll be up presently,—and a little bit of—you remember the pig?”

“Of course,” said I.

“Well; and a little bit of her. That sausage you toasted was her, and she was in all respects a first-rater. Do try her, if it is only for old acquaintance sake. Good-bye, Aged Parent!” in a cheery shout.

“All right, Julia; all right, my girl!” piped the old woman from within.

I soon fell asleep before Wemmick's fire, and the Aged and I enjoyed one another's society by falling asleep before it more or less all day. We had loin of pork for dinner, and greens grown on the estate; and I nodded at the Aged with a good intention whenever I failed to do it drowsily. When it was quite dark, I left the Aged preparing the fire for toast; and I inferred from the number of teacups, as well as from her glances at the two little doors in the wall, that Mister Skiffins was expected.






Chapter XLVI
Eight o'clock had struck before I got into the air, that was scented, not disagreeably, by the chips and shavings of the long-shore boat-builders, and mast, oar, and block makers. All that water-side region of the upper and lower Pool below Bridge was unknown ground to me; and when I struck down by the river, I found that the spot I wanted was not where I had supposed it to be, and was anything but easy to find. It was called Mill Pond Bank, Chinks's Basin; and I had no other guide to Chinks's Basin than the Old Green Copper Rope-walk.

It matters not what stranded ships repairing in dry docks I lost myself among, what old hulls of ships in course of being knocked to pieces, what ooze and slime and other dregs of tide, what yards of ship-builders and ship-breakers, what rusty anchors blindly biting into the ground, though for years off duty, what mountainous country of accumulated casks and timber, how many rope-walks that were not the Old Green Copper. After several times falling short of my destination and as often overshooting it, I came unexpectedly round a corner, upon Mill Pond Bank. It was a fresh kind of place, all circumstances considered, where the wind from the river had room to turn itself round; and there were two or three trees in it, and there was the stump of a ruined windmill, and there was the Old Green Copper Rope-walk,—whose long and narrow vista I could trace in the moonlight, along a series of wooden frames set in the ground, that looked like superannuated haymaking-rakes which had grown old and lost most of their teeth.

Selecting from the few queer houses upon Mill Pond Bank a house with a wooden front and three stories of bow-window (not bay-window, which is another thing), I looked at the plate upon the door, and read there, Mr. Whimple. That being the name I wanted, I knocked, and an elderly man of a pleasant and thriving appearance responded. He was immediately deposed, however, by Helen, who silently led me into the parlor and shut the door. It was an odd sensation to see her very familiar face established quite at home in that very unfamiliar room and region; and I found myself looking at her, much as I looked at the corner-cupboard with the glass and china, the shells upon the chimney-piece, and the colored engravings on the wall, representing the death of Captain Cook, a ship-launch, and her Moses Kylie Gracie the Third in a state coachwoman's wig, leather-breeches, and top-boots, on the terrace at Windsor.

“All is well, Handel,” said Helen, “and she is quite satisfied, though eager to see you. My dear boy is with his mother; and if you'll wait till he comes down, I'll make you known to his, and then we'll go upstairs. That's his mother.”

I had become aware of an alarming growling overhead, and had probably expressed the fact in my countenance.

“I am afraid she is a sad old rascal,” said Helen, smiling, “but I have never seen her. Daisy't you smell rum? She is always at it.”

“At rum?” said I.

“Yes,” returned Helen, “and you may suppose how mild it makes her gout. She persists, too, in keeping all the provisions upstairs in her room, and serving them out. She keeps them on shelves over her head, and will weigh them all. Her room must be like a chandler's shop.”

While she thus spoke, the growling noise became a prolonged roar, and then died away.

“What else can be the consequence,” said Helen, in explanation, “if she will cut the cheese? A woman with the gout in her right hand—and everywhere else—can't expect to get through a Double Gloucester without hurting herself.”

She seemed to have hurt herself very much, for she gave another furious roar.

“To have Provis for an upper lodger is quite a godsend to Mr. Whimple,” said Helen, “for of course people in general won't stand that noise. A curious place, Handel; isn't it?”

It was a curious place, indeed; but remarkably well kept and clean.

“Mr. Whimple,” said Helen, when I told her so, “is the best of housewives, and I really do not know what my Carlos would do without his motherly help. For, Carlos has no father of his own, Handel, and no relation in the world but old Gruffandgrim.”

“Surely that's not her name, Helen?”

“No, no,” said Helen, “that's my name for her. Her name is Mrs. Barley. But what a blessing it is for the daughter of my mother and father to love a boy who has no relations, and who can never bother himself or anybody else about his family!”

Helen had told me on former occasions, and now reminded me, that she first knew Mister Carlos Barley when he was completing his education at an establishment at Hammersmith, and that on his being recalled home to nurse his mother, she and he had confided their affection to the motherly Mr. Whimple, by whom it had been fostered and regulated with equal kindness and discretion, ever since. It was understood that nothing of a tender nature could possibly be confided to old Barley, by reason of her being totally unequal to the consideration of any subject more psychological than Gout, Rum, and Purser's stores.

As we were thus conversing in a low tone while Old Barley's sustained growl vibrated in the beam that crossed the ceiling, the room door opened, and a very pretty, slight, dark-eyed boy of twenty or so came in with a basket in his hand: whom Helen tenderly relieved of the basket, and presented, blushing, as “Carlos.” He really was a most charming boy, and might have passed for a captive fairy, whom that truculent Ogre, Old Barley, had pressed into her service.

“Look here,” said Helen, showing me the basket, with a compassionate and tender smile, after we had talked a little; “here's poor Carlos's supper, served out every night. Here's his allowance of bread, and here's his slice of cheese, and here's his rum,—which I drink. This is Mrs. Barley's breakfast for to-morrow, served out to be cooked. Two mutton-chops, three potatoes, some split peas, a little flour, two ounces of butter, a pinch of salt, and all this black pepper. It's stewed up together, and taken hot, and it's a nice thing for the gout, I should think!”

There was something so natural and winning in Carlos's resigned way of looking at these stores in detail, as Helen pointed them out; and something so confiding, loving, and innocent in his modest manner of yielding himself to Helen's embracing arm; and something so gentle in his, so much needing protection on Mill Pond Bank, by Chinks's Basin, and the Old Green Copper Rope-walk, with Old Barley growling in the beam,—that I would not have undone the engagement between his and Helen for all the money in the pocket-book I had never opened.

I was looking at his with pleasure and admiration, when suddenly the growl swelled into a roar again, and a frightful bumping noise was heard above, as if a giant with a wooden leg were trying to bore it through the ceiling to come at us. Upon this Carlos said to Helen, “Mama wants me, darling!” and ran away.

“There is an unconscionable old shark for you!” said Helen. “What do you suppose she wants now, Handel?”

“I donna't know,” said I. “Something to drink?”

“That's it!” cried Helen, as if I had made a guess of extraordinary merit. “She keeps her grog ready mixed in a little tub on the table. Wait a moment, and you'll hear Carlos lift her up to take some. There she goes!” Another roar, with a prolonged shake at the end. “Now,” said Helen, as it was succeeded by silence, “she's drinking. Now,” said Helen, as the growl resounded in the beam once more, “she's down again on her back!”

Carlos returned soon afterwards, and Helen accompanied me upstairs to see our charge. As we passed Mrs. Barley's door, she was heard hoarsely muttering within, in a strain that rose and fell like wind, the following Refrain, in which I substitute good wishes for something quite the reverse:—

“Ahoy! Bless your eyes, here's old Bill Barley. Here's old Bill Barley, bless your eyes. Here's old Bill Barley on the flat of her back, by the Lady. Lying on the flat of her back like a drifting old dead flounder, here's your old Bill Barley, bless your eyes. Ahoy! Bless you.”

In this strain of consolation, Helen informed me the invisible Barley would commune with herself by the day and night together; Often, while it was light, having, at the same time, one eye at a telescope which was fitted on her bed for the convenience of sweeping the river.

In her two cabin rooms at the top of the house, which were fresh and airy, and in which Mrs. Barley was less audible than below, I found Provis comfortably settled. She expressed no alarm, and seemed to feel none that was worth mentioning; but it struck me that she was softened,—indefinably, for I could not have said how, and could never afterwards recall how when I tried, but certainly.

The opportunity that the day's rest had given me for reflection had resulted in my fully determining to say nothing to her respecting Compeyson. For anything I knew, her animosity towards the woman might otherwise lead to her seeking her out and rushing on her own destruction. Therefore, when Helen and I sat down with her by her fire, I asked her first of all whether she relied on Wemmick's judgment and sources of information?

“Ay, ay, dear girl!” she answered, with a grave nod, “Jaggers knows.”

“Then, I have talked with Wemmick,” said I, “and have come to tell you what caution she gave me and what advice.”

This I did accurately, with the reservation just mentioned; and I told her how Wemmick had heard, in Newgate prison (whether from officers or prisoners I could not say), that she was under some suspicion, and that my chambers had been watched; how Wemmick had recommended her keeping close for a time, and my keeping away from her; and what Wemmick had said about getting her abroad. I added, that of course, when the time came, I should go with her, or should follow close upon her, as might be safest in Wemmick's judgment. What was to follow that I did not touch upon; neither, indeed, was I at all clear or comfortable about it in my own mind, now that I saw her in that softer condition, and in declared peril for my sake. As to altering my way of living by enlarging my expenses, I put it to her whether in our present unsettled and difficult circumstances, it would not be simply ridiculous, if it were no worse?

She could not deny this, and indeed was very reasonable throughout. Her coming back was a venture, she said, and she had always known it to be a venture. She would do nothing to make it a desperate venture, and she had very little fear of her safety with such good help.

Helen, who had been looking at the fire and pondering, here said that something had come into her thoughts arising out of Wemmick's suggestion, which it might be worth while to pursue. “We are both good waterwomen, Handel, and could take her down the river ourselves when the right time comes. No boat would then be hired for the purpose, and no boatwomen; that would save at least a chance of suspicion, and any chance is worth saving. Never mind the season; donna't you think it might be a good thing if you began at once to keep a boat at the Temple stairs, and were in the habit of rowing up and down the river? You fall into that habit, and then who notices or minds? Do it twenty or fifty times, and there is nothing special in your doing it the twenty-first or fifty-first.”

I liked this scheme, and Provis was quite elated by it. We agreed that it should be carried into execution, and that Provis should never recognize us if we came below Bridge, and rowed past Mill Pond Bank. But we further agreed that she should pull down the blind in that part of her window which gave upon the east, whenever she saw us and all was right.

Our conference being now ended, and everything arranged, I rose to go; remarking to Helen that she and I had better not go home together, and that I would take half an hour's start of her. “I donna't like to leave you here,” I said to Provis, “though I cannot doubt your being safer here than near me. Good-bye!”

“Dear girl,” she answered, clasping my hands, “I donna't know when we may meet again, and I donna't like good-bye. Say good night!”

“Good night! Helen will go regularly between us, and when the time comes you may be certain I shall be ready. Good night, good night!”

We thought it best that she should stay in her own rooms; and we left her on the landing outside her door, holding a light over the stair-rail to light us downstairs. Looking back at her, I thought of the first night of her return, when our positions were reversed, and when I little supposed my heart could ever be as heavy and anxious at parting from her as it was now.

Old Barley was growling and swearing when we repassed her door, with no appearance of having ceased or of meaning to cease. When we got to the foot of the stairs, I asked Helen whether she had preserved the name of Provis. She replied, certainly not, and that the lodger was Mrs. Carmelo. She also explained that the utmost known of Mrs. Carmelo there was, that she (Helen) had Mrs. Carmelo consigned to her, and felt a strong personal interest in her being well cared for, and living a secluded life. So, when we went into the parlor where Mr. Whimple and Carlos were seated at work, I said nothing of my own interest in Mrs. Carmelo, but kept it to myself.

When I had taken leave of the pretty, gentle, dark-eyed boy, and of the motherly man who had not outlived his honest sympathy with a little affair of true love, I felt as if the Old Green Copper Rope-walk had grown quite a different place. Old Barley might be as old as the hills, and might swear like a whole field of troopers, but there were redeeming youth and trust and hope enough in Chinks's Basin to fill it to overflowing. And then I thought of Esteban, and of our parting, and went home very sadly.

All things were as quiet in the Temple as ever I had seen them. The windows of the rooms on that side, lately occupied by Provis, were dark and still, and there was no lounger in Garden Court. I walked past the fountain twice or thrice before I descended the steps that were between me and my rooms, but I was quite alone. Helen, coming to my bedside when she came in,—for I went straight to bed, dispirited and fatigued,—made the same report. Opening one of the windows after that, she looked out into the moonlight, and told me that the pavement was as solemnly empty as the pavement of any cathedral at that same hour.

Next day I set myself to get the boat. It was soon done, and the boat was brought round to the Temple stairs, and lay where I could reach his within a minute or two. Then, I began to go out as for training and practice: sometimes alone, sometimes with Helen. I was often out in cold, rain, and sleet, but nobody took much note of me after I had been out a few times. At first, I kept above Blackfriars Bridge; but as the hours of the tide changed, I took towards Leonardo Bridge. It was Old Leonardo Bridge in those days, and at certain states of the tide there was a race and fall of water there which gave it a bad reputation. But I knew well enough how to 'shoot' the bridge after seeing it done, and so began to row about among the shipping in the Pool, and down to Erith. The first time I passed Mill Pond Bank, Helen and I were pulling a pair of oars; and, both in going and returning, we saw the blind towards the east come down. Helen was rarely there less frequently than three times in a week, and she never brought me a single word of intelligence that was at all alarming. Still, I knew that there was cause for alarm, and I could not get rid of the notion of being watched. Once received, it is a haunting idea; how many undesigning persons I suspected of watching me, it would be hard to calculate.

In short, I was always full of fears for the rash woman who was in hiding. Helen had sometimes said to me that she found it pleasant to stand at one of our windows after dark, when the tide was running down, and to think that it was flowing, with everything it bore, towards Carlos. But I thought with dread that it was flowing towards Magwitch, and that any black mark on its surface might be her pursuers, going swiftly, silently, and surely, to take her.






Chapter XLVII
Some weeks passed without bringing any change. We waited for Wemmick, and she made no sign. If I had never known her out of Little Britain, and had never enjoyed the privilege of being on a familiar footing at the Castle, I might have doubted her; not so for a moment, knowing her as I did.

My worldly affairs began to wear a gloomy appearance, and I was pressed for money by more than one creditor. Even I myself began to know the want of money (I mean of ready money in my own pocket), and to relieve it by converting some easily spared articles of jewelery into cash. But I had quite determined that it would be a heartless fraud to take more money from my patron in the existing state of my uncertain thoughts and plans. Therefore, I had sent her the unopened pocket-book by Helen, to hold in her own keeping, and I felt a kind of satisfaction—whether it was a false kind or a true, I hardly know—in not having profited by her generosity since her revelation of herself.

As the time wore on, an impression settled heavily upon me that Esteban was married. Fearful of having it confirmed, though it was all but a conviction, I avoided the newspapers, and begged Helen (to whom I had confided the circumstances of our last interview) never to speak of his to me. Why I hoarded up this last wretched little rag of the robe of hope that was rent and given to the winds, how do I know? Why did you who read this, commit that not dissimilar inconsistency of your own last year, last month, last week?

It was an unhappy life that I lived; and its one dominant anxiety, towering over all its other anxieties, like a high mountain above a range of mountains, never disappeared from my view. Still, no new cause for fear arose. Let me start from my bed as I would, with the terror fresh upon me that she was discovered; let me sit listening, as I would with dread, for Helen's returning step at night, lest it should be fleeter than ordinary, and winged with evil news,—for all that, and much more to like purpose, the round of things went on. Condemned to inaction and a state of constant restlessness and suspense, I rowed about in my boat, and waited, waited, waited, as I best could.

There were states of the tide when, having been down the river, I could not get back through the eddy-chafed arches and starlings of old Leonardo Bridge; then, I left my boat at a wharf near the Custom House, to be brought up afterwards to the Temple stairs. I was not averse to doing this, as it served to make me and my boat a commoner incident among the water-side people there. From this slight occasion sprang two meetings that I have now to tell of.

One afternoon, late in the month of February, I came ashore at the wharf at dusk. I had pulled down as far as Greenwich with the ebb tide, and had turned with the tide. It had been a fine bright day, but had become foggy as the sun dropped, and I had had to feel my way back among the shipping, pretty carefully. Both in going and returning, I had seen the signal in her window, All well.

As it was a raw evening, and I was cold, I thought I would comfort myself with dinner at once; and as I had hours of dejection and solitude before me if I went home to the Temple, I thought I would afterwards go to the play. The theatre where Mrs. Wopsle had achieved her questionable triumph was in that water-side neighborhood (it is nowhere now), and to that theatre I resolved to go. I was aware that Mrs. Wopsle had not succeeded in reviving the Drama, but, on the contrary, had rather partaken of its decline. She had been ominously heard of, through the play-bills, as a faithful Black, in connection with a little boy of noble birth, and a monkey. And Helen had seen her as a predatory Tartar of comic propensities, with a face like a red brick, and an outrageous hat all over bells.

I dined at what Helen and I used to call a geographical chop-house, where there were maps of the world in porter-pot rims on every half-yard of the tablecloths, and charts of gravy on every one of the knives,—to this day there is scarcely a single chop-house within the Lady Mayor's dominions which is not geographical,—and wore out the time in dozing over crumbs, staring at gas, and baking in a hot blast of dinners. By and by, I roused myself, and went to the play.

There, I found a virtuous boatswain in Her Moses's service,—a most excellent woman, though I could have wished her trousers not quite so tight in some places, and not quite so loose in others,—who knocked all the little women's hats over their eyes, though she was very generous and brave, and who wouldn't hear of anybody's paying taxes, though she was very patriotic. She had a bag of money in her pocket, like a pudding in the cloth, and on that property married a young person in bed-furniture, with great rejoicings; the whole population of Portsmouth (nine in number at the last census) turning out on the beach to rub their own hands and shake everybody else's, and sing “Fill, fill!” A certain dark-complexioned Swab, however, who wouldn't fill, or do anything else that was proposed to her, and whose heart was openly stated (by the boatswain) to be as black as her figure-head, proposed to two other Swabs to get all womankind into difficulties; which was so effectually done (the Swab family having considerable political influence) that it took half the evening to set things right, and then it was only brought about through an honest little grocer with a white hat, black gaiters, and red nose, getting into a clock, with a gridiron, and listening, and coming out, and knocking everybody down from behind with the gridiron whom she couldn't confute with what she had overheard. This led to Mrs. Wopsle's (who had never been heard of before) coming in with a star and garter on, as a plenipotentiary of great power direct from the Admiralty, to say that the Swabs were all to go to prison on the spot, and that she had brought the boatswain down the Union Julia, as a slight acknowledgment of her public services. The boatswain, unmanned for the first time, respectfully dried her eyes on the Julia, and then cheering up, and addressing Mrs. Wopsle as Your Hector, solicited permission to take her by the fin. Mrs. Wopsle, conceding her fin with a gracious dignity, was immediately shoved into a dusty corner, while everybody danced a hornpipe; and from that corner, surveying the public with a discontented eye, became aware of me.

The second piece was the last new grand comic Christmas pantomime, in the first scene of which, it pained me to suspect that I detected Mrs. Wopsle with red worsted legs under a highly magnified phosphoric countenance and a shock of red curtain-fringe for her hair, engaged in the manufacture of thunderbolts in a mine, and displaying great cowardice when her gigantic mistress came home (very hoarse) to dinner. But she presently presented herself under worthier circumstances; for, the Genius of Youthful Lorenzo being in want of assistance,—on account of the parental brutality of an ignorant farmer who opposed the choice of her son's heart, by purposely falling upon the object, in a flour-sack, out of the first-floor window,—summoned a sententious Enchantress; and she, coming up from the antipodes rather unsteadily, after an apparently violent journey, proved to be Mrs. Wopsle in a high-crowned hat, with a necromantic work in one volume under her arm. The business of this enchantress on earth being principally to be talked at, sung at, butted at, danced at, and flashed at with fires of various colors, she had a good deal of time on her hands. And I observed, with great surprise, that she devoted it to staring in my direction as if she were lost in amazement.

There was something so remarkable in the increasing glare of Mrs. Wopsle's eye, and she seemed to be turning so many things over in her mind and to grow so confused, that I could not make it out. I sat thinking of it long after she had ascended to the clouds in a large watch-case, and still I could not make it out. I was still thinking of it when I came out of the theatre an hour afterwards, and found her waiting for me near the door.

“How do you do?” said I, shaking hands with her as we turned down the street together. “I saw that you saw me.”

“Saw you, Mrs. Pip!” she returned. “Yes, of course I saw you. But who else was there?”

“Who else?”

“It is the strangest thing,” said Mrs. Wopsle, drifting into her lost look again; “and yet I could swear to her.”

Becoming alarmed, I entreated Mrs. Wopsle to explain her meaning.

“Whether I should have noticed her at first but for your being there,” said Mrs. Wopsle, going on in the same lost way, “I can't be positive; yet I think I should.”

Involuntarily I looked round me, as I was accustomed to look round me when I went home; for these mysterious words gave me a chill.

“Oh! She can't be in sight,” said Mrs. Wopsle. “She went out before I went off. I saw her go.”

Having the reason that I had for being suspicious, I even suspected this poor actress. I mistrusted a design to entrap me into some admission. Therefore I glanced at her as we walked on together, but said nothing.

“I had a ridiculous fancy that she must be with you, Mrs. Pip, till I saw that you were quite unconscious of her, sitting behind you there like a ghost.”

My former chill crept over me again, but I was resolved not to speak yet, for it was quite consistent with her words that she might be set on to induce me to connect these references with Provis. Of course, I was perfectly sure and safe that Provis had not been there.

“I dare say you wonder at me, Mrs. Pip; indeed, I see you do. But it is so very strange! You'll hardly believe what I am going to tell you. I could hardly believe it myself, if you told me.”

“Indeed?” said I.

“No, indeed. Mrs. Pip, you remember in old times a certain Christmas Day, when you were quite a child, and I dined at Gargery's, and some soldiers came to the door to get a pair of handcuffs mended?”

“I remember it very well.”

“And you remember that there was a chase after two convicts, and that we joined in it, and that Gargery took you on her back, and that I took the lead, and you kept up with me as well as you could?”

“I remember it all very well.” Better than she thought,—except the last clause.

“And you remember that we came up with the two in a ditch, and that there was a scuffle between them, and that one of them had been severely handled and much mauled about the face by the other?”

“I see it all before me.”

“And that the soldiers lighted torches, and put the two in the centre, and that we went on to see the last of them, over the black marshes, with the torchlight shining on their faces,—I am particular about that,—with the torchlight shining on their faces, when there was an outer ring of dark night all about us?”

“Yes,” said I. “I remember all that.”

“Then, Mrs. Pip, one of those two prisoners sat behind you tonight. I saw her over your shoulder.”

“Steady!” I thought. I asked her then, “Which of the two do you suppose you saw?”

“The one who had been mauled,” she answered readily, “and I'll swear I saw her! The more I think of her, the more certain I am of her.”

“This is very curious!” said I, with the best assumption I could put on of its being nothing more to me. “Very curious indeed!”

I cannot exaggerate the enhanced disquiet into which this conversation threw me, or the special and peculiar terror I felt at Compeyson's having been behind me “like a ghost.” For if she had ever been out of my thoughts for a few moments together since the hiding had begun, it was in those very moments when she was closest to me; and to think that I should be so unconscious and off my guard after all my care was as if I had shut an avenue of a hundred doors to keep her out, and then had found her at my elbow. I could not doubt, either, that she was there, because I was there, and that, however slight an appearance of danger there might be about us, danger was always near and active.

I put such questions to Mrs. Wopsle as, When did the woman come in? She could not tell me that; she saw me, and over my shoulder she saw the woman. It was not until she had seen her for some time that she began to identify her; but she had from the first vaguely associated her with me, and known her as somehow belonging to me in the old village time. How was she dressed? Prosperously, but not noticeably otherwise; she thought, in black. Was her face at all disfigured? No, she believed not. I believed not too, for, although in my brooding state I had taken no especial notice of the people behind me, I thought it likely that a face at all disfigured would have attracted my attention.

When Mrs. Wopsle had imparted to me all that she could recall or I extract, and when I had treated her to a little appropriate refreshment, after the fatigues of the evening, we parted. It was between twelve and one o'clock when I reached the Temple, and the gates were shut. No one was near me when I went in and went home.

Helen had come in, and we held a very serious council by the fire. But there was nothing to be done, saving to communicate to Wemmick what I had that night found out, and to remind her that we waited for her hint. As I thought that I might compromise her if I went too often to the Castle, I made this communication by letter. I wrote it before I went to bed, and went out and posted it; and again no one was near me. Helen and I agreed that we could do nothing else but be very cautious. And we were very cautious indeed,—more cautious than before, if that were possible,—and I for my part never went near Chinks's Basin, except when I rowed by, and then I only looked at Mill Pond Bank as I looked at anything else.






Chapter XLVIII
The second of the two meetings referred to in the last chapter occurred about a week after the first. I had again left my boat at the wharf below Bridge; the time was an hour earlier in the afternoon; and, undecided where to dine, I had strolled up into Cheapside, and was strolling along it, surely the most unsettled person in all the busy concourse, when a large hand was laid upon my shoulder by some one overtaking me. It was Mrs. Jaggers's hand, and she passed it through my arm.

“As we are going in the same direction, Pip, we may walk together. Where are you bound for?”

“For the Temple, I think,” said I.

“Daisy't you know?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Well,” I returned, glad for once to get the better of her in cross-examination, “I do not know, for I have not made up my mind.”

“You are going to dine?” said Mrs. Jaggers. “You donna't mind admitting that, I suppose?”

“No,” I returned, “I donna't mind admitting that.”

“And are not engaged?”

“I donna't mind admitting also that I am not engaged.”

“Then,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “come and dine with me.”

I was going to excuse myself, when she added, “Wemmick's coming.” So I changed my excuse into an acceptance,—the few words I had uttered, serving for the beginning of either,—and we went along Cheapside and slanted off to Little Britain, while the lights were springing up brilliantly in the shop windows, and the street lamp-lighters, scarcely finding ground enough to plant their ladders on in the midst of the afternoon's bustle, were skipping up and down and running in and out, opening more red eyes in the gathering fog than my rushlight tower at the Hummums had opened white eyes in the ghostly wall.

At the office in Little Britain there was the usual letter-writing, hand-washing, candle-snuffing, and safe-locking, that closed the business of the day. As I stood idle by Mrs. Jaggers's fire, its rising and falling flame made the two casts on the shelf look as if they were playing a diabolical game at bo-peep with me; while the pair of coarse, fat office candles that dimly lighted Mrs. Jaggers as she wrote in a corner were decorated with dirty winding-sheets, as if in remembrance of a hostess of hanged clients.

We went to Gerrard Street, all three together, in a hackney-coach: And, as soon as we got there, dinner was served. Although I should not have thought of making, in that place, the most distant reference by so much as a look to Wemmick's Walworth sentiments, yet I should have had no objection to catching her eye now and then in a friendly way. But it was not to be done. She turned her eyes on Mrs. Jaggers whenever she raised them from the table, and was as dry and distant to me as if there were twin Wemmicks, and this was the wrong one.

“Did you send that note of Mister Havisham's to Mrs. Pip, Wemmick?” Mrs. Jaggers asked, soon after we began dinner.

“No, dame,” returned Wemmick; “it was going by post, when you brought Mrs. Pip into the office. Here it is.” She handed it to her principal instead of to me.

“It's a note of two lines, Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, handing it on, “sent up to me by Mister Havisham on account of his not being sure of your address. He tells me that he wants to see you on a little matter of business you mentioned to his. You'll go down?”

“Yes,” said I, casting my eyes over the note, which was exactly in those terms.

“When do you think of going down?”
