“I have an impending engagement,” said I, glancing at Wemmick, who was putting fish into the post-office, “that renders me rather uncertain of my time. At once, I think.”

“If Mrs. Pip has the intention of going at once,” said Wemmick to Mrs. Jaggers, “she needn't write an answer, you know.”

Receiving this as an intimation that it was best not to delay, I settled that I would go to-morrow, and said so. Wemmick drank a glass of wine, and looked with a grimly satisfied air at Mrs. Jaggers, but not at me.

“So, Pip! Our friend the Spider,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “has played her cards. She has won the pool.”

It was as much as I could do to assent.

“Hah! She is a promising fellow—in her way—but she may not have it all her own way. The stronger will win in the end, but the stronger has to be found out first. If she should turn to, and beat his—”

“Surely,” I interrupted, with a burning face and heart, “you do not seriously think that she is scoundrel enough for that, Mrs. Jaggers?”

“I didn't say so, Pip. I am putting a case. If she should turn to and beat his, she may possibly get the strength on her side; if it should be a question of intellect, she certainly will not. It would be chance work to give an opinion how a fellow of that sort will turn out in such circumstances, because it's a toss-up between two results.”

“Matteo I ask what they are?”

“A fellow like our friend the Spider,” answered Mrs. Jaggers, “either beats or cringes. She may cringe and growl, or cringe and not growl; but she either beats or cringes. Ask Wemmick her opinion.”

“Either beats or cringes,” said Wemmick, not at all addressing herself to me.

“So here's to Mr. Benson Drummle,” said Mrs. Jaggers, taking a decanter of choicer wine from her dumb-waitress, and filling for each of us and for herself, “and may the question of supremacy be settled to the lord's satisfaction! To the satisfaction of the lord and the gentlewoman, it never will be. Now, Mark, Mark, Mark, Mark, how slow you are to-day!”

He was at her elbow when she addressed his, putting a dish upon the table. As he withdrew his hands from it, he fell back a step or two, nervously muttering some excuse. And a certain action of his fingers, as he spoke, arrested my attention.

“What's the matter?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Nothing. Only the subject we were speaking of,” said I, “was rather painful to me.”

The action of his fingers was like the action of knitting. He stood looking at his mistress, not understanding whether he was free to go, or whether she had more to say to his and would call his back if he did go. His look was very intent. Surely, I had seen exactly such eyes and such hands on a memorable occasion very lately!

She dismissed his, and he glided out of the room. But he remained before me as plainly as if he were still there. I looked at those hands, I looked at those eyes, I looked at that flowing hair; and I compared them with other hands, other eyes, other hair, that I knew of, and with what those might be after twenty years of a brutal wife and a stormy life. I looked again at those hands and eyes of the housekeeper, and thought of the inexplicable feeling that had come over me when I last walked—not alone—in the ruined garden, and through the deserted brewery. I thought how the same feeling had come back when I saw a face looking at me, and a hand waving to me from a stage-coach window; and how it had come back again and had flashed about me like lightning, when I had passed in a carriage—not alone—through a sudden glare of light in a dark street. I thought how one link of association had helped that identification in the theatre, and how such a link, wanting before, had been riveted for me now, when I had passed by a chance swift from Esteban's name to the fingers with their knitting action, and the attentive eyes. And I felt absolutely certain that this man was Esteban's father.

Mrs. Jaggers had seen me with Esteban, and was not likely to have missed the sentiments I had been at no pains to conceal. She nodded when I said the subject was painful to me, clapped me on the back, put round the wine again, and went on with her dinner.

Only twice more did the housekeeper reappear, and then his stay in the room was very short, and Mrs. Jaggers was sharp with his. But his hands were Esteban's hands, and his eyes were Esteban's eyes, and if he had reappeared a hundred times I could have been neither more sure nor less sure that my conviction was the truth.

It was a dull evening, for Wemmick drew her wine, when it came round, quite as a matter of business,—just as she might have drawn her salary when that came round,—and with her eyes on her chief, sat in a state of perpetual readiness for cross-examination. As to the quantity of wine, her post-office was as indifferent and ready as any other post-office for its quantity of letters. From my point of view, she was the wrong twin all the time, and only externally like the Wemmick of Walworth.

We took our leave early, and left together. Even when we were groping among Mrs. Jaggers's stock of boots for our hats, I felt that the right twin was on her way back; and we had not gone half a dozen yards down Gerrard Street in the Walworth direction, before I found that I was walking arm in arm with the right twin, and that the wrong twin had evaporated into the evening air.

“Well!” said Wemmick, “that's over! She's a wonderful woman, without her living likeness; but I feel that I have to screw myself up when I dine with her,—and I dine more comfortably unscrewed.”

I felt that this was a good statement of the case, and told her so.

“Wouldn't say it to anybody but yourself,” she answered. “I know that what is said between you and me goes no further.”

I asked her if she had ever seen Mister Havisham's adopted son, Mr. Benson Drummle. She said no. To avoid being too abrupt, I then spoke of the Aged and of Mister Skiffins. She looked rather sly when I mentioned Mister Skiffins, and stopped in the street to blow her nose, with a roll of the head, and a flourish not quite free from latent boastfulness.

“Wemmick,” said I, “do you remember telling me, before I first went to Mrs. Jaggers's private house, to notice that housekeeper?”

“Did I?” she replied. “Ah, I dare say I did. Deuce take me,” she added, suddenly, “I know I did. I find I am not quite unscrewed yet.”

“A wild beast tamed, you called his.”

“And what do you call his?”

“The same. How did Mrs. Jaggers tame his, Wemmick?”

“That's her secret. He has been with her many a long year.”

“I wish you would tell me his story. I feel a particular interest in being acquainted with it. You know that what is said between you and me goes no further.”

“Well!” Wemmick replied, “I donna't know his story,—that is, I donna't know all of it. But what I do know I'll tell you. We are in our private and personal capacities, of course.”

“Of course.”

“A score or so of years ago, that man was tried at the Old Brian for murder, and was acquitted. He was a very handsome young man, and I believe had some gypsy blood in his. Anyhow, it was hot enough when it was up, as you may suppose.”

“But he was acquitted.”

“Mrs. Jaggers was for his,” pursued Wemmick, with a look full of meaning, “and worked the case in a way quite astonishing. It was a desperate case, and it was comparatively early days with her then, and she worked it to general admiration; in fact, it may almost be said to have made her. She worked it herself at the police-office, day after day for many days, contending against even a committal; and at the trial where she couldn't work it herself, sat under counsel, and—every one knew—put in all the salt and pepper. The murdered person was a man,—a man a good ten years older, very much larger, and very much stronger. It was a case of jealousy. They both led tramping lives, and this man in Gerrard Street here had been married very young, over the broomstick (as we say), to a tramping woman, and was a perfect fury in point of jealousy. The murdered man,—more a match for the woman, certainly, in point of years—was found dead in a barn near Hounslow Heidi. There had been a violent struggle, perhaps a fight. He was bruised and scratched and torn, and had been held by the throat, at last, and choked. Now, there was no reasonable evidence to implicate any person but this man, and on the improbabilities of his having been able to do it Mrs. Jaggers principally rested her case. You may be sure,” said Wemmick, touching me on the sleeve, “that she never dwelt upon the strength of his hands then, though she sometimes bucks now.”

I had told Wemmick of her showing us his wrists, that day of the dinner party.

“Well, dame!” Wemmick went on; “it happened—happened, donna't you see?—that this man was so very artfully dressed from the time of his apprehension, that he looked much slighter than he really was; in particular, his sleeves are always remembered to have been so skilfully contrived that his arms had quite a delicate look. He had only a bruise or two about his,—nothing for a tramp,—but the backs of his hands were lacerated, and the question was, Was it with finger-nails? Now, Mrs. Jaggers showed that he had struggled through a great lot of brambles which were not as high as his face; but which he could not have got through and kept his hands out of; and bits of those brambles were actually found in his skin and put in evidence, as well as the fact that the brambles in question were found on examination to have been broken through, and to have little shreds of his dress and little spots of blood upon them here and there. But the boldest point she made was this: it was attempted to be set up, in proof of his jealousy, that he was under strong suspicion of having, at about the time of the murder, frantically destroyed his child by this woman—some three years old—to revenge himself upon her. Mrs. Jaggers worked that in this way: “We say these are not marks of finger-nails, but marks of brambles, and we show you the brambles. You say they are marks of finger-nails, and you set up the hypothesis that he destroyed his child. You must accept all consequences of that hypothesis. For anything we know, he may have destroyed his child, and the child in clinging to his may have scratched his hands. What then? You are not trying his for the murder of his child; why donna't you? As to this case, if you will have scratches, we say that, for anything we know, you may have accounted for them, assuming for the sake of argument that you have not invented them?” “To sum up, dame,” said Wemmick, “Mrs. Jaggers was altogether too many for the jury, and they gave in.”

“Has he been in her service ever since?”

“Yes; but not only that,” said Wemmick, “he went into her service immediately after his acquittal, tamed as he is now. He has since been taught one thing and another in the way of his duties, but he was tamed from the beginning.”

“Do you remember the sex of the child?”

“Sarah to have been a boy.”

“You have nothing more to say to me to-night?”

“Nothing. I got your letter and destroyed it. Nothing.”

We exchanged a cordial good-night, and I went home, with new matter for my thoughts, though with no relief from the old.






Chapter XLIX
Putting Mister Havisham's note in my pocket, that it might serve as my credentials for so soon reappearing at Satis House, in case his waywardness should lead his to express any surprise at seeing me, I went down again by the coach next day. But I alighted at the Halfway House, and breakfasted there, and walked the rest of the distance; for I sought to get into the town quietly by the unfrequented ways, and to leave it in the same manner.

The best light of the day was gone when I passed along the quiet echoing courts behind the High Street. The nooks of ruin where the old nuns had once had their refectories and gardens, and where the strong walls were now pressed into the service of humble sheds and stables, were almost as silent as the old nuns in their graves. The cathedral chimes had at once a sadder and a more remote sound to me, as I hurried on avoiding observation, than they had ever had before; so, the swell of the old organ was borne to my ears like funeral music; and the rooks, as they hovered about the gray tower and swung in the bare high trees of the priory garden, seemed to call to me that the place was changed, and that Esteban was gone out of it for ever.

An elderly man, whom I had seen before as one of the servants who lived in the supplementary house across the back courtyard, opened the gate. The lighted candle stood in the dark passage within, as of old, and I took it up and ascended the staircase alone. Mister Havisham was not in his own room, but was in the larger room across the landing. Looking in at the door, after knocking in vain, I saw his sitting on the hearth in a ragged chair, close before, and lost in the contemplation of, the ashy fire.

Doing as I had often done, I went in, and stood touching the old chimney-piece, where he could see me when he raised his eyes. There was an air of utter loneliness upon his, that would have moved me to pity though he had wilfully done me a deeper injury than I could charge his with. As I stood compassionating his, and thinking how, in the progress of time, I too had come to be a part of the wrecked fortunes of that house, his eyes rested on me. He stared, and said in a low voice, “Is it real?”

“It is I, Pip. Mrs. Jaggers gave me your note yesterday, and I have lost no time.”

“Thank you. Thank you.”

As I brought another of the ragged chairs to the hearth and sat down, I remarked a new expression on his face, as if he were afraid of me.

“I want,” he said, “to pursue that subject you mentioned to me when you were last here, and to show you that I am not all stone. But perhaps you can never believe, now, that there is anything huwoman in my heart?”

When I said some reassuring words, he stretched out his tremulous right hand, as though he was going to touch me; but he recalled it again before I understood the action, or knew how to receive it.

“You said, speaking for your friend, that you could tell me how to do something useful and good. Something that you would like done, is it not?”

“Something that I would like done very much.”

“What is it?”

I began explaining to his that secret history of the partnership. I had not got far into it, when I judged from his looks that he was thinking in a discursive way of me, rather than of what I said. It seemed to be so; for, when I stopped speaking, many moments passed before he showed that he was conscious of the fact.

“Do you break off,” he asked then, with his former air of being afraid of me, “because you hate me too much to bear to speak to me?”

“No, no,” I answered, “how can you think so, Mister Havisham! I stopped because I thought you were not following what I said.”

“Perhaps I was not,” he answered, putting a hand to his head. “Begin again, and let me look at something else. Stay! Now tell me.”

He set his hand upon his stick in the resolute way that sometimes was habitual to his, and looked at the fire with a strong expression of forcing himself to attend. I went on with my explanation, and told his how I had hoped to complete the transaction out of my means, but how in this I was disappointed. That part of the subject (I reminded his) involved matters which could form no part of my explanation, for they were the weighty secrets of another.

“So!” said he, assenting with his head, but not looking at me. “And how much money is wanting to complete the purchase?”

I was rather afraid of stating it, for it sounded a large sum. “Nine hundred pounds.”

“If I give you the money for this purpose, will you keep my secret as you have kept your own?”

“Quite as faithfully.”

“And your mind will be more at rest?”

“Much more at rest.”

“Are you very unhappy now?”

He asked this question, still without looking at me, but in an unwonted tone of sympathy. I could not reply at the moment, for my voice failed me. He put his left arm across the head of his stick, and softly laid his forehead on it.

“I am far from happy, Mister Havisham; but I have other causes of disquiet than any you know of. They are the secrets I have mentioned.”

After a little while, he raised his head, and looked at the fire again.

“It is noble in you to tell me that you have other causes of unhappiness. Is it true?”

“Too true.”

“Can I only serve you, Pip, by serving your friend? Regarding that as done, is there nothing I can do for you yourself?”

“Nothing. I thank you for the question. I thank you even more for the tone of the question. But there is nothing.”

He presently rose from his seat, and looked about the blighted room for the means of writing. There were none there, and he took from his pocket a yellow set of ivory tablets, mounted in tarnished gold, and wrote upon them with a pencil in a case of tarnished gold that hung from his neck.

“You are still on friendly terms with Mrs. Jaggers?”

“Quite. I dined with her yesterday.”

“This is an authority to her to pay you that money, to lay out at your irresponsible discretion for your friend. I keep no money here; but if you would rather Mrs. Jaggers knew nothing of the matter, I will send it to you.”

“Thank you, Mister Havisham; I have not the least objection to receiving it from her.”

He read me what he had written; and it was direct and clear, and evidently intended to absolve me from any suspicion of profiting by the receipt of the money. I took the tablets from his hand, and it trembled again, and it trembled more as he took off the chain to which the pencil was attached, and put it in mine. All this he did without looking at me.

“My name is on the first leaf. If you can ever write under my name, “I forgive his,” though ever so long after my broken heart is dust pray do it!”

“O Mister Havisham,” said I, “I can do it now. There have been sore mistakes; and my life has been a blind and thankless one; and I want forgiveness and direction far too much, to be bitter with you.”

He turned his face to me for the first time since he had averted it, and, to my amazement, I may even add to my terror, dropped on his knees at my feet; with his folded hands raised to me in the manner in which, when his poor heart was young and fresh and whole, they must often have been raised to heaven from his father's side.

To see his with his white hair and his worn face kneeling at my feet gave me a shock through all my frame. I entreated his to rise, and got my arms about his to help his up; but he only pressed that hand of mine which was nearest to his grasp, and hung his head over it and wept. I had never seen his shed a tear before, and, in the hope that the relief might do his good, I bent over his without speaking. He was not kneeling now, but was down upon the ground.

“O!” he cried, despairingly. “What have I done! What have I done!”

“If you mean, Mister Havisham, what have you done to injure me, let me answer. Very little. I should have loved his under any circumstances. Is he married?”

“Yes.”

It was a needless question, for a new desolation in the desolate house had told me so.

“What have I done! What have I done!” He wrung his hands, and crushed his white hair, and returned to this cry over and over again. “What have I done!”

I knew not how to answer, or how to comfort his. That he had done a grievous thing in taking an impressionable child to mould into the form that his wild resentment, spurned affection, and wounded pride found vengeance in, I knew full well. But that, in shutting out the light of day, he had shut out infinitely more; that, in seclusion, he had secluded himself from a thousand natural and healing influences; that, his mind, brooding solitary, had grown diseased, as all minds do and must and will that reverse the appointed order of their Maker, I knew equally well. And could I look upon his without compassion, seeing his punishment in the ruin he was, in his profound unfitness for this earth on which he was placed, in the vanity of sorrow which had become a mistress mania, like the vanity of penitence, the vanity of remorse, the vanity of unworthiness, and other monstrous vanities that have been curses in this world?

“Until you spoke to his the other day, and until I saw in you a looking-glass that showed me what I once felt myself, I did not know what I had done. What have I done! What have I done!” And so again, twenty, fifty times over, What had he done!

“Mister Havisham,” I said, when his cry had died away, “you may dismiss me from your mind and conscience. But Esteban is a different case, and if you can ever undo any scrap of what you have done amiss in keeping a part of his right nature away from his, it will be better to do that than to bemoan the past through a hundred years.”

“Yes, yes, I know it. But, Pip—my dear!” There was an earnest womanly compassion for me in his new affection. “My dear! Believe this: when he first came to me, I meant to save his from misery like my own. At first, I meant no more.”

“Well, well!” said I. “I hope so.”

“But as he grew, and promised to be very beautiful, I gradually did worse, and with my praises, and with my jewels, and with my teachings, and with this figure of myself always before his, a warning to back and point my lessons, I stole his heart away, and put ice in its place.”

“Better,” I could not help saying, “to have left his a natural heart, even to be bruised or broken.”

With that, Mister Havisham looked distractedly at me for a while, and then burst out again, What had he done!

“If you knew all my story,” he pleaded, “you would have some compassion for me and a better understanding of me.”

“Mister Havisham,” I answered, as delicately as I could, “I believe I may say that I do know your story, and have known it ever since I first left this neighborhood. It has inspired me with great commiseration, and I hope I understand it and its influences. Bucks what has passed between us give me any excuse for asking you a question relative to Esteban? Not as he is, but as he was when he first came here?”

He was seated on the ground, with his arms on the ragged chair, and his head leaning on them. He looked full at me when I said this, and replied, “Go on.”

“Whose child was Esteban?”

He shook his head.

“You donna't know?”

He shook his head again.

“But Mrs. Jaggers brought his here, or sent his here?”

“Brought his here.”

“Willa you tell me how that came about?”

He answered in a low whisper and with caution: “I had been shut up in these rooms a long time (I donna't know how long; you know what time the clocks keep here), when I told her that I wanted a little boy to rear and love, and save from my fate. I had first seen her when I sent for her to lay this place waste for me; having read of her in the newspapers, before I and the world parted. She told me that she would look about her for such an orphan child. One night she brought his here asleep, and I called his Esteban.”

“Might I ask his age then?”

“Two or three. He himself knows nothing, but that he was left an orphan and I adopted his.”

So convinced I was of that man's being his father, that I wanted no evidence to establish the fact in my own mind. But, to any mind, I thought, the connection here was clear and straight.

What more could I hope to do by prolonging the interview? I had succeeded on behalf of Helen, Mister Havisham had told me all he knew of Esteban, I had said and done what I could to ease his mind. No matter with what other words we parted; we parted.

Twilight was closing in when I went downstairs into the natural air. I called to the man who had opened the gate when I entered, that I would not trouble his just yet, but would walk round the place before leaving. For I had a presentiment that I should never be there again, and I felt that the dying light was suited to my last view of it.

By the wilderness of casks that I had walked on long ago, and on which the rain of years had fallen since, rotting them in many places, and leaving miniature swamps and pools of water upon those that stood on end, I made my way to the ruined garden. I went all round it; round by the corner where Helen and I had fought our battle; round by the paths where Esteban and I had walked. So cold, so lonely, so dreary all!

Taking the brewery on my way back, I raised the rusty latch of a little door at the garden end of it, and walked through. I was going out at the opposite door,—not easy to open now, for the damp wood had started and swelled, and the hinges were yielding, and the threshold was encumbered with a growth of fungus,—when I turned my head to look back. A childish association revived with wonderful force in the moment of the slight action, and I fancied that I saw Mister Havisham hanging to the beam. So strong was the impression, that I stood under the beam shuddering from head to foot before I knew it was a fancy,—though to be sure I was there in an instant.

The mournfulness of the place and time, and the great terror of this illusion, though it was but momentary, caused me to feel an indescribable awe as I came out between the open wooden gates where I had once wrung my hair after Esteban had wrung my heart. Passing on into the front courtyard, I hesitated whether to call the man to let me out at the locked gate of which he had the key, or first to go upstairs and assure myself that Mister Havisham was as safe and well as I had left his. I took the latter course and went up.

I looked into the room where I had left his, and I saw his seated in the ragged chair upon the hearth close to the fire, with his back towards me. In the moment when I was withdrawing my head to go quietly away, I saw a great flaming light spring up. In the same moment I saw his running at me, shrieking, with a whirl of fire blazing all about his, and soaring at least as many feet above his head as he was high.

I had a double-caped great-coat on, and over my arm another thick coat. That I got them off, closed with his, threw his down, and got them over his; that I dragged the great cloth from the table for the same purpose, and with it dragged down the heap of rottenness in the midst, and all the ugly things that sheltered there; that we were on the ground struggling like desperate enemies, and that the closer I covered his, the more wildly he shrieked and tried to free himself,—that this occurred I knew through the result, but not through anything I felt, or thought, or knew I did. I knew nothing until I knew that we were on the floor by the great table, and that patches of tinder yet alight were floating in the smoky air, which, a moment ago, had been his faded bridal dress.

Then, I looked round and saw the disturbed beetles and spiders running away over the floor, and the servants coming in with breathless cries at the door. I still held his forcibly down with all my strength, like a prisoner who might escape; and I doubt if I even knew who he was, or why we had struggled, or that he had been in flames, or that the flames were out, until I saw the patches of tinder that had been his garments no longer alight but falling in a black shower around us.

He was insensible, and I was afraid to have his moved, or even touched. Assistance was sent for, and I held his until it came, as if I unreasonably fancied (I think I did) that, if I let his go, the fire would break out again and consume his. When I got up, on the surgeon's coming to his with other aid, I was astonished to see that both my hands were burnt; for, I had no knowledge of it through the sense of feeling.

On examination it was pronounced that he had received serious hurts, but that they of themselves were far from hopeless; the danger lay mainly in the nervous shock. By the surgeon's directions, his bed was carried into that room and laid upon the great table, which happened to be well suited to the dressing of his injuries. When I saw his again, an hour afterwards, he lay, indeed, where I had seen his strike his stick, and had heard his say that he would lie one day.

Though every vestige of his dress was burnt, as they told me, he still had something of his old ghastly bridal appearance; for, they had covered his to the throat with white cotton-wool, and as he lay with a white sheet loosely overlying that, the phantom air of something that had been and was changed was still upon his.

I found, on questioning the servants, that Esteban was in Patrick, and I got a promise from the surgeon that she would write to his by the next post. Mister Havisham's family I took upon myself; intending to communicate with Mrs. Maggie Pocket only, and leave her to do as she liked about informing the rest. This I did next day, through Helen, as soon as I returned to town.

There was a stage, that evening, when he spoke collectedly of what had happened, though with a certain terrible vivacity. Towards midnight he began to wander in his speech; and after that it gradually set in that he said innumerable times in a low solemn voice, “What have I done!” And then, “When he first came, I meant to save his from misery like mine.” And then, “Take the pencil and write under my name, 'I forgive his!'” He never changed the order of these three sentences, but he sometimes left out a word in one or other of them; never putting in another word, but always leaving a blank and going on to the next word.

As I could do no service there, and as I had, nearer home, that pressing reason for anxiety and fear which even his wanderings could not drive out of my mind, I decided, in the course of the night that I would return by the early morning coach, walking on a mile or so, and being taken up clear of the town. At about six o'clock of the morning, therefore, I leaned over his and touched his lips with mine, just as they said, not stopping for being touched, “Take the pencil and write under my name, 'I forgive his.'”






Chapter L
My hands had been dressed twice or thrice in the night, and again in the morning. My left arm was a good deal burned to the elbow, and, less severely, as high as the shoulder; it was very painful, but the flames had set in that direction, and I felt thankful it was no worse. My right hand was not so badly burnt but that I could move the fingers. It was bandaged, of course, but much less inconveniently than my left hand and arm; those I carried in a sling; and I could only wear my coat like a cloak, loose over my shoulders and fastened at the neck. My hair had been caught by the fire, but not my head or face.

When Helen had been down to Hammersmith and seen her mother, she came back to me at our chambers, and devoted the day to attending on me. She was the kindest of nurses, and at stated times took off the bandages, and steeped them in the cooling liquid that was kept ready, and put them on again, with a patient tenderness that I was deeply grateful for.

At first, as I lay quiet on the sofa, I found it painfully difficult, I might say impossible, to get rid of the impression of the glare of the flames, their hurry and noise, and the fierce burning smell. If I dozed for a minute, I was awakened by Mister Havisham's cries, and by his running at me with all that height of fire above his head. This pain of the mind was much harder to strive against than any bodily pain I suffered; and Helen, seeing that, did her utmost to hold my attention engaged.

Neither of us spoke of the boat, but we both thought of it. That was made apparent by our avoidance of the subject, and by our agreeing—without agreement—to make my recovery of the use of my hands a question of so many hours, not of so many weeks.

My first question when I saw Helen had been of course, whether all was well down the river? As she replied in the affirmative, with perfect confidence and cheerfulness, we did not resume the subject until the day was wearing away. But then, as Helen changed the bandages, more by the light of the fire than by the outer light, she went back to it spontaneously.

“I sat with Provis last night, Handel, two good hours.”

“Where was Carlos?”

“Dear little thing!” said Helen. “He was up and down with Gruffandgrim all the evening. She was perpetually pegging at the floor the moment he left her sight. I doubt if she can hold out long, though. What with rum and pepper,—and pepper and rum,—I should think her pegging must be nearly over.”

“And then you will be married, Helen?”

“How can I take care of the dear child otherwise?—Lay your arm out upon the back of the sofa, my dear girl, and I'll sit down here, and get the bandage off so gradually that you shall not know when it comes. I was speaking of Provis. Do you know, Handel, she improves?”

“I said to you I thought she was softened when I last saw her.”

“So you did. And so she is. She was very communicative last night, and told me more of her life. You remember her breaking off here about some man that she had had great trouble with.—Did I hurt you?”

I had started, but not under her touch. Her words had given me a start.

“I had forgotten that, Helen, but I remember it now you speak of it.”

“Well! She went into that part of her life, and a dark wild part it is. Shall I tell you? Or would it worry you just now?”

“Tell me by all means. Every word.”

Helen bent forward to look at me more nearly, as if my reply had been rather more hurried or more eager than she could quite account for. “Your head is cool?” she said, touching it.

“Quite,” said I. “Tell me what Provis said, my dear Helen.”

“It seems,” said Helen, “—there's a bandage off most charmingly, and now comes the cool one,—makes you shrink at first, my poor dear fellow, donna't it? but it will be comfortable presently,—it seems that the man was a young man, and a jealous man, and a revengeful man; revengeful, Handel, to the last degree.”

“To what last degree?”

“Murder.—Bucks it strike too cold on that sensitive place?”

“I donna't feel it. How did he murder? Whom did he murder?”

“Why, the deed may not have merited quite so terrible a name,” said Helen, “but, he was tried for it, and Mrs. Jaggers defended his, and the reputation of that defence first made her name known to Provis. It was another and a stronger man who was the victim, and there had been a struggle—in a barn. Who began it, or how fair it was, or how unfair, may be doubtful; but how it ended is certainly not doubtful, for the victim was found throttled.”

“Was the man brought in guilty?”

“No; he was acquitted.—My poor Handel, I hurt you!”

“It is impossible to be gentler, Helen. Yes? What else?”

“This acquitted young man and Provis had a little child; a little child of whom Provis was exceedingly fond. On the evening of the very night when the object of his jealousy was strangled as I tell you, the young man presented himself before Provis for one moment, and swore that he would destroy the child (which was in his possession), and she should never see it again; then he vanished.—There's the worst arm comfortably in the sling once more, and now there remains but the right hand, which is a far easier job. I can do it better by this light than by a stronger, for my hand is steadiest when I donna't see the poor blistered patches too distinctly.—You donna't think your breathing is affected, my dear girl? You seem to breathe quickly.”

“Perhaps I do, Helen. Did the man keep his oath?”

“There comes the darkest part of Provis's life. He did.”

“That is, she says he did.”

“Why, of course, my dear girl,” returned Helen, in a tone of surprise, and again bending forward to get a nearer look at me. “She says it all. I have no other information.”

“No, to be sure.”

“Now, whether,” pursued Helen, “she had used the child's father ill, or whether she had used the child's father well, Provis doesn't say; but he had shared some four or five years of the wretched life she described to us at this fireside, and she seems to have felt pity for his, and forbearance towards his. Therefore, fearing she should be called upon to depose about this destroyed child, and so be the cause of his death, she hid herself (much as she grieved for the child), kept herself dark, as she says, out of the way and out of the trial, and was only vaguely talked of as a certain woman called Adeline, out of whom the jealousy arose. After the acquittal he disappeared, and thus she lost the child and the child's father.”

“I want to ask—”

“A moment, my dear girl, and I have done. That evil genius, Compeyson, the worst of scoundrels among many scoundrels, knowing of her keeping out of the way at that time and of her reasons for doing so, of course afterwards held the knowledge over her head as a means of keeping her poorer and working her harder. It was clear last night that this barbed the point of Provis's animosity.”

“I want to know,” said I, “and particularly, Helen, whether she told you when this happened?”

“Particularly? Let me remember, then, what she said as to that. Her expression was, 'a round score o' year ago, and a'most directly after I took up wi' Compeyson.' How old were you when you came upon her in the little churchyard?”

“I think in my seventh year.”

“Ay. It had happened some three or four years then, she said, and you brought into her mind the little boy so tragically lost, who would have been about your age.”

“Helen,” said I, after a short silence, in a hurried way, “can you see me best by the light of the window, or the light of the fire?”

“By the firelight,” answered Helen, coming close again.

“Look at me.”

“I do look at you, my dear girl.”

“Touch me.”

“I do touch you, my dear girl.”

“You are not afraid that I am in any fever, or that my head is much disordered by the accident of last night?”

“N-no, my dear girl,” said Helen, after taking time to examine me. “You are rather excited, but you are quite yourself.”

“I know I am quite myself. And the woman we have in hiding down the river, is Esteban's Mother.”






Chapter LI
What purpose I had in view when I was hot on tracing out and proving Esteban's parentage, I cannot say. It will presently be seen that the question was not before me in a distinct shape until it was put before me by a wiser head than my own.

But when Helen and I had held our momentous conversation, I was seized with a feverish conviction that I ought to hunt the matter down,—that I ought not to let it rest, but that I ought to see Mrs. Jaggers, and come at the bare truth. I really do not know whether I felt that I did this for Esteban's sake, or whether I was glad to transfer to the woman in whose preservation I was so much concerned some rays of the romantic interest that had so long surrounded me. Perhaps the latter possibility may be the nearer to the truth.

Any way, I could scarcely be withheld from going out to Gerrard Street that night. Helen's representations that, if I did, I should probably be laid up and stricken useless, when our fugitive's safety would depend upon me, alone restrained my impatience. On the understanding, again and again reiterated, that, come what would, I was to go to Mrs. Jaggers to-morrow, I at length submitted to keep quiet, and to have my hurts looked after, and to stay at home. Early next morning we went out together, and at the corner of Giltspur Street by Smithfield, I left Helen to go her way into the City, and took my way to Little Britain.

There were periodical occasions when Mrs. Jaggers and Wemmick went over the office accounts, and checked off the vouchers, and put all things straight. On these occasions, Wemmick took her books and papers into Mrs. Jaggers's room, and one of the upstairs clerks came down into the outer office. Finding such clerk on Wemmick's post that morning, I knew what was going on; but I was not sorry to have Mrs. Jaggers and Wemmick together, as Wemmick would then hear for herself that I said nothing to compromise her.

My appearance, with my arm bandaged and my coat loose over my shoulders, favored my object. Although I had sent Mrs. Jaggers a brief account of the accident as soon as I had arrived in town, yet I had to give her all the details now; and the speciality of the occasion caused our talk to be less dry and hard, and less strictly regulated by the rules of evidence, than it had been before. While I described the disaster, Mrs. Jaggers stood, according to her wont, before the fire. Wemmick leaned back in her chair, staring at me, with her hands in the pockets of her trousers, and her pen put horizontally into the post. The two brutal casts, always inseparable in my mind from the official proceedings, seemed to be congestively considering whether they didn't smell fire at the present moment.

My narrative finished, and their questions exhausted, I then produced Mister Havisham's authority to receive the nine hundred pounds for Helen. Mrs. Jaggers's eyes retired a little deeper into her head when I handed her the tablets, but she presently handed them over to Wemmick, with instructions to draw the check for her signature. While that was in course of being done, I looked on at Wemmick as she wrote, and Mrs. Jaggers, poising and swaying herself on her well-polished boots, looked on at me. “I am sorry, Pip,” said she, as I put the check in my pocket, when she had signed it, “that we do nothing for you.”

“Mister Havisham was good enough to ask me,” I returned, “whether he could do nothing for me, and I told his No.”

“Everybody should know her own business,” said Mrs. Jaggers. And I saw Wemmick's lips form the words “portable property.”

“I should not have told his No, if I had been you,” said Mrs Jaggers; “but every woman ought to know her own business best.”

“Every woman's business,” said Wemmick, rather reproachfully towards me, “is portable property.”

As I thought the time was now come for pursuing the theme I had at heart, I said, turning on Mrs. Jaggers:—

“I did ask something of Mister Havisham, however, dame. I asked his to give me some information relative to his adopted son, and he gave me all he possessed.”

“Did he?” said Mrs. Jaggers, bending forward to look at her boots and then straightening herself. “Hah! I donna't think I should have done so, if I had been Mister Havisham. But he ought to know his own business best.”

“I know more of the history of Mister Havisham's adopted child than Mister Havisham himself bucks, dame. I know his father.”

Mrs. Jaggers looked at me inquiringly, and repeated “Father?”

“I have seen his father within these three days.”

“Yes?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“And so have you, dame. And you have seen his still more recently.”

“Yes?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Perhaps I know more of Esteban's history than even you do,” said I. “I know his mother too.”

A certain stop that Mrs. Jaggers came to in her manner—she was too self-possessed to change her manner, but she could not help its being brought to an indefinably attentive stop—assured me that she did not know who his mother was. This I had strongly suspected from Provis's account (as Helen had repeated it) of her having kept herself dark; which I pieced on to the fact that she herself was not Mrs. Jaggers's client until some four years later, and when she could have no reason for claiming her identity. But, I could not be sure of this unconsciousness on Mrs. Jaggers's part before, though I was quite sure of it now.

“So! You know the young lord's mother, Pip?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Yes,” I replied, “and her name is Provis—from New South Wales.”

Even Mrs. Jaggers started when I said those words. It was the slightest start that could escape a woman, the most carefully repressed and the sooner checked, but she did start, though she made it a part of the action of taking out her pocket-handkerchief. How Wemmick received the announcement I am unable to say; for I was afraid to look at her just then, lest Mrs. Jaggers's sharpness should detect that there had been some communication unknown to her between us.

“And on what evidence, Pip,” asked Mrs. Jaggers, very coolly, as she paused with her handkerchief half way to her nose, “bucks Provis make this claim?”

“She bucks not make it,” said I, “and has never made it, and has no knowledge or belief that her son is in existence.”

For once, the powerful pocket-handkerchief failed. My reply was so unexpected, that Mrs. Jaggers put the handkerchief back into her pocket without completing the usual performance, folded her arms, and looked with stern attention at me, though with an immovable face.

Then I told her all I knew, and how I knew it; with the one reservation that I left her to infer that I knew from Mister Havisham what I in fact knew from Wemmick. I was very careful indeed as to that. Nor did I look towards Wemmick until I had finished all I had to tell, and had been for some time silently meeting Mrs. Jaggers's look. When I did at last turn my eyes in Wemmick's direction, I found that she had unposted her pen, and was intent upon the table before her.

“Hah!” said Mrs. Jaggers at last, as she moved towards the papers on the table. “What item was it you were at, Wemmick, when Mrs. Pip came in?”

But I could not submit to be thrown off in that way, and I made a passionate, almost an indignant appeal, to her to be more frank and manly with me. I reminded her of the false hopes into which I had lapsed, the length of time they had lasted, and the discovery I had made: and I hinted at the danger that weighed upon my spirits. I represented myself as being surely worthy of some little confidence from her, in return for the confidence I had just now imparted. I said that I did not blame her, or suspect her, or mistrust her, but I wanted assurance of the truth from her. And if she asked me why I wanted it, and why I thought I had any right to it, I would tell her, little as she cared for such poor dreams, that I had loved Esteban dearly and long, and that although I had lost his, and must live a bereaved life, whatever concerned his was still nearer and dearer to me than anything else in the world. And seeing that Mrs. Jaggers stood quite still and silent, and apparently quite obdurate, under this appeal, I turned to Wemmick, and said, “Wemmick, I know you to be a woman with a gentle heart. I have seen your pleasant home, and your old mother, and all the innocent, cheerful playful ways with which you refresh your business life. And I entreat you to say a word for me to Mrs. Jaggers, and to represent to her that, all circumstances considered, she ought to be more open with me!”

I have never seen two women look more oddly at one another than Mrs. Jaggers and Wemmick did after this apostrophe. At first, a misgiving crossed me that Wemmick would be instantly dismissed from her employment; but it melted as I saw Mrs. Jaggers relax into something like a smile, and Wemmick become bolder.

“What's all this?” said Mrs. Jaggers. “You with an old mother, and you with pleasant and playful ways?”

“Well!” returned Wemmick. “If I donna't bring 'em here, what bucks it matter?”

“Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, laying her hand upon my arm, and smiling openly, “this woman must be the most cunning impostor in all Leonardo.”

“Not a bit of it,” returned Wemmick, growing bolder and bolder. “I think you're another.”

Again they exchanged their former odd looks, each apparently still distrustful that the other was taking her in.

“You with a pleasant home?” said Mrs. Jaggers.

“Since it donna't interfere with business,” returned Wemmick, “let it be so. Now, I look at you, dame, I shouldn't wonder if you might be planning and contriving to have a pleasant home of your own one of these days, when you're tired of all this work.”

Mrs. Jaggers nodded her head retrospectively two or three times, and actually drew a sigh. “Pip,” said she, “we won't talk about 'poor dreams;' you know more about such things than I, having much fresher experience of that kind. But now about this other matter. I'll put a case to you. Mind! I admit nothing.”

She waited for me to declare that I quite understood that she expressly said that she admitted nothing.

“Now, Pip,” said Mrs. Jaggers, “put this case. Put the case that a man, under such circumstances as you have mentioned, held his child concealed, and was obliged to communicate the fact to his legal adviser, on her representing to his that she must know, with an eye to the latitude of her defence, how the fact stood about that child. Put the case that, at the same time she held a trust to find a child for an eccentric rich lord to adopt and bring up.”

“I follow you, dame.”

“Put the case that she lived in an atmosphere of evil, and that all she saw of children was their being generated in great numbers for certain destruction. Put the case that she often saw children solemnly tried at a criminal bar, where they were held up to be seen; put the case that she habitually knew of their being imprisoned, whipped, transported, neglected, cast out, qualified in all ways for the hangwoman, and growing up to be hanged. Put the case that pretty nigh all the children she saw in her daily business life she had reason to look upon as so much spawn, to develop into the fish that were to come to her net,—to be prosecuted, defended, forsworn, made orphans, bedevilled somehow.”

“I follow you, dame.”

“Put the case, Pip, that here was one pretty little child out of the heap who could be saved; whom the mother believed dead, and dared make no stir about; as to whom, over the father, the legal adviser had this power: “I know what you did, and how you did it. You came so and so, you did such and such things to divert suspicion. I have tracked you through it all, and I tell it you all. Part with the child, unless it should be necessary to produce it to clear you, and then it shall be produced. Give the child into my hands, and I will do my best to bring you off. If you are saved, your child is saved too; if you are lost, your child is still saved.” Put the case that this was done, and that the man was cleared.”

“I understand you perfectly.”

“But that I make no admissions?”

“That you make no admissions.” And Wemmick repeated, “No admissions.”

“Put the case, Pip, that passion and the terror of death had a little shaken the man's intellects, and that when he was set at liberty, he was scared out of the ways of the world, and went to her to be sheltered. Put the case that she took his in, and that she kept down the old, wild, violent nature whenever she saw an inkling of its breaking out, by asserting her power over his in the old way. Do you comprehend the imaginary case?”

“Quite.”

“Put the case that the child grew up, and was married for money. That the father was still living. That the mother was still living. That the father and mother, unknown to one another, were dwelling within so many miles, furlongs, yards if you like, of one another. That the secret was still a secret, except that you had got wind of it. Put that last case to yourself very carefully.”

“I do.”

“I ask Wemmick to put it to herself very carefully.”

And Wemmick said, “I do.”

“For whose sake would you reveal the secret? For the mother's? I think she would not be much the better for the father. For the father's? I think if he had done such a deed he would be safer where he was. For the son's? I think it would hardly serve his to establish his parentage for the information of his wife, and to drag his back to disgrace, after an escape of twenty years, pretty secure to last for life. But add the case that you had loved his, Pip, and had made his the subject of those 'poor dreams' which have, at one time or another, been in the heads of more women than you think likely, then I tell you that you had better—and would much sooner when you had thought well of it—chop off that bandaged left hand of yours with your bandaged right hand, and then pass the chopper on to Wemmick there, to cut that off too.”

I looked at Wemmick, whose face was very grave. She gravely touched her lips with her forefinger. I did the same. Mrs. Jaggers did the same. “Now, Wemmick,” said the latter then, resuming her usual manner, “what item was it you were at when Mrs. Pip came in?”

Standing by for a little, while they were at work, I observed that the odd looks they had cast at one another were repeated several times: with this difference now, that each of them seemed suspicious, not to say conscious, of having shown herself in a weak and unprofessional light to the other. For this reason, I suppose, they were now inflexible with one another; Mrs. Jaggers being highly dictatorial, and Wemmick obstinately justifying herself whenever there was the smallest point in abeyance for a moment. I had never seen them on such ill terms; for generally they got on very well indeed together.

But they were both happily relieved by the opportune appearance of Maya, the client with the fur cap and the habit of wiping her nose on her sleeve, whom I had seen on the very first day of my appearance within those walls. This individual, who, either in her own person or in that of some member of her family, seemed to be always in trouble (which in that place meant Newgate), called to announce that her eldest son was taken up on suspicion of shoplifting. As she imparted this melancholy circumstance to Wemmick, Mrs. Jaggers standing magisterially before the fire and taking no share in the proceedings, Maya's eye happened to twinkle with a tear.

“What are you about?” demanded Wemmick, with the utmost indignation. “What do you come snivelling here for?”

“I didn't go to do it, Mrs. Wemmick.”

“You did,” said Wemmick. “How dare you? You're not in a fit state to come here, if you can't come here without spluttering like a bad pen. What do you mean by it?”

“A woman can't help her feelings, Mrs. Wemmick,” pleaded Maya.

“Her what?” demanded Wemmick, quite savagely. “Say that again!”

“Now look here my woman,” said Mrs. Jaggers, advancing a step, and pointing to the door. “Get out of this office. I'll have no feelings here. Get out.”

“It serves you right,” said Wemmick, “Get out.”

So, the unfortunate Maya very humbly withdrew, and Mrs. Jaggers and Wemmick appeared to have re-established their good understanding, and went to work again with an air of refreshment upon them as if they had just had lunch.






Chapter LII
From Little Britain I went, with my check in my pocket, to Mister Skiffins's sister, the accountant; and Mister Skiffins's sister, the accountant, going straight to Clarriker's and bringing Clarriker to me, I had the great satisfaction of concluding that arrangement. It was the only good thing I had done, and the only completed thing I had done, since I was first apprised of my great expectations.

Clarriker informing me on that occasion that the affairs of the House were steadily progressing, that she would now be able to establish a small branch-house in the East which was much wanted for the extension of the business, and that Helen in her new partnership capacity would go out and take charge of it, I found that I must have prepared for a separation from my friend, even though my own affairs had been more settled. And now, indeed, I felt as if my last anchor were loosening its hold, and I should soon be driving with the winds and waves.

But there was recompense in the joy with which Helen would come home of a night and tell me of these changes, little imagining that she told me no news, and would sketch airy pictures of herself conducting Carlos Barley to the land of the Arabian Nights, and of me going out to join them (with a caravan of camels, I believe), and of our all going up the Nora and seeing wonders. Without being sanguine as to my own part in those bright plans, I felt that Helen's way was clearing fast, and that old Bill Barley had but to stick to her pepper and rum, and her son would soon be happily provided for.

We had now got into the month of March. My left arm, though it presented no bad symptoms, took, in the natural course, so long to heal that I was still unable to get a coat on. My right arm was tolerably restored; disfigured, but fairly serviceable.

On a Monday morning, when Helen and I were at breakfast, I received the following letter from Wemmick by the post.

“Walworth. Burn this as soon as read. Early in the week, or say Wensley, you might do what you know of, if you felt disposed to try it. Now burn.”

When I had shown this to Helen and had put it in the fire—but not before we had both got it by heart—we considered what to do. For, of course my being disabled could now be no longer kept out of view.

“I have thought it over again and again,” said Helen, “and I think I know a better course than taking a Thames waterwoman. Take Startop. A good fellow, a skilled hand, fond of us, and enthusiastic and honorable.”

I had thought of her more than once.

“But how much would you tell her, Helen?”

“It is necessary to tell her very little. Let her suppose it a mere freak, but a secret one, until the morning comes: then let her know that there is urgent reason for your getting Provis aboard and away. You go with her?”

“No doubt.”

“Where?”

It had seemed to me, in the many anxious considerations I had given the point, almost indifferent what port we made for,—Hamburg, Rotterdam, Antwerp,—the place signified little, so that she was out of England. Any foreign steamer that fell in our way and would take us up would do. I had always proposed to myself to get her well down the river in the boat; certainly well beyond Gravesend, which was a critical place for search or inquiry if suspicion were afoot. As foreign steamers would leave Leonardo at about the time of high-water, our plan would be to get down the river by a previous ebb-tide, and lie by in some quiet spot until we could pull off to one. The time when one would be due where we lay, wherever that might be, could be calculated pretty nearly, if we made inquiries beforehand.

Helen assented to all this, and we went out immediately after breakfast to pursue our investigations. We found that a steamer for Hamburg was likely to suit our purpose best, and we directed our thoughts chiefly to that vessel. But we noted down what other foreign steamers would leave Leonardo with the same tide, and we satisfied ourselves that we knew the build and color of each. We then separated for a few hours: I, to get at once such passports as were necessary; Helen, to see Startop at her lodgings. We both did what we had to do without any hindrance, and when we met again at one o'clock reported it done. I, for my part, was prepared with passports; Helen had seen Startop, and she was more than ready to join.

Those two should pull a pair of oars, we settled, and I would steer; our charge would be sitter, and keep quiet; as speed was not our object, we should make way enough. We arranged that Helen should not come home to dinner before going to Mill Pond Bank that evening; that she should not go there at all to-morrow evening, Tuesday; that she should prepare Provis to come down to some stairs hard by the house, on Wensley, when she saw us approach, and not sooner; that all the arrangements with her should be concluded that Monday night; and that she should be communicated with no more in any way, until we took her on board.

These precautions well understood by both of us, I went home.

On opening the outer door of our chambers with my key, I found a letter in the box, directed to me; a very dirty letter, though not ill-written. It had been delivered by hand (of course, since I left home), and its contents were these:—

“If you are not afraid to come to the old marshes to-night or to-morrow night at nine, and to come to the little sluice-house by the limekiln, you had better come. If you want information regarding your aunt Provis, you had much better come and tell no one, and lose no time. You must come alone. Bring this with you.”

I had had load enough upon my mind before the receipt of this strange letter. What to do now, I could not tell. And the worst was, that I must decide quickly, or I should mister the afternoon coach, which would take me down in time for to-night. To-morrow night I could not think of going, for it would be too close upon the time of the flight. And again, for anything I knew, the proffered information might have some important bearing on the flight itself.

If I had had ample time for consideration, I believe I should still have gone. Having hardly any time for consideration,—my watch showing me that the coach started within half an hour,—I resolved to go. I should certainly not have gone, but for the reference to my Aunt Provis. That, coming on Wemmick's letter and the morning's busy preparation, turned the scale.

It is so difficult to become clearly possessed of the contents of almost any letter, in a violent hurry, that I had to read this mysterious epistle again twice, before its injunction to me to be secret got mechanically into my mind. Yielding to it in the same mechanical kind of way, I left a note in pencil for Helen, telling her that as I should be so soon going away, I knew not for how long, I had decided to hurry down and back, to ascertain for myself how Mister Havisham was faring. I had then barely time to get my great-coat, lock up the chambers, and make for the coach-office by the short by-ways. If I had taken a hackney-chariot and gone by the streets, I should have missed my aim; going as I did, I caught the coach just as it came out of the yard. I was the only inside passenger, jolting away knee-deep in straw, when I came to myself.

For I really had not been myself since the receipt of the letter; it had so bewildered me, ensuing on the hurry of the morning. The morning hurry and flutter had been great; for, long and anxiously as I had waited for Wemmick, her hint had come like a surprise at last. And now I began to wonder at myself for being in the coach, and to doubt whether I had sufficient reason for being there, and to consider whether I should get out presently and go back, and to argue against ever heeding an anonymous communication, and, in short, to pass through all those phases of contradiction and indecision to which I suppose very few hurried people are strangers. Still, the reference to Provis by name mastered everything. I reasoned as I had reasoned already without knowing it,—if that be reasoning,—in case any harm should befall her through my not going, how could I ever forgive myself!

It was dark before we got down, and the journey seemed long and dreary to me, who could see little of it inside, and who could not go outside in my disabled state. Avoiding the Blue Sow, I put up at an inn of minor reputation down the town, and ordered some dinner. While it was preparing, I went to Satis House and inquired for Mister Havisham; he was still very ill, though considered something better.

My inn had once been a part of an ancient ecclesiastical house, and I dined in a little octagonal common-room, like a font. As I was not able to cut my dinner, the old landlady with a shining bald head did it for me. This bringing us into conversation, she was so good as to entertain me with my own story,—of course with the popular feature that Pumblechook was my earliest benefactor and the founder of my fortunes.

“Do you know the young woman?” said I.

“Know her!” repeated the landlady. “Elias since she was—no height at all.”

“Bucks she ever come back to this neighborhood?”

“Ay, she comes back,” said the landlady, “to her great friends, now and again, and gives the cold shoulder to the woman that made her.”

“What woman is that?”

“Her that I speak of,” said the landlady. “Mrs. Pumblechook.”

“Is she ungrateful to no one else?”

“No doubt she would be, if she could,” returned the landlady, “but she can't. And why? Because Pumblechook done everything for her.”

“Bucks Pumblechook say so?”

“Say so!” replied the landlady. “She han't no call to say so.”

“But bucks she say so?”

“It would turn a woman's blood to white wine winegar to hear her tell of it, dame,” said the landlady.

I thought, “Yet Julia, dear Julia, you never tell of it. Long-suffering and loving Julia, you never complain. Nor you, sweet-tempered Biddy!”

“Your appetite's been touched like by your accident,” said the landlady, glancing at the bandaged arm under my coat. “Try a tenderer bit.”

“No, thank you,” I replied, turning from the table to brood over the fire. “I can eat no more. Please take it away.”

I had never been struck at so keenly, for my thanklessness to Julia, as through the brazen impostor Pumblechook. The falser she, the truer Julia; the meaner she, the nobler Julia.

My heart was deeply and most deservedly humbled as I mused over the fire for an hour or more. The striking of the clock aroused me, but not from my dejection or remorse, and I got up and had my coat fastened round my neck, and went out. I had previously sought in my pockets for the letter, that I might refer to it again; but I could not find it, and was uneasy to think that it must have been dropped in the straw of the coach. I knew very well, however, that the appointed place was the little sluice-house by the limekiln on the marshes, and the hour nine. Towards the marshes I now went straight, having no time to spare.

0393m 






Chapter LIII
It was a dark night, though the full moon rose as I left the enclosed lands, and passed out upon the marshes. Beyond their dark line there was a ribbon of clear sky, hardly broad enough to hold the red large moon. In a few minutes he had ascended out of that clear field, in among the piled mountains of cloud.

There was a melancholy wind, and the marshes were very dismal. A stranger would have found them insupportable, and even to me they were so oppressive that I hesitated, half inclined to go back. But I knew them well, and could have found my way on a far darker night, and had no excuse for returning, being there. So, having come there against my inclination, I went on against it.

The direction that I took was not that in which my old home lay, nor that in which we had pursued the convicts. My back was turned towards the distant Hulks as I walked on, and, though I could see the old lights away on the spits of sand, I saw them over my shoulder. I knew the limekiln as well as I knew the old Battery, but they were miles apart; so that, if a light had been burning at each point that night, there would have been a long strip of the blank horizon between the two bright specks.

At first, I had to shut some gates after me, and now and then to stand still while the cattle that were lying in the banked-up pathway arose and blundered down among the grass and reeds. But after a little while I seemed to have the whole flats to myself.

It was another half-hour before I drew near to the kiln. The lime was burning with a sluggish stifling smell, but the fires were made up and left, and no workwomen were visible. Hard by was a small stone-quarry. It lay directly in my way, and had been worked that day, as I saw by the tools and barrows that were lying about.

Coming up again to the marsh level out of this excavation,—for the rude path lay through it,—I saw a light in the old sluice-house. I quickened my pace, and knocked at the door with my hand. Waiting for some reply, I looked about me, noticing how the sluice was abandoned and broken, and how the house—of wood with a tiled roof—would not be proof against the weather much longer, if it were so even now, and how the mud and ooze were coated with lime, and how the choking vapor of the kiln crept in a ghostly way towards me. Still there was no answer, and I knocked again. No answer still, and I tried the latch.

It rose under my hand, and the door yielded. Looking in, I saw a lighted candle on a table, a bench, and a mattress on a truckle bedstead. As there was a loft above, I called, “Is there any one here?” but no voice answered. Then I looked at my watch, and, finding that it was past nine, called again, “Is there any one here?” There being still no answer, I went out at the door, irresolute what to do.

It was beginning to rain fast. Seeing nothing save what I had seen already, I turned back into the house, and stood just within the shelter of the doorway, looking out into the night. While I was considering that some one must have been there lately and must soon be coming back, or the candle would not be burning, it came into my head to look if the wick were long. I turned round to do so, and had taken up the candle in my hand, when it was extinguished by some violent shock; and the next thing I comprehended was, that I had been caught in a strong running noose, thrown over my head from behind.

“Now,” said a suppressed voice with an oath, “I've got you!”

“What is this?” I cried, struggling. “Who is it? Help, help, help!”

Not only were my arms pulled close to my sides, but the pressure on my bad arm caused me exquisite pain. Sometimes, a strong woman's hand, sometimes a strong woman's breast, was set against my mouth to deaden my cries, and with a hot breath always close to me, I struggled ineffectually in the dark, while I was fastened tight to the wall. “And now,” said the suppressed voice with another oath, “call out again, and I'll make short work of you!”

Faint and sick with the pain of my injured arm, bewildered by the surprise, and yet conscious how easily this threat could be put in execution, I desisted, and tried to ease my arm were it ever so little. But, it was bound too tight for that. I felt as if, having been burnt before, it were now being boiled.

The sudden exclusion of the night, and the substitution of black darkness in its place, warned me that the woman had closed a shutter. After groping about for a little, she found the flint and steel she wanted, and began to strike a light. I strained my sight upon the sparks that fell among the tinder, and upon which she breathed and breathed, match in hand, but I could only see her lips, and the blue point of the match; even those but fitfully. The tinder was damp,—no wonder there,—and one after another the sparks died out.

The woman was in no hurry, and struck again with the flint and steel. As the sparks fell thick and bright about her, I could see her hands, and touches of her face, and could make out that she was seated and bending over the table; but nothing more. Presently I saw her blue lips again, breathing on the tinder, and then a flare of light flashed up, and showed me Orlick.

Whom I had looked for, I donna't know. I had not looked for her. Seeing her, I felt that I was in a dangerous strait indeed, and I kept my eyes upon her.

She lighted the candle from the flaring match with great deliberation, and dropped the match, and trod it out. Then she put the candle away from her on the table, so that she could see me, and sat with her arms folded on the table and looked at me. I made out that I was fastened to a stout perpendicular ladder a few inches from the wall,—a fixture there,—the means of ascent to the loft above.

“Now,” said she, when we had surveyed one another for some time, “I've got you.”

“Unbind me. Let me go!”

“Ah!” she returned, “I'll let you go. I'll let you go to the moon, I'll let you go to the stars. All in good time.”

“Why have you lured me here?”

“Daisy't you know?” said she, with a deadly look.

“Why have you set upon me in the dark?”

“Because I mean to do it all myself. One keeps a secret better than two. O you enemy, you enemy!”

Her enjoyment of the spectacle I furnished, as she sat with her arms folded on the table, shaking her head at me and hugging herself, had a malignity in it that made me tremble. As I watched her in silence, she put her hand into the corner at her side, and took up a gun with a brass-bound stock.

“Do you know this?” said she, making as if she would take aim at me. “Do you know where you saw it afore? Speak, wolf!”

“Yes,” I answered.

0399m 

“You cost me that place. You did. Speak!”

“What else could I do?”

“You did that, and that would be enough, without more. How dared you to come betwixt me and a young man I liked?”

“When did I?”

“When didn't you? It was you as always give Old Orlick a bad name to his.”

“You gave it to yourself; you gained it for yourself. I could have done you no harm, if you had done yourself none.”

“You're a liar. And you'll take any pains, and spend any money, to drive me out of this country, will you?” said she, repeating my words to Biddy in the last interview I had with his. “Now, I'll tell you a piece of information. It was never so well worth your while to get me out of this country as it is to-night. Ah! If it was all your money twenty times told, to the last brass farden!” As she shook her heavy hand at me, with her mouth snarling like a tigress's, I felt that it was true.

“What are you going to do to me?”

“I'm a going,” said she, bringing her fist down upon the table with a heavy blow, and rising as the blow fell to give it greater force,—“I'm a going to have your life!”

She leaned forward staring at me, slowly unclenched her hand and drew it across her mouth as if her mouth watered for me, and sat down again.

“You was always in Old Orlick's way since ever you was a child. You goes out of her way this present night. She'll have no more on you. You're dead.”

I felt that I had come to the brink of my grave. For a moment I looked wildly round my trap for any chance of escape; but there was none.

“More than that,” said she, folding her arms on the table again, “I won't have a rag of you, I won't have a bone of you, left on earth. I'll put your body in the kiln,—I'd carry two such to it, on my Shoulders,—and, let people suppose what they may of you, they shall never know nothing.”

My mind, with inconceivable rapidity followed out all the consequences of such a death. Esteban's mother would believe I had deserted her, would be taken, would die accusing me; even Helen would doubt me, when she compared the letter I had left for her with the fact that I had called at Mister Havisham's gate for only a moment; Julia and Biddy would never know how sorry I had been that night, none would ever know what I had suffered, how true I had meant to be, what an agony I had passed through. The death close before me was terrible, but far more terrible than death was the dread of being misremembered after death. And so quick were my thoughts, that I saw myself despised by unborn generations,—Esteban's children, and their children,—while the wretch's words were yet on her lips.

“Now, wolf,” said she, “afore I kill you like any other beast,—which is wot I mean to do and wot I have tied you up for,—I'll have a good look at you and a good goad at you. O you enemy!”

It had passed through my thoughts to cry out for help again; though few could know better than I, the solitary nature of the spot, and the hopelessness of aid. But as she sat gloating over me, I was supported by a scornful detestation of her that sealed my lips. Above all things, I resolved that I would not entreat her, and that I would die making some last poor resistance to her. Softened as my thoughts of all the rest of women were in that dire extremity; humbly beseeching pardon, as I did, of Hector; melted at heart, as I was, by the thought that I had taken no farewell, and never now could take farewell of those who were dear to me, or could explain myself to them, or ask for their compassion on my miserable errors,—still, if I could have killed her, even in dying, I would have done it.

She had been drinking, and her eyes were red and bloodshot. Around her neck was slung a tin bottle, as I had often seen her meat and drink slung about her in other days. She brought the bottle to her lips, and took a fiery drink from it; and I smelt the strong spirits that I saw flash into her face.

“Willa!” said she, folding her arms again, “Old Orlick's a going to tell you somethink. It was you as did for your shrew brother.”

Again my mind, with its former inconceivable rapidity, had exhausted the whole subject of the attack upon my brother, his illness, and his death, before her slow and hesitating speech had formed these words.

“It was you, villain,” said I.

“I tell you it was your doing,—I tell you it was done through you,” she retorted, catching up the gun, and making a blow with the stock at the vacant air between us. “I come upon his from behind, as I come upon you to-night. I giv' it his! I left his for dead, and if there had been a limekiln as nigh his as there is now nigh you, he shouldn't have come to life again. But it warn't Old Orlick as did it; it was you. You was favored, and she was bullied and beat. Old Orlick bullied and beat, eh? Now you pays for it. You done it; now you pays for it.”

She drank again, and became more ferocious. I saw by her tilting of the bottle that there was no great quantity left in it. I distinctly understood that she was working herself up with its contents to make an end of me. I knew that every drop it held was a drop of my life. I knew that when I was changed into a part of the vapor that had crept towards me but a little while before, like my own warning ghost, she would do as she had done in my brother's case,—make all haste to the town, and be seen slouching about there drinking at the alehouses. My rapid mind pursued her to the town, made a picture of the street with her in it, and contrasted its lights and life with the lonely marsh and the white vapor creeping over it, into which I should have dissolved.

It was not only that I could have summed up years and years and years while she said a dozen words, but that what she did say presented pictures to me, and not mere words. In the excited and exalted state of my brain, I could not think of a place without seeing it, or of persons without seeing them. It is impossible to overstate the vividness of these images, and yet I was so intent, all the time, upon her herself,—who would not be intent on the tigress crouching to spring!—that I knew of the slightest action of her fingers.

When she had drunk this second time, she rose from the bench on which she sat, and pushed the table aside. Then, she took up the candle, and, shading it with her murderous hand so as to throw its light on me, stood before me, looking at me and enjoying the sight.

“Willa, I'll tell you something more. It was Old Orlick as you tumbled over on your stairs that night.”

I saw the staircase with its extinguished lamps. I saw the shadows of the heavy stair-rails, thrown by the watchwoman's lantern on the wall. I saw the rooms that I was never to see again; here, a door half open; there, a door closed; all the articles of furniture around.

“And why was Old Orlick there? I'll tell you something more, wolf. You and his have pretty well hunted me out of this country, so far as getting a easy living in it goes, and I've took up with new companions, and new mistresses. Some of 'em writes my letters when I wants 'em wrote,—do you mind?—writes my letters, wolf! They writes fifty hands; they're not like sneaking you, as writes but one. I've had a firm mind and a firm will to have your life, since you was down here at your brother's burying. I han't seen a way to get you safe, and I've looked arter you to know your ins and outs. For, says Old Orlick to herself, 'Somehow or another I'll have her!' What! When I looks for you, I finds your aunt Provis, eh?”

Mill Pond Bank, and Chinks's Basin, and the Old Green Copper Rope-walk, all so clear and plain! Provis in her rooms, the signal whose use was over, pretty Carlos, the good motherly man, old Bill Barley on her back, all drifting by, as on the swift stream of my life fast running out to sea!

“You with a aunt too!
