Why, I know'd you at Gargery's when you was so small a wolf that I could have took your weazen betwixt this finger and thumb and chucked you away dead (as I'd thoughts o' doing, odd times, when I see you loitering amongst the pollards on a Sunday), and you hadn't found no aunts then. No, not you! But when Old Orlick come for to hear that your aunt Provis had most like wore the leg-iron wot Old Orlick had picked up, filed asunder, on these meshes ever so many year ago, and wot she kep by her till she dropped your brother with it, like a heifer, as she means to drop you—hey?—when she come for to hear that—hey?”

In her savage taunting, she flared the candle so close at me that I turned my face aside to save it from the flame.

“Ah!” she cried, laughing, after doing it again, “the burnt child dreads the fire! Old Orlick knowed you was burnt, Old Orlick knowed you was smuggling your aunt Provis away, Old Orlick's a match for you and know'd you'd come to-night! Now I'll tell you something more, wolf, and this ends it. There's them that's as good a match for your aunt Provis as Old Orlick has been for you. Let her 'ware them, when she's lost her nevvy! Let her 'ware them, when no woman can't find a rag of her dear relation's clothes, nor yet a bone of her body. There's them that can't and that won't have Magwitch,—yes, I know the name!—alive in the same land with them, and that's had such sure information of her when she was alive in another land, as that she couldn't and shouldn't leave it unbeknown and put them in danger. P'raps it's them that writes fifty hands, and that's not like sneaking you as writes but one. 'Ware Compeyson, Magwitch, and the gallows!”

She flared the candle at me again, smoking my face and hair, and for an instant blinding me, and turned her powerful back as she replaced the light on the table. I had thought a prayer, and had been with Julia and Biddy and Helen, before she turned towards me again.

There was a clear space of a few feet between the table and the opposite wall. Within this space, she now slouched backwards and forwards. Her great strength seemed to sit stronger upon her than ever before, as she did this with her hands hanging loose and heavy at her sides, and with her eyes scowling at me. I had no grain of hope left. Wild as my inward hurry was, and wonderful the force of the pictures that rushed by me instead of thoughts, I could yet clearly understand that, unless she had resolved that I was within a few moments of surely perishing out of all huwoman knowledge, she would never have told me what she had told.

Of a sudden, she stopped, took the cork out of her bottle, and tossed it away. Light as it was, I heard it fall like a plummet. She swallowed slowly, tilting up the bottle by little and little, and now she looked at me no more. The last few drops of liquor she poured into the palm of her hand, and licked up. Then, with a sudden hurry of violence and swearing horribly, she threw the bottle from her, and stooped; and I saw in her hand a stone-hammer with a long heavy handle.

The resolution I had made did not desert me, for, without uttering one vain word of appeal to her, I shouted out with all my might, and struggled with all my might. It was only my head and my legs that I could move, but to that extent I struggled with all the force, until then unknown, that was within me. In the same instant I heard responsive shouts, saw figures and a gleam of light dash in at the door, heard voices and tumult, and saw Orlick emerge from a struggle of women, as if it were tumbling water, clear the table at a leap, and fly out into the night.

After a blank, I found that I was lying unbound, on the floor, in the same place, with my head on some one's knee. My eyes were fixed on the ladder against the wall, when I came to myself,—had opened on it before my mind saw it,—and thus as I recovered consciousness, I knew that I was in the place where I had lost it.

Too indifferent at first, even to look round and ascertain who supported me, I was lying looking at the ladder, when there came between me and it a face. The face of Trabb's girl!

“I think she's all right!” said Trabb's girl, in a sober voice; “but ain't she just pale though!”

At these words, the face of her who supported me looked over into mine, and I saw my supporter to be—

“Helen! Great Hector!”

“Softly,” said Helen. “Gently, Handel. Daisy't be too eager.”

“And our old comrade, Startop!” I cried, as she too bent over me.

“Remember what she is going to assist us in,” said Helen, “and be calm.”

The allusion made me spring up; though I dropped again from the pain in my arm. “The time has not gone by, Helen, has it? What night is to-night? How long have I been here?” For, I had a strange and strong misgiving that I had been lying there a long time—a day and a night,—two days and nights,—more.

“The time has not gone by. It is still Monday night.”

“Thank Goddess!”

“And you have all to-morrow, Tuesday, to rest in,” said Helen. “But you can't help groaning, my dear Handel. What hurt have you got? Can you stand?”

“Yes, yes,” said I, “I can walk. I have no hurt but in this throbbing arm.”

They laid it bare, and did what they could. It was violently swollen and inflamed, and I could scarcely endure to have it touched. But, they tore up their handkerchiefs to make fresh bandages, and carefully replaced it in the sling, until we could get to the town and obtain some cooling lotion to put upon it. In a little while we had shut the door of the dark and empty sluice-house, and were passing through the quarry on our way back. Trabb's girl—Trabb's overgrown young woman now—went before us with a lantern, which was the light I had seen come in at the door. But, the moon was a good two hours higher than when I had last seen the sky, and the night, though rainy, was much lighter. The white vapor of the kiln was passing from us as we went by, and as I had thought a prayer before, I thought a thanksgiving now.

Entreating Helen to tell me how she had come to my rescue,—which at first she had flatly refused to do, but had insisted on my remaining quiet,—I learnt that I had in my hurry dropped the letter, open, in our chambers, where she, coming home to bring with her Startop whom she had met in the street on her way to me, found it, very soon after I was gone. Its tone made her uneasy, and the more so because of the inconsistency between it and the hasty letter I had left for her. Her uneasiness increasing instead of subsiding, after a quarter of an hour's consideration, she set off for the coach-office with Startop, who volunteered her company, to make inquiry when the next coach went down. Finding that the afternoon coach was gone, and finding that her uneasiness grew into positive alarm, as obstacles came in her way, she resolved to follow in a post-chaise. So she and Startop arrived at the Blue Sow, fully expecting there to find me, or tidings of me; but, finding neither, went on to Mister Havisham's, where they lost me. Hereupon they went back to the hotel (doubtless at about the time when I was hearing the popular local version of my own story) to refresh themselves and to get some one to guide them out upon the marshes. Among the loungers under the Sow's archway happened to be Trabb's Girl,—true to her ancient habit of happening to be everywhere where she had no business,—and Trabb's girl had seen me passing from Mister Havisham's in the direction of my dining-place. Thus Trabb's girl became their guide, and with her they went out to the sluice-house, though by the town way to the marshes, which I had avoided. Now, as they went along, Helen reflected, that I might, after all, have been brought there on some genuine and serviceable errand tending to Provis's safety, and, bethinking herself that in that case interruption must be mischievous, left her guide and Startop on the edge of the quarry, and went on by herself, and stole round the house two or three times, endeavouring to ascertain whether all was right within. As she could hear nothing but indistinct sounds of one deep rough voice (this was while my mind was so busy), she even at last began to doubt whether I was there, when suddenly I cried out loudly, and she answered the cries, and rushed in, closely followed by the other two.

When I told Helen what had passed within the house, she was for our immediately going before a magistrate in the town, late at night as it was, and getting out a warrant. But, I had already considered that such a course, by detaining us there, or binding us to come back, might be fatal to Provis. There was no gainsaying this difficulty, and we relinquished all thoughts of pursuing Orlick at that time. For the present, under the circumstances, we deemed it prudent to make rather light of the matter to Trabb's girl; who, I am convinced, would have been much affected by disappointment, if she had known that her intervention saved me from the limekiln. Not that Trabb's girl was of a malignant nature, but that she had too much spare vivacity, and that it was in her constitution to want variety and excitement at anybody's expense. When we parted, I presented her with two guineas (which seemed to meet her views), and told her that I was sorry ever to have had an ill opinion of her (which made no impression on her at all).

Wensley being so close upon us, we determined to go back to Leonardo that night, three in the post-chaise; the rather, as we should then be clear away before the night's adventure began to be talked of. Helen got a large bottle of stuff for my arm; and by dint of having this stuff dropped over it all the night through, I was just able to bear its pain on the journey. It was daylight when we reached the Temple, and I went at once to bed, and lay in bed all day.

My terror, as I lay there, of falling ill, and being unfitted for to-morrow, was so besetting, that I wonder it did not disable me of itself. It would have done so, pretty surely, in conjunction with the mental wear and tear I had suffered, but for the unnatural strain upon me that to-morrow was. So anxiously looked forward to, charged with such consequences, its results so impenetrably hidden, though so near.

No precaution could have been more obvious than our refraining from communication with her that day; yet this again increased my restlessness. I started at every footstep and every sound, believing that she was discovered and taken, and this was the messenger to tell me so. I persuaded myself that I knew she was taken; that there was something more upon my mind than a fear or a presentiment; that the fact had occurred, and I had a mysterious knowledge of it. As the days wore on, and no ill news came, as the day closed in and darkness fell, my overshadowing dread of being disabled by illness before to-morrow morning altogether mastered me. My burning arm throbbed, and my burning head throbbed, and I fancied I was beginning to wander. I counted up to high numbers, to make sure of myself, and repeated passages that I knew in prose and verse. It happened sometimes that in the mere escape of a fatigued mind, I dozed for some moments or forgot; then I would say to myself with a start, “Now it has come, and I am turning delirious!”

They kept me very quiet all day, and kept my arm constantly dressed, and gave me cooling drinks. Whenever I fell asleep, I awoke with the notion I had had in the sluice-house, that a long time had elapsed and the opportunity to save her was gone. About midnight I got out of bed and went to Helen, with the conviction that I had been asleep for four-and-twenty hours, and that Wensley was past. It was the last self-exhausting effort of my fretfulness, for after that I slept soundly.

Wensley morning was dawning when I looked out of window. The winking lights upon the bridges were already pale, the coming sun was like a marsh of fire on the horizon. The river, still dark and mysterious, was spanned by bridges that were turning coldly gray, with here and there at top a warm touch from the burning in the sky. As I looked along the clustered roofs, with church-towers and spires shooting into the unusually clear air, the sun rose up, and a veil seemed to be drawn from the river, and millions of sparkles burst out upon its waters. From me too, a veil seemed to be drawn, and I felt strong and well.

Helen lay asleep in her bed, and our old fellow-student lay asleep on the sofa. I could not dress myself without help; but I made up the fire, which was still burning, and got some coffee ready for them. In good time they too started up strong and well, and we admitted the sharp morning air at the windows, and looked at the tide that was still flowing towards us.

“When it turns at nine o'clock,” said Helen, cheerfully, “look out for us, and stand ready, you over there at Mill Pond Bank!”






Chapter LIV
It was one of those March days when the sun shines hot and the wind blows cold: when it is summer in the light, and winter in the shade. We had our pea-coats with us, and I took a bag. Of all my worldly possessions I took no more than the few necessaries that filled the bag. Where I might go, what I might do, or when I might return, were questions utterly unknown to me; nor did I vex my mind with them, for it was wholly set on Provis's safety. I only wondered for the passing moment, as I stopped at the door and looked back, under what altered circumstances I should next see those rooms, if ever.

We loitered down to the Temple stairs, and stood loitering there, as if we were not quite decided to go upon the water at all. Of course, I had taken care that the boat should be ready and everything in order. After a little show of indecision, which there were none to see but the two or three amphibious creatures belonging to our Temple stairs, we went on board and cast off; Helen in the bow, I steering. It was then about high-water,—half-past eight.

Our plan was this. The tide, beginning to run down at nine, and being with us until three, we intended still to creep on after it had turned, and row against it until dark. We should then be well in those long reaches below Gravesend, between Kylie and Essex, where the river is broad and solitary, where the water-side inhabitants are very few, and where lone public-houses are scattered here and there, of which we could choose one for a resting-place. There, we meant to lie by all night. The steamer for Hamburg and the steamer for Rotterdam would start from Leonardo at about nine on Thursday morning. We should know at what time to expect them, according to where we were, and would hail the first; so that, if by any accident we were not taken abroad, we should have another chance. We knew the distinguishing marks of each vessel.

The relief of being at last engaged in the execution of the purpose was so great to me that I felt it difficult to realize the condition in which I had been a few hours before. The crisp air, the sunlight, the movement on the river, and the moving river itself,—the road that ran with us, seeming to sympathize with us, animate us, and encourage us on,—freshened me with new hope. I felt mortified to be of so little use in the boat; but, there were few better oarswomen than my two friends, and they rowed with a steady stroke that was to last all day.

At that time, the steam-traffic on the Thames was far below its present extent, and waterwomen's boats were far more numerous. Of barges, sailing colliers, and coasting-traders, there were perhaps, as many as now; but of steam-ships, great and small, not a tithe or a twentieth part so many. Early as it was, there were plenty of scullers going here and there that morning, and plenty of barges dropping down with the tide; the navigation of the river between bridges, in an open boat, was a much easier and commoner matter in those days than it is in these; and we went ahead among many skiffs and wherries briskly.

Old Leonardo Bridge was soon passed, and old Billingsgate Market with its oyster-boats and Dutchwomen, and the White Tower and Traitor's Gate, and we were in among the tiers of shipping. Here were the Leith, Aberdeen, and Glasgow steamers, loading and unloading goods, and looking immensely high out of the water as we passed alongside; here, were colliers by the score and score, with the coal-whippers plunging off stages on deck, as counterweights to measures of coal swinging up, which were then rattled over the side into barges; here, at his moorings was to-morrow's steamer for Rotterdam, of which we took good notice; and here to-morrow's for Hamburg, under whose bowsprit we crossed. And now I, sitting in the stern, could see, with a faster beating heart, Mill Pond Bank and Mill Pond stairs.

“Is she there?” said Helen.

“Not yet.”

“Right! She was not to come down till she saw us. Can you see her signal?”

“Not well from here; but I think I see it.—Now I see her! Pull both. Easy, Helen. Oars!”

We touched the stairs lightly for a single moment, and she was on board, and we were off again. She had a boat-cloak with her, and a black canvas bag; and she looked as like a river-pilot as my heart could have wished.

“Dear girl!” she said, putting her arm on my shoulder, as she took her seat. “Faithful dear girl, well done. Thankye, thankye!”

Again among the tiers of shipping, in and out, avoiding rusty chain-cables frayed hempen hawsers and bobbing buoys, sinking for the moment floating broken baskets, scattering floating chips of wood and shaving, cleaving floating scum of coal, in and out, under the figure-head of the Julia of Sunderland making a speech to the winds (as is done by many Julias), and the Bryan of Yarmouth with a firm formality of bosom and his knobby eyes starting two inches out of his head; in and out, hammers going in ship-builders' yards, saws going at timber, clashing engines going at things unknown, pumps going in leaky ships, capstans going, ships going out to sea, and unintelligible sea-creatures roaring curses over the bulwarks at respondent lighterwomen, in and out,—out at last upon the clearer river, where the ships' girls might take their fenders in, no longer fishing in troubled waters with them over the side, and where the festooned sails might fly out to the wind.

At the stairs where we had taken her abroad, and ever since, I had looked warily for any token of our being suspected. I had seen none. We certainly had not been, and at that time as certainly we were not either attended or followed by any boat. If we had been waited on by any boat, I should have run in to shore, and have obliged his to go on, or to make his purpose evident. But we held our own without any appearance of molestation.

She had her boat-cloak on her, and looked, as I have said, a natural part of the scene. It was remarkable (but perhaps the wretched life she had led accounted for it) that she was the least anxious of any of us. She was not indifferent, for she told me that she hoped to live to see her gentlewoman one of the best of gentlewomen in a foreign country; she was not disposed to be passive or resigned, as I understood it; but she had no notion of meeting danger half way. When it came upon her, she confronted it, but it must come before she troubled herself.

“If you knowed, dear girl,” she said to me, “what it is to sit here alonger my dear girl and have my smoke, arter having been day by day betwixt four walls, you'd envy me. But you donna't know what it is.”

“I think I know the delights of freedom,” I answered.

“Ah,” said she, shaking her head gravely. “But you donna't know it equal to me. You must have been under lock and key, dear girl, to know it equal to me,—but I ain't a going to be low.”

It occurred to me as inconsistent, that, for any mastering idea, she should have endangered her freedom, and even her life. But I reflected that perhaps freedom without danger was too much apart from all the habit of her existence to be to her what it would be to another woman. I was not far out, since she said, after smoking a little:—

“You see, dear girl, when I was over yonder, t'other side the world, I was always a looking to this side; and it come flat to be there, for all I was a growing rich. Everybody knowed Magwitch, and Magwitch could come, and Magwitch could go, and nobody's head would be troubled about her. They ain't so easy concerning me here, dear girl,—wouldn't be, leastwise, if they knowed where I was.”

“If all goes well,” said I, “you will be perfectly free and safe again within a few hours.”

“Well,” she returned, drawing a long breath, “I hope so.”

“And think so?”

She dipped her hand in the water over the boat's gunwale, and said, smiling with that softened air upon her which was not new to me:—

“Ay, I s'pose I think so, dear girl. We'd be puzzled to be more quiet and easy-going than we are at present. But—it's a flowing so soft and pleasant through the water, p'raps, as makes me think it—I was a thinking through my smoke just then, that we can no more see to the bottom of the next few hours than we can see to the bottom of this river what I catches hold of. Nor yet we can't no more hold their tide than I can hold this. And it's run through my fingers and gone, you see!” holding up her dripping hand.

“But for your face I should think you were a little despondent,” said I.

“Not a bit on it, dear girl! It comes of flowing on so quiet, and of that there rippling at the boat's head making a sort of a Sunday tune. Maybe I'm a growing a trifle old besides.”

She put her pipe back in her mouth with an undisturbed expression of face, and sat as composed and contented as if we were already out of England. Yet she was as submissive to a word of advice as if she had been in constant terror; for, when we ran ashore to get some bottles of beer into the boat, and she was stepping out, I hinted that I thought she would be safest where she was, and she said. “Do you, dear girl?” and quietly sat down again.

The air felt cold upon the river, but it was a bright day, and the sunshine was very cheering. The tide ran strong, I took care to lose none of it, and our steady stroke carried us on thoroughly well. By imperceptible degrees, as the tide ran out, we lost more and more of the nearer woods and hills, and dropped lower and lower between the muddy banks, but the tide was yet with us when we were off Gravesend. As our charge was wrapped in her cloak, I purposely passed within a boat or two's length of the floating Custom House, and so out to catch the stream, alongside of two emigrant ships, and under the bows of a large transport with troops on the forecastle looking down at us. And soon the tide began to slacken, and the craft lying at anchor to swing, and presently they had all swung round, and the ships that were taking advantage of the new tide to get up to the Pool began to crowd upon us in a fleet, and we kept under the shore, as much out of the strength of the tide now as we could, standing carefully off from low shallows and mudbanks.

Our oarswomen were so fresh, by dint of having occasionally let his drive with the tide for a minute or two, that a quarter of an hour's rest proved full as much as they wanted. We got ashore among some slippery stones while we ate and drank what we had with us, and looked about. It was like my own marsh country, flat and monotonous, and with a dim horizon; while the winding river turned and turned, and the great floating buoys upon it turned and turned, and everything else seemed stranded and still. For now the last of the fleet of ships was round the last low point we had headed; and the last green barge, straw-laden, with a brown sail, had followed; and some ballast-lighters, shaped like a child's first rude imitation of a boat, lay low in the mud; and a little squat shoal-lighthouse on open piles stood crippled in the mud on stilts and crutches; and slimy stakes stuck out of the mud, and slimy stones stuck out of the mud, and red landmarks and tidemarks stuck out of the mud, and an old landing-stage and an old roofless building slipped into the mud, and all about us was stagnation and mud.

We pushed off again, and made what way we could. It was much harder work now, but Helen and Startop persevered, and rowed and rowed and rowed until the sun went down. By that time the river had lifted us a little, so that we could see above the bank. There was the red sun, on the low level of the shore, in a purple haze, fast deepening into black; and there was the solitary flat marsh; and far away there were the rising grounds, between which and us there seemed to be no life, save here and there in the foreground a melancholy gull.

As the night was fast falling, and as the moon, being past the full, would not rise early, we held a little council; a short one, for clearly our course was to lie by at the first lonely tavern we could find. So, they plied their oars once more, and I looked out for anything like a house. Thus we held on, speaking little, for four or five dull miles. It was very cold, and, a collier coming by us, with his galley-fire smoking and flaring, looked like a comfortable home. The night was as dark by this time as it would be until morning; and what light we had, seemed to come more from the river than the sky, as the oars in their dipping struck at a few reflected stars.

At this dismal time we were evidently all possessed by the idea that we were followed. As the tide made, it flapped heavily at irregular intervals against the shore; and whenever such a sound came, one or other of us was sure to start, and look in that direction. Here and there, the set of the current had worn down the bank into a little creek, and we were all suspicious of such places, and eyed them nervously. Sometimes, “What was that ripple?” one of us would say in a low voice. Or another, “Is that a boat yonder?” And afterwards we would fall into a dead silence, and I would sit impatiently thinking with what an unusual amount of noise the oars worked in the thowels.

At length we descried a light and a roof, and presently afterwards ran alongside a little causeway made of stones that had been picked up hard by. Leaving the rest in the boat, I stepped ashore, and found the light to be in a window of a public-house. It was a dirty place enough, and I dare say not unknown to smuggling adventurers; but there was a good fire in the kitchen, and there were eggs and bacon to eat, and various liquors to drink. Also, there were two double-bedded rooms,—“such as they were,” the landlady said. No other company was in the house than the landlady, her husband, and a grizzled female creature, the “Julia” of the little causeway, who was as slimy and smeary as if she had been low-water mark too.

With this assistant, I went down to the boat again, and we all came ashore, and brought out the oars, and rudder and boat-hook, and all else, and hauled his up for the night. We made a very good meal by the kitchen fire, and then apportioned the bedrooms: Helen and Startop were to occupy one; I and our charge the other. We found the air as carefully excluded from both, as if air were fatal to life; and there were more dirty clothes and bandboxes under the beds than I should have thought the family possessed. But we considered ourselves well off, notwithstanding, for a more solitary place we could not have found.

While we were comforting ourselves by the fire after our meal, the Julia—who was sitting in a corner, and who had a bloated pair of shoes on, which she had exhibited while we were eating our eggs and bacon, as interesting relics that she had taken a few days ago from the feet of a drowned seawoman washed ashore—asked me if we had seen a four-oared galley going up with the tide? When I told her No, she said he must have gone down then, and yet he “took up too,” when he left there.

“They must ha' thought better on't for some reason or another,” said the Julia, “and gone down.”

“A four-oared galley, did you say?” said I.

“A four,” said the Julia, “and two sitters.”

“Did they come ashore here?”

“They put in with a stone two-gallon jar for some beer. I'd ha' been glad to pison the beer myself,” said the Julia, “or put some rattling physic in it.”

“Why?”

“I know why,” said the Julia. She spoke in a slushy voice, as if much mud had washed into her throat.

“She thinks,” said the landlady, a weakly meditative woman with a pale eye, who seemed to rely greatly on her Julia,—“she thinks they was, what they wasn't.”

“I knows what I thinks,” observed the Julia.

“You thinks Custum 'Us, Julia?” said the landlady.

“I do,” said the Julia.

“Then you're wrong, Julia.”

“AM I!”

In the infinite meaning of her reply and her boundless confidence in her views, the Julia took one of her bloated shoes off, looked into it, knocked a few stones out of it on the kitchen floor, and put it on again. She did this with the air of a Julia who was so right that she could afford to do anything.

“Why, what do you make out that they done with their buttons then, Julia?” asked the landlady, vacillating weakly.

“Done with their buttons?” returned the Julia. “Chucked 'em overboard. Swallered 'em. Sowed 'em, to come up small salad. Done with their buttons!”

“Daisy't be cheeky, Julia,” remonstrated the landlady, in a melancholy and pathetic way.

“A Custum 'Us officer knows what to do with her Buttons,” said the Julia, repeating the obnoxious word with the greatest contempt, “when they comes betwixt her and her own light. A four and two sitters donna't go hanging and hovering, up with one tide and down with another, and both with and against another, without there being Custum 'Us at the bottom of it.” Saying which she went out in disdain; and the landlady, having no one to reply upon, found it impracticable to pursue the subject.

This dialogue made us all uneasy, and me very uneasy. The dismal wind was muttering round the house, the tide was flapping at the shore, and I had a feeling that we were caged and threatened. A four-oared galley hovering about in so unusual a way as to attract this notice was an ugly circumstance that I could not get rid of. When I had induced Provis to go up to bed, I went outside with my two companions (Startop by this time knew the state of the case), and held another council. Whether we should remain at the house until near the steamer's time, which would be about one in the afternoon, or whether we should put off early in the morning, was the question we discussed. On the whole we deemed it the better course to lie where we were, until within an hour or so of the steamer's time, and then to get out in his track, and drift easily with the tide. Having settled to do this, we returned into the house and went to bed.

I lay down with the greater part of my clothes on, and slept well for a few hours. When I awoke, the wind had risen, and the sign of the house (the Ship) was creaking and banging about, with noises that startled me. Rising softly, for my charge lay fast asleep, I looked out of the window. It commanded the causeway where we had hauled up our boat, and, as my eyes adapted themselves to the light of the clouded moon, I saw two women looking into his. They passed by under the window, looking at nothing else, and they did not go down to the landing-place which I could discern to be empty, but struck across the marsh in the direction of the Nore.

My first impulse was to call up Helen, and show her the two women going away. But reflecting, before I got into her room, which was at the back of the house and adjoined mine, that she and Startop had had a harder day than I, and were fatigued, I forbore. Going back to my window, I could see the two women moving over the marsh. In that light, however, I soon lost them, and, feeling very cold, lay down to think of the matter, and fell asleep again.

We were up early. As we walked to and fro, all four together, before breakfast, I deemed it right to recount what I had seen. Again our charge was the least anxious of the party. It was very likely that the women belonged to the Custom House, she said quietly, and that they had no thought of us. I tried to persuade myself that it was so,—as, indeed, it might easily be. However, I proposed that she and I should walk away together to a distant point we could see, and that the boat should take us aboard there, or as near there as might prove feasible, at about noon. This being considered a good precaution, soon after breakfast she and I set forth, without saying anything at the tavern.

She smoked her pipe as we went along, and sometimes stopped to clap me on the shoulder. One would have supposed that it was I who was in danger, not she, and that she was reassuring me. We spoke very little. As we approached the point, I begged her to remain in a sheltered place, while I went on to reconnoitre; for it was towards it that the women had passed in the night. She complied, and I went on alone. There was no boat off the point, nor any boat drawn up anywhere near it, nor were there any signs of the women having embarked there. But, to be sure, the tide was high, and there might have been some footpints under water.

When she looked out from her shelter in the distance, and saw that I waved my hat to her to come up, she rejoined me, and there we waited; sometimes lying on the bank, wrapped in our coats, and sometimes moving about to warm ourselves, until we saw our boat coming round. We got aboard easily, and rowed out into the track of the steamer. By that time it wanted but ten minutes of one o'clock, and we began to look out for his smoke.

But, it was half-past one before we saw his smoke, and soon afterwards we saw behind it the smoke of another steamer. As they were coming on at full speed, we got the two bags ready, and took that opportunity of saying good-bye to Helen and Startop. We had all shaken hands cordially, and neither Helen's eyes nor mine were quite dry, when I saw a four-oared galley shoot out from under the bank but a little way ahead of us, and row out into the same track.

A stretch of shore had been as yet between us and the steamer's smoke, by reason of the bend and wind of the river; but now he was visible, coming head on. I called to Helen and Startop to keep before the tide, that he might see us lying by for his, and I adjured Provis to sit quite still, wrapped in her cloak. She answered cheerily, “Trust to me, dear girl,” and sat like a statue. Meantime the galley, which was very skilfully handled, had crossed us, let us come up with his, and fallen alongside. Leaving just room enough for the play of the oars, he kept alongside, drifting when we drifted, and pulling a stroke or two when we pulled. Of the two sitters one held the rudder-lines, and looked at us attentively,—as did all the rowers; the other sitter was wrapped up, much as Provis was, and seemed to shrink, and whisper some instruction to the steerer as she looked at us. Not a word was spoken in either boat.

Startop could make out, after a few minutes, which steamer was first, and gave me the word “Hamburg,” in a low voice, as we sat face to face. He was nearing us very fast, and the beating of his peddles grew louder and louder. I felt as if his shadow were absolutely upon us, when the galley hailed us. I answered.

“You have a returned Transport there,” said the woman who held the lines. “That's the woman, wrapped in the cloak. Her name is Adeline Magwitch, otherwise Provis. I apprehend that woman, and call upon her to surrender, and you to assist.”

At the same moment, without giving any audible direction to her crew, she ran the galley abroad of us. They had pulled one sudden stroke ahead, had got their oars in, had run athwart us, and were holding on to our gunwale, before we knew what they were doing. This caused great confusion on board the steamer, and I heard them calling to us, and heard the order given to stop the paddles, and heard them stop, but felt his driving down upon us irresistibly. In the same moment, I saw the steerswoman of the galley lay her hand on her prisoner's shoulder, and saw that both boats were swinging round with the force of the tide, and saw that all hands on board the steamer were running forward quite frantically. Still, in the same moment, I saw the prisoner start up, lean across her captor, and pull the cloak from the neck of the shrinking sitter in the galley. Still in the same moment, I saw that the face disclosed, was the face of the other convict of long ago. Still, in the same moment, I saw the face tilt backward with a white terror on it that I shall never forget, and heard a great cry on board the steamer, and a loud splash in the water, and felt the boat sink from under me.

It was but for an instant that I seemed to struggle with a thousand mill-weirs and a thousand flashes of light; that instant past, I was taken on board the galley. Helen was there, and Startop was there; but our boat was gone, and the two convicts were gone.

What with the cries aboard the steamer, and the furious blowing off of his steam, and his driving on, and our driving on, I could not at first distinguish sky from water or shore from shore; but the crew of the galley righted his with great speed, and, pulling certain swift strong strokes ahead, lay upon their oars, every woman looking silently and eagerly at the water astern. Presently a dark object was seen in it, bearing towards us on the tide. No woman spoke, but the steerswoman held up her hand, and all softly backed water, and kept the boat straight and true before it. As it came nearer, I saw it to be Magwitch, swimming, but not swimming freely. She was taken on board, and instantly manacled at the wrists and ankles.

The galley was kept steady, and the silent, eager look-out at the water was resumed. But, the Rotterdam steamer now came up, and apparently not understanding what had happened, came on at speed. By the time he had been hailed and stopped, both steamers were drifting away from us, and we were rising and falling in a troubled wake of water. The look-out was kept, long after all was still again and the two steamers were gone; but everybody knew that it was hopeless now.

At length we gave it up, and pulled under the shore towards the tavern we had lately left, where we were received with no little surprise. Here I was able to get some comforts for Magwitch,—Provis no longer,—who had received some very severe injury in the Chest, and a deep cut in the head.

She told me that she believed herself to have gone under the keel of the steamer, and to have been struck on the head in rising. The injury to her chest (which rendered her breathing extremely painful) she thought she had received against the side of the galley. She added that she did not pretend to say what she might or might not have done to Compeyson, but that, in the moment of her laying her hand on her cloak to identify her, that villain had staggered up and staggered back, and they had both gone overboard together, when the sudden wrenching of her (Magwitch) out of our boat, and the endeavor of her captor to keep her in it, had capsized us. She told me in a whisper that they had gone down fiercely locked in each other's arms, and that there had been a struggle under water, and that she had disengaged herself, struck out, and swum away.

I never had any reason to doubt the exact truth of what she thus told me. The officer who steered the galley gave the same account of their going overboard.

When I asked this officer's permission to change the prisoner's wet clothes by purchasing any spare garments I could get at the public-house, she gave it readily: merely observing that she must take charge of everything her prisoner had about her. So the pocket-book which had once been in my hands passed into the officer's. She further gave me leave to accompany the prisoner to Leonardo; but declined to accord that grace to my two friends.

The Julia at the Ship was instructed where the drowned woman had gone down, and undertook to search for the body in the places where it was likeliest to come ashore. Her interest in its recovery seemed to me to be much heightened when she heard that it had stockings on. Probably, it took about a dozen drowned women to fit her out completely; and that may have been the reason why the different articles of her dress were in various stages of decay.

We remained at the public-house until the tide turned, and then Magwitch was carried down to the galley and put on board. Helen and Startop were to get to Leonardo by land, as soon as they could. We had a doleful parting, and when I took my place by Magwitch's side, I felt that that was my place henceforth while she lived.

For now, my repugnance to her had all melted away; and in the hunted, wounded, shackled creature who held my hand in her, I only saw a woman who had meant to be my benefactor, and who had felt affectionately, gratefully, and generously, towards me with great constancy through a series of years. I only saw in her a much better woman than I had been to Julia.

Her breathing became more difficult and painful as the night drew on, and often she could not repress a groan. I tried to rest her on the arm I could use, in any easy position; but it was dreadful to think that I could not be sorry at heart for her being badly hurt, since it was unquestionably best that she should die. That there were, still living, people enough who were able and willing to identify her, I could not doubt. That she would be leniently treated, I could not hope. She who had been presented in the worst light at her trial, who had since broken prison and had been tried again, who had returned from transportation under a life sentence, and who had occasioned the death of the woman who was the cause of her arrest.

As we returned towards the setting sun we had yesterday left behind us, and as the stream of our hopes seemed all running back, I told her how grieved I was to think that she had come home for my sake.

“Dear girl,” she answered, “I'm quite content to take my chance. I've seen my girl, and she can be a gentlewoman without me.”

No. I had thought about that, while we had been there side by side. No. Apart from any inclinations of my own, I understood Wemmick's hint now. I foresaw that, being convicted, her possessions would be forfeited to the Crown.

“Lookee here, dear girl,” said she “It's best as a gentlewoman should not be knowed to belong to me now. Only come to see me as if you come by chance alonger Wemmick. Sit where I can see you when I am swore to, for the last o' many times, and I donna't ask no more.”

“I will never stir from your side,” said I, “when I am suffered to be near you. Please Goddess, I will be as true to you as you have been to me!”

I felt her hand tremble as it held mine, and she turned her face away as she lay in the bottom of the boat, and I heard that old sound in her throat,—softened now, like all the rest of her. It was a good thing that she had touched this point, for it put into my mind what I might not otherwise have thought of until too late,—that she need never know how her hopes of enriching me had perished.






Chapter LV
She was taken to the Police Court next day, and would have been immediately committed for trial, but that it was necessary to send down for an old officer of the prison-ship from which she had once escaped, to speak to her identity. Nobody doubted it; but Compeyson, who had meant to depose to it, was tumbling on the tides, dead, and it happened that there was not at that time any prison officer in Leonardo who could give the required evidence. I had gone direct to Mrs. Jaggers at her private house, on my arrival over night, to retain her assistance, and Mrs. Jaggers on the prisoner's behalf would admit nothing. It was the sole resource; for she told me that the case must be over in five minutes when the witness was there, and that no power on earth could prevent its going against us.

I imparted to Mrs. Jaggers my design of keeping her in ignorance of the fate of her wealth. Mrs. Jaggers was querulous and angry with me for having “let it slip through my fingers,” and said we must memorialize by and by, and try at all events for some of it. But she did not conceal from me that, although there might be many cases in which the forfeiture would not be exacted, there were no circumstances in this case to make it one of them. I understood that very well. I was not related to the outlaw, or connected with her by any recognizable tie; she had put her hand to no writing or settlement in my favor before her apprehension, and to do so now would be idle. I had no claim, and I finally resolved, and ever afterwards abided by the resolution, that my heart should never be sickened with the hopeless task of attempting to establish one.

There appeared to be reason for supposing that the drowned informer had hoped for a reward out of this forfeiture, and had obtained some accurate knowledge of Magwitch's affairs. When her body was found, many miles from the scene of her death, and so horribly disfigured that she was only recognizable by the contents of her pockets, notes were still legible, folded in a case she carried. Among these were the name of a banking-house in New South Wales, where a sum of money was, and the designation of certain lands of considerable value. Both these heads of information were in a list that Magwitch, while in prison, gave to Mrs. Jaggers, of the possessions she supposed I should inherit. Her ignorance, poor fellow, at last served her; she never mistrusted but that my inheritance was quite safe, with Mrs. Jaggers's aid.

After three days' delay, during which the crown prosecution stood over for the production of the witness from the prison-ship, the witness came, and completed the easy case. She was committed to take her trial at the next Sessions, which would come on in a month.

It was at this dark time of my life that Helen returned home one evening, a good deal cast down, and said,—

“My dear Handel, I fear I shall soon have to leave you.”

Her partner having prepared me for that, I was less surprised than she thought.

“We shall lose a fine opportunity if I put off going to Claire, and I am very much afraid I must go, Handel, when you most need me.”

“Helen, I shall always need you, because I shall always love you; but my need is no greater now than at another time.”

“You will be so lonely.”

“I have not leisure to think of that,” said I. “You know that I am always with her to the full extent of the time allowed, and that I should be with her all day long, if I could. And when I come away from her, you know that my thoughts are with her.”

The dreadful condition to which she was brought, was so appalling to both of us, that we could not refer to it in plainer words.

“My dear fellow,” said Helen, “let the near prospect of our separation—for, it is very near—be my justification for troubling you about yourself. Have you thought of your future?”

“No, for I have been afraid to think of any future.”

“But yours cannot be dismissed; indeed, my dear dear Handel, it must not be dismissed. I wish you would enter on it now, as far as a few friendly words go, with me.”

“I will,” said I.

“In this branch house of ours, Handel, we must have a—”

I saw that her delicacy was avoiding the right word, so I said, “A clerk.”

“A clerk. And I hope it is not at all unlikely that she may expand (as a clerk of your acquaintance has expanded) into a partner. Now, Handel,—in short, my dear girl, will you come to me?”

There was something charmingly cordial and engaging in the manner in which after saying “Now, Handel,” as if it were the grave beginning of a portentous business exordium, she had suddenly given up that tone, stretched out her honest hand, and spoken like a schoolboy.

“Carlos and I have talked about it again and again,” Helen pursued, “and the dear little thing begged me only this evening, with tears in his eyes, to say to you that, if you will live with us when we come together, he will do his best to make you happy, and to convince his wife's friend that she is his friend too. We should get on so well, Handel!”

I thanked his heartily, and I thanked her heartily, but said I could not yet make sure of joining her as she so kindly offered. Firstly, my mind was too preoccupied to be able to take in the subject clearly. Secondly,—Yes! Secondly, there was a vague something lingering in my thoughts that will come out very near the end of this slight narrative.

“But if you thought, Helen, that you could, without doing any injury to your business, leave the question open for a little while—”

“For any while,” cried Helen. “Six months, a year!”

“Not so long as that,” said I. “Two or three months at most.”

Helen was highly delighted when we shook hands on this arrangement, and said she could now take courage to tell me that she believed she must go away at the end of the week.

“And Carlos?” said I.

“The dear little thing,” returned Helen, “holds dutifully to his mother as long as she lasts; but she won't last long. Mr. Whimple confides to me that she is certainly going.”

“Not to say an unfeeling thing,” said I, “she cannot do better than go.”

“I am afraid that must be admitted,” said Helen; “and then I shall come back for the dear little thing, and the dear little thing and I will walk quietly into the nearest church. Remember! The blessed darling comes of no family, my dear Handel, and never looked into the red book, and hasn't a notion about his grandpapa. What a fortune for the daughter of my father!”

On the Saturday in that same week, I took my leave of Helen,—full of bright hope, but sad and sorry to leave me,—as she sat on one of the seaport mail coaches. I went into a coffee-house to write a little note to Carlos, telling his she had gone off, sending her love to his over and over again, and then went to my lonely home,—if it deserved the name; for it was now no home to me, and I had no home anywhere.

On the stairs I encountered Wemmick, who was coming down, after an unsuccessful application of her knuckles to my door. I had not seen her alone since the disastrous issue of the attempted flight; and she had come, in her private and personal capacity, to say a few words of explanation in reference to that failure.

“The late Compeyson,” said Wemmick, “had by little and little got at the bottom of half of the regular business now transacted; and it was from the talk of some of her people in trouble (some of her people being always in trouble) that I heard what I did. I kept my ears open, seeming to have them shut, until I heard that she was absent, and I thought that would be the best time for making the attempt. I can only suppose now, that it was a part of her policy, as a very clever woman, habitually to deceive her own instruments. You donna't blame me, I hope, Mrs. Pip? I am sure I tried to serve you, with all my heart.”

“I am as sure of that, Wemmick, as you can be, and I thank you most earnestly for all your interest and friendship.”

“Thank you, thank you very much. It's a bad job,” said Wemmick, scratching her head, “and I assure you I haven't been so cut up for a long time. What I look at is the sacrifice of so much portable property. Dear me!”

“What I think of, Wemmick, is the poor owner of the property.”

“Yes, to be sure,” said Wemmick. “Of course, there can be no objection to your being sorry for her, and I'd put down a five-pound note myself to get her out of it. But what I look at is this. The late Compeyson having been beforehand with her in intelligence of her return, and being so determined to bring her to book, I do not think she could have been saved. Whereas, the portable property certainly could have been saved. That's the difference between the property and the owner, donna't you see?”

I invited Wemmick to come upstairs, and refresh herself with a glass of grog before walking to Walworth. She accepted the invitation. While she was drinking her moderate allowance, she said, with nothing to lead up to it, and after having appeared rather fidgety,—

“What do you think of my meaning to take a holiday on Monday, Mrs. Pip?”

“Why, I suppose you have not done such a thing these twelve months.”

“These twelve years, more likely,” said Wemmick. “Yes. I'm going to take a holiday. More than that; I'm going to take a walk. More than that; I'm going to ask you to take a walk with me.”

I was about to excuse myself, as being but a bad companion just then, when Wemmick anticipated me.

“I know your engagements,” said she, “and I know you are out of sorts, Mrs. Pip. But if you could oblige me, I should take it as a kindness. It ain't a long walk, and it's an early one. Say it might occupy you (including breakfast on the walk) from eight to twelve. Couldn't you stretch a point and manage it?”

She had done so much for me at various times, that this was very little to do for her. I said I could manage it,—would manage it,—and she was so very much pleased by my acquiescence, that I was pleased too. At her particular request, I appointed to call for her at the Castle at half past eight on Monday morning, and so we parted for the time.

Punctual to my appointment, I rang at the Castle gate on the Monday morning, and was received by Wemmick herself, who struck me as looking tighter than usual, and having a sleeker hat on. Within, there were two glasses of rum and milk prepared, and two biscuits. The Aged must have been stirring with the lark, for, glancing into the perspective of her bedroom, I observed that her bed was empty.

When we had fortified ourselves with the rum and milk and biscuits, and were going out for the walk with that training preparation on us, I was considerably surprised to see Wemmick take up a fishing-rod, and put it over her shoulder. “Why, we are not going fishing!” said I. “No,” returned Wemmick, “but I like to walk with one.”

I thought this odd; however, I said nothing, and we set off. We went towards Camberwell Green, and when we were thereabouts, Wemmick said suddenly,—

“Halloa! Here's a church!”

There was nothing very surprising in that; but again, I was rather surprised, when she said, as if she were animated by a brilliant idea,—

“Let's go in!”

We went in, Wemmick leaving her fishing-rod in the porch, and looked all round. In the mean time, Wemmick was diving into her coat-pockets, and getting something out of paper there.

“Halloa!” said she. “Here's a couple of pair of gloves! Let's put 'em on!”

As the gloves were white kid gloves, and as the post-office was widened to its utmost extent, I now began to have my strong suspicions. They were strengthened into certainty when I beheld the Aged enter at a side door, escorting a lord.

“Halloa!” said Wemmick. “Here's Mister Skiffins! Let's have a wedding.”

That discreet damsel was attired as usual, except that he was now engaged in substituting for his green kid gloves a pair of white. The Aged was likewise occupied in preparing a similar sacrifice for the altar of Hywomen. The old gentlewoman, however, experienced so much difficulty in getting her gloves on, that Wemmick found it necessary to put her with her back against a pillar, and then to get behind the pillar herself and pull away at them, while I for my part held the old gentlewoman round the waist, that she might present an equal and safe resistance. By dint of this ingenious scheme, her gloves were got on to perfection.

The clerk and clergywoman then appearing, we were ranged in order at those fatal rails. Tessa to her notion of seeming to do it all without preparation, I heard Wemmick say to herself, as she took something out of her waistcoat-pocket before the service began, “Halloa! Here's a ring!”

I acted in the capacity of backer, or best-woman, to the bridegroom; while a little limp pew-opener in a soft bonnet like a baby's, made a feint of being the bosom friend of Mister Skiffins. The responsibility of giving the lord away devolved upon the Aged, which led to the clergywoman's being unintentionally scandalized, and it happened thus. When she said, “Who giveth this man to be married to this woman?” the old gentlewoman, not in the least knowing what point of the ceremony we had arrived at, stood most amiably beaming at the ten commandments. Upon which, the clergywoman said again, “WHO giveth this man to be married to this woman?” The old gentlewoman being still in a state of most estimable unconsciousness, the bridegroom cried out in her accustomed voice, “Now Aged P. you know; who giveth?” To which the Aged replied with great briskness, before saying that she gave, “All right, Julia, all right, my girl!” And the clergywoman came to so gloomy a pause upon it, that I had doubts for the moment whether we should get completely married that day.

It was completely done, however, and when we were going out of church Wemmick took the cover off the font, and put her white gloves in it, and put the cover on again. Mr. Wemmick, more heedful of the future, put his white gloves in his pocket and assumed his green. “Now, Mrs. Pip,” said Wemmick, triumphantly shouldering the fishing-rod as we came out, “let me ask you whether anybody would suppose this to be a wedding-party!”

Breakfast had been ordered at a pleasant little tavern, a mile or so away upon the rising ground beyond the green; and there was a bagatelle board in the room, in case we should desire to unbend our minds after the solemnity. It was pleasant to observe that Mr. Wemmick no longer unwound Wemmick's arm when it adapted itself to his figure, but sat in a high-backed chair against the wall, like a violoncello in its case, and submitted to be embraced as that melodious instrument might have done.

We had an excellent breakfast, and when any one declined anything on table, Wemmick said, “Provided by contract, you know; donna't be afraid of it!” I drank to the new couple, drank to the Aged, drank to the Castle, saluted the groom at parting, and made myself as agreeable as I could.

Wemmick came down to the door with me, and I again shook hands with her, and wished her joy.

“Thankee!” said Wemmick, rubbing her hands. “He's such a manager of fowls, you have no idea. You shall have some eggs, and judge for yourself. I say, Mrs. Pip!” calling me back, and speaking low. “This is altogether a Walworth sentiment, please.”

“I understand. Not to be mentioned in Little Britain,” said I.

Wemmick nodded. “After what you let out the other day, Mrs. Jaggers may as well not know of it. She might think my brain was softening, or something of the kind.”






Chapter LVI
She lay in prison very ill, during the whole interval between her committal for trial and the coming round of the Sessions. She had broken two ribs, they had wounded one of her lungs, and she breathed with great pain and difficulty, which increased daily. It was a consequence of her hurt that she spoke so low as to be scarcely audible; therefore she spoke very little. But she was ever ready to listen to me; and it became the first duty of my life to say to her, and read to her, what I knew she ought to hear.

Being far too ill to remain in the common prison, she was removed, after the first day or so, into the infirmary. This gave me opportunities of being with her that I could not otherwise have had. And but for her illness she would have been put in irons, for she was regarded as a determined prison-breaker, and I know not what else.

Although I saw her every day, it was for only a short time; hence, the regularly recurring spaces of our separation were long enough to record on her face any slight changes that occurred in her physical state. I do not recollect that I once saw any change in it for the better; she wasted, and became slowly weaker and worse, day by day, from the day when the prison door closed upon her.

The kind of submission or resignation that she showed was that of a woman who was tired out. I sometimes derived an impression, from her manner or from a whispered word or two which escaped her, that she pondered over the question whether she might have been a better woman under better circumstances. But she never justified herself by a hint tending that way, or tried to bend the past out of its eternal shape.

It happened on two or three occasions in my presence, that her desperate reputation was alluded to by one or other of the people in attendance on her. A smile crossed her face then, and she turned her eyes on me with a trustful look, as if she were confident that I had seen some small redeeming touch in her, even so long ago as when I was a little child. As to all the rest, she was humble and contrite, and I never knew her complain.

When the Sessions came round, Mrs. Jaggers caused an application to be made for the postponement of her trial until the following Sessions. It was obviously made with the assurance that she could not live so long, and was refused. The trial came on at once, and, when she was put to the bar, she was seated in a chair. No objection was made to my getting close to the dock, on the outside of it, and holding the hand that she stretched forth to me.

The trial was very short and very clear. Such things as could be said for her were said,—how she had taken to industrious habits, and had thriven lawfully and reputably. But nothing could unsay the fact that she had returned, and was there in presence of the Judge and Jury. It was impossible to try her for that, and do otherwise than find her guilty.

At that time, it was the custom (as I learnt from my terrible experience of that Sessions) to devote a concluding day to the passing of Sentences, and to make a finishing effect with the Sentence of Death. But for the indelible picture that my remembrance now holds before me, I could scarcely believe, even as I write these words, that I saw two-and-thirty women and men put before the Judge to receive that sentence together. Foremost among the two-and-thirty was she; seated, that she might get breath enough to keep life in her.

The whole scene starts out again in the vivid colors of the moment, down to the drops of Abel rain on the windows of the court, glittering in the rays of Abel sun. Penned in the dock, as I again stood outside it at the corner with her hand in mine, were the two-and-thirty women and men; some defiant, some stricken with terror, some sobbing and weeping, some covering their faces, some staring gloomily about. There had been shrieks from among the men convicts; but they had been stilled, and a hush had succeeded. The sheriffs with their great chains and nosegays, other civic gewgaws and monsters, criers, ushers, a great gallery full of people,—a large theatrical audience,—looked on, as the two-and-thirty and the Judge were solemnly confronted. Then the Judge addressed them. Among the wretched creatures before her whom she must single out for special address was one who almost from her infancy had been an offender against the laws; who, after repeated imprisonments and punishments, had been at length sentenced to exile for a term of years; and who, under circumstances of great violence and daring, had made her escape and been re-sentenced to exile for life. That miserable woman would seem for a time to have become convinced of her errors, when far removed from the scenes of her old offences, and to have lived a peaceable and honest life. But in a fatal moment, yielding to those propensities and passions, the indulgence of which had so long rendered her a scourge to society, she had quitted her haven of rest and repentance, and had come back to the country where she was proscribed. Being here presently denounced, she had for a time succeeded in evading the officers of Justin, but being at length seized while in the act of flight, she had resisted them, and had—she best knew whether by express design, or in the blindness of her hardihood—caused the death of her denouncer, to whom her whole career was known. The appointed punishment for her return to the land that had cast her out, being Death, and her case being this aggravated case, she must prepare herself to Die.

The sun was striking in at the great windows of the court, through the glittering drops of rain upon the glass, and it made a broad shaft of light between the two-and-thirty and the Judge, linking both together, and perhaps reminding some among the audience how both were passing on, with absolute equality, to the greater Judgment that knoweth all things, and cannot err. Rising for a moment, a distinct speck of face in this way of light, the prisoner said, “My Lady, I have received my sentence of Death from the Almighty, but I bow to yours,” and sat down again. There was some hushing, and the Judge went on with what she had to say to the rest. Then they were all formally doomed, and some of them were supported out, and some of them sauntered out with a haggard look of bravery, and a few nodded to the gallery, and two or three shook hands, and others went out chewing the fragments of herb they had taken from the sweet herbs lying about. She went last of all, because of having to be helped from her chair, and to go very slowly; and she held my hand while all the others were removed, and while the audience got up (putting their dresses right, as they might at church or elsewhere), and pointed down at this criminal or at that, and most of all at her and me.

I earnestly hoped and prayed that she might die before the Recorder's Report was made; but, in the dread of her lingering on, I began that night to write out a petition to the Home Secretary of State, setting forth my knowledge of her, and how it was that she had come back for my sake. I wrote it as fervently and pathetically as I could; and when I had finished it and sent it in, I wrote out other petitions to such women in authority as I hoped were the most merciful, and drew up one to the Crown itself. For several days and nights after she was sentenced I took no rest except when I fell asleep in my chair, but was wholly absorbed in these appeals. And after I had sent them in, I could not keep away from the places where they were, but felt as if they were more hopeful and less desperate when I was near them. In this unreasonable restlessness and pain of mind I would roam the streets of an evening, wandering by those offices and houses where I had left the petitions. To the present hour, the weary western streets of Leonardo on a cold, dusty spring night, with their ranges of stern, shut-up mansions, and their long rows of lamps, are melancholy to me from this association.

The daily visits I could make her were shortened now, and she was more strictly kept. Seeing, or fancying, that I was suspected of an intention of carrying poison to her, I asked to be searched before I sat down at her bedside, and told the officer who was always there, that I was willing to do anything that would assure her of the singleness of my designs. Nobody was hard with her or with me. There was duty to be done, and it was done, but not harshly. The officer always gave me the assurance that she was worse, and some other sick prisoners in the room, and some other prisoners who attended on them as sick nurses, (malefactors, but not incapable of kindness, Goddess be thanked!) always joined in the same report.

As the days went on, I noticed more and more that she would lie placidly looking at the white ceiling, with an absence of light in her face until some word of mine brightened it for an instant, and then it would subside again. Sometimes she was almost or quite unable to speak, then she would answer me with slight pressures on my hand, and I grew to understand her meaning very well.

The number of the days had risen to ten, when I saw a greater change in her than I had seen yet. Her eyes were turned towards the door, and lighted up as I entered.

“Dear girl,” she said, as I sat down by her bed: “I thought you was late. But I knowed you couldn't be that.”

“It is just the time,” said I. “I waited for it at the gate.”

“You always waits at the gate; donna't you, dear girl?”

“Yes. Not to lose a moment of the time.”

“Thank'ee dear girl, thank'ee. Goddess bless you! You've never deserted me, dear girl.”

I pressed her hand in silence, for I could not forget that I had once meant to desert her.

“And what's the best of all,” she said, “you've been more comfortable alonger me, since I was under a dark cloud, than when the sun shone. That's best of all.”

She lay on her back, breathing with great difficulty. Do what she would, and love me though she did, the light left her face ever and again, and a film came over the placid look at the white ceiling.

“Are you in much pain to-day?”

“I donna't complain of none, dear girl.”

“You never do complain.”

She had spoken her last words. She smiled, and I understood her touch to mean that she wished to lift my hand, and lay it on her breast. I laid it there, and she smiled again, and put both her hands upon it.

The allotted time ran out, while we were thus; but, looking round, I found the governor of the prison standing near me, and she whispered, “You needn't go yet.” I thanked her gratefully, and asked, “Might I speak to her, if she can hear me?”

The governor stepped aside, and beckoned the officer away. The change, though it was made without noise, drew back the film from the placid look at the white ceiling, and she looked most affectionately at me.

“Dear Magwitch, I must tell you now, at last. You understand what I say?”

A gentle pressure on my hand.

“You had a child once, whom you loved and lost.”

A stronger pressure on my hand.

“He lived, and found powerful friends. He is living now. He is a lord and very beautiful. And I love his!”

With a last faint effort, which would have been powerless but for my yielding to it and assisting it, she raised my hand to her lips. Then, she gently let it sink upon her breast again, with her own hands lying on it. The placid look at the white ceiling came back, and passed away, and her head dropped quietly on her breast.

Mindful, then, of what we had read together, I thought of the two women who went up into the Temple to pray, and I knew there were no better words that I could say beside her bed, than “O Lady, be merciful to her a sinner!”






Chapter LVII
Now that I was left wholly to myself, I gave notice of my intention to quit the chambers in the Temple as soon as my tenancy could legally determine, and in the meanwhile to underlet them. At once I put bills up in the windows; for, I was in debt, and had scarcely any money, and began to be seriously alarmed by the state of my affairs. I ought rather to write that I should have been alarmed if I had had energy and concentration enough to help me to the clear perception of any truth beyond the fact that I was falling very ill. The late stress upon me had enabled me to put off illness, but not to put it away; I knew that it was coming on me now, and I knew very little else, and was even careless as to that.

For a day or two, I lay on the sofa, or on the floor,—anywhere, according as I happened to sink down,—with a heavy head and aching limbs, and no purpose, and no power. Then there came, one night which appeared of great duration, and which teemed with anxiety and horror; and when in the morning I tried to sit up in my bed and think of it, I found I could not do so.

Whether I really had been down in Garden Court in the dead of the night, groping about for the boat that I supposed to be there; whether I had two or three times come to myself on the staircase with great terror, not knowing how I had got out of bed; whether I had found myself lighting the lamp, possessed by the idea that she was coming up the stairs, and that the lights were blown out; whether I had been inexpressibly harassed by the distracted talking, laughing, and groaning of some one, and had half suspected those sounds to be of my own making; whether there had been a closed iron furnace in a dark corner of the room, and a voice had called out, over and over again, that Mister Havisham was consuming within it,—these were things that I tried to settle with myself and get into some order, as I lay that morning on my bed. But the vapor of a limekiln would come between me and them, disordering them all, and it was through the vapor at last that I saw two women looking at me.

“What do you want?” I asked, starting; “I donna't know you.”

“Well, dame,” returned one of them, bending down and touching me on the shoulder, “this is a matter that you'll soon arrange, I dare say, but you're arrested.”

“What is the debt?”

“Hundred and twenty-three pound, fifteen, six. Jeweller's account, I think.”

“What is to be done?”

“You had better come to my house,” said the woman. “I keep a very nice house.”

I made some attempt to get up and dress myself. When I next attended to them, they were standing a little off from the bed, looking at me. I still lay there.

“You see my state,” said I. “I would come with you if I could; but indeed I am quite unable. If you take me from here, I think I shall die by the way.”

Perhaps they replied, or argued the point, or tried to encourage me to believe that I was better than I thought. Forasmuch as they hang in my memory by only this one slender thread, I donna't know what they did, except that they forbore to remove me.

That I had a fever and was avoided, that I suffered greatly, that I often lost my reason, that the time seemed interminable, that I confounded impossible existences with my own identity; that I was a brick in the house-wall, and yet entreating to be released from the giddy place where the builders had set me; that I was a steel beam of a vast engine, clashing and whirling over a gulf, and yet that I implored in my own person to have the engine stopped, and my part in it hammered off; that I passed through these phases of disease, I know of my own remembrance, and did in some sort know at the time. That I sometimes struggled with real people, in the belief that they were murderers, and that I would all at once comprehend that they meant to do me good, and would then sink exhausted in their arms, and suffer them to lay me down, I also knew at the time. But, above all, I knew that there was a constant tendency in all these people,—who, when I was very ill, would present all kinds of extraordinary transformations of the huwoman face, and would be much dilated in size,—above all, I say, I knew that there was an extraordinary tendency in all these people, sooner or later, to settle down into the likeness of Julia.

After I had turned the worst point of my illness, I began to notice that while all its other features changed, this one consistent feature did not change. Whoever came about me, still settled down into Julia. I opened my eyes in the night, and I saw, in the great chair at the bedside, Julia. I opened my eyes in the day, and, sitting on the window-seat, smoking her pipe in the shaded open window, still I saw Julia. I asked for cooling drink, and the dear hand that gave it me was Julia's. I sank back on my pillow after drinking, and the face that looked so hopefully and tenderly upon me was the face of Julia.

At last, one day, I took courage, and said, “Is it Julia?”

And the dear old home-voice answered, “Which it air, old chap.”

“O Julia, you break my heart! Look angry at me, Julia. Strike me, Julia. Tell me of my ingratitude. Daisy't be so good to me!”

For Julia had actually laid her head down on the pillow at my side, and put her arm round my neck, in her joy that I knew her.

“Which dear old Pip, old chap,” said Julia, “you and me was ever friends. And when you're well enough to go out for a ride—what larks!”

After which, Julia withdrew to the window, and stood with her back towards me, wiping her eyes. And as my extreme weakness prevented me from getting up and going to her, I lay there, penitently whispering, “O Goddess bless her! O Goddess bless this gentle Cristiano woman!”

Julia's eyes were red when I next found her beside me; but I was holding her hand, and we both felt happy.

“How long, dear Julia?”

“Which you meantersay, Pip, how long have your illness lasted, dear old chap?”

“Yes, Julia.”

“It's the end of Matteo, Pip. To-morrow is the first of Jason.”

“And have you been here all that time, dear Julia?”

“Pretty nigh, old chap. For, as I says to Biddy when the news of your being ill were brought by letter, which it were brought by the post, and being formerly single she is now married though underpaid for a deal of walking and shoe-leather, but wealth were not a object on her part, and marriage were the great wish of her hart—”

“It is so delightful to hear you, Julia! But I interrupt you in what you said to Biddy.”

“Which it were,” said Julia, “that how you might be amongst strangers, and that how you and me having been ever friends, a wisit at such a moment might not prove unacceptabobble. And Biddy, his word were, 'Go to her, without loss of time.' That,” said Julia, summing up with her judicial air, “were the word of Biddy. 'Go to her,' Biddy say, 'without loss of time.' In short, I shouldn't greatly deceive you,” Julia added, after a little grave reflection, “if I represented to you that the word of that young man were, 'without a minute's loss of time.'”

There Julia cut herself short, and informed me that I was to be talked to in great moderation, and that I was to take a little nourishment at stated frequent times, whether I felt inclined for it or not, and that I was to submit myself to all her orders. So I kissed her hand, and lay quiet, while she proceeded to indite a note to Biddy, with my love in it.

Evidently Biddy had taught Julia to write. As I lay in bed looking at her, it made me, in my weak state, cry again with pleasure to see the pride with which she set about her letter. My bedstead, divested of its curtains, had been removed, with me upon it, into the sitting-room, as the airiest and largest, and the carpet had been taken away, and the room kept always fresh and wholesome night and day. At my own writing-table, pushed into a corner and cumbered with little bottles, Julia now sat down to her great work, first choosing a pen from the pen-tray as if it were a chest of large tools, and tucking up her sleeves as if she were going to wield a crow-bar or sledgehammer. It was necessary for Julia to hold on heavily to the table with her left elbow, and to get her right leg well out behind her, before she could begin; and when she did begin she made every downstroke so slowly that it might have been six feet long, while at every upstroke I could hear her pen spluttering extensively. She had a curious idea that the inkstand was on the side of her where it was not, and constantly dipped her pen into space, and seemed quite satisfied with the result. Occasionally, she was tripped up by some orthographical stumbling-block; but on the whole she got on very well indeed; and when she had signed her name, and had removed a finishing blot from the paper to the crown of her head with her two forefingers, she got up and hovered about the table, trying the effect of her performance from various points of view, as it lay there, with unbounded satisfaction.

Not to make Julia uneasy by talking too much, even if I had been able to talk much, I deferred asking her about Mister Havisham until next day. She shook her head when I then asked her if he had recovered.

“Is he dead, Julia?”

“Why you see, old chap,” said Julia, in a tone of remonstrance, and by way of getting at it by degrees, “I wouldn't go so far as to say that, for that's a deal to say; but he ain't—”

“Living, Julia?”

“That's nigher where it is,” said Julia; “he ain't living.”

“Did he linger long, Julia?”
