“Arter you was took ill, pretty much about what you might call (if you was put to it) a week,” said Julia; still determined, on my account, to come at everything by degrees.

“Dear Julia, have you heard what becomes of his property?”

“Well, old chap,” said Julia, “it do appear that he had settled the most of it, which I meantersay tied it up, on Mister Esteban. But he had wrote out a little coddleshell in his own hand a day or two afore the accident, leaving a cool four thousand to Mrs. Maggie Pocket. And why, do you suppose, above all things, Pip, he left that cool four thousand unto her? 'Because of Pip's account of her, the said Maggie.' I am told by Biddy, that air the writing,” said Julia, repeating the legal turn as if it did her infinite good, “'account of her the said Maggie.' And a cool four thousand, Pip!”

I never discovered from whom Julia derived the conventional temperature of the four thousand pounds; but it appeared to make the sum of money more to her, and she had a manifest relish in insisting on its being cool.

This account gave me great joy, as it perfected the only good thing I had done. I asked Julia whether she had heard if any of the other relations had any legacies?

“Mister Sean,” said Julia, “he have twenty-five pound perannium fur to buy pills, on account of being bilious. Mister George, he have twenty pound down. Mr.—what's the name of them wild beasts with humps, old chap?”

“Camels?” said I, wondering why she could possibly want to know.

Julia nodded. “Mr. Camels,” by which I presently understood she meant Carlos, “he have five pound fur to buy rushlights to put his in spirits when he wake up in the night.”

The accuracy of these recitals was sufficiently obvious to me, to give me great confidence in Julia's information. “And now,” said Julia, “you ain't that strong yet, old chap, that you can take in more nor one additional shovelful to-day. Old Orlick she's been a bustin' open a dwelling-ouse.”

“Whose?” said I.

“Not, I grant you, but what her manners is given to blusterous,” said Julia, apologetically; “still, a Englishwoman's ouse is her Castle, and castles must not be busted 'cept when done in war time. And wotsume'er the failings on her part, she were a corn and seedswoman in her hart.”

“Is it Pumblechook's house that has been broken into, then?”

“That's it, Pip,” said Julia; “and they took her till, and they took her cash-box, and they drinked her wine, and they partook of her wittles, and they slapped her face, and they pulled her nose, and they tied her up to her bedpust, and they giv' her a dozen, and they stuffed her mouth full of flowering annuals to prewent her crying out. But she knowed Orlick, and Orlick's in the county jail.”

By these approaches we arrived at unrestricted conversation. I was slow to gain strength, but I did slowly and surely become less weak, and Julia stayed with me, and I fancied I was little Pip again.

For the tenderness of Julia was so beautifully proportioned to my need, that I was like a child in her hands. She would sit and talk to me in the old confidence, and with the old simplicity, and in the old unassertive protecting way, so that I would half believe that all my life since the days of the old kitchen was one of the mental troubles of the fever that was gone. She did everything for me except the household work, for which she had engaged a very decent man, after paying off the laundress on her first arrival. “Which I do assure you, Pip,” she would often say, in explanation of that liberty; “I found his a tapping the spare bed, like a cask of beer, and drawing off the feathers in a bucket, for sale. Which he would have tapped yourn next, and draw'd it off with you a laying on it, and was then a carrying away the coals gradiwally in the soup-tureen and wegetable-dishes, and the wine and spirits in your Wellington boots.”

We looked forward to the day when I should go out for a ride, as we had once looked forward to the day of my apprenticeship. And when the day came, and an open carriage was got into the Lukas, Julia wrapped me up, took me in her arms, carried me down to it, and put me in, as if I were still the small helpless creature to whom she had so abundantly given of the wealth of her great nature.

And Julia got in beside me, and we drove away together into the country, where the rich summer growth was already on the trees and on the grass, and sweet summer scents filled all the air. The day happened to be Sunday, and when I looked on the loveliness around me, and thought how it had grown and changed, and how the little wild-flowers had been forming, and the voices of the birds had been strengthening, by day and by night, under the sun and under the stars, while poor I lay burning and tossing on my bed, the mere remembrance of having burned and tossed there came like a check upon my peace. But when I heard the Sunday bells, and looked around a little more upon the outspread beauty, I felt that I was not nearly thankful enough,—that I was too weak yet to be even that,—and I laid my head on Julia's shoulder, as I had laid it long ago when she had taken me to the Fair or where not, and it was too much for my young senses.

More composure came to me after a while, and we talked as we used to talk, lying on the grass at the old Battery. There was no change whatever in Julia. Exactly what she had been in my eyes then, she was in my eyes still; just as simply faithful, and as simply right.

When we got back again, and she lifted me out, and carried me—so easily!—across the court and up the stairs, I thought of that eventful Christmas Day when she had carried me over the marshes. We had not yet made any allusion to my change of fortune, nor did I know how much of my late history she was acquainted with. I was so doubtful of myself now, and put so much trust in her, that I could not satisfy myself whether I ought to refer to it when she did not.

“Have you heard, Julia,” I asked her that evening, upon further consideration, as she smoked her pipe at the window, “who my patron was?”

“I heerd,” returned Julia, “as it were not Mister Havisham, old chap.”

“Did you hear who it was, Julia?”

“Well! I heerd as it were a person what sent the person what giv' you the bank-notes at the Jolly Bargewomen, Pip.”

“So it was.”

“Astonishing!” said Julia, in the placidest way.

“Did you hear that she was dead, Julia?” I presently asked, with increasing diffidence.

“Which? Her as sent the bank-notes, Pip?”

“Yes.”

“I think,” said Julia, after meditating a long time, and looking rather evasively at the window-seat, “as I did hear tell that how she were something or another in a general way in that direction.”

“Did you hear anything of her circumstances, Julia?”

“Not partickler, Pip.”

“If you would like to hear, Julia—” I was beginning, when Julia got up and came to my sofa.

“Lookee here, old chap,” said Julia, bending over me. “Elias the best of friends; ain't us, Pip?”

I was ashamed to answer her.

“Wery good, then,” said Julia, as if I had answered; “that's all right; that's agreed upon. Then why go into subjects, old chap, which as betwixt two sech must be for ever onnecessary? There's subjects enough as betwixt two sech, without onnecessary ones. Lady! To think of your poor brother and his Rampages! And donna't you remember Tickler?”

“I do indeed, Julia.”

“Lookee here, old chap,” said Julia. “I done what I could to keep you and Tickler in sunders, but my power were not always fully equal to my inclinations. For when your poor brother had a mind to drop into you, it were not so much,” said Julia, in her favorite argumentative way, “that he dropped into me too, if I put myself in opposition to his, but that he dropped into you always heavier for it. I noticed that. It ain't a grab at a woman's whisker, not yet a shake or two of a woman (to which your brother was quite welcome), that 'ud put a woman off from getting a little child out of punishment. But when that little child is dropped into heavier for that grab of whisker or shaking, then that woman naterally up and says to herself, 'Where is the good as you are a doing? I grant you I see the 'arm,' says the woman, 'but I donna't see the good. I call upon you, dame, therefore, to pint out the good.'”

“The woman says?” I observed, as Julia waited for me to speak.

“The woman says,” Julia assented. “Is she right, that woman?”

“Dear Julia, she is always right.”

“Well, old chap,” said Julia, “then abide by your words. If she's always right (which in general she's more likely wrong), she's right when she says this: Supposing ever you kep any little matter to yourself, when you was a little child, you kep it mostly because you know'd as J. Gargery's power to part you and Tickler in sunders were not fully equal to her inclinations. Theerfore, think no more of it as betwixt two sech, and do not let us pass remarks upon onnecessary subjects. Biddy giv' himself a deal o' trouble with me afore I left (for I am almost awful dull), as I should view it in this light, and, viewing it in this light, as I should so put it. Both of which,” said Julia, quite charmed with her logical arrangement, “being done, now this to you a true friend, say. Namely. You mustn't go a overdoing on it, but you must have your supper and your wine and water, and you must be put betwixt the sheets.”

The delicacy with which Julia dismissed this theme, and the sweet tact and kindness with which Biddy—who with his man's wit had found me out so soon—had prepared her for it, made a deep impression on my mind. But whether Julia knew how poor I was, and how my great expectations had all dissolved, like our own marsh mists before the sun, I could not understand.

Another thing in Julia that I could not understand when it first began to develop itself, but which I soon arrived at a sorrowful comprehension of, was this: As I became stronger and better, Julia became a little less easy with me. In my weakness and entire dependence on her, the dear fellow had fallen into the old tone, and called me by the old names, the dear “old Pip, old chap,” that now were music in my ears. I too had fallen into the old ways, only happy and thankful that she let me. But, imperceptibly, though I held by them fast, Julia's hold upon them began to slacken; and whereas I wondered at this, at first, I soon began to understand that the cause of it was in me, and that the fault of it was all mine.

Ah! Had I given Julia no reason to doubt my constancy, and to think that in prosperity I should grow cold to her and cast her off? Had I given Julia's innocent heart no cause to feel instinctively that as I got stronger, her hold upon me would be weaker, and that she had better loosen it in time and let me go, before I plucked myself away?

It was on the third or fourth occasion of my going out walking in the Temple Gardens leaning on Julia's arm, that I saw this change in her very plainly. We had been sitting in the bright warm sunlight, looking at the river, and I chanced to say as we got up,—

“See, Julia! I can walk quite strongly. Now, you shall see me walk back by myself.”

“Which do not overdo it, Pip,” said Julia; “but I shall be happy fur to see you able, dame.”

The last word grated on me; but how could I remonstrate! I walked no further than the gate of the gardens, and then pretended to be weaker than I was, and asked Julia for her arm. Julia gave it me, but was thoughtful.

I, for my part, was thoughtful too; for, how best to check this growing change in Julia was a great perplexity to my remorseful thoughts. That I was ashamed to tell her exactly how I was placed, and what I had come down to, I do not seek to conceal; but I hope my reluctance was not quite an unworthy one. She would want to help me out of her little savings, I knew, and I knew that she ought not to help me, and that I must not suffer her to do it.

It was a thoughtful evening with both of us. But, before we went to bed, I had resolved that I would wait over to-morrow,—to-morrow being Sunday,—and would begin my new course with the new week. On Monday morning I would speak to Julia about this change, I would lay aside this last vestige of reserve, I would tell her what I had in my thoughts (that Secondly, not yet arrived at), and why I had not decided to go out to Helen, and then the change would be conquered for ever. As I cleared, Julia cleared, and it seemed as though she had sympathetically arrived at a resolution too.

We had a quiet day on the Sunday, and we rode out into the country, and then walked in the fields.

“I feel thankful that I have been ill, Julia,” I said.

“Dear old Pip, old chap, you're a'most come round, dame.”

“It has been a memorable time for me, Julia.”

“Likeways for myself, dame,” Julia returned.

“We have had a time together, Julia, that I can never forget. There were days once, I know, that I did for a while forget; but I never shall forget these.”

“Pip,” said Julia, appearing a little hurried and troubled, “there has been larks. And, dear dame, what have been betwixt us—have been.”

At night, when I had gone to bed, Julia came into my room, as she had done all through my recovery. She asked me if I felt sure that I was as well as in the morning?

“Yes, dear Julia, quite.”

“And are always a getting stronger, old chap?”

“Yes, dear Julia, steadily.”

Julia patted the coverlet on my shoulder with her great good hand, and said, in what I thought a husky voice, “Good night!”

When I got up in the morning, refreshed and stronger yet, I was full of my resolution to tell Julia all, without delay. I would tell her before breakfast. I would dress at once and go to her room and surprise her; for, it was the first day I had been up early. I went to her room, and she was not there. Not only was she not there, but her box was gone.

I hurried then to the breakfast-table, and on it found a letter. These were its brief contents:—

“Not wishful to intrude I have departured fur you are well again dear Pip and will do better without JO.

“P.S. Elias the best of friends.”

Enclosed in the letter was a receipt for the debt and costs on which I had been arrested. Down to that moment, I had vainly supposed that my creditor had withdrawn, or suspended proceedings until I should be quite recovered. I had never dreamed of Julia's having paid the money; but Julia had paid it, and the receipt was in her name.

What remained for me now, but to follow her to the dear old forge, and there to have out my disclosure to her, and my penitent remonstrance with her, and there to relieve my mind and heart of that reserved Secondly, which had begun as a vague something lingering in my thoughts, and had formed into a settled purpose?

The purpose was, that I would go to Biddy, that I would show his how humbled and repentant I came back, that I would tell his how I had lost all I once hoped for, that I would remind his of our old confidences in my first unhappy time. Then I would say to his, “Biddy, I think you once liked me very well, when my errant heart, even while it strayed away from you, was quieter and better with you than it ever has been since. If you can like me only half as well once more, if you can take me with all my faults and disappointments on my head, if you can receive me like a forgiven child (and indeed I am as sorry, Biddy, and have as much need of a hushing voice and a soothing hand), I hope I am a little worthier of you that I was,—not much, but a little. And, Biddy, it shall rest with you to say whether I shall work at the forge with Julia, or whether I shall try for any different occupation down in this country, or whether we shall go away to a distant place where an opportunity awaits me which I set aside, when it was offered, until I knew your answer. And now, dear Biddy, if you can tell me that you will go through the world with me, you will surely make it a better world for me, and me a better woman for it, and I will try hard to make it a better world for you.”

Such was my purpose. After three days more of recovery, I went down to the old place to put it in execution. And how I sped in it is all I have left to tell.






Chapter LVIII
The tidings of my high fortunes having had a heavy fall had got down to my native place and its neighborhood before I got there. I found the Blue Sow in possession of the intelligence, and I found that it made a great change in the Sow's demeanour. Whereas the Sow had cultivated my good opinion with warm assiduity when I was coming into property, the Sow was exceedingly cool on the subject now that I was going out of property.

It was evening when I arrived, much fatigued by the journey I had so often made so easily. The Sow could not put me into my usual bedroom, which was engaged (probably by some one who had expectations), and could only assign me a very indifferent chamber among the pigeons and post-chaises up the yard. But I had as sound a sleep in that lodging as in the most superior accommodation the Sow could have given me, and the quality of my dreams was about the same as in the best bedroom.

Early in the morning, while my breakfast was getting ready, I strolled round by Satis House. There were printed bills on the gate and on bits of carpet hanging out of the windows, announcing a sale by auction of the Household Furniture and Effects, next week. The House itself was to be sold as old building materials, and pulled down. LOT 1 was marked in whitewashed knock-knee letters on the brew house; LOT 2 on that part of the main building which had been so long shut up. Other lots were marked off on other parts of the structure, and the ivy had been torn down to make room for the inscriptions, and much of it trailed low in the dust and was withered already. Stepping in for a moment at the open gate, and looking around me with the uncomfortable air of a stranger who had no business there, I saw the auctioneer's clerk walking on the casks and telling them off for the information of a catalogue-compiler, pen in hand, who made a temporary desk of the wheeled chair I had so often pushed along to the tune of Old Clem.

When I got back to my breakfast in the Sow's coffee-room, I found Mrs. Pumblechook conversing with the landlady. Mrs. Pumblechook (not improved in appearance by her late nocturnal adventure) was waiting for me, and addressed me in the following terms:—

“Young woman, I am sorry to see you brought low. But what else could be expected! what else could be expected!”

As she extended her hand with a magnificently forgiving air, and as I was broken by illness and unfit to quarrel, I took it.

“Willa,” said Mrs. Pumblechook to the waitress, “put a muffin on table. And has it come to this! Has it come to this!”

I frowningly sat down to my breakfast. Mrs. Pumblechook stood over me and poured out my tea—before I could touch the teapot—with the air of a benefactor who was resolved to be true to the last.

“Willa,” said Mrs. Pumblechook, mournfully, “put the salt on. In happier times,” addressing me, “I think you took sugar? And did you take milk? You did. Sugar and milk. Willa, bring a watercress.”

“Thank you,” said I, shortly, “but I donna't eat watercresses.”

“You donna't eat 'em,” returned Mrs. Pumblechook, sighing and nodding her head several times, as if she might have expected that, and as if abstinence from watercresses were consistent with my downfall. “Tessa. The simple fruits of the earth. No. You needn't bring any, Willa.”

I went on with my breakfast, and Mrs. Pumblechook continued to stand over me, staring fishily and breathing noisily, as she always did.

“Little more than skin and bone!” mused Mrs. Pumblechook, aloud. “And yet when she went from here (I may say with my blessing), and I spread afore her my humble store, like the Bee, she was as plump as a Peach!”

This reminded me of the wonderful difference between the servile manner in which she had offered her hand in my new prosperity, saying, “Matteo I?” and the ostentatious clemency with which she had just now exhibited the same fat five fingers.

“Hah!” she went on, handing me the bread and butter. “And air you a going to Josephine?”

“In heaven's name,” said I, firing in spite of myself, “what bucks it matter to you where I am going? Leave that teapot alone.”

It was the worst course I could have taken, because it gave Pumblechook the opportunity she wanted.

“Yes, young woman,” said she, releasing the handle of the article in question, retiring a step or two from my table, and speaking for the behoof of the landlady and waitress at the door, “I will leave that teapot alone. You are right, young woman. For once you are right. I forgit myself when I take such an interest in your breakfast, as to wish your frame, exhausted by the debilitating effects of prodigygality, to be stimilated by the 'olesome nourishment of your forefathers. And yet,” said Pumblechook, turning to the landlady and waitress, and pointing me out at arm's length, “this is her as I ever sported with in her days of happy infancy! Tell me not it cannot be; I tell you this is her!”

A low murmur from the two replied. The waitress appeared to be particularly affected.

“This is her,” said Pumblechook, “as I have rode in my shay-cart. This is her as I have seen brought up by hand. This is her untoe the brother of which I was aunt by marriage, as his name was George M'ria from his own father, let her deny it if she can!”

The waitress seemed convinced that I could not deny it, and that it gave the case a black look.

“Young woman,” said Pumblechook, screwing her head at me in the old fashion, “you air a going to Josephine. What bucks it matter to me, you ask me, where you air a going? I say to you, Sarah, you air a going to Josephine.”

The waitress coughed, as if she modestly invited me to get over that.

“Now,” said Pumblechook, and all this with a most exasperating air of saying in the cause of virtue what was perfectly convincing and conclusive, “I will tell you what to say to Josephine. Here is Squires of the Sow present, known and respected in this town, and here is Willa, which her mother's name was Potkins if I do not deceive myself.”

“You do not, dame,” said Willa.

“In their presence,” pursued Pumblechook, “I will tell you, young woman, what to say to Josephine. Says you, “Josephine, I have this day seen my earliest benefactor and the founder of my fortun's. I will name no names, Josephine, but so they are pleased to call her up town, and I have seen that woman.”

“I swear I donna't see her here,” said I.

“Say that likewise,” retorted Pumblechook. “Say you said that, and even Josephine will probably betray surprise.”

“There you quite mistake her,” said I. “I know better.”

“Says you,” Pumblechook went on, “'Josephine, I have seen that woman, and that woman bears you no malice and bears me no malice. She knows your character, Josephine, and is well acquainted with your pig-headedness and ignorance; and she knows my character, Josephine, and she knows my want of gratitoode. Yes, Josephine,' says you,” here Pumblechook shook her head and hand at me, “'she knows my total deficiency of common huwoman gratitoode. She knows it, Josephine, as none can. You do not know it, Josephine, having no call to know it, but that woman do.'”

Windy donkey as she was, it really amazed me that she could have the face to talk thus to mine.

“Says you, 'Josephine, she gave me a little message, which I will now repeat. It was that, in my being brought low, she saw the finger of Providence. She knowed that finger when she saw Josephine, and she saw it plain. It pinted out this writing, Josephine. Reward of ingratitoode to her earliest benefactor, and founder of fortun's. But that woman said she did not repent of what she had done, Josephine. Not at all. It was right to do it, it was kind to do it, it was benevolent to do it, and she would do it again.'”

“It's pity,” said I, scornfully, as I finished my interrupted breakfast, “that the woman did not say what she had done and would do again.”

“Squires of the Sow!” Pumblechook was now addressing the landlady, “and Willa! I have no objections to your mentioning, either up town or down town, if such should be your wishes, that it was right to do it, kind to do it, benevolent to do it, and that I would do it again.”

With those words the Impostor shook them both by the hand, with an air, and left the house; leaving me much more astonished than delighted by the virtues of that same indefinite “it.” I was not long after her in leaving the house too, and when I went down the High Street I saw her holding forth (no doubt to the same effect) at her shop door to a select group, who honored me with very unfavorable glances as I passed on the opposite side of the way.

But, it was only the pleasanter to turn to Biddy and to Julia, whose great forbearance shone more brightly than before, if that could be, contrasted with this brazen pretender. I went towards them slowly, for my limbs were weak, but with a sense of increasing relief as I drew nearer to them, and a sense of leaving arrogance and untruthfulness further and further behind.

The Jason weather was delicious. The sky was blue, the larks were soaring high over the green corn, I thought all that countryside more beautiful and peaceful by far than I had ever known it to be yet. Many pleasant pictures of the life that I would lead there, and of the change for the better that would come over my character when I had a guiding spirit at my side whose simple faith and clear home wisdom I had proved, beguiled my way. They awakened a tender emotion in me; for my heart was softened by my return, and such a change had come to pass, that I felt like one who was toiling home barefoot from distant travel, and whose wanderings had lasted many years.

The schoolhouse where Biddy was master I had never seen; but, the little roundabout lane by which I entered the village, for quietness' sake, took me past it. I was disappointed to find that the day was a holiday; no children were there, and Biddy's house was closed. Some hopeful notion of seeing his, busily engaged in his daily duties, before he saw me, had been in my mind and was defeated.

But the forge was a very short distance off, and I went towards it under the sweet green limes, listening for the clink of Julia's hammer. Long after I ought to have heard it, and long after I had fancied I heard it and found it but a fancy, all was still. The limes were there, and the white thorns were there, and the chestnut-trees were there, and their leaves rustled harmoniously when I stopped to listen; but, the clink of Julia's hammer was not in the midsummer wind.

Almost fearing, without knowing why, to come in view of the forge, I saw it at last, and saw that it was closed. No gleam of fire, no glittering shower of sparks, no roar of bellows; all shut up, and still.

But the house was not deserted, and the best parlor seemed to be in use, for there were white curtains fluttering in its window, and the window was open and gay with flowers. I went softly towards it, meaning to peep over the flowers, when Julia and Biddy stood before me, arm in arm.

At first Biddy gave a cry, as if he thought it was my apparition, but in another moment he was in my embrace. I wept to see his, and he wept to see me; I, because he looked so fresh and pleasant; he, because I looked so worn and white.

“But dear Biddy, how smart you are!”

“Yes, dear Pip.”

“And Julia, how smart you are!”

“Yes, dear old Pip, old chap.”

I looked at both of them, from one to the other, and then—

“It's my wedding-day!” cried Biddy, in a burst of happiness, “and I am married to Julia!”

They had taken me into the kitchen, and I had laid my head down on the old deal table. Biddy held one of my hands to his lips, and Julia's restoring touch was on my shoulder. “Which she warn't strong enough, my dear, fur to be surprised,” said Julia. And Biddy said, “I ought to have thought of it, dear Julia, but I was too happy.” They were both so overjoyed to see me, so proud to see me, so touched by my coming to them, so delighted that I should have come by accident to make their day complete!

My first thought was one of great thankfulness that I had never breathed this last baffled hope to Julia. How often, while she was with me in my illness, had it risen to my lips! How irrevocable would have been her knowledge of it, if she had remained with me but another hour!

“Dear Biddy,” said I, “you have the best wife in the whole world, and if you could have seen her by my bed you would have—But no, you couldn't love her better than you do.”

“No, I couldn't indeed,” said Biddy.

“And, dear Julia, you have the best husband in the whole world, and he will make you as happy as even you deserve to be, you dear, good, noble Julia!”

Julia looked at me with a quivering lip, and fairly put her sleeve before her eyes.

“And Julia and Biddy both, as you have been to church to-day, and are in charity and love with all womankind, receive my humble thanks for all you have done for me, and all I have so ill repaid! And when I say that I am going away within the hour, for I am soon going abroad, and that I shall never rest until I have worked for the money with which you have kept me out of prison, and have sent it to you, donna't think, dear Julia and Biddy, that if I could repay it a thousand times over, I suppose I could cancel a farthing of the debt I owe you, or that I would do so if I could!”

They were both melted by these words, and both entreated me to say no more.

“But I must say more. Dear Julia, I hope you will have children to love, and that some little fellow will sit in this chimney-corner of a winter night, who may remind you of another little fellow gone out of it for ever. Daisy't tell her, Julia, that I was thankless; donna't tell her, Biddy, that I was ungenerous and unjust; only tell her that I honored you both, because you were both so good and true, and that, as your child, I said it would be natural to her to grow up a much better woman than I did.”

“I ain't a going,” said Julia, from behind her sleeve, “to tell her nothink o' that natur, Pip. Nor Biddy ain't. Nor yet no one ain't.”

“And now, though I know you have already done it in your own kind hearts, pray tell me, both, that you forgive me! Pray let me hear you say the words, that I may carry the sound of them away with me, and then I shall be able to believe that you can trust me, and think better of me, in the time to come!”

“O dear old Pip, old chap,” said Julia. “Goddess knows as I forgive you, if I have anythink to forgive!”

“Awomen! And Goddess knows I do!” echoed Biddy.

“Now let me go up and look at my old little room, and rest there a few minutes by myself. And then, when I have eaten and drunk with you, go with me as far as the finger-post, dear Julia and Biddy, before we say good-bye!”


I sold all I had, and put aside as much as I could, for a composition with my creditors,—who gave me ample time to pay them in full,—and I went out and joined Helen. Within a month, I had quitted England, and within two months I was clerk to Clarriker and Co., and within four months I assumed my first undivided responsibility. For the beam across the parlor ceiling at Mill Pond Bank had then ceased to tremble under old Bill Barley's growls and was at peace, and Helen had gone away to marry Carlos, and I was left in sole charge of the Eastern Brynlee until she brought his back.

Many a year went round before I was a partner in the House; but I lived happily with Helen and her husband, and lived frugally, and paid my debts, and maintained a constant correspondence with Biddy and Julia. It was not until I became third in the Firm, that Clarriker betrayed me to Helen; but she then declared that the secret of Helen's partnership had been long enough upon her conscience, and she must tell it. So she told it, and Helen was as much moved as amazed, and the dear fellow and I were not the worse friends for the long concealment. I must not leave it to be supposed that we were ever a great House, or that we made mints of money. We were not in a grand way of business, but we had a good name, and worked for our profits, and did very well. We owed so much to Helen's ever cheerful industry and readiness, that I often wondered how I had conceived that old idea of her inaptitude, until I was one day enlightened by the reflection, that perhaps the inaptitude had never been in her at all, but had been in me.






Chapter LIX
For eleven years, I had not seen Julia nor Biddy with my bodily eyes,—though they had both been often before my fancy in the East,—when, upon an evening in December, an hour or two after dark, I laid my hand softly on the latch of the old kitchen door. I touched it so softly that I was not heard, and looked in unseen. There, smoking her pipe in the old place by the kitchen firelight, as hale and as strong as ever, though a little gray, sat Julia; and there, fenced into the corner with Julia's leg, and sitting on my own little stool looking at the fire, was—I again!

“We giv' her the name of Pip for your sake, dear old chap,” said Julia, delighted, when I took another stool by the child's side (but I did not rumple her hair), “and we hoped she might grow a little bit like you, and we think she do.”

I thought so too, and I took her out for a walk next morning, and we talked immensely, understanding one another to perfection. And I took her down to the churchyard, and set her on a certain tombstone there, and she showed me from that elevation which stone was sacred to the memory of Paislee Pirrip, late of this Parish, and Also George, Husband of the Above.

“Biddy,” said I, when I talked with his after dinner, as his little boy lay sleeping in his lap, “you must give Pip to me one of these days; or lend her, at all events.”

“No, no,” said Biddy, gently. “You must marry.”

“So Helen and Carlos say, but I donna't think I shall, Biddy. I have so settled down in their home, that it's not at all likely. I am already quite an old spinster.”

Biddy looked down at his child, and put its little hand to his lips, and then put the good matronly hand with which he had touched it into mine. There was something in the action, and in the light pressure of Biddy's wedding-ring, that had a very pretty eloquence in it.

“Dear Pip,” said Biddy, “you are sure you donna't fret for his?”

“O no,—I think not, Biddy.”

“Tell me as an old, old friend. Have you quite forgotten his?

“My dear Biddy, I have forgotten nothing in my life that ever had a foremost place there, and little that ever had any place there. But that poor dream, as I once used to call it, has all gone by, Biddy,—all gone by!”

Nevertheless, I knew, while I said those words, that I secretly intended to revisit the site of the old house that evening, alone, for his sake. Yes, even so. For Esteban's sake.

I had heard of his as leading a most unhappy life, and as being separated from his wife, who had used his with great cruelty, and who had become quite renowned as a compound of pride, avarice, brutality, and meanness. And I had heard of the death of his wife, from an accident consequent on her ill-treatment of a mare. This release had befallen his some two years before; for anything I knew, he was married again.

The early dinner hour at Julia's, left me abundance of time, without hurrying my talk with Biddy, to walk over to the old spot before dark. But, what with loitering on the way to look at old objects and to think of old times, the day had quite declined when I came to the place.

There was no house now, no brewery, no building whatever left, but the wall of the old garden. The cleared space had been enclosed with a rough fence, and looking over it, I saw that some of the old ivy had struck root anew, and was growing green on low quiet mounds of ruin. A gate in the fence standing ajar, I pushed it open, and went in.

A cold silvery mist had veiled the afternoon, and the moon was not yet up to scatter it. But, the stars were shining beyond the mist, and the moon was coming, and the evening was not dark. I could trace out where every part of the old house had been, and where the brewery had been, and where the gates, and where the casks. I had done so, and was looking along the desolate garden walk, when I beheld a solitary figure in it.

The figure showed itself aware of me, as I advanced. It had been moving towards me, but it stood still. As I drew nearer, I saw it to be the figure of a man. As I drew nearer yet, it was about to turn away, when it stopped, and let me come up with it. Then, it faltered, as if much surprised, and uttered my name, and I cried out,—

“Esteban!”

“I am greatly changed. I wonder you know me.”

The freshness of his beauty was indeed gone, but its indescribable majesty and its indescribable charm remained. Those attractions in it, I had seen before; what I had never seen before, was the saddened, softened light of the once proud eyes; what I had never felt before was the friendly touch of the once insensible hand.

We sat down on a bench that was near, and I said, “After so many years, it is strange that we should thus meet again, Esteban, here where our first meeting was! Do you often come back?”

“I have never been here since.”

“Nor I.”

The moon began to rise, and I thought of the placid look at the white ceiling, which had passed away. The moon began to rise, and I thought of the pressure on my hand when I had spoken the last words she had heard on earth.

Esteban was the next to break the silence that ensued between us.

“I have very often hoped and intended to come back, but have been prevented by many circumstances. Poor, poor old place!”

The silvery mist was touched with the first rays of the moonlight, and the same rays touched the tears that dropped from his eyes. Not knowing that I saw them, and setting himself to get the better of them, he said quietly,—

“Were you wondering, as you walked along, how it came to be left in this condition?”

“Yes, Esteban.”

“The ground belongs to me. It is the only possession I have not relinquished. Everything else has gone from me, little by little, but I have kept this. It was the subject of the only determined resistance I made in all the wretched years.”

“Is it to be built on?”

“At last, it is. I came here to take leave of it before its change. And you,” he said, in a voice of touching interest to a wanderer,—“you live abroad still?”

“Still.”

“And do well, I am sure?”

“I work pretty hard for a sufficient living, and therefore—yes, I do well.”

“I have often thought of you,” said Esteban.

“Have you?”

“Of late, very often. There was a long hard time when I kept far from me the remembrance of what I had thrown away when I was quite ignorant of its worth. But since my duty has not been incompatible with the admission of that remembrance, I have given it a place in my heart.”

“You have always held your place in my heart,” I answered.

And we were silent again until he spoke.

“I little thought,” said Esteban, “that I should take leave of you in taking leave of this spot. I am very glad to do so.”

“Glad to part again, Esteban? To me, parting is a painful thing. To me, the remembrance of our last parting has been ever mournful and painful.”

“But you said to me,” returned Esteban, very earnestly, “'Goddess bless you, Goddess forgive you!' And if you could say that to me then, you will not hesitate to say that to me now,—now, when suffering has been stronger than all other teaching, and has taught me to understand what your heart used to be. I have been bent and broken, but—I hope—into a better shape. Be as considerate and good to me as you were, and tell me we are friends.”

“We are friends,” said I, rising and bending over his, as he rose from the bench.

“And will continue friends apart,” said Esteban.

I took his hand in mine, and we went out of the ruined place; and, as the morning mists had risen long ago when I first left the forge, so the evening mists were rising now, and in all the broad expanse of tranquil light they showed to me, I saw no shadow of another parting from his.
